# Annotated Bibliography

> Updated 2026-04-01. Covers 367 papers in `processed/`.
> Prepared for book project: how mental phenotypes are made via evolution, development, and the tools of psychological science.

## Conventions
- Correlations marked with \* (p < .05) and \*\* (p < .01) where significance is reported
- No omnibus statistics (F-tests) or regression coefficients are included
- Relevance ratings: **HIGH** = directly addresses book themes; **MODERATE** = relevant supporting evidence; **LOW** = peripherally related; **TANGENTIAL** = minimal direct connection

---

### 1. Allemand & Flückiger (2017)

**Citation:** Allemand, M., & Flückiger, C. (2017). Changing personality traits: Some considerations from psychotherapy process-outcome research for intervention efforts on intentional personality change. *Journal of Psychotherapy Integration, 27*(4), 476–494. https://doi.org/10.1037/int0000094

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality change, intervention, heuristic framework, common change mechanisms, psychotherapy
- *Additional terms:* Grawe (2004), problem actuation, resource activation, TESSERA framework, state-to-trait pathway, deliberate practice, therapeutic common factors

#### Core Themes
- Adapts Grawe's (2004) four general change mechanisms from psychotherapy — problem actuation, resource activation, motivational clarification, mastery/coping — into four intervention design principles: discrepancy awareness, strengths-orientation, insight, and practice
- Adds intrapersonal (self-regulation, emotion regulation) and interpersonal (social support, reputation change) perspectives as additional design dimensions
- Grounds change in a hierarchical model: states → habits → traits, with bottom-up propagation; direct "top-down" trait instruction is less effective than targeting behavioral substrates
- Cites Hennecke et al.'s (2014) three preconditions for change: genuine desire, perceived feasibility, and successful habituation
- Explicitly positions the TESSERA framework (Wrzus & Roberts, 2017) as the micro-level mechanistic account of how behavioral episodes accumulate into trait change
- Acknowledges the evidence base was sparse at time of writing (four primary studies); the framework is heuristic

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides the theoretical bridge between psychotherapy research and volitional personality change science; the six-perspective framework gives vocabulary for characterizing what any personality change intervention does mechanistically.

---

### 2. Anglim et al. (2020)

**Citation:** Anglim, J., Horwood, S., Smillie, L. D., Marrero, R. J., & Wood, J. K. (2020). Predicting psychological and subjective well-being from personality: A meta-analysis. *Psychological Bulletin, 146*(4), 279–323. https://doi.org/10.1037/bul0000226

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R, IPIP-NEO, HEXACO-PI-R, BFAS, BFI
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, HEXACO, subjective well-being, psychological well-being, life satisfaction, positive affect, negative affect, meta-analysis, facets
- *Additional terms:* hedonic well-being, eudaimonic well-being, Ryff dimensions, incremental validity, facet-level prediction, reliability correction

#### Results Summary

**Meta-analytic domain-level correlations (observed r) with SWB and PWB:**

| Trait | SWL | PA | NA | Pos Rel | Autonomy | Env Mast | Pers Growth | Purpose | Self-Accept |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| N | -.39 | -.34 | .56 | -.43 | -.45 | -.58 | -.34 | -.45 | -.60 |
| E | .32 | .44 | -.21 | .47 | .26 | .38 | .39 | .39 | .43 |
| O | .08 | .24 | -.05 | .20 | .24 | .11 | .44 | .21 | .16 |
| A | .20 | .19 | -.25 | .39 | .10 | .28 | .31 | .28 | .28 |
| C | .27 | .35 | -.25 | .32 | .30 | .51 | .32 | .50 | .44 |

**HEXACO Extraversion** showed the strongest single-trait prediction (mean ρ = .48 across all 9 WB outcomes).

**Key reliability-corrected SWB correlations (k, n, ρ):**

| Association | k | n | ρ | 95% CI |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| N × Life Satisfaction | 224 | 158,934 | -.46 | [-.48, -.44] |
| E × Positive Affect | 157 | 51,731 | .51 | [.49, .53] |
| N × Negative Affect | 172 | 55,495 | .65 | [.63, .67] |
| C × Env Mastery | 16 | 6,160 | .61 | — |
| N × Self-Acceptance | 14 | 5,488 | -.69 | — |

**NEO facets — strongest predictors (N = 1,673):**

| Facet | Mean |r| across 9 WB outcomes |
|---|---|
| N3: Depression | .46 |
| N6: Vulnerability | .44 |
| C1: Competence | .41 |
| E6: Positive Emotions | .36 |
| C5: Self-Discipline | .36 |

**Incremental prediction — facets over domains (adjusted R²):**

| Instrument | Domains R² | Facets R² | % Increase |
|---|---|---|---|
| NEO-PI-R | .36 | .42 | 17% |
| IPIP-NEO | .50 | .60 | 20% |
| HEXACO-PI-R | .41 | .50 | 22% |
| BFAS | .56 | .60 | 7% |

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The most comprehensive meta-analytic synthesis of personality–well-being links (N = 334,567, k = 462), providing canonical effect-size benchmarks and the definitive empirical case for why facet-level measurement yields substantially better prediction than domain-level measurement.

---

### 3. Anusic et al. (2014)

**Citation:** Anusic, I., Yap, S. C. Y., & Lucas, R. E. (2014). Testing set-point theory in a Swiss national sample: Reaction and adaptation to major life events. *Social Indicators Research, 119*(3), 1265–1288. https://doi.org/10.1007/s11205-013-0541-2

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal
**Instruments:** Single-item life satisfaction (0–10 scale)
**Level of Detail:** Other (SWB adaptation)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* set-point theory, subjective well-being, hedonic adaptation, life satisfaction, life events
- *Additional terms:* hedonic treadmill, Swiss Household Panel, propensity score matching, nonlinear growth curve, normative age change

#### Results Summary

**Event effects on life satisfaction (comparison-group-corrected model, SD units):**

| Life Event | Peak Change | Long-Term Asymptote vs. Counterfactual |
|---|---|---|
| Marriage | +0.49 SD above counterfactual | Non-significant (may protect against normative decline) |
| Childbirth | +0.42 SD above counterfactual | Non-significant (decline explained by normative trends) |
| Widowhood | −1.10 SD below counterfactual | Small sample; lasting decline possible |
| Unemployment | −0.38 SD during bout | Long-term: non-significant |
| Disability | −0.70 SD below counterfactual | −0.37 SD lasting decline beyond normative trends |

Adaptation is not inevitable — disability produces durable SWB reduction. Many "lasting" effects of marriage, childbirth, and unemployment dissolve once normative age-related decline is controlled.

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates that the "hedonic treadmill" metaphor is not uniformly accurate and that methodological choices (normative trend controls, comparison groups) fundamentally alter conclusions about psychological stability — relevant to the book's discussion of how measurement shapes understanding.

---

### 4. Beck & Jackson (2022)

**Citation:** Beck, E. D., & Jackson, J. J. (2022). A mega-analysis of personality prediction: Robustness and boundary conditions. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 122*(3), 523–553. https://doi.org/10.1037/pspp0000386

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Mega-Analysis, Longitudinal
**Instruments:** Big Five (study-specific: BFI, NEO, IPIP-NEO, MIDI)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, mega-analysis, longitudinal prediction, life outcomes, propensity score matching, specification curve analysis
- *Additional terms:* robustness, boundary conditions, GSOEP, MIDUS, HRS, BHPS, HILDA, odds ratio

#### Results Summary

**Mega-analytic ORs across 10 international panels (N = 171,395), 14 outcomes (89% CIs):**

| Outcome | N | E | O | A | C |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Self-Rated Health | .79\*\* | 1.08\*\* | 1.02 | 1.04\*\* | 1.10\*\* |
| Unemployment | 1.09\*\* | .97 | .95\*\* | .95\*\* | .88\*\* |
| Divorce | 1.02 | .98 | .99 | .92\*\* | .93\*\* |
| Relationship Satisfaction | .86\*\* | 1.06\*\* | 1.01 | 1.13\*\* | 1.07\*\* |
| Smoking | 1.13\*\* | .97 | .94\*\* | .90\*\* | .84\*\* |
| Income | .98 | 1.04\* | 1.07\*\* | .98 | 1.09\*\* |
| Educational Attainment | .99 | 1.03 | 1.10\*\* | .99 | 1.09\*\* |
| Volunteering | .98 | 1.04\*\* | 1.08\*\* | 1.10\*\* | 1.06\*\* |
| Mortality | .98 | .98 | .96\* | .99 | .96 |

Conscientiousness is the most broadly predictive trait. Neuroticism most strongly predicts lower self-rated health (OR = .79\*\*). Overall, 37.14% of fixed effects were robust across the full specification curve.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The most methodologically rigorous test of whether Big Five traits predict real-world outcomes, using propensity score matching and specification curves across 10 panels — directly relevant to the real-world validity of personality measurement.

---

### 5. Bleidorn (2024)

**Citation:** Bleidorn, W. (2024). Toward a theory of lifespan personality trait development. *Annual Review of Developmental Psychology, 6*, 455–478. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-devpsych-010923-101709

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality development, lifespan, trait change, stability, causal theory
- *Additional terms:* maturity principle, cumulative continuity, construct validity, phenomenon robustness, causal explanation, WEIRD samples

#### Core Themes
- Evaluates 20 years of personality development research against three criteria for a strong psychological theory: construct validity, phenomenon robustness, and causal explanation
- Mean-level maturity principle (increases in A, C, E stability with age) is well-established phenomenologically but causally unexplained
- Cumulative continuity principle (rank-order stability increases with age) is robust but mechanism is unclear
- Facets within the same domain can show divergent developmental trajectories, challenging domain-level developmental theories
- The field has rich description but weak causal inference — most evidence is correlational/observational
- Calls for stronger theory-building integrating biology, social roles, and identity with personality development
- Notes WEIRD sample dominance as a limitation on generalizability
- Urges the field to move from "what changes" to "why it changes"

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly maps the gap between descriptive knowledge and causal understanding of personality development — the exact space the book aims to fill with its evolutionary, developmental, and measurement-science framing.

---

### 6. Bonner et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Bonner, C. V., Hankin, B. L., Young, J. F., & Roberts, B. W. (2025). Growth following adversity is rare: Evidence from a multi-informant longitudinal study of children and adolescents. *Journal of Research in Personality, 117*, 104628. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jrp.2025.104628

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal
**Instruments:** EATQ-R (effortful control: activation control, attention, inhibitory control; emotional stability: fear, frustration, shyness, aggression reversed), YLSI
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet, Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* effortful control, emotional stability, childhood adversity, temperament change, adolescent development
- *Additional terms:* latent growth curve, EATQ-R, stress, multi-informant, post-adversity growth

#### Results Summary

| Adversity Variable | Outcome | r | Sig |
|---|---|---|---|
| Initial adversity (T1) | EC slope (youth) | +.01 to +.04 | ns |
| Initial adversity (T1) | ES slope (youth) | +.01 to +.04 | ns |
| Increasing adversity (T2−T1) | EC slope (youth) | −.50 | \*\* |
| Increasing adversity (T2−T1) | EC slope (parent) | −.31 | \*\* |
| Increasing adversity (T2−T1) | ES slope (youth) | −.54 | \*\* |

Initial adversity at T1 was not associated with temperament trajectory. Only adversity that *increased* during early adolescence predicted declining temperament growth. Of those showing positive growth, 79–83% experienced low adversity.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Distinguishes timing and type of environmental stressor as critical for temperament development — escalating stress during adolescence disrupts growth while stable adversity does not, directly supporting the book's thesis about how developmental context shapes emerging phenotypes.

---

### 7. Border et al. (2019)

**Citation:** Border, R., Johnson, E. C., Evans, L. M., Smolen, A., Berley, N., Sullivan, P. F., & Keller, M. C. (2019). No support for historical candidate gene or candidate gene-by-interaction hypotheses for major depression across multiple large samples. *American Journal of Psychiatry, 176*(5), 376–387. https://doi.org/10.1176/appi.ajp.2018.18070881

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Twin/Genetic
**Instruments:** Depression phenotypes (DSM, PHQ, CES-D); 18 candidate gene polymorphisms (5-HTTLPR, BDNF Val66Met, COMT, DRD2, etc.)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* candidate gene, gene-by-environment interaction, 5-HTTLPR, major depression, replication failure
- *Additional terms:* Bonferroni correction, polygenic score, false positive, UK Biobank, publication bias, SKAT

#### Results Summary
- N = 62,138–443,264 across samples. Pre-registered.
- After Bonferroni correction: **NO** candidate gene polymorphism showed a significant main effect on depression
- **NO** candidate gene showed a significant G×E interaction with stress
- Candidate genes were no more associated with depression than randomly selected non-candidate SNPs
- Measurement error ruled out as explanation (more reliable composite measures produced identical nulls)
- Historical candidate gene findings are likely false positives from small samples, publication bias, and inadequate multiple-testing correction

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
A methodological landmark establishing the failure of the candidate gene paradigm — essential context for the transition to GWAS and for understanding how study design and measurement interact with replication in genetic research on behavioral phenotypes.

---

### 8. Conner & Norman (2026)

**Citation:** Conner, M., & Norman, P. (2026). Attitudes, intentions, and behavior change. *Annual Review of Psychology, 77*, 311–337. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-psych-013125-042110

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Systematic Review, Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* attitudes, intentions, behavior change, intervention
- *Additional terms:* experimental medicine approach, Operating Conditions Framework, mechanisms of action, behavior change techniques

#### Core Themes
- Applies a four-stage experimental medicine framework (identify, validate, engage, intervene) to attitude–intention–behavior pathways
- Attitude-behavior correlations are small-to-medium; experimental attitude change produces small behavior change
- Moderation is pervasive — person, context, behavior type, and measurement approach all matter
- Challenges the assumption that changing a psychological variable straightforwardly changes behavior

**Relevance to Book Project:** LOW
Operates in social-cognitive / health behavior space largely orthogonal to the book's focus. The experimental medicine framework has abstract methodological relevance for validating any psychological construct's causal role.

---

### 9. Donnellan & Lucas (2008)

**Citation:** Donnellan, M. B., & Lucas, R. E. (2008). Age differences in the Big Five across the life span: Evidence from two national samples. *Psychology and Aging, 23*(3), 558–566. https://doi.org/10.1037/a0012897

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** BFI (15-item version)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, age differences, lifespan, cross-sectional, national samples
- *Additional terms:* BHPS, GSOEP, maturity principle, measurement invariance, cross-national comparison

#### Results Summary

Two large national samples: British Household Panel Study (BHPS, N ≥ 14,039) and German Socio-Economic Panel Study (GSOEP, N ≥ 20,852), ages 16–mid 80s.

| Trait | BHPS Pattern | GSOEP Pattern | Consistent? |
|---|---|---|---|
| Extraversion | Negative with age | Negative with age | Yes |
| Agreeableness | Positive with age | Positive with age | Yes |
| Conscientiousness | Peak in middle age | Peak in middle age | Yes |
| Openness | Negative with age | Negative with age | Yes |
| Neuroticism | Negative with age | Positive with age | **No** |

The Neuroticism discrepancy across samples highlights that cross-national measurement differences can produce opposite age-pattern conclusions.

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Establishes cross-national Big Five age patterns and flags an important measurement cautionary tale (Neuroticism showing opposite trends in different samples).

---

### 10. Graham et al. (2020)

**Citation:** Graham, E. K., Weston, S. J., Gerstorf, D., Yoneda, T. B., Booth, T., Beam, C. R., ... & Mroczek, D. K. (2020). Trajectories of Big Five personality traits: A coordinated analysis of 16 longitudinal samples. *European Journal of Personality, 34*, 301–321. https://doi.org/10.1002/per.2259

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal, Mega-Analysis
**Instruments:** Various Big Five measures across 16 panel studies
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, trajectories, coordinated analysis, longitudinal, personality change
- *Additional terms:* IALSA network, multilevel growth models, individual differences in change, random slopes

#### Results Summary

Coordinated analysis of 16 longitudinal samples (N > 60,000), IALSA network. Meta-analytic slope estimates (annual change in standardized units):

| Trait | Mean Annual Slope | Direction |
|---|---|---|
| Neuroticism | Small negative | Decline |
| Extraversion | Small negative | Decline |
| Openness | Small negative | Decline |
| Agreeableness | Near zero | Stable/slight increase |
| Conscientiousness | Near zero to small negative | Stable/slight decline in old age |

Critical finding: **Individual differences in change are large and consistent** — population-level mean trajectories do not describe individual trajectories. Random slope variances were significant for all five traits across virtually all samples.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that personality trajectories are genuinely heterogeneous across individuals, supporting the book's argument that traits are products of individual developmental histories rather than fixed species-typical loadings.

---

### 11. Graham & Lachman (2014)

**Citation:** Graham, E. K., & Lachman, M. E. (2014). Personality traits, facets and cognitive performance: Age differences in their relations. *Personality and Individual Differences, 59*, 89–95. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.paid.2013.11.011

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R, BTACT (Brief Test of Adult Cognition by Telephone)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet, Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* neuroticism, openness, cognitive performance, facets, age moderation
- *Additional terms:* Ideas facet, Assertiveness facet, Depression facet, verbal fluency, inductive reasoning, processing speed

#### Results Summary

N = 154, ages 22–84.

**Facet-level associations (incremental over domains):**

| Facet | Outcome | Std. Coeff. | Sig |
|---|---|---|---|
| O5: Ideas | Verbal fluency | +.24 | \*\* |
| E3: Assertiveness | Inductive reasoning | −.25 | \*\* |
| N3: Depression | Inductive reasoning | −.23 | \* |
| N2: Angry Hostility | Processing speed | −.19 | \* |
| C1: Competence | Processing speed | +.19 | \* |

Age moderation: Neuroticism facet–cognition links generally stronger at older ages. N3 Depression × Age predicted inductive reasoning\*. No significant effects emerged for episodic memory or working memory.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates facet-level specificity in personality–cognition associations obscured at the domain level, directly supporting the argument that facets are the appropriate unit for linking mental phenotypes to cognitive outcomes.

---

### 12. Greenwood & Garfinkel (2025)

**Citation:** Greenwood, B. M., & Garfinkel, S. N. (2025). Interoceptive mechanisms and emotional processing. *Annual Review of Psychology, 76*, 59–86. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-psych-020924-125202

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Systematic Review
**Instruments:** Heartbeat detection tasks, interoceptive self-report scales
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* interoception, emotion, affect, mental health, interventions
- *Additional terms:* afferent signals, interoceptive accuracy, predictive processing, visceral signals, emotional granularity

#### Core Themes
- Interoception is a hierarchical, multi-dimensional process spanning afferent signal detection, accuracy, beliefs, insight, and attribution — paralleling facet-level analysis in personality
- Emotion defined as a "programmed neural response that evolved to serve adaptive functions" — explicitly evolutionary framing
- Distinct interoceptive dimensions have differential relationships with emotional processing capacities
- Clinical conditions (anxiety, depression, PTSD, BPD, autism) show condition-specific interoceptive profiles
- Bridges embodied and cognitive levels: interoception operates across conscious and unconscious registers

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Interoception offers a plausible sub-personal mechanism through which bodily/developmental variation gets transduced into emotional phenotypic variation. The dimensional decomposition of a previously unitary construct is methodologically instructive.

---

### 13. Haehner et al. (2025a) — Life Events

**Citation:** Haehner, P., Krämer, M. D., Wright, A. J., & Bleidorn, W. (2025). Life events and personality trait change: A coordinated data analysis. *European Journal of Personality*. https://doi.org/10.1177/08902070251356148

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal, Meta-Analysis (coordinated data analysis)
**Instruments:** Big Five (panel-specific measures)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* life events, personality change, coordinated data analysis, panel data, longitudinal
- *Additional terms:* fixed-effects regression, meta-analytic aggregation, set-point theory, mean-level change

#### Results Summary

CDA across 7 panel studies (HILDA, HRS, LISS, MIDUS, NLSY, PAIRFAM, SOEP; N = 196,256).

| Life Event | Outcome | b_std | Sig |
|---|---|---|---|
| New employment | Conscientiousness | +0.05 | \*\* |
| New employment | Emotional stability | +0.04–0.05 | \*\* |
| Marriage | Openness | −0.05 | \* |
| Widowhood | Emotional stability | −0.07 to −0.11 | \*\* |
| Widowhood | Extraversion | −0.08 | \*\* |
| Widowhood | Life satisfaction | −0.47 (1 yr) | \*\* |
| Retirement | Big Five traits | ~0.00–0.02 | ns |

Average effect across all events and traits: b_std ≈ 0.05. Most personality changes were temporary, returning toward baseline within 2+ years.

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Quantifies environmental malleability of personality using the most rigorous multi-panel design available, establishing realistic baseline effect sizes for experiential influences on personality.

---

### 14. Haehner et al. (2025b) — Timing

**Citation:** Haehner, P., Wright, A. J., Lubczyk, T., Andrae, R., Asselmann, E., Buecker, S., Hopwood, C. J., & Bleidorn, W. (2025). Tailoring personality interventions: How timing, context, and strategies influence proximal intervention outcomes. [Preprint]

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention, Experience Sampling, Longitudinal
**Instruments:** Smartphone ESM (m-Path), mobile sensing, 12 TONS-classified strategies
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* JITAI, CHILL Study, TONS, proximal intervention outcomes, intervention adherence
- *Additional terms:* state-level vs. habit-level interventions, mobile sensing, ecological momentary intervention, receptivity

#### Results Summary

CHILL Study: N = 399, 39,878 ESM assessments over 6 weeks. Bonferroni-corrected α = .0125.

**Best strategy:** Emotional support (State/Affective) — Response OR = 1.77\*\*, Compliance OR = 2.15\*\*, Satisfaction and Positive affect both significant\*\*

**Poorest response:** Skill building via modeling OR = 0.52\*\*; Cognitive restructuring OR = 0.66\*\*

**Timing effects:** Response increases later in day (OR = 5.40\*\*) but compliance decreases (OR = 0.79\*\*). Both response and compliance decline over weeks (OR = 0.60\*\*, 0.88\*\*).

**Context effects:** State neuroticism → lower compliance (OR = 0.93\*\*) and positive affect (β = −0.19\*\*). Stressor occurrence → lower satisfaction (β = −0.11\*\*) and affect (β = −0.28\*\*). Home location → higher compliance (OR = 1.34\*\*).

**Strategy × context interactions:** Cognitive restructuring declined most steeply over weeks; emotional support remained robust under high neuroticism states.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides rare within-intervention ESM evidence that timing and situational context differentially moderate personality state expressions — the micro-environmental trigger mechanism the book treats as the bridge between environment and trait change.

---

### 15. Hanfstingl et al. (2024)

**Citation:** Hanfstingl, B., Oberleiter, S., Pietschnig, J., Tran, U. S., & Voracek, M. (2024). Detecting jingle and jangle fallacies by identifying consistencies and variabilities in study specifications. *Frontiers in Psychology, 15*, 1404060. https://doi.org/10.3389/fpsyg.2024.1404060

**Purpose:** Commentary/Editorial
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* jingle fallacy, jangle fallacy, construct validity, specification curve analysis
- *Additional terms:* construct proliferation, discriminant validity, NLP/GPT-based detection, harvest plots

#### Core Themes
- Proposes a 4-step framework: problem definition → specification curve analysis → harvest plot visualization → systematic reconceptualization
- Argues NLP/LLM tools can automate detection of semantic overlap across instrument item banks
- Situates jingle/jangle within the broader crisis of construct proliferation in psychology

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides methodological vocabulary for diagnosing construct validity problems relevant to the book's treatment of personality measurement — especially the overlap between traits and skills.

---

### 16. Huang et al. (2026)

**Citation:** Huang, M., Zhang, X., Soto, C., & Evans, J. (2026). Designing AI-agents with personalities: A psychometric approach. *Personality Science, 7*, 1–26. https://doi.org/10.1177/25700071025140647

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** BFI-2, Mini-Markers
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* large language models, Big Five, BFI-2, AI agents, personality prompting, convergent validity
- *Additional terms:* cosine similarity, t-SNE, measurement equivalence, safety fine-tuning, response acquiescence

#### Results Summary

Three studies evaluating personality prompting approaches for LLMs (GPT-4o, Llama, DeepSeek).

**Input-output correlations (BFI-2 input → Mini-Markers output):**
- Human reference: Mean r = .80
- Best LLMs (BFI-2-Likert condition): GPT-4o Mean r = .91; Llama = .90; DeepSeek = .90 — exceeding human reference
- Binary prompt conditions produced artifactual near-perfect factor loadings (.94–.99), far exceeding human norms (.36–.84) — indicating overconfident/acquiescent responding

**Behavioral validity (Study 3, BFI-2-Expanded):** Openness and Extraversion positively predicted risk-taking\*\*; Neuroticism negatively predicted risk-taking — consistent with human data. But LLM moral dilemma responses were inflated by safety fine-tuning.

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Establishes psychometric conditions under which AI agents can validly instantiate personality phenotypes; demonstrates how measurement procedures can generate phenotypic artifacts rather than capture real individual differences.

---

### 17. Hudson & Fraley (2015)

**Citation:** Hudson, N. W., & Fraley, R. C. (2015). Volitional personality trait change: Can people choose to change their personality traits? *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 109*(3), 490–507. https://doi.org/10.1037/pspp0000021

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal, Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** BFI (44 items), Change-BFI (C-BFI), Daily Behavior Questionnaire (Study 2)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* volitional personality change, change goals, Big Five, implementation intentions, daily behavior
- *Additional terms:* bottom-up change, state-to-trait pathway, goal dissipation, behavioral mediation, self-regulation

#### Results Summary

**Study 1 (n = 135): Trait–goal correlations at T1:**

| Trait | Trait–Goal r |
|---|---|
| Extraversion | −.44** |
| Agreeableness | −.47** |
| Conscientiousness | −.47** |
| Emotional Stability | −.59** |
| Openness | −.37** |

All negative: lower-scoring individuals desire more increase.

**Study 1: Month × Change-Goal interaction (b per month per 1-SD goal):**

| Trait | b | 95% CI |
|---|---|---|
| Extraversion | .04 | [.02, .06] |
| Agreeableness | .05 | [.03, .08] |
| Conscientiousness | .05 | [.02, .07] |
| Emotional Stability | .07 | [.05, .09] |
| Openness | .02 | [−.00, .04] |

**Study 2 (n = 151): Implementation intention intervention (Goal × Treatment × Month):**

| Trait | b | 95% CI |
|---|---|---|
| Extraversion | .04 | [.01, .08] |
| Conscientiousness | .08 | [.04, .12] |
| Emotional Stability | .06 | [.02, .10] |

Bidirectional behavior–trait mediation confirmed. Goal dissipation: achieved change reduced desire to change further (Extraversion β = −.17*).

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational empirical paper for volitional personality change. Establishes that ordinary people systematically move toward change goals; bidirectional behavior–trait mediation directly instantiates the bottom-up state → habit → trait mechanistic account.

---

### 18. Hudson et al. (2019)

**Citation:** Hudson, N. W., Briley, D. A., Chopik, W. J., & Derringer, J. (2019). You have to follow through: Attaining behavioral change goals predicts volitional personality change. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 117*(4), 839–857. https://doi.org/10.1037/pspp0000221

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal, Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** BFI-2 (60 items), Change-BFI-2, 250 behavioral challenges (50 per Big Five domain)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* behavioral challenges, follow-through, volitional personality change, self-regulation
- *Additional terms:* action-outcome gap, challenge completion vs. acceptance, micro-intervention, scaffolded behavior

#### Results Summary

N = 377, 15-week intensive longitudinal.

**Month × Challenge interactions:**

| Challenge Type | E | A | C | ES | O |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Completed | .04* | .01 ns | .04* | .04* | −.03* |
| Accepted (not completed) | −.03* | −.05* | −.03* | −.02* | .02 ns |

**Critical finding:** Completing challenges → growth; accepting but NOT completing → *decline*. The follow-through failure effect undercuts change rather than supporting it. Openness shows a paradoxical negative completion effect.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Essential for the book's account: behavioral action — not intention or planning — is the proximate cause of trait change. The phenotype literally fails to materialize when the behavioral substrate is absent.

---

### 19. Hudson et al. (2020)

**Citation:** Hudson, N. W., Fraley, R. C., Chopik, W. J., & Briley, D. A. (2020). Change goals robustly predict trait growth: A mega-analysis of a dozen intensive longitudinal studies examining volitional change. *Social Psychological and Personality Science*. https://doi.org/10.1177/1948550619878423

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Mega-Analysis, Longitudinal
**Instruments:** BFI (35%), BFI-2 (59%), hybrid IPIP-120/BFI (6%)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* mega-analysis, volitional personality change, Big Five, change goals, domain-specificity
- *Additional terms:* participant-level pooling, random slopes, cross-domain spillover

#### Results Summary

n = 2,238 across 12 studies.

**Cumulative 16-week growth per 1-SD change goal:**

| Trait | b (cumulative) | 95% CI |
|---|---|---|
| Emotional Stability | .181 | [.159, .203] |
| Extraversion | .142 | [.122, .162] |
| Conscientiousness | .092 | [.069, .115] |
| Agreeableness | .053 | [.028, .078] |
| Openness | .039 | [.015, .062] |

All five significant. Random-slope variances significant for all traits — people differ reliably in rate and direction of change.

Cross-domain specificity confirmed: cross-domain average b ≈ .002 (near-zero). Change goals are largely domain-specific.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The most statistically precise estimates in the volitional change literature. The cumulative 16-week coefficients translate into the book's argument that people who want to change accumulate meaningful differences within a semester. Random-slope heterogeneity supports traits as products of individual developmental histories.

---

### 20. Hudson (2023)

**Citation:** Hudson, N. W. (2023). Lighten the darkness: Personality interventions targeting agreeableness also reduce participants' levels of the dark triad. *Journal of Personality, 91*, 901–916. https://doi.org/10.1111/jopy.12714

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal, Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** BFI-2-S (30 items), Short Dark Triad (SD3, 27 items)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Dark Triad, Machiavellianism, narcissism, psychopathy, volitional personality change, agreeableness
- *Additional terms:* collateral trait change, cross-domain intervention effects, trait interconnection

#### Results Summary

N = 467, 16-wave weekly design.

**Selected bivariate correlations:**

| Variables | r |
|---|---|
| Agreeableness × Machiavellianism | −.45** |
| Agreeableness × Narcissism | −.30** |
| Agreeableness × Psychopathy | −.35** |
| Mach trait × Mach goal | +.30** (desire more Mach) |

**Agreeableness challenges → Dark Triad change:**

| Dark Triad Trait | b | 95% CI |
|---|---|---|
| Machiavellianism | −.02 | [−.03, −.01]* |
| Narcissism | −.01 | [−.02, −.00]* |
| Psychopathy | −.01 | [−.02, −.00]* |

All three significant and negative. Agreeableness trait change fully mediated psychopathy reduction; Mach and Narc reductions persisted after controlling for A change (partially independent pathways).

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Extends volitional change to antisocial traits and introduces collateral intervention logic — intervening on one trait node propagates through the phenotypic network. The positive Mach trait-goal correlation (higher Mach → desire more Mach) challenges the universalist assumption that self-improvement is always prosocial.

### 21. Jackson et al. (2015)

**Citation:** Jackson, J. J., Connolly, J. J., Garrison, S. M., Leveille, M. M., & Connolly, S. L. (2015). Your friends know how long you will live: A 75-year study of peer-rated personality traits. *Psychological Science, 26*(3), 335–340. https://doi.org/10.1177/0956797614561800

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal
**Instruments:** Kelly Personality Rating Scale, BFI, Mini-IPIP
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, mortality, longevity, peer-rated personality, 75-year follow-up
- *Additional terms:* Kelly Longitudinal Study, informant accuracy, social accuracy model

#### Results Summary

Kelly/Connolly Longitudinal Study, N ≈ 600, followed 1935–2013.

**Peer-rated personality predicting mortality (Cox regression):**

| Trait (Peer-Rated) | Direction | Sig |
|---|---|---|
| Conscientiousness | Protective | * |
| Openness | Protective | * |
| Neuroticism | Non-significant | ns |
| Extraversion | Non-significant | ns |
| Agreeableness | Non-significant | ns |

Self-rated personality showed weaker and generally non-significant mortality prediction. Peers' ratings outperform self-ratings in predicting longevity.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that personality phenotypes carry biologically meaningful predictive signal for survival across the lifespan, even via peer report. The self vs. peer differential speaks directly to the book's measurement themes.

### 22. King & Hicks (2021)

**Citation:** King, L. A., & Hicks, J. A. (2021). The science of meaning in life. *Annual Review of Psychology, 72*, 561–584. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-psych-072420-122921

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** Meaning in Life Questionnaire (MLQ), Purpose in Life Test (PIL)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* meaning in life, purpose in life, comprehension, significance, eudaimonic well-being
- *Additional terms:* MLQ Presence, narrative identity, mortality awareness, terror management, positive affect, social connections

#### Core Themes
- Meaning is commonplace: across 122 mean MLQ-Presence scores (N = 27,635), only 10 fell below the midpoint
- Three constituent facets: comprehension/coherence, purpose, and existential mattering/significance
- Positive affect is a robust causal antecedent (not just correlate) of meaning
- Social connections and religion are the strongest correlates across samples and methods
- Self-continuity and mental time travel (autobiographical memory, nostalgia, prospection) enhance meaning through coherence
- Mortality awareness can enhance rather than undermine meaning (via dual-existential systems)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides the authoritative review of meaning as a psychological construct. Relevant to chapters on eudaimonic well-being and how personality traits (C, E) foster meaning through goal engagement and social connection.

---

### 23. Kteily & Brandt (2025)

**Citation:** Kteily, N. S., & Brandt, M. J. (2025). Ideology: Psychological similarities and differences across the ideological spectrum reexamined. *Annual Review of Psychology, 76*, 501–529. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-psych-020124-115253

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** RWA, LWA scales; Big Five (implied)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* ideology, ideological asymmetry, authoritarianism, motivated reasoning, prejudice
- *Additional terms:* cognitive rigidity, threat-sensitivity, dogmatism, political personality, measurement artifacts

#### Core Themes
- Whether left and right are psychologically asymmetric or symmetric depends heavily on which scales are used and whether items confound ideological content with psychological style
- Dispositions (values, personality, cognitive rigidity, threat-sensitivity) organize the first major section
- Measurement artifacts are shown to be decisive for conclusions about asymmetry
- Motivated reasoning and misinformation susceptibility evidence is weaker than older literature assumed

**Relevance to Book Project:** LOW
Engages personality instrumentally (traits as predictors of ideology). The most usable material is methodological: conclusions about psychological constructs shift dramatically with measurement choices.

### 24. Kuper et al. (2025a) — Behavior

**Citation:** Kuper, N., Breil, S. M., Utesch, K., & Back, M. D. (2025). Predicting more behavior more of the time: On the behavioral nature of different personality trait measures. [PsyArXiv preprint]

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experience Sampling, Cross-Sectional, Longitudinal
**Instruments:** BFI-SOEP (15 items), bipolar adjective scales, trained behavioral coding
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* global vs. average-state measurement, self vs. other-report, behavioral prediction, incremental validity
- *Additional terms:* 2×2 framework, acquaintance effect, social accuracy model, multi-method assessment

#### Results Summary

N = 88, CONNECT longitudinal cohort. Six trait operationalizations compared in predicting lab behavior.

**Correlations with laboratory behavior (Extraversion composite):**

| Measure | Mean r |
|---|---|
| Global self-report | .23 |
| Global informant-report | .30 |
| Time-based self-reported states | .19 |
| Time-based peer-reported states | .39 |
| Event-based self-reported states | .20 |
| Event-based peer-reported states | .34 |

**Key findings:** Other-report consistently outperformed self-report at every level. Time-based peer-reported states significantly incremented global informant-report on all four behavioral dimensions. Self-reported states rarely added beyond global self-report.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly addresses the measurement architecture of the personality phenotype. The consistent superiority of other-report has implications for the book's discussion of measurement as capturing versus constructing personality.

---

### 25. Kuper et al. (2025b) — Dynamics

**Citation:** Kuper, N., Juchem, C., Allemand, M., Kroencke, L., Olaru, G., Stieger, M., Wrzus, C., & Haehner, P. (2025). Leveraging personality dynamics for volitional personality change interventions. [PsyArXiv preprint]

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A (references ESM, diary methods, network analysis)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality dynamics, intraindividual variability, state-state associations, situational contingencies, attractors
- *Additional terms:* JITAIs, personalized interventions, idiographic assessment, between-person differences in within-person processes

#### Core Themes
- Defines five types of personality dynamics: mean-level change, variability, state-state associations, situational contingencies, and temporal dynamics/attractors
- **Opportunity 1 — Dynamics as intervention targets:** Reducing excessive variability in negative affect is itself a meaningful change goal, distinct from reducing mean negative affect
- **Opportunity 2 — Between-person personalization:** Individual dynamic profiles predict which strategies work for whom (social-triggered vs. rumination-triggered negative affect require different approaches)
- **Opportunity 3 — Within-person momentary triggers (JITAIs):** Real-time ESM monitoring can trigger tailored interventions at moments of peak need
- Current meta-analytic effect size (d = 0.22) is small and likely improvable through dynamics-informed personalization
- Methodological infrastructure (ESM, multilevel modeling, network analysis) already exists — the barrier is conceptual integration

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The three-opportunity framework directly supports the book's argument that mental phenotypes are dynamic, environmentally coupled processes; provides the intervention-science translation of ideas the book develops at evolutionary, developmental, and psychometric levels.

---

### 26. Laskowski et al. (2022)

**Citation:** Laskowski, K. L., Chang, C.-C., Sheehy, K., & Aguiñaga, J. (2022). Consistent individual behavioral variation: What do we know and where are we going? *Annual Review of Ecology, Evolution, and Systematics, 53*, 161–182. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-ecolsys-102220-011451

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Systematic Review, Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other (behavioral ecology)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* animal personality, behavioral syndromes, temperament, coping styles
- *Additional terms:* among-individual variation, within-individual variation, pace-of-life syndrome, state-dependent feedback, behavioral repeatability

#### Core Themes
- Individual behavioral variation is both the raw material for and the product of natural selection — not noise around an optimum
- Behavioral variance can be partitioned into among-individual and within-individual components, directly paralleling psychometric true-score/error variance decomposition
- Two competing theoretical frameworks: pace-of-life syndrome hypothesis (fast/slow life history co-varies with bold/shy personality) vs. state-dependent feedback models (current state locks individuals into consistent trajectories)
- Behavioral syndromes (correlated suites of behaviors) across taxa from fish to primates suggest deep evolutionary origins for trait covariation
- Terminology fragmentation (personality, temperament, coping styles, behavioral types) initially impeded the field — parallel to human personality labeling debates

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides the strongest evolutionary anchor: individual behavioral variation as both raw material for and product of natural selection maps directly onto the book's evolutionary layer. The among/within variance decomposition parallels psychometric true-score models.

---

### 27. Löckenhoff et al. (2009)

**Citation:** Löckenhoff, C. E., Terracciano, A., & Costa, P. T., Jr. (2009). Five-factor model personality traits and the retirement transition. *Psychology and Aging, 24*(3), 722–728. https://doi.org/10.1037/a0015121

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal, Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* retirement, personality change, NEO-PI-R, facets, aging
- *Additional terms:* Compliance facet, Activity facet, occupational transition, life transition

#### Results Summary

Longitudinal (N = 367 retirees, Wave 1 1995 → Wave 2 2004–05):

| Domain/Facet | Change | Magnitude | Sig |
|---|---|---|---|
| Agreeableness (domain) | Increase | ~0.4 SD | * |
| A4: Compliance | Increase (drove A effect) | — | * |
| E4: Activity | Decrease | ~0.4 SD | * |
| Neuroticism | No change | — | ns |
| Conscientiousness | No change | — | ns |

Cross-sectional predictors of retirement satisfaction (N = 144): N3: Depression (−*), N6: Vulnerability (−*), E1: Warmth (+*), E2: Gregariousness (+*), E4: Activity (+*), E6: Positive Emotions (+*).

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Occupational role transitions produce narrow, facet-specific personality change rather than broad domain shifts, supporting the argument that facets capture developmentally meaningful specificity.

### 28. Lu et al. (2023)

**Citation:** Lu, J. G., Benet-Martínez, V., & Wang, L. C. (2023). A socioecological-genetic framework of culture and personality. *Annual Review of Psychology, 74*, 363–390. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-psych-032420-032631

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review, Twin/Genetic
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* culture and personality, socioecological-genetic framework, individualism-collectivism, gene-culture co-evolution
- *Additional terms:* subsistence style, modernization, climato-economic theory, frontier settlement, pathogen prevalence, epigenetics

#### Core Themes
- Bidirectional causation: culture shapes personality (via socialization, norms, ecology) and personality aggregates shape culture (via selective migration, institutional design)
- Five socioecological traditions synthesized: subsistence style (rice vs. wheat), modernization (industrialization → individualism), climato-economic (harsh climates → high C), frontier settlement (migrants → high E, O), pathogen prevalence (high pathogen → low O, E)
- Heritability ≈ 40–50% cross-culturally, but gene-culture interactions modulate expression (e.g., 5-HTTLPR × collectivism)
- Epigenetics provides a mechanism by which cultural/socioecological conditions leave biological traces without altering DNA sequence
- Cross-temporal trends: rising E and individualism in developing nations over the 20th century → socioecological change, not genetic change

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides the broadest ecological and cultural frame, demonstrating that even substantially heritable traits are embedded in socioecological systems that modulate their expression, distribution, and meaning.

---

### 29. Ma et al. (under review)

**Citation:** Ma, W., Nissen, A. T., Hawks, Z. W., Graham, E. K., Mroczek, D. K., & Beck, E. D. (manuscript under review). Personality trait and state associations with cognitive functioning and variability in middle and older adults. [Preprint]

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experience Sampling, Longitudinal
**Instruments:** BFI-2, Toronto Mobile Battery (N-back, Verbal Paired Associates, Digit Symbol Matching)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* experience sampling, intraindividual variability, mixed-effect location scale models, Bayesian estimation
- *Additional terms:* conscientiousness paradox, trait-state distinction, daily cognition, within-person fluctuation

#### Results Summary

N = 183, ages 45–74, 20 days × 5 assessments/day. Bayesian MELSMs.

**Trait-level effects on cognitive mean performance:**

| Trait | Outcome | γ | Sig |
|---|---|---|---|
| Conscientiousness | Working memory | −.150 | * (worse WM) |
| Openness | Working memory | +.248 | * |
| Openness | Episodic memory | +.312 | * |

**State-level effects (within-person):**

| State | Outcome | γ | Sig |
|---|---|---|---|
| Conscientiousness state | Working memory | +.020 | * (better WM on high-C days) |
| Extraversion state | Episodic memory | −.024 | * |

**Critical divergence:** Conscientiousness *trait* predicts worse WM, while conscientiousness *state* predicts better WM on high-C days. These operate at different levels of analysis and should not be conflated.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Illustrates how the same construct yields contradictory predictions depending on temporal grain of measurement — directly relevant to the book's argument that level of analysis (trait vs. state) is a fundamental measurement decision that shapes what phenotypes are detected.

---

### 30. McGeehan et al. (2025)

**Citation:** McGeehan, M., Sutin, A. R., Gallagher, S., Terracciano, A., Turiano, N. A., Ahern, E., ... & O'Súilleabháin, P. S. (2025). Personality and mortality risk: A systematic review and meta-analysis of longitudinal data. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology*. https://dx.doi.org/10.1037/pspp0000577

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Systematic Review, Meta-Analysis, Longitudinal
**Instruments:** N/A (meta-analysis across studies)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, all-cause mortality, hazard ratio, conscientiousness, meta-analysis, preregistered
- *Additional terms:* PET-PEESE, trim-and-fill, publication bias, geographic moderation, age moderation

#### Results Summary

Preregistered. j = 158 effect sizes, N = 569,859, 43,851 deaths.

**Univariate HRs (HR < 1.0 = protective):**

| Trait | HR | 95% CI | Sig |
|---|---|---|---|
| Conscientiousness | **0.90** | [0.87, 0.94] | ** |
| Neuroticism | 1.03 | [1.02, 1.04] | ** |
| Extraversion | 0.97 | [0.94, 0.99] | * |
| Openness | 0.96 | [0.93, 0.99] | * (did not survive bias correction) |
| Agreeableness | 0.98 | [0.95, 1.02] | ns |

**Multivariate HRs (all 5 entered):** Conscientiousness HR = 0.95**, Neuroticism HR = 1.02**. E, O, A non-significant or marginal.

Moderators: Neuroticism effect stronger in younger samples*. Extraversion effect present in North American/Australian samples but not UK/European*. Conscientiousness attenuated when health covariates controlled.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The most rigorous meta-analysis of personality and mortality (N = 569,859, preregistered), establishing conscientiousness as robustly protective (HR = .90) — the authoritative benchmark for the book's treatment of personality as a predictor of biological survival.

### 31. Napolitano et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Napolitano, C. M., Soto, C. J., Sewell, M. N., Yoon, H. J., & Roberts, B. W. (2025). Better with age: How social, emotional, and behavioral skills change across the life span. [Preprint]

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** BESSI (192 items; 5 domains, 32 facets)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet, Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* lifespan development, BESSI, adolescent disruption, maturity principle, generalized additive models
- *Additional terms:* nonlinear development, d-like effect sizes, gender differences, growth spurt, disruption principle

#### Results Summary

N = 31,315, ages 10–70. GAM curves.

**Domain-level d-like effect sizes by age interval:**

| Domain | 10–20 (disruption) | 10–40 | 10–70 |
|---|---|---|---|
| Self-Management | −.01 | .62 | .98 |
| Social Engagement | −.15 | .39 | .61 |
| Cooperation | −.04 | .49 | .68 |
| Emotional Resilience | +.01 | .61 | 1.00 |
| Innovation | −.15 | .20 | .36 |

13 of 32 facets (41%) showed negative d during ages 10–20 (disruption). Two growth spurts: ages 15–25 and late 20s through mid-30s.

**Facet extremes (10–70):** Ethical Competence d = +1.44; Persuasive Skill d = −.43.

Gender × Age significant for Self-Management, Cooperation, Emotional Resilience, Innovation. Non-binary individuals showed particularly large gains for Emotional Resilience (d = 1.22, ages 10–40).

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The most comprehensive portrait of SEB skill development across the full lifespan. The disruption-recovery-growth narrative and gender moderation are especially valuable for chapters on development and individual differences.

### 32. Nigg et al. (2020)

**Citation:** Nigg, J. T., Sibley, M. H., Thapar, A., & Karalunas, S. L. (2020). Development of ADHD: Etiology, heterogeneity, and early life course. *Annual Review of Developmental Psychology, 2*, 559–583. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-devpsych-060320-093413

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review, Longitudinal, Twin/Genetic
**Instruments:** N/A (DSM-5 criteria, neuropsychological batteries)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* ADHD, temperament, executive functioning, adolescent development
- *Additional terms:* phenotypic heterogeneity, dimensional trait model, early-risk phenotype, dysregulation, effortful control

#### Core Themes
- ADHD explicitly framed as a dimensional trait in the population — DSM-5 cut point is actuarial, not ontological (parallel to personality trait thresholds)
- Phenotypic heterogeneity is the central problem: at least two neurobiologically distinct subtypes (inattention-disorganization vs. hyperactivity-impulsivity), making facet-level decomposition scientifically necessary
- Temperament (emotional reactivity, effortful control) maps onto early ADHD precursors — connecting personality and psychopathology dimensionally
- Multi-pathway etiology: genetic liability interacts with early environmental risks (fetal origins, psychosocial adversity)
- Developmental bifurcation in adolescence: apparent remission vs. persistent trajectory raises fundamental questions about what the phenotype measures across time
- Advocates reconceptualizing ADHD within broader dysregulation (emotion regulation, reward sensitivity, effortful control) — core personality facet dimensions

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
ADHD as a case study in why phenotypic precision matters: the core argument that blunt diagnostic categories obscure distinct causal mechanisms emerging only under facet decomposition is precisely the argument the book makes about traits versus facets.

### 33. Ringwald et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Ringwald, W. R., Napolitano, C. M., Sewell, M. N., Soto, C. J., Yoon, H. J., & Wright, A. G. C. (2025). More skill than trait, or more trait than skill? *European Journal of Personality*. https://doi.org/10.1177/08902070241309960

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** BFI-2-XS (15 items), BESSI-45 (45 items)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality traits, SEB skills, response surface analysis, mismatch hypothesis, complementarity
- *Additional terms:* BFI-2-XS, BESSI-45, additive effects, functional capacity, trait-skill distinction

#### Results Summary

N = 840 high school students. 55 RSA models (5 trait-skill pairs × 11 outcomes).

- No models supported H1 (congruence best) or H2 (matched high levels best)
- 17 of 55 models (31%) supported H3: **higher-skill-than-trait mismatch predicts better outcomes**
- a1 (additive effects) significant and positive in 86% of models — higher levels of both are generally beneficial
- Civic engagement and social responsibility predicted by skill > trait mismatches across nearly all pairs
- Skills had numerically larger unique associations with outcomes than traits across most models

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly tests the trait-skill distinction, demonstrating that having more skill than one's trait baseline predicts independently better outcomes — central to the book's argument that functional capacity (skill) is a distinct phenotypic dimension from average-tendency (trait).

---

### 34. Roberts & DelVecchio (2000)

**Citation:** Roberts, B. W., & DelVecchio, W. F. (2000). The rank-order consistency of personality traits from childhood to old age: A quantitative review of longitudinal studies. *Psychological Bulletin, 126*(1), 3–25. https://doi.org/10.1037/0033-2909.126.1.3

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis, Longitudinal
**Instruments:** Diverse (NEO-PI, CPI, MMPI, EPI, Q-sort, temperament measures)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* rank-order consistency, test-retest reliability, trait stability, longitudinal, meta-analysis
- *Additional terms:* cumulative continuity principle, plaster hypothesis, differential continuity, attenuation correction

#### Results Summary

152 longitudinal studies, 3,217 test-retest correlations, ages 0–73.

**Test-retest correlations (ρ, population estimate) by age decade:**

| Age Range | ρ (6.7-yr interval) |
|---|---|
| 0–2.9 yrs | .35 |
| 3–5.9 | .34 |
| 6–11.9 | .43 |
| 12–17.9 | .47 |
| 18–21.9 | .51 |
| 22–29 | .57 |
| 30–39 | .62 |
| 40–49 | .59 |
| 50–59 | .75 |
| 60–73 | .72 |

Consistency peaks at ~.75 in the 50s–60s, directly refuting the plaster hypothesis (personality set by age 30). No trait category was significantly more consistent than another. Observer-report and self-report showed comparable stability.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational paper establishing the empirical baseline for trait stability. The systematic increase in rank-order consistency across the lifespan is a key empirical phenomenon the book's evolutionary/developmental framework must explain.

---

### 35. Roberts & Jackson (2008)

**Citation:** Roberts, B. W., & Jackson, J. J. (2008). Sociogenomic personality psychology. *Journal of Personality, 76*(6), 1523–1544. https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1467-6494.2008.00530.x

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* sociogenomic model, gene expression, epigenetics, DNA methylation, heritability
- *Additional terms:* glucocorticoid receptor, serotonin transporter, GxE interaction, phenotypic plasticity, state-trait model, blue-headed wrasse, honeybee, rat maternal behavior

#### Core Themes
- The classical Eysenckian biological model is structurally inadequate: it treats heritability as equivalent to immutability
- **Heritability does not imply immutability:** genes require environmental triggers to be expressed, and the same gene can produce different phenotypes under different conditions
- The sociogenomic model: environments → regulate gene expression → produce personality states → which aggregate into traits through repeated experience
- **States are the prime mediator:** the state-trait aggregation process is the central problem for personality science
- **Epigenetic modification is the molecular bridge:** rat maternal licking → glucocorticoid receptor methylation → stable offspring stress reactivity changes; COMT methylation discordance in MZ twins
- Prescribes a new research agenda: comparative/cross-species approaches, systematic GxE research, improved state measurement to track the aggregation process

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
A cornerstone paper: provides the mechanistic architecture linking evolution (gene-environment coevolution), development (epigenetic programming, state-to-trait aggregation), and measurement (why states must be measured to understand traits) — foundational for every chapter.

---

### 36. Roberts & Yoon (2022)

**Citation:** Roberts, B. W., & Yoon, H. J. (2022). Personality psychology. *Annual Review of Psychology, 73*, 489–516. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-psych-020821-114927

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet, Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality psychology, traits, development, skills, narrative identity
- *Additional terms:* phenotype-first approach, four-unit framework, corresponsive principle, niche-picking, WEIRD critique

#### Core Themes
- Organizes personality psychology around four units of analysis: traits, motivation, skills, and narrative identity
- Argues for a "phenotype-first" approach: start with robust trait descriptions and work backward to mechanisms, rather than imposing biological models prematurely
- The corresponsive and niche-picking principles: life experiences that change personality tend to be the same ones that personality selects people into
- Integrates social, emotional, and behavioral skills as a distinct personality unit alongside classic Big Five traits
- WEIRD/institutional critique: most evidence comes from Western, educated samples, limiting generalizability
- Calls for expanded measurement approaches beyond self-report (informant, behavioral, dynamic assessment)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The most current broad field overview, providing the four-unit framework and phenotype-first argument that aligns with the book's organizing structure.

---

### 37. Schwaba et al. (2021) — Lead

**Citation:** Schwaba, T., Bleidorn, W., Hopwood, C. J., Gebauer, J. E., Rentfrow, P. J., Potter, J., & Gosling, S. D. (2021). The impact of childhood lead exposure on adult personality. *PNAS, 118*(29), e2020104118. https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.2020104118

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Longitudinal
**Instruments:** BFI (44 items)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* lead exposure, neurotoxin, natural experiment, Clean Air Act, Big Five
- *Additional terms:* environmental personality determinants, regression discontinuity, developmental neurotoxicology

#### Results Summary

N > 1.5 million adults. Preregistered.

**Correlational (OLS) — county lead level → adult Big Five:**

| Trait | B | Sig |
|---|---|---|
| Agreeableness | −0.031 | * |
| Conscientiousness | −0.079 | * |
| Openness | +0.010 | * (contrary to hypothesis) |

**Natural experiment (born post–phase-out vs. pre–phase-out):**

| Trait | B | Sig |
|---|---|---|
| Agreeableness | +0.088 | ** |
| Conscientiousness | +0.217 | ** |
| Neuroticism | −0.049 | ** |

Average r ≈ .05. Age moderation: effects confined primarily to adults in 20s–30s (heaviest childhood exposure). European replication supported C and A patterns.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Rare causal evidence via natural experiment that a neurotoxic developmental exposure shapes adult personality — specifically targeting the socially normative traits (C, A). Directly supports the thesis that mental phenotypes are partly products of the physical/chemical developmental environment.

### 38. Schwaba et al. (2025) — GWAS

**Citation:** Schwaba, T., Clapp Sullivan, M. L., Akingbuwa, W. A., ... & Tucker-Drob, E. M. (2025). Robust inference and widespread genetic correlates from a large-scale genetic association study of human personality. [bioRxiv preprint]. https://doi.org/10.1101/2025.05.16.648988

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Mega-Analysis, Twin/Genetic
**Instruments:** Multiple Big Five measures pooled across 46 cohorts
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* GWAS mega-analysis, Big Five, SNP heritability, polygenic index (PGI), within-family GWAS
- *Additional terms:* LDSC, direct genetic effects, assortative mating, polygenicity

#### Results Summary

Discovery N = 611,153–1,145,730.

**SNP heritability (h²_SNP):**

| Trait | h²_SNP |
|---|---|
| Extraversion | ~13–14% |
| Neuroticism | ~12–13% |
| Openness | ~13–14% |
| Conscientiousness | ~10–11% |
| Agreeableness | ~7–8% |

**Genome-wide significant loci:** N: 703; E: 258; C: 131; O: 126; A: 39. Total: 1,257 (823 novel).

**PGI standardized betas (out-of-sample):** E: .19; O: .17; C: .16; N: .16; A: .10. All significant.

**Direct genetic effects:** Within-family PGI accounted for 96.2% of total PGI prediction — population stratification and indirect effects are minimal. Assortative mating negligible (r_g = .01–.04).

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The largest personality GWAS to date, providing definitive heritability estimates, loci counts, and evidence that genetic effects are overwhelmingly direct. The single most important empirical anchor for the genomic architecture of personality.

### 39. Schwaba et al. (2026) — Genomics

**Citation:** Schwaba, T., Nivard, M. G., Tucker-Drob, E. M., & Boomsma, D. I. (2026). Personality genomics. *Annual Review of Psychology, 77*, 485–511. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-psych-013125-042402

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review, Twin/Genetic
**Instruments:** N/A (reviews GWAS studies broadly)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality genomics, genetic architecture, SNP heritability, Mendelian randomization, pleiotropy
- *Additional terms:* CADM2, FOXP2, polygenicity, genetic correlation, within-family design, phenotypic generality

#### Core Themes
- **Massive polygenicity:** Thousands of variants per trait with tiny effects; median SNP h² ≈ 10%, far below twin-based 40–60%
- **Widespread pleiotropy:** Personality loci overlap with psychiatric, cognitive, and health trait loci
- **High phenotypic generality:** Substantial genetic signal shared across all five traits (genetic GFP)
- **Minimal familial confounding:** Within-family GWAS produces results consistent with population-level
- **CADM2 and FOXP2 as exemplar loci:** CADM2 shows the largest cross-trait associations; FOXP2 linked to verbal-communicative personality aspects
- **Mendelian randomization applications:** Personality as instrument or outcome in MR allows causal inference about health/psychopathology relationships
- Personality serves as a useful genomic organizing phenotype because it correlates with diverse biological and social outcomes

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Essential overview of what personality genomics has established — maps the gap between molecular and psychometric approaches and provides vocabulary for framing how genetic science bears on personality measurement and construct validity.

### 40. Shnabel & Ullrich (2026)

**Citation:** Shnabel, N., & Ullrich, J. (2026). Identity needs in intergroup relations. *Annual Review of Psychology, 77*, 285–310. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-psych-022625-112840

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* victim groups, perpetrator groups, reconciliation, competitive victimhood, identity needs
- *Additional terms:* social identity theory, agentic identity, moral identity, concept creep

#### Core Themes
- Two-dimensional model: victimized groups seek to restore agentic identity; perpetrator groups seek moral identity — structural parallel to Big Two agency/communion models
- Competitive victimhood as pathological manifestation of identity needs
- Social representations shape individual identity needs top-down
- Concept creep (expanding definitions of victimhood/trauma) changes what counts as identity-relevant events — implications for measurement validity over time

**Relevance to Book Project:** TANGENTIAL
Operates in social psychology/intergroup relations, orthogonal to the book's focus. The concept creep point has methodological relevance for measurement validity, but neither evolutionary nor developmental layers find footing here.

### 41. Soto et al. (2008)

**Citation:** Soto, C. J., John, O. P., Gosling, S. D., & Potter, J. (2008). The developmental psychometrics of Big Five self-reports: Acquiescence, factor structure, coherence, and differentiation from ages 10 to 20. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 94*(4), 718–737. https://doi.org/10.1037/0022-3514.94.4.718

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** BFI (44 items)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, developmental psychometrics, acquiescence, ipsatization, factor structure, coherence, differentiation
- *Additional terms:* Tucker's phi, within-domain coherence, between-domain differentiation, item difficulty, ages 10–20

#### Results Summary

N = 230,047, ages 10–20.

**Acquiescence:** ~14% of item variance at age 10, declining to ~7% by age 20 (halving). Ipsatization substantially improved all indices.

**Factor structure replicability (Tucker's phi, ipsatized vs. age-20 standard):** All five domains reached phi ≥ .97 by age 13 after ipsatization. Raw congruences were meaningfully lower at age 10.

**Within-domain coherence (mean inter-item r):** Rose from ~.23 at age 10 to ~.36 at age 20 (r with age = .97). Extraversion showed largest gain; Agreeableness showed trivial coherence gain but largest differentiation gain.

**Between-domain differentiation (mean |inter-scale r|):** Declined from ~.30 at age 10 to ~.18 at age 20 (r with age = −.89). Agreeableness showed sharpest divergence from other traits.

Item difficulty did not significantly predict developmental coherence gains, indicating improvement is not simply a verbal comprehension artifact.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational developmental psychometric evidence that Big Five self-reports become increasingly valid instruments across adolescence through three independent mechanisms — directly informing how measurement interacts with development.

---

### 42. Soto et al. (2022) — BESSI

**Citation:** Soto, C. J., Napolitano, C. M., Sewell, M. N., Yoon, H. J., & Roberts, B. W. (2022). An integrative framework for conceptualizing and assessing social, emotional, and behavioral skills: The BESSI. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 123*(1), 192–222. https://doi.org/10.1037/pspp0000401

**Purpose:** Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Instrument Validation, Factor Analysis, Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** BESSI (192 items), BFI-2, TEIQue, Self-Control Scale
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet, Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* BESSI, SEB skills, functional capacity, personality traits vs. skills, factor analysis, incremental validity
- *Additional terms:* lexical approach, bifactor-ESEM, Tucker's phi, 32 facets, 5 domains

#### Results Summary

Five studies, N = 6,309.

**5-Domain / 32-Facet Structure:** Self-Management (9 facets), Social Engagement (5), Cooperation (5), Emotional Resilience (5), Innovation (5), plus 3 compound facets.

**Cross-sample replication (Tucker's phi):** Mean congruence .88–.98 across domains.

**Domain-level convergent validity:**

| BESSI Domain | BFI-2 Trait | r |
|---|---|---|
| Self-Management | Conscientiousness | .74** |
| Social Engagement | Extraversion | .79** |
| Emotional Resilience | Emotional Stability | .79** |
| Innovation | Open-Mindedness | .76** |
| Cooperation | Agreeableness | .68** |

**Incremental validity:** BESSI beyond Big Five: average ΔR² = .10. Big Five beyond BESSI: average ΔR² = .04. BESSI accounts for ~2.5× the unique outcome variance.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The primary psychometric foundation for the BESSI framework, establishing that SEB skills are a measurable construct distinct from personality traits with substantial incremental predictive validity. Essential for chapters on measurement and phenotypic structure.
