### 43. Soto et al. (2025) — Intervention

**Citation:** Soto, C. J., Hudson, N. W., Napolitano, C. M., Sewell, M. N., Yoon, H. J., & Roberts, B. W. (2025). Practice makes perfect: A behavioral challenge intervention to develop social, emotional, and behavioral skills. *European Journal of Personality*. https://doi.org/10.1177/08902070251392639

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention, Longitudinal
**Instruments:** BESSI (domain-level), behavioral challenge protocol
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* SEB skills, behavioral intervention, skill development, practice effects, plasticity
- *Additional terms:* maturity principle, malleability, randomized experiment, pre-registration, domain-specificity

#### Results Summary

N = 470, 16-week randomized experiment. Participants assigned behavioral challenges targeting one BESSI domain or waitlist control.

- Targeted domain gains: small-to-medium effect sizes, significant at both post-intervention and follow-up
- Cross-domain spillover: limited (supporting domain-specificity)
- Moderation: gains largest for younger participants and those with lower baseline skill levels
- Provides causal (not just correlational) evidence that SEB skills are trainable

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The only experimental/causal evidence that SEB skills are plastic and respond to structured behavioral practice — directly supporting the book's argument that mental phenotypes can be shaped by intervention across the lifespan.

---

### 44. Stieger et al. (2023)

**Citation:** Stieger, M., Flückiger, C., & Allemand, M. (2023). One year later: Longer-term maintenance effects of a digital intervention to change personality traits. *Journal of Personality*. https://doi.org/10.1111/jopy.12898

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention, Longitudinal
**Instruments:** BFI-2 (60 items), PEACH digital intervention
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* PEACH, personality change, chatbot, maintenance, follow-up
- *Additional terms:* digital health intervention, intent-to-treat, attrition bias, self-sustaining feedback loops

#### Results Summary

1-year follow-up of PEACH RCT (N_pretest = 1,523; 1-year FU n = 157).

**Attrition bias (completers vs. non-completers, Cohen's d):** Openness d = 0.19, Neuroticism d = 0.20, Agreeableness d = 0.35, Conscientiousness d = 0.27 — completers were more open, less neurotic, more agreeable, more conscientious.

**Maintenance trajectories:**
- Extraversion and Conscientiousness: gains maintained through 1-year FU, no reversion
- **Neuroticism: continued to decrease** after intervention ended (unique finding)
- No change-goal group showed statistically significant reversion to baseline

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Addresses the durability question: do traits revert when behavioral scaffolding is removed? Answer: no — gains maintained or extended 1 year post-intervention. The continued neuroticism decline suggests self-sustaining feedback loops (reduced anxiety → more approach behavior → further N decrease).

---

### 45. Tucker-Drob & Briley (2017)

**Citation:** Tucker-Drob, E. M., & Briley, D. A. (2017). Theoretical concepts in the behavioral genetics of personality development. In D. P. McAdams, R. L. Shiner, & J. L. Tackett (Eds.), *Handbook of Personality Development*.

**Purpose:** Book Chapter
**Methodology:** Narrative Review, Twin/Genetic
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* heritability, ACE model, nonshared environment, genetic set point, personality development
- *Additional terms:* moving set point, random walk model, gene-environment interaction, reaction norm, rank-order stability, learning curve with decay

#### Results Summary

**Meta-analytic heritability across the lifespan:**

| Period | h² | e² (nonshared env) |
|---|---|---|
| Early childhood | ~.70 | ~.30 |
| Late adulthood | ~.35 | ~.65 |

Heritability *declines* from childhood to old age. Nonshared environmental contribution *increases*. Shared environment near-zero in adulthood.

**Genetic contribution to rank-order stability:** Approximately constant at r_genetic ≈ .35 throughout the lifespan.
**Environmental contribution to rank-order stability:** Increases from ~0 in early childhood to ~.35 by adulthood.

**Five mechanistic models reviewed:** Genetic set point (stable attractor), Moving set point (shifting attractor), Random walk (cumulative perturbations), Heterogeneity in transition points, Learning curves with decay/GxE reaction norms.

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Authoritative developmental synthesis establishing that the genetic architecture of personality is not static but shows systematic trends — essential context for understanding how phenotypic measurement at different life stages indexes different ratios of genetic to environmental signal.

---

### 46. Turkheimer et al. (2014)

**Citation:** Turkheimer, E., Pettersson, E., & Horn, E. E. (2014). A phenotypic null hypothesis for the genetics of personality. *Annual Review of Psychology, 65*, 515–540. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-psych-113011-143752

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review, Twin/Genetic
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* phenotypic null hypothesis, Three Laws of Behavior Genetics, heritability, genetic correlation
- *Additional terms:* parallel structures, ACE model, endophenotype, genetic confounding

#### Core Themes
- Proposes the "phenotypic null hypothesis": genetic variance in personality reflects phenotypic processes, not biologically independent genetic mechanisms
- Cross-occasion genetic correlations approach 1.0 in adulthood — same genetic factors operate at all adult assessment points, as predicted by the phenotypic null
- Genetic and phenotypic covariance structures are essentially isomorphic ("parallel structures") — the same factor structure appears whether variance is decomposed genetically or phenotypically
- Neuroticism–eating disorders genetic association substantially reduced when controlling for comorbid phenotypes → genetic confounding rather than direct biological pathway
- Neuroticism–depression genetic correlation (r_g ≈ .70+) is largely attributable to shared phenotypic structure
- The phenotypic null hypothesis is the default against which genetic findings should be evaluated

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Essential conceptual counter-argument to naive biologically reductionist readings of heritability — the phenotypic null hypothesis is a foundational tool for understanding why genetic findings do not automatically imply biological independence.

---

### 47. Wright et al. (2026)

**Citation:** Wright, A. J., Haehner, P., Hopwood, C. J., & Bleidorn, W. (2026). A systematic review and taxonomy of neuroticism interventions for the general public. *Personality Science, 7*, 1–32. https://doi.org/10.1177/27000710261421531

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Systematic Review
**Instruments:** N/A (TONS taxonomy; BFI-2 and NEO measures coded from primary studies)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Aspect

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* TONS, neuroticism intervention, systematic review, TESSERA, habit-level strategies
- *Additional terms:* cognitive restructuring, skill building, behavioral activation, bottom-up change, active ingredients

#### Core Themes
- **TONS (Taxonomy of Neuroticism Strategies):** 19 strategies organized by three dimensions: level targeted (trait/habit/state), aspect (affective/behavioral/cognitive), mechanism direction (bottom-up/top-down)
- Habit-level strategies dominate (64% of constructs targeted; 89% of interventions include at least one)
- Most-used: Skill Building via Practice (71%), Psychoeducation (66%), Cognitive Restructuring (41%)
- 74% of interventions coded as efficacious, but active ingredients cannot be isolated because interventions bundle multiple strategies
- State-level strategies are theoretically motivated (TESSERA) but empirically neglected
- **Critical gap:** No study systematically varied timing, dosing, or sequencing of strategies
- Similarity with General Change Mechanisms Theory = .576; Self-Regulation Model = .425; TESSERA = .371

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The TONS provides the most complete current map of how science attempts to engineer neuroticism phenotype change. The three-axis structure (level × aspect × direction) maps onto the book's framework for how traits are instantiated across biological, developmental, and measurement levels.

---

### 48. Wrzus & Roberts (2017)

**Citation:** Wrzus, C., & Roberts, B. W. (2017). Processes of personality development in adulthood: The TESSERA framework. *Personality and Social Psychology Review, 21*, 253–277. https://doi.org/10.1177/1088868316652279

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* TESSERA framework, personality development, triggering situations, expectancy, states, reactions
- *Additional terms:* CAPS, AMP, PERSOC, associative pathway, reflective pathway, state expression, accumulation

#### Core Themes
- Introduces the TESSERA framework: **T**riggering situations → **E**xpectancy → **S**tates/**S**tate **E**xpressions → **R**eactions → **A**ccumulation
- Each "TESSERA unit" consists of a situational trigger activating a personality-relevant state, which generates a state expression (behavior), which produces a social/intrapersonal reaction, which adjusts expectancy for future episodes
- Two pathways: associative (automatic, habit-based) and reflective (deliberate, goal-directed)
- Repeated TESSERA cycles on the same dimension cumulate into habitual and eventually trait-level change
- Extends social-cognitive theories (CAPS, AMP, PERSOC) by specifying the temporal aggregation mechanism
- Applied across three lifespan periods: emerging adulthood (identity exploration), midlife (role deepening), older age (loss compensation)
- Trait change requires repeated state–reaction cycles — single events rarely produce lasting change without repetition

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides the micro-level mechanistic account of how single behavioral episodes accumulate into trait change — the processual complement to the book's structural and evolutionary arguments. The TESSERA unit is the atomic building block for the book's account of how phenotypes get constructed moment-to-moment.

---

### 49. Ackerman & Heggestad (1997)

**Citation:** Ackerman, P. L., & Heggestad, E. D. (1997). Intelligence, personality, and interests: Evidence for overlapping traits. *Psychological Bulletin, 121*(2), 219–245. https://doi.org/10.1037/0033-2909.121.2.219

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis, Narrative Review
**Instruments:** Various (NEO-PI, EPQ, MPQ, TIE, SVIB, UNIACT, Carroll ability taxonomy)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* intelligence, personality, interests, overlapping traits, trait complexes, meta-analysis
- *Additional terms:* PPIK theory, intelligence-as-typical-performance, TIE, Gc, Gf, Holland themes, maximal vs. typical performance, Intellectence

#### Core Themes
- Meta-analysis of 135 studies (188 samples, N=64,592) examining personality–intelligence and interest–intelligence relations using Carroll's (1993) hierarchical ability taxonomy
- Personality traits associated with Neuroticism/NEM (Stress Reaction, Test Anxiety) show consistent negative correlations with all ability factors; traits in the Extraversion/PEM category show small positive correlations
- Openness to Experience, TIE, and Intellectence form a trait complex with substantial positive correlations to Gc and Ideational Fluency, and moderate correlations to Gf
- Four "trait complexes" identified integrating personality, interests, and abilities: (1) social (Extraversion + Social/Enterprising interests), (2) clerical/conventional (Conscientiousness + Perceptual Speed + Conventional interests), (3) science/math (Math/Spatial abilities + Realistic/Investigative interests), (4) intellectual/cultural (Gc + Openness/TIE + Artistic/Investigative interests)
- Reviews history of intelligence testing paradigm from Binet–Simon through Army Alpha to modern era, distinguishing maximal vs. typical performance
- Interest–ability associations: Investigative and Realistic interests positively correlate with math/spatial abilities; Social and Enterprising interests show negative correlations with many ability measures

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational integration of the three major individual-differences domains (ability, personality, interests) that the book project needs. The trait complex framework and PPIK theory provide the structural vocabulary for how cognitive and non-cognitive phenotypes cohere into functional constellations. The meta-analytic data on Openness/TIE–Gc relations are directly relevant to understanding how personality and intelligence overlap at the phenotypic level.

---

### 50. Ackerman, Beier & Boyle (2005)

**Citation:** Ackerman, P. L., Beier, M. E., & Boyle, M. O. (2005). Working memory and intelligence: The same or different constructs? *Psychological Bulletin, 131*(1), 30–60. https://doi.org/10.1037/0033-2909.131.1.30

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis
**Instruments:** Various WM span tasks, Raven's Progressive Matrices, WAIS, various ability batteries
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, g

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* working memory, intelligence, meta-analysis, Gf, g, executive attention
- *Additional terms:* complex span tasks, n-back, STM, Engle, Kane, attentional control, isomorphism claim

#### Core Themes
- Meta-analysis of 86 samples testing whether WM and g (or Gf) are isomorphic constructs
- Average true-score correlation between WM and g = .479 — substantial but far from unity
- WM–Gf correlation somewhat higher but still not isomorphic; WM accounts for ~25% of Gf variance
- STM–g correlation (.260) significantly smaller than WM–g correlation (.397), but STM is not negligible
- Verbal WM tasks correlate more highly with g than spatial/figural WM tasks
- n-back tasks show substantially lower correlations with g than complex span tasks — raises concerns about n-back as a WM/intelligence proxy
- Concludes that WM and intelligence share considerable but not complete variance; they are related but distinct constructs
- Challenges the "isomorphism" claim of Kyllonen & Christal (1990) and the executive-attention framework of Engle/Kane

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly relevant to the book's treatment of cognitive ability structure and the relationship between processing capacity and intelligence. The WM ≠ g finding constrains claims about the biological unity of cognitive ability and is relevant to understanding what intelligence tests measure vs. what they miss.

---

### 51. Ackerman & Kanfer (2025)

**Citation:** Ackerman, P. L., & Kanfer, R. (2025). Cognitive ability and non-ability trait predictors of academic achievement: A four-year longitudinal study. *Journal of Intelligence, 13*, 79. https://doi.org/10.3390/jintelligence13070079

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal
**Instruments:** CogAT, NEO-FFI (N, E, O, A, C), TIE, Need for Achievement, Self-Discipline, Numerical Preferences, UNIACT, self-concept/self-estimates of ability, MTQ, domain knowledge tests
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* achievement, cognitive ability, personality, interests, validity
- *Additional terms:* PPIK theory, trait complexes, AP courses, incremental validity, Openness, TIE, engagement traits, Conscientiousness, GPA

#### Core Themes
- N=910 9th-graders at 15 schools in Atlanta metro area, followed longitudinally through 12th grade (N=738 with complete transcripts)
- Cognitive abilities (CogAT verbal, quantitative, figural) predict 15–50% of variance in 12th-grade outcomes (vocabulary, math ability, domain knowledge, GPA, AP course completion)
- Personality traits in isolation predict substantial variance (10–29%), but incremental validity after cognitive abilities is modest (~5–10%)
- "Engagement" traits (Openness, TIE, Need for Achievement, Numerical Preferences) are the personality measures most consistently correlated with achievement criteria
- Neuroticism shows modest negative correlations with domain knowledge; Extraversion near-zero with most criteria
- Self-concept and self-estimates of abilities show consistent positive correlations with all criteria, even after controlling for cognitive abilities
- Motivational traits (Desire to Learn, Mastery) show positive correlations; aversive traits (Worry, Emotionality) show negative correlations with domain knowledge
- Total variance accounted for (abilities + all non-ability traits) ranges from 26–63% across criteria
- Supplement provides full intercorrelation matrix (N=178 complete cases)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The most comprehensive longitudinal test of the PPIK "trait complex" framework. Provides the empirical basis for how cognitive and non-cognitive phenotypes jointly determine real-world achievement outcomes. The engagement-trait findings (Openness, TIE) connect directly to the intellectual/cultural trait complex from Ackerman & Heggestad (1997).

---

### 52. Eronen & Bringmann (2021)

**Citation:** Eronen, M. I., & Bringmann, L. F. (2021). The theory crisis in psychology: How to move forward. *Perspectives on Psychological Science, 16*(4), 779–788. https://doi.org/10.1177/1745691620970586

**Purpose:** Commentary/Editorial
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* theory crisis, Meehl, construct validity, phenomena, causal inference
- *Additional terms:* epistemic iteration, fat-handed interventions, robust phenomena, operationalism, jingle-jangle fallacy

#### Core Themes
- Argues psychology faces a "theory crisis" (extending Meehl's critique): theories are vague, hard to test, and rarely abandoned
- Three obstacles to good psychological theories: (1) lack of robust phenomena — the field often models statistical regularities rather than stable empirical patterns that constrain theory; (2) problems of construct validity and epistemic iteration — psychological constructs are not fixed but evolve through cycles of operationalization and revision, yet this iterative process is rarely conducted rigorously; (3) difficulty discovering causal relationships — psychological interventions are "fat-handed" (manipulate many things simultaneously), making it difficult to isolate causal mechanisms
- Proposes that progress requires (a) establishing robust phenomena first, before building elaborate theories; (b) treating construct validation as an ongoing, serious process; (c) developing methods suited to the complexity of psychological causation
- Connects to broader philosophy-of-science debates about the relationship between measurement, constructs, and explanation

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides the meta-scientific framework for understanding why personality structure debates persist and why the Big Five architecture may reflect measurement conventions rather than natural kinds. The "robust phenomena" criterion is directly applicable to evaluating whether the published facet-to-domain mappings constitute genuine empirical regularities.

---

### 53. Graham & Lachman (2012)

**Citation:** Graham, E. K., & Lachman, M. E. (2012). Personality stability is associated with better cognitive performance in adulthood: Are the stable more able? *Journals of Gerontology, Series B: Psychological Sciences and Social Sciences, 67*(5), 545–554. https://doi.org/10.1093/geronb/gbr149

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal
**Instruments:** MIDUS personality scales (Big Five adjective scales), BTACT (Brief Test of Adult Cognition by Telephone)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality stability, cognitive performance, MIDUS, neuroticism, aging
- *Additional terms:* intraindividual change, reaction time, episodic memory, executive functioning, personality–cognition interface

#### Core Themes
- N=4,974 adults from MIDUS (Midlife in the United States), assessed at two time points approximately 10 years apart
- Personality stability (low absolute change) was associated with better cognitive performance on BTACT (episodic memory, executive functioning, reaction time)
- Low Neuroticism and high Openness at baseline predicted better cognitive outcomes; stability in these traits amplified the advantage
- Stability was more beneficial than change in either direction — even "positive" change (becoming more open, less neurotic) did not fully compensate for the cognitive advantage of having been stable
- Neuroticism increase × age interaction: among older adults, those who increased in Neuroticism had particularly poor reaction time performance
- Suggests a bidirectional personality–cognition link: stable personality may reflect (and reinforce) intact cognitive self-regulation

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides evidence that personality and cognition are not independent systems — they co-develop and co-constrain each other across the lifespan. The stability advantage is relevant to the book's argument that trait stability reflects an underlying coherence of the phenotype, not merely test-retest reliability.

---

### 54. Kanfer & Ackerman (1989)

**Citation:** Kanfer, R., & Ackerman, P. L. (1989). Motivation and cognitive abilities: An integrative/aptitude–treatment interaction approach to skill acquisition. *Journal of Applied Psychology, 74*(4), 657–690. https://doi.org/10.1037/0021-9010.74.4.657

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** ASVAB, Kanfer–Ackerman Air Traffic Controller (ATC) task, CIQ (Cognitive Interference Questionnaire), self-report measures
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, g

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* motivation, cognitive abilities, skill acquisition, aptitude–treatment interaction, goal setting, self-regulation
- *Additional terms:* attentional resources, performance-resource function, declarative/procedural knowledge, resource-dependent/insensitive tasks, distal/proximal motivation

#### Core Themes
- Integrates Kahneman's (1973) attentional capacity model with motivational theory to explain ability–performance relations during skill acquisition
- Three phases of skill acquisition: declarative knowledge (resource-dependent, g-loaded), knowledge compilation (transitional), procedural knowledge (resource-insensitive, perceptual speed-loaded)
- Three experiments with 1,010 U.S. Air Force trainees on a complex ATC simulation task
- **Experiment 1 (N=316):** General ability strongly predicts early performance (r=.45–.49) but correlations decline with practice; perceptual speed correlations increase then decrease. Early goal-setting intervention reduced general ability–performance correlations more than no-goal condition
- **Experiment 2 (N=142):** Late goal-setting (introduced at intermediate skill phase) enhanced performance — consistent with prediction that self-regulation benefits from available cognitive resources after declarative demands decline
- **Experiment 3 (N=547):** Simultaneous test of ability, motivation (goal timing), and task training (part-task vs. whole-task). Part-task training reduced resource demands, allowing goals to enhance performance
- Self-regulatory activities (self-monitoring, self-evaluation, self-reaction) compete with task performance for attentional resources during declarative phase
- Low-ability subjects showed more off-task attention, negative self-reactions, and perceived difficulty

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The foundational empirical paper for understanding how cognitive abilities and motivation interact dynamically during complex learning. The resource-allocation framework explains why intelligence tests (which measure maximal performance under controlled conditions) predict differently from real-world contexts where motivation, self-regulation, and task familiarity all vary. Directly relevant to the book's account of how ability phenotypes are expressed through motivational and self-regulatory mechanisms.

---

### 55. Kanfer & Ackerman (2004)

**Citation:** Kanfer, R., & Ackerman, P. L. (2004). Aging, adult development, and work motivation. *Academy of Management Review, 29*(3), 440–458. https://doi.org/10.5465/amr.2004.13670969

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* aging, adult development, work motivation, Gf, Gc, life-span
- *Additional terms:* loss/growth/reorganization/exchange, effort-performance function, performance-utility function, effort-utility function, socioemotional selectivity, generativity, self-concept protection

#### Core Themes
- Integrates life-span developmental research on cognition, personality, affect, interests, values, and self-concept with work motivation theory
- Four patterns of age-related change: **loss** (Gf decline from early 20s), **growth** (Gc accumulation through midlife+), **reorganization** (personality/motive restructuring around midlife — increased conscientiousness, decreased openness, emergence of generativity motives), **exchange** (shift in motive primacy from achievement/status to identity/affect protection)
- Maps developmental trajectories onto Kanfer's (1987) expectancy-based motivation model through three functions: (a) effort-performance function (shifted by Gf/Gc balance and task demands), (b) performance-utility function (shifted by changing motive primacy), (c) effort-utility function (shifted by declining attractiveness of high effort)
- Four formal propositions: (1a) increased effort is first-use strategy for high-Gf tasks with aging; (1b) this strategy is self-undermining through negative self-efficacy effects; (2a/b) high-Gc jobs maintain performance without increased effort; (2c) stronger incentives needed for midlife workers in high-Gc jobs; (3a/b) attractiveness of high performance declines with age; (4) attractiveness of high effort declines with age
- Older workers' avoidance of new technology/training explained by self-concept protection — avoiding Gf-demanding tasks that expose declining abilities

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides the motivational-developmental framework for understanding how cognitive ability changes across the lifespan interact with personality and motivational changes to determine real-world performance. The four-pattern developmental taxonomy (loss, growth, reorganization, exchange) is a powerful organizing framework for the book's treatment of how phenotypes change across the lifespan.

---

### 56. Menon (2019)

**Citation:** Menon, B. (2019). Towards a new model of understanding – The triple network, psychopathology and the structure of the mind. *Medical Hypotheses, 133*, 109385. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.mehy.2019.109385

**Purpose:** Commentary/Editorial
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* triple network model, psychopathology, salience network, central executive network, default mode network
- *Additional terms:* SN, CEN, DMN, insula, dACC, dlPFC, schizophrenia, depression, OCD, ipseity, unitary psychosis

#### Core Themes
- Proposes that the "mind" emerges from the harmonious, interconnected functioning of three large-scale brain networks: the **Salience Network** (SN: insula, dACC — detects and filters salient stimuli), the **Central Executive Network** (CEN: dlPFC, posterior parietal — working memory, planning, goal-directed behavior), and the **Default Mode Network** (DMN: medial PFC, PCC, angular gyrus — self-referential processing, autobiographical memory, mind-wandering)
- The SN acts as a dynamic switch between CEN and DMN engagement
- Table 1 maps psychiatric disorders to patterns of network dysfunction: schizophrenia (all three networks), depression (predominantly DMN + SN), OCD (CEN via fronto-striatal circuits), anxiety disorders (DMN + SN), dissociative disorders (DMN + SN/amygdala), addictions (CEN/dlPFC + ventral striatum)
- DSM-5 personality disorder clusters can be understood through network dysfunction: Cluster A (odd/eccentric) resembles autistic spectrum → triple network; Cluster B (mood/affective) → predominantly DMN/SN; Cluster C (anxiety) → CEN + OCD circuits
- Reversibility of disorders depends on which networks are affected: DMN/SN disorders are more often reversible; CEN dysfunction (especially when developmental/inherited) is less reversible
- "Organic" mental disorders result from direct brain injury affecting one or more network nodes; psychiatric symptoms depend on site, severity, and which networks are involved

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides a neuroanatomical framework for understanding how cognitive and personality phenotypes might be implemented in brain network architecture. The triple network model is relevant to the book's argument about biological substrates, though the paper is speculative (hypothesis paper, not empirical) and simplified.

---

### 57. Stanovich (2012)

**Citation:** Stanovich, K. E. (2012). On the distinction between rationality and intelligence: Implications for understanding individual differences in reasoning. In K. J. Holyoak & R. G. Morrison (Eds.), *The Oxford Handbook of Thinking and Reasoning* (pp. 433–455). Oxford University Press. https://doi.org/10.1093/oxfordhb/9780199734689.013.0022

**Purpose:** Book Chapter
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* rationality, intelligence, dual process theory, tripartite model, individual differences
- *Additional terms:* autonomous mind, algorithmic mind, reflective mind, Type 1/Type 2 processing, thinking dispositions, mindware, cognitive decoupling, override detection, epistemic vs. instrumental rationality

#### Core Themes
- Develops the tripartite model of mind: **autonomous mind** (Type 1: fast, parallel, automatic), **algorithmic mind** (Type 2 computational: cognitive capacity, fluid intelligence), **reflective mind** (Type 2 dispositional: thinking dispositions like AOT, need for cognition, reflectivity)
- IQ tests primarily measure the algorithmic mind — computational power for decoupling and simulation — but miss the reflective mind's contribution to rational thought
- Two conditions for rational responding: (1) the reflective mind must detect the need for override (requires appropriate thinking dispositions), and (2) the algorithmic mind must have sufficient computational power to sustain the override (requires cognitive ability)
- When both conditions must be met, cognitive ability and rationality correlate; when only override detection is needed (naturalistic situations), they dissociate
- "Mindware" = the rules, strategies, and knowledge structures needed for rational thought (probability rules, logic, scientific reasoning, etc.) — these are acquired, not innate, and represent a third source of rational thinking failure distinct from processing capacity and thinking dispositions
- Argues for a comprehensive assessment of rational thinking (CART) that measures all three sources of individual differences: processing capacity, thinking dispositions, and mindware

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The theoretical keystone for the book's argument that intelligence tests capture only part of the cognitive phenotype. The tripartite model provides the architecture for understanding why the "bright" can be irrational and why rationality and intelligence are dissociable — a core claim needed for the book's account of how mental phenotypes are assembled from partially independent components.

---

### 58. Stanovich & Toplak (2024)

**Citation:** Stanovich, K. E., & Toplak, M. E. (2024). Conspiracy beliefs in the context of a comprehensive rationality assessment. *Thinking & Reasoning*. https://doi.org/10.1080/13546783.2024.2368026

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** CART (Comprehensive Assessment of Rational Thinking), Generic Conspiracist Beliefs Scale (GCBS), AOT, superstitious thinking subscale
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* conspiracy beliefs, rationality, CART, superstitious thinking, actively open-minded thinking
- *Additional terms:* contaminated mindware, probabilistic reasoning, epistemic rationality, thinking dispositions, cognitive ability

#### Core Themes
- N=747 adults completed the full CART battery and the Generic Conspiracist Beliefs Scale
- Superstitious thinking (a subscale of the CART measuring contaminated mindware) was the single strongest predictor of conspiracy beliefs
- Actively open-minded thinking (AOT) and probabilistic/statistical reasoning were also significant predictors
- Cognitive ability (SAT/ACT scores) showed modest negative correlations with conspiracy beliefs, but was not among the strongest predictors after CART subtests were entered
- Conspiracy beliefs are more a function of (a) contaminated mindware (beliefs in paranormal phenomena, superstition) and (b) thinking dispositions (closed-minded thinking) than of raw cognitive processing power
- The CART framework allowed simultaneous evaluation of multiple sources of irrational belief — processing capacity, thinking dispositions, and mindware — providing a more nuanced picture than studies examining only intelligence

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates the practical utility of the rationality–intelligence distinction for understanding real-world belief formation. Relevant to the book's treatment of how cognitive phenotypes are assembled from multiple partially independent components.

---

### 59. Stanovich & West (1998)

**Citation:** Stanovich, K. E., & West, R. F. (1998). Individual differences in rational thought. *Journal of Experimental Psychology: General, 127*(2), 161–188. https://doi.org/10.1037/0096-3445.127.2.161

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** SAT (cognitive ability proxy), various heuristics-and-biases tasks, AOT, Need for Cognition, Superstitious Thinking, Dogmatism
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* rational thinking, individual differences, heuristics and biases, cognitive ability, thinking dispositions
- *Additional terms:* belief bias, argument evaluation, outcome bias, sunk cost, base rate neglect, covariation detection, override, cognitive decoupling

#### Core Themes
- Four studies (total N≈954 university students) systematically examining which heuristics-and-biases tasks show individual differences related to cognitive ability and which do not
- Cognitive ability (SAT scores) correlates with performance on some H&B tasks — particularly those requiring decontextualization from prior beliefs (belief bias in syllogistic reasoning, argument evaluation quality)
- Cognitive ability does NOT correlate with performance on other H&B tasks — outcome bias, sunk cost fallacy, and some base rate problems show near-zero ability correlations
- Thinking dispositions (AOT, need for cognition, superstitious thinking, dogmatism) predict H&B task performance even after statistically controlling for cognitive ability
- Establishes the empirical foundation for the claim that rationality and intelligence are partially dissociable individual-differences constructs
- The pattern of which tasks do vs. don't correlate with ability is systematic, not random — it depends on whether the task structure requires cognitive decoupling from prior beliefs

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The foundational empirical paper for the rationality–intelligence dissociation. Establishes that thinking dispositions represent a genuine source of individual differences in rational thought that is partially independent of cognitive ability — a key finding for the book's argument that the cognitive phenotype has multiple partially independent components.

---

### 60. Stanovich & West (2008)

**Citation:** Stanovich, K. E., & West, R. F. (2008). On the relative independence of thinking biases and cognitive ability. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 94*(4), 672–695. https://doi.org/10.1037/0022-3514.94.4.672

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** SAT (cognitive ability proxy), various bias tasks (framing, base rate, conjunction, belief bias, myside bias, denominator neglect, etc.)
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* thinking biases, cognitive ability, independence, heuristics and biases, individual differences
- *Additional terms:* performance errors, mindware gaps, override failures, cognitive decoupling, sustained inhibition, attribute substitution

#### Core Themes
- Seven studies demonstrating that many thinking biases are uncorrelated with cognitive ability
- Develops a three-category framework for classifying processing errors:
  - **(1) Performance errors** — correctable by higher ability; occur when the task cues the need for analytic processing and ability provides the computational power (e.g., belief bias in syllogistic reasoning with explicit decontextualization instructions)
  - **(2) Mindware gaps** — correctable by knowledge acquisition; occur when the person lacks the rules, strategies, or knowledge needed for correct responding (e.g., not knowing probability rules, base rate principles)
  - **(3) Override failures** — resist ability-based correction; occur in naturalistic settings where the task does not explicitly signal the need to suppress heuristic processing (e.g., myside bias, framing effects in between-subjects designs)
- This framework explains why some biases correlate with intelligence (Type 1) while others do not (Type 3): the key variable is whether the task signals the need for cognitive decoupling
- Across the seven studies, many common biases (framing, denominator neglect, myside bias in naturalistic settings) showed near-zero correlations with SAT scores
- People of higher cognitive ability are no more likely to recognize the need to dampen myside bias while reasoning in naturalistic settings

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The key theoretical paper distinguishing three types of processing errors. The three-category framework is essential for the book's argument that different aspects of the cognitive phenotype (processing capacity vs. thinking dispositions vs. mindware) can fail independently, producing characteristically different patterns of irrational behavior.

---

### 61. Stanovich, West & Toplak (2011)

**Citation:** Stanovich, K. E., West, R. F., & Toplak, M. E. (2011). The complexity of developmental predictions from dual process models. *Developmental Review, 31*, 103–118. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.dr.2011.07.003

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* dual process theory, development, Type 1/Type 2 processing, tripartite model
- *Additional terms:* autonomous mind, algorithmic mind, reflective mind, U-shaped development, mindware acquisition, override detection, serial associative cognition

#### Core Themes
- Applies the tripartite model to developmental predictions, arguing that common intuitions about "development = more Type 2 processing = better performance" are oversimplified
- Five factors determine whether a child at a given age will respond normatively: (1) strength of autonomous (Type 1) response, (2) whether override detection occurs, (3) whether sufficient cognitive capacity exists for override, (4) whether appropriate mindware has been acquired, (5) whether the correct response requires Type 1 or Type 2 processing
- U-shaped developmental functions are possible when autonomous mind learning (through experience) produces associations that conflict with normative responding — in these cases, older children may perform worse than younger children on certain tasks
- The autonomous mind is not always the "enemy" of rationality — for some tasks (e.g., base rate sensitivity in young children), Type 1 processing produces the normatively correct response, and development of Type 2 override capacity can actually impair performance
- Criticizes overly simple "dual process" framing in developmental psychology that assumes all developmental progress involves increasing Type 2 control

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Adds developmental nuance to the tripartite model. Relevant to the book's treatment of how cognitive phenotypes are assembled across development — not simply by adding Type 2 capacity on top of Type 1, but through a complex interaction where both systems contribute to both rational and irrational responding.

---

### 62. Stanovich, West & Toplak (2013)

**Citation:** Stanovich, K. E., West, R. F., & Toplak, M. E. (2013). Myside bias, rational thinking, and intelligence. *Current Directions in Psychological Science, 22*(4), 259–264. https://doi.org/10.1177/0963721413480174

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* myside bias, rationality, intelligence, individual differences
- *Additional terms:* decontextualization, confirmation bias, naturalistic reasoning, belief bias, argument evaluation, between-subjects design

#### Core Themes
- Reviews extensive evidence that myside bias is largely independent of cognitive ability across multiple paradigms
- Critical distinction explains the apparent contradiction with findings that belief bias in formal reasoning *is* correlated with ability: when tasks explicitly instruct participants to decouple prior beliefs from reasoning (formal reasoning tasks), intelligence correlates with avoiding bias because decontextualization is a necessary requirement and ability enables it
- When tasks do NOT explicitly instruct decontextualization (naturalistic reasoning — argument generation, argument evaluation, between-subjects belief paradigms), intelligence provides no inoculation against myside bias
- In naturalistic settings, people of higher cognitive ability show myside bias of equal magnitude to those of lower ability — across 15 policy positions, not one showed smaller myside bias in the higher-ability group
- Myside bias avoidance is a component of the multifarious concept of rational thought that is "missing from intelligence tests"
- Rationality is a more encompassing construct than intelligence; IQ tests miss important aspects of good thinking

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates one specific case of the rationality–intelligence dissociation in accessible, compelling detail. The decontextualization distinction is a useful pedagogical tool for the book's argument about what intelligence tests capture and miss.

---

### 63. Teovanović, Knežević & Stankov (2015)

**Citation:** Teovanović, P., Knežević, G., & Stankov, L. (2015). Individual differences in cognitive biases: Evidence against one-factor theory of rationality. *Intelligence, 50*, 75–86. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.intell.2015.02.008

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** Raven's Advanced Progressive Matrices, CRT (Cognitive Reflection Test), NEO-FFI (Openness/Intellect), Need for Cognition, custom bias tasks (anchoring, base rate neglect, conjunction fallacy, framing, overconfidence, outcome bias, sunk cost)
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, g

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cognitive biases, rationality, individual differences, factor analysis, one-factor theory
- *Additional terms:* ecological rationality, normative irrationality, RQ (rationality quotient), positive manifold, inter-bias correlations

#### Core Themes
- N=243 participants assessed on 7 cognitive biases, plus Gf (Raven's), CRT, Openness/Intellect, and Need for Cognition
- Low inter-bias correlations (most rs < .20) — biases do not cohere into a unitary "rationality" factor
- EFA yields 2 bias factors: "normative irrationality" (conjunction, base rate, anchoring) and "ecological rationality" (framing, sunk cost, overconfidence)
- Biases are relatively independent of Gf, Gc, CRT, Openness, and NFC — correlations are weak and inconsistent
- No support for a "positive manifold" of rationality analogous to the positive manifold of intelligence (g)
- Challenges Stanovich's implicit assumption that rational thinking skills might cohere into a "rationality quotient" (RQ) — the data suggest rationality is fragmented, not unitary
- Individual differences in cognitive biases form a "near-zero" correlation landscape similar to what would be expected if biases were driven by distinct, task-specific processes rather than a common underlying capacity

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides critical empirical evidence against a unitary "rationality" construct. Relevant to the book's broader argument about the fragmented nature of mental phenotypes — just as facets within personality domains do not always cohere genetically, biases within the "rationality" domain do not cohere psychometrically.

---

### 64. Toplak & Stanovich (2024)

**Citation:** Toplak, M. E., & Stanovich, K. E. (2024). Measuring rational thinking in adolescents: The Assessment of Rational Thinking for Youth (ART-Y). *Journal of Behavioral Decision Making, 37*, e2381. https://doi.org/10.1002/bdm.2381

**Purpose:** Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Instrument Validation, Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** ART-Y (Probabilistic/Statistical Reasoning, Scientific Reasoning, Avoidance of Framing, Knowledge Calibration, Rational Temporal Discounting), AOT, Deliberative Thinking Scale, Shipley-2, verbal-numerical reasoning, problem-solving task
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* adolescents, youth, assessment, rationality, real-world skills
- *Additional terms:* ART-Y, CART, formative measurement, epistemic rationality, instrumental rationality, financial literacy, academic achievement

#### Core Themes
- N=143 high school students (all male, ages 13–18, mean=15.4) from a rural area near a U.S. metropolitan city
- ART-Y is a youth-adapted version of the adult CART (Comprehensive Assessment of Rational Thinking), with 5 subtests measuring disparate aspects of rational thinking
- Probabilistic Reasoning and Scientific Reasoning correlate substantially (r=.43) and are well predicted by cognitive ability (composite r=.51–.61) and problem-solving
- Knowledge Calibration, Avoidance of Framing, and Rational Temporal Discounting are relatively independent of cognitive ability and of each other — consistent with the adult CART pattern
- The ART-Y predicted two real-world skills: financial literacy (Scientific Reasoning, Knowledge Calibration, and Probabilistic Numeracy as unique predictors; R²=.28) and academic achievement (R²=.645 with cognitive ability + ART-Y subtests)
- Formative (not reflective) measurement model recommended — rational thinking is a multidimensional construct where indicators define (not reflect) the construct; a composite score should not be interpreted as a psychometric g of rationality
- Thinking dispositions (AOT, Deliberative Thinking) correlated with Probabilistic Reasoning and Scientific Reasoning but not the other three subtests — weaker than in adult samples

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Extends the rationality assessment framework to a developmental sample. Relevant to the book's treatment of how rational thinking phenotypes develop across the lifespan and how formative measurement models differ from reflective models — a distinction with implications for personality structure as well.

---

### 65. Toplak, West & Stanovich (2014)

**Citation:** Toplak, M. E., West, R. F., & Stanovich, K. E. (2014). Rational thinking and cognitive sophistication: Development, cognitive abilities, and thinking dispositions. *Developmental Psychology, 50*(4), 1037–1048. https://doi.org/10.1037/a0034910

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** WASI, Stroop, Trail Making, Sentence Span (WM), AOT, Need for Cognition, Superstitious Thinking, custom rational thinking tasks
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* rational thinking, cognitive sophistication, development, cognitive ability, thinking dispositions
- *Additional terms:* denominator neglect, belief bias, framing, base rate sensitivity, otherside thinking, executive functioning

#### Core Themes
- N=204 children in grades 2–9 assessed on 5 rational thinking tasks (denominator neglect, belief bias syllogisms, resistance to framing, base rate sensitivity, otherside thinking) plus cognitive ability (WASI), executive functioning (Stroop, Trail Making, Sentence Span), and thinking dispositions (AOT, NFC, Superstitious Thinking)
- All 5 rational thinking tasks correlated significantly with age, cognitive ability, EF, and thinking dispositions
- WASI composite was the dominant predictor of rational thinking performance (β=.447 in stepwise regression)
- Thinking dispositions added incremental variance beyond cognitive ability for some rational thinking tasks, consistent with the adult literature
- Executive functioning measures (Stroop, WM) correlated moderately with rational thinking but did not add substantial unique variance beyond WASI
- Establishes that the individual-differences landscape for rational thinking in children is broadly similar to that in adults — cognitive ability is the strongest predictor, but thinking dispositions provide additional explanatory power
- Age was a modest but significant predictor, consistent with the developmental acquisition of both cognitive capacity and mindware

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Extends the Stanovich rationality framework to children, showing that the intelligence–rationality dissociation and the role of thinking dispositions are present from middle childhood. Relevant to the book's treatment of how cognitive phenotypes develop and the age at which partially independent components of the cognitive phenotype become visible.

---

### 66. Barrett (2017)

**Citation:** Barrett, L. F. (2017). The theory of constructed emotion: An active inference account of interoception and categorization. *Social Cognitive and Affective Neuroscience, 12*(1), 1–23. https://doi.org/10.1093/scan/nsw154

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* constructed emotion, predictive coding, active inference, interoception, allostasis, categorization, affect
- *Additional terms:* degeneracy, default mode network, salience network, visceromotor cortex, population thinking, emotion concepts, agranular cortex

#### Core Themes
- Emotions are not biologically hardwired categories with dedicated neural circuits; they are constructed by the brain through predictive coding and conceptual categorization
- The brain's primary function is allostasis (anticipatory body regulation); interoception is at the core of the brain's internal model
- Agranular limbic cortices (visceromotor regions) issue predictions that drive both body regulation and interoceptive experience, with prediction errors computed in more granular cortical regions
- Concepts are embodied, multimodal predictions that categorize sensory events; emotion concepts are a special case
- The default mode, salience, and frontoparietal control networks form the computational core for constructing all mental categories, including emotions
- Population thinking (from Darwin) is essential: emotion categories are populations of variable instances, not fixed types with biological essences

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Core theoretical framework for how mental phenotypes (emotions) are constructed via brain architecture, predictive processing, and evolutionary principles. The constructionist account directly challenges essentialist views of emotion and exemplifies the book's central argument.

---

### 67. Barrett (2022)

**Citation:** Barrett, L. F. (2022). Context reconsidered: Complex signal ensembles, relational meaning, and population thinking in psychological science. *American Psychologist, 77*(8), 894–920. https://doi.org/10.1037/amp0001054

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* context, emotion, construction, variation, relational meaning, population thinking
- *Additional terms:* complex signal ensembles, typological view, facial expressions, cultural inheritance, predictive processing, machine metaphor, methodology critique

#### Core Themes
- Three methodological debates about emotional expressions reveal that contextual features embedded in experimental designs inflate evidence for universal emotion prototypes
- Psychological meaning is entirely relational — physical signals have no inherent psychological meaning but derive meaning from their relationship to ensembles of other signals
- Population thinking should replace typological thinking: emotion categories are populations of variable, situation-dependent instances, not fixed types
- The "machine metaphor" (reductionist, modular causation) underlying much of psychological science is fundamentally misguided
- Cultural inheritance transmits conceptual categories across generations; evolution produced brains capable of flexible meaning-making rather than fixed emotion circuits

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly addresses how mental phenotypes are made via evolution, development, and the tools of psychological science. Critiques the methodology and ontological assumptions that shape psychological measurement.

---

### 68. Barrett & Simmons (2015)

**Citation:** Barrett, L. F., & Simmons, W. K. (2015). Interoceptive predictions in the brain. *Nature Reviews Neuroscience, 16*(7), 419–429. https://doi.org/10.1038/nrn3950

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* interoception, predictive coding, active inference, allostasis, visceromotor cortex
- *Additional terms:* EPIC model, agranular cortex, laminar organization, prediction error, anterior insula, default mode network, depression, precision

#### Core Themes
- The EPIC model proposes interoceptive experience is largely a construction of predictions from agranular visceromotor cortices, constrained rather than caused by ascending visceral sensations
- Laminar gradients in cortical architecture determine the flow of predictions (agranular → granular) and prediction errors (granular → agranular)
- Agranular visceromotor cortices are structurally insensitive to prediction error, meaning interoceptive perception is more prediction-driven than exteroceptive perception
- Visceromotor regions are rich-club network hubs, making interoceptive representations central to every mental event
- Aberrant interoceptive predictions may function as a common vulnerability mechanism for depression and other mental/physical illnesses

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational for understanding how interoceptive predictions shape mental phenotypes. The EPIC model connects brain architecture, predictive processing, and bodily regulation to the construction of experience.

---

### 69. Blanco, Otto, Maddox, Beevers & Love (2013)

**Citation:** Blanco, N. J., Otto, A. R., Maddox, W. T., Beevers, C. G., & Love, B. C. (2013). The influence of depression symptoms on exploratory decision-making. *Cognition, 129*(3), 563–568. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cognition.2013.08.018

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** CES-D (Center for Epidemiological Studies Depression Scale), Leapfrog task (two-armed bandit variant)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* depression, decision-making, exploration, exploitation, reinforcement learning
- *Additional terms:* computational modeling, reflexive vs. reflective strategies, Bayesian updating, Naive RL, Ideal Actor model

#### Core Themes
- N=133 undergraduates (38 depressive, 95 non-depressive). Depressive participants explored more often but less strategically
- Depressive participants' choices were better described by a reflexive Naive RL model maintaining minimal task representations; non-depressives were better fit by a Bayesian Ideal Actor
- Higher CES-D scores within the depressive group increased likelihood of being fit by the simpler model, suggesting dose-response
- The key distinction is the nature of exploration — reflexive (random) vs. reflective (uncertainty-driven)
- Depression may impair working memory and planning capacities needed for maintaining rich environmental representations

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates how a mental phenotype (depressive symptoms) alters fundamental cognitive processes (decision-making strategy), relevant to understanding how mood states shape information processing.

---

### 70. Blanco & Sloutsky (2024)

**Citation:** Blanco, N. J., & Sloutsky, V. M. (2024). Exploration, exploitation, and development: Developmental shifts in decision-making. *Child Development, 95*(4), 1287–1298. https://doi.org/10.1111/cdev.14070

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** Custom 4-option choice task (virtual candy game), computational model with reward-value and choice-lag parameters
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* exploration, exploitation, decision-making, cognitive development
- *Additional terms:* novelty preference, systematic exploration, computational modeling, exploration-exploitation dilemma, epistemic uncertainty, prolonged immaturity

#### Core Themes
- N=188 children (ages 3–8) + 26 adults. Young children (3–4) explored systematically via a "least recently chosen" strategy, not randomly
- Developmental shift is from novelty-preference-driven exploration to reward-value-driven exploitation
- Decision-making development is protracted: even 7–8-year-olds differ substantially from adults
- Computational modeling reveals choice-lag influence (systematic exploration) decreases with age while random exploration remains stable
- Early extensive exploration may serve an adaptive function, setting the stage for efficient learning across the lifespan
- The prolonged period of immaturity and exploration in human childhood may be a key contributor to human intelligence

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly addresses how a fundamental cognitive capacity (decision-making) develops across childhood. The exploration-to-exploitation shift exemplifies how mental phenotypes are made through development, with implications for the adaptive function of prolonged human immaturity.

---

### 71. Bogdanov & Pizzagalli (2025)

**Citation:** Bogdanov, M., & Pizzagalli, D. A. (2025). Effort-based decision-making paradigms in humans: A comprehensive review. *Cognitive, Affective, & Behavioral Neuroscience*. Advance online publication.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** EEfRT, BET, COG-ED, AGT, DST, EFT, gsEDT, aEDT, DCET, ESCT, PRT, OCT
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* effort-based decision-making, effort discounting, motivation, anhedonia, dopamine
- *Additional terms:* reward, cognitive effort, physical effort, amotivation, cost-benefit analysis, apathy, schizophrenia, depression, Parkinson's, ventral striatum, dACC, vmPFC

#### Core Themes
- Effort paradigms vary along key dimensions (prospective vs. retrospective, explicit vs. implicit, physical vs. cognitive) that systematically influence results
- Core neural circuit: dACC (effort costs), vmPFC (subjective value), ventral striatum (reward encoding), dopaminergic signaling
- Clinical populations show altered effort processing: schizophrenia (diminished reward sensitivity), depression (heightened effort aversion linked to anhedonia), Parkinson's (dopamine-dependent physical effort deficits)
- Dopaminergic manipulation consistently increases willingness to exert effort across paradigms and populations
- Important inconsistencies across paradigms suggest task-specific mechanisms rather than a single "effort" construct

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly addresses how motivated behavior phenotypes are constructed through neural mechanisms, with implications for understanding individual differences in effort allocation and their disruption in psychopathology.

### 72. Castanheira, Otto et al. (2026)

**Citation:** Castanheira, K. da S., Otto, A. R., et al. (2026). A neural currency for rewards and effort. *bioRxiv* preprint. https://doi.org/10.1101/2022.10.28.513278

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis (ALE coordinate-based neuroimaging)
**Instruments:** fMRI (BOLD), Activation Likelihood Estimation (ALE) method
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cognitive effort, reward, dorsal anterior cingulate cortex, fMRI, ALE meta-analysis
- *Additional terms:* neural currency, cost-benefit tradeoff, anterior insula, lateral prefrontal cortex, ventral striatum, Expected Value of Control

#### Core Themes
- ALE meta-analysis (~45 experiments, ~1,000 participants) examining neural responses to rewards and cognitive effort
- dACC and anterior insula reliably co-activated by both reward and cognitive effort manipulations, suggesting a shared neural currency
- Ventral striatum engaged by rewards but also sensitive to effort costs
- Rostral vs. caudal dACC may show functional specialization along a reward-to-demand gradient
- Cost-benefit models of effort allocation (e.g., Expected Value of Control) are supported by the overlapping activations

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides meta-analytic evidence for shared neural substrates of reward and effort processing, central to understanding how the brain's cost-benefit architecture shapes motivated behavior.

### 73. Decker, Otto, Daw & Hartley (2016)

**Citation:** Decker, J. H., Otto, A. R., Daw, N. D., & Hartley, C. A. (2016). From creatures of habit to goal-directed learners: Tracking the developmental emergence of model-based reinforcement learning. *Psychological Science, 27*(6), 848–858. https://doi.org/10.1177/0956797616639301

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** Two-stage sequential RL task ("spaceship task," adapted from Daw et al., 2011)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cognitive development, reinforcement learning, model-based learning, model-free learning
- *Additional terms:* goal-directed behavior, habitual behavior, executive function, prefrontal cortex, developmental trajectory

#### Core Themes
- Children (8–12, n=20), adolescents (13–17, n=20), adults (18–25, n=19) completed a two-stage sequential decision task
- Model-free (habitual) RL present across all age groups; model-based (goal-directed) RL absent in children, emerged in adolescents, strengthened in adults
- Children showed purely model-free patterns despite having explicit knowledge of the task's transition structure — a knowledge-action dissociation
- Developmental emergence of model-based RL may reflect protracted maturation of prefrontal-hippocampal-striatal circuitry
- Computational modeling confirmed age selectively increased the model-based parameter without affecting model-free weights

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly demonstrates how a core mental phenotype (goal-directed vs. habitual decision-making) is constructed through developmental maturation of neural systems.

---

### 74. Dercon et al. (2024)

**Citation:** Dercon, Q., Mehrhof, S. Z., Sandhu, T. R., Hitchcock, C., Lawson, R. P., Pizzagalli, D. A., Dalgleish, T., & Nord, C. L. (2024). A core component of psychological therapy causes adaptive changes in computational learning mechanisms. *Psychological Medicine, 54*(2), 327–337. https://doi.org/10.1017/S0033291723001587

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention, Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** Probabilistic Selection Task (PST; Frank et al., 2004, 2007), OCI-R, EAT, AES, SDS, STAI, BIS, LSAS, AUDIT; transdiagnostic factor scores
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* computational psychiatry, cognitive distancing, emotion regulation, reinforcement learning, psychotherapy
- *Additional terms:* Q-learning, inverse temperature, loss learning rate, transdiagnostic, anxiety, depression, compulsive behavior

#### Core Themes
- N=935. Cognitive distancing (core CBT technique) causally altered computational RL mechanisms in a large online experiment
- Distanced participants showed higher inverse temperatures (clearer value representations) and progressively greater loss learning rates (~24.6% higher by end of training)
- Higher compulsive behavior scores associated with reduced value sensitivity (lower inverse temperatures)
- The therapeutic mechanism may be more effective engagement with negative information rather than reduced engagement
- Effects emerged over time, suggesting practice is critical for therapeutic benefit

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates how a psychological intervention reshapes the computational architecture of learning, directly relevant to how mental phenotypes are shaped by experience and therapeutic tools.

### 75. Dolcos et al. (2020)

**Citation:** Dolcos, F., Katsumi, Y., Moore, M., Berggren, N., de Gelder, B., Derakshan, N., ... & Dolcos, S. (2020). Neural correlates of emotion-attention interactions: From perception, learning, and memory to social cognition, individual differences, and training interventions. *Neuroscience and Biobehavioral Reviews, 108*, 559–601. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.neubiorev.2019.08.017

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A (review of fMRI, ERP, and behavioral studies)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* emotion-attention interactions, affective neuroscience, amygdala, prefrontal cortex
- *Additional terms:* working memory, episodic memory, emotion regulation, attentional control, PTSD, anxiety, depression, cognitive training, mindfulness, Human Affectome Project

#### Core Themes
- Emotion and attention interact bidirectionally: emotional stimuli capture attention (bottom-up) and attention regulates emotional processing (top-down)
- The amygdala and PFC are central nodes, with distinct roles in enhancing vs. impairing cognitive performance
- Individual differences (sex, age, personality, clinical status) modulate emotion-attention interactions
- PTSD, anxiety, and depression involve characteristic disturbances in emotion-attention interactions
- Cognitive training interventions (WM training, attentional control training, mindfulness) can harness neuroplasticity to improve emotion regulation

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly relevant to understanding how cognitive and emotional systems interact to shape mental phenotypes, covering both individual differences and intervention/plasticity mechanisms.

### 76. Hoemann et al. (2021)

**Citation:** Hoemann, K., Nielson, C., Yuen, A., Gurera, J. W., Quigley, K. S., & Barrett, L. F. (2021). Expertise in emotion: A scoping review and unifying framework for individual differences in the mental representation of emotional experience. *Psychological Bulletin, 147*(11), 1159–1183. https://doi.org/10.1037/bul0000327

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Systematic Review
**Instruments:** TAS-20, LEAS, TMMS, RDEES, MSCEIT, PEC, and many others
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* alexithymia, emotional awareness, emotional clarity, emotional complexity, emotional intelligence
- *Additional terms:* emotional creativity, emotional granularity, emotional competence, emotional diversity, emotional flexibility, expertise, construct proliferation, emergent variable

#### Core Themes
- Scoping review of 141 publications covering 10 constructs related to individual differences in emotional experience
- Domain-general expertise framework (12 features) unifies constructs including alexithymia, awareness, clarity, complexity, creativity, diversity, flexibility, granularity, intelligence, and competence
- Network analyses reveal overlapping but distinct clusters, consistent with an emergent variable model (indicators jointly constitute the construct) rather than a latent variable model
- Emotional expertise involves learnable skills (not just traits), suggesting pathways for deliberate improvement through practice
- Key gaps: underrepresentation of flexibility and diversity constructs, limited cross-cultural research

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Comprehensive framework for how the mental phenotype of emotional experience is constructed, organized, and measured. The emergent variable model has implications for personality structure measurement as well.

### 77. Kaiser, Andrews-Hanna, Wager & Pizzagalli (2015)

**Citation:** Kaiser, R. H., Andrews-Hanna, J. R., Wager, T. D., & Pizzagalli, D. A. (2015). Large-scale network dysfunction in major depressive disorder: Meta-analysis of resting-state functional connectivity. *JAMA Psychiatry, 72*(6), 603–611. https://doi.org/10.1001/jamapsychiatry.2015.0071

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis
**Instruments:** Resting-state fMRI, Multilevel Kernel Density Analysis (MKDA) toolbox
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* depression, major depressive disorder, resting-state functional connectivity, meta-analysis
- *Additional terms:* frontoparietal network, default mode network, dorsal attention network, affective network, rumination, cognitive control, network dysfunction

#### Core Themes
- First meta-analysis of resting-state FC in MDD (27 datasets; 556 MDD, 518 HC)
- Hypoconnectivity within frontoparietal control network: reduced coordination for cognitive control and emotion regulation
- Hyperconnectivity within default network (hippocampus, MPFC): potentially reflecting enhanced self-referential thinking and rumination
- Imbalanced frontoparietal–default network connectivity: bias toward internal thoughts at cost of external attention
- Hypoconnectivity between affective network and MPFC: disrupted emotion regulation communication
- Network abnormalities consistent across demographic and clinical subgroups

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Meta-analytic evidence for the neural architecture underlying the depressive phenotype. Directly relevant to understanding how brain network organization gives rise to mood-related mental phenotypes.

### 78. Katsumi, Theriault, Quigley & Barrett (2022)

**Citation:** Katsumi, Y., Theriault, J. E., Quigley, K. S., & Barrett, L. F. (2022). Allostasis as a core feature of hierarchical gradients in the human brain. *Network Neuroscience, 6*(4), 1010–1031. https://doi.org/10.1162/netn_a_00240

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A (fMRI data from >500 participants referenced from prior studies)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* predictive processing, interoception, functional networks, energetics, allostasis, hierarchical gradients
- *Additional terms:* default mode network, salience network, cerebral cortex, cerebellum, hippocampus, lamination gradient, active inference

#### Core Themes
- The brain's fundamental job is to predictively regulate the body's energy resources (allostasis), not merely react to stimuli
- Two hierarchical cortical gradients (association-sensorimotor and representation-modulation) can be interpreted as components of predictive processing
- Limbic cortices (agranular/dysgranular) generate top-down predictions; granular sensory cortices process prediction errors, following a cytoarchitectural gradient
- Default mode and salience networks overlap to form an intrinsic allostatic neural system
- Corresponding gradients exist in cerebellum and hippocampus, suggesting allostasis is organized across the entire brain
- Psychological phenomena may emerge from the same domain-general computational architecture that serves allostasis

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides a neurobiological framework for understanding how brain architecture evolved to serve predictive energy regulation, and how mental phenotypes emerge from this organization.

---

### 79. Khalsa et al. (2018)

**Citation:** Khalsa, S. S., Adolphs, R., Cameron, O. G., Critchley, H. D., Davenport, P. W., Feinstein, J. S., ... & Paulus, M. P. (2018). Interoception and mental health: A roadmap. *Biological Psychiatry: Cognitive Neuroscience and Neuroimaging, 3*(6), 501–513. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.bpsc.2017.12.004

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review (NIMH-sponsored consensus workshop)
**Instruments:** N/A (reviews heartbeat detection tasks, MAIA, body perception questionnaires)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* interoception, interoceptive accuracy, interoceptive sensibility, interoceptive awareness, mental health
- *Additional terms:* transdiagnostic, heartbeat detection, insula, anterior cingulate cortex, predictive coding, allostasis, anxiety, depression, eating disorders, body awareness

#### Core Themes
- Interoception is a multidimensional construct (accuracy, sensibility, awareness) requiring distinct measurement approaches
- Disrupted interoception is transdiagnostic: implicated in anxiety, depression, eating disorders, somatic symptom disorders, substance use
- Insula and anterior cingulate cortex are key neural substrates for interoceptive processing
- Computational models (predictive coding, active inference) provide a promising framework for understanding interoceptive dysfunction
- Standardized assessment methods and lifespan/developmental approaches are critical unmet needs
- Targeted interoceptive interventions represent an underexplored treatment avenue

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Interoception is a foundational mechanism by which bodily signals shape mental phenotypes. This roadmap defines the construct dimensionally and links it to transdiagnostic psychopathology.

### 80. Kurzban, Duckworth, Kable & Myers (2013)

**Citation:** Kurzban, R., Duckworth, A., Kable, J. W., & Myers, J. (2013). An opportunity cost model of subjective effort and task performance. *Behavioral and Brain Sciences, 36*(6), 661–679. https://doi.org/10.1017/S0140525X12003196

**Purpose:** Commentary/Editorial (target article with peer commentary)
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* evolutionary psychology, mental effort, neuroeconomics, self-control, opportunity cost
- *Additional terms:* ego depletion, willpower, executive function, vigilance, cost-benefit computation, resource allocation, prioritization

#### Core Themes
- Mental effort reflects opportunity cost computations, not depletion of a physical resource (e.g., glucose)
- Certain computational processes can serve multiple functions but not all at once, requiring prioritization via cost/benefit analysis
- Subjective phenomenology of effort (fatigue, boredom) functions as a motivational signal to reallocate computational resources
- Performance decrements result from dynamic learning about opportunity costs, not irreversible resource depletion
- Incentives restore performance by changing the cost/benefit calculus — incompatible with strict depletion models
- Directly challenges Baumeister's ego depletion / willpower-as-resource model

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides an evolutionary-computational framework for understanding effort, self-control, and motivation as adaptations shaped by natural selection.

### 81. Mehrhof, Fleming & Nord (2025)

**Citation:** Mehrhof, S. Z., Fleming, H., & Nord, C. L. (2025). An interoceptive model of energy allostasis linking metabolic and mental health. *Science Advances, 11*(39), eady4356. https://doi.org/10.1126/sciadv.ady4356

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* interoception, allostasis, energy regulation, metabolic health, depression
- *Additional terms:* insulin resistance, leptin, ghrelin, GLP-1, dopamine, reward processing, effort-based decision-making, inflammation, predictive processing, cardiometabolic comorbidity

#### Core Themes
- Proposes "interoceptive energy allostasis" framework for depression–cardiometabolic disease comorbidity
- Neuroendocrine signals (insulin, leptin, ghrelin, GLP-1) cross the blood-brain barrier and directly modulate dopaminergic reward circuits
- The interoceptive allostatic system can be disrupted at multiple levels: faulty metabolic signals, disrupted interoception, disconnected internal models, imprecise prediction errors, altered learning
- A "vicious cycle" model: metabolic changes → altered neural processing → changed behavior → worsened metabolism
- Shared cognitive phenotypes (disrupted reward sensitivity and effort-based decision-making) may represent common risk factors for both conditions
- The framework generates specific clinical predictions, including interoceptive interventions for comorbidity

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Integrates interoception, allostasis, and computational psychiatry to explain how metabolic and mental phenotypes co-emerge from shared biological mechanisms.
