### 82. Milaneschi, Simmons, van Rossum & Penninx (2019)

**Citation:** Milaneschi, Y., Simmons, W. K., van Rossum, E. F. C., & Penninx, B. W. J. H. (2019). Depression and obesity: Evidence of shared biological mechanisms. *Molecular Psychiatry, 24*(1), 18–33. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41380-018-0017-5

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* depression, obesity, inflammation, cortisol, leptin, insulin resistance
- *Additional terms:* gut microbiome, HPA axis, atypical depression, metabolic syndrome, reward circuitry, interoception, insula, hypothalamus, comorbidity

#### Core Themes
- Depression and obesity share bidirectional biological mechanisms: HPA-axis dysregulation, inflammation, metabolic signaling
- Atypical depression (increased appetite/weight) is specifically linked to immunometabolic dysregulation, distinct from melancholic depression
- Leptin resistance and insulin resistance are shared mechanisms connecting obesity and depression at neuroendocrine and brain levels
- The gut microbiome is an emerging pathway through immune signaling and metabolic effects
- Brain regions involved in interoception (insula) and reward (striatum, OFC, vmPFC) mediate behavioral manifestations of both conditions
- Treatment implications include targeting shared mechanisms (anti-inflammatory agents, bupropion, lifestyle interventions)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Illustrates how biological mechanisms (immune, metabolic, neuroendocrine) shape the joint expression of mood and body-weight phenotypes — a case study in multi-system phenotype construction.

### 83. Miller, Rassaby, Collins & Milad (2022)

**Citation:** Miller, D. B., Rassaby, M. M., Collins, K. A., & Milad, M. R. (2022). Behavioral and neural mechanisms of latent inhibition. *Learning & Memory, 29*, 38–47. https://doi.org/10.1101/lm.053439.121

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* latent inhibition, fear conditioning, threat extinction, pre-exposure
- *Additional terms:* attentional theory, retrieval/competition theory, nucleus accumbens, hippocampus, amygdala, vmPFC, PTSD, resilience, schizophrenia, virtual reality

#### Core Themes
- Latent inhibition (LI) reduces conditioned fear responses by prior non-reinforced exposure to a to-be-conditioned stimulus
- Neural circuit involves nucleus accumbens (shell vs. core dissociation), hippocampus, basolateral amygdala, and vmPFC, substantially overlapping with threat extinction circuitry
- LI is highly sensitive to context: context shifts between pre-exposure and conditioning abolish it
- Two competing theoretical accounts: attentional gating vs. retrieval competition
- Clinical potential as PTSD prophylactic via VR pre-exposure to build resilience in at-risk populations
- Human neuroimaging studies remain scarce; translating animal findings is a critical gap

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Relevant to understanding how evolved attentional and learning mechanisms shape fear responses and resilience, focused on a specific conditioning paradigm.

### 84. Nord & Garfinkel (2022)

**Citation:** Nord, C. L., & Garfinkel, S. N. (2022). Interoceptive pathways to understand and treat mental health conditions. *Trends in Cognitive Sciences, 26*(6), 499–513. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.tics.2022.03.004

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A (reviews heartbeat detection tasks, respiratory tests, interoceptive self-report measures)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* interoception, body-brain coupling, emotion, mental health treatment
- *Additional terms:* interoceptive accuracy, interoceptive insight, cardiac cycle, immune system, inflammation, alexithymia, SSRIs, propranolol, mindfulness, precision medicine

#### Core Themes
- Peripheral physiological signals from heart, lungs, gut, and immune system shape emotion via interoceptive pathways operating across fast (cardiac) to slow (immune) timescales
- Multiple dimensions of interoception (accuracy, insight, attention, attribution) are differentially disrupted across psychiatric conditions
- Existing treatments (SSRIs, beta-blockers, CBT, mindfulness) may exert therapeutic effects partly through interoceptive mechanisms
- Novel pharmacological approaches targeting peripheral systems (renin-angiotensin, anti-inflammatory, statins) show promise
- A precision medicine approach is needed: match interoceptive interventions to individual disruption profiles
- Back-translation research — from effective therapies to their interoceptive mechanisms — is essential

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly relevant to understanding how the body-brain interface constructs emotional and mental health phenotypes, with implications for interoceptive individual differences.

### 85. Otto, Raio, Chiang, Phelps & Daw (2013)

**Citation:** Otto, A. R., Raio, C. M., Chiang, A., Phelps, E. A., & Daw, N. D. (2013). Working-memory capacity protects model-based learning from stress. *Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences, 110*(52), 20941–20946. https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1312011110

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention, Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** Two-step RL task, Cold Pressor Test (CPT), Operation Span (OSPAN), salivary cortisol
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* model-based learning, model-free learning, working memory capacity, acute stress, cortisol
- *Additional terms:* HPA axis, prefrontal cortex, dual-system decision-making, cognitive reserve, cold pressor test

#### Core Themes
- N=56 (48 after exclusions). Acute stress (cortisol) selectively attenuated model-based but not model-free RL
- High WM capacity (OSPAN) protected against stress-induced impairment — "cognitive reserve" for goal-directed decision-making
- The two-step RL task dissociates model-based and model-free contributions via distinct trial-by-trial behavioral signatures
- Connects dual-process theories of decision-making with neuroscience of stress, PFC function, and executive resources
- Individual differences in WM/executive capacity explain heterogeneity in stress effects on decision-making

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly demonstrates how individual differences in a core cognitive capacity (WM) shape the expression of distinct decision-making systems under environmental challenge.

### 86. Otto, Skatova, Madlon-Kay & Daw (2015)

**Citation:** Otto, A. R., Skatova, A., Madlon-Kay, S., & Daw, N. D. (2015). Cognitive control predicts use of model-based reinforcement learning. *Journal of Cognitive Neuroscience, 27*(2), 319–333. https://doi.org/10.1162/jocn_a_00709

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention, Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** Stroop color-naming task, Dot Pattern Expectancy (DPX) task / AX-CPT variant, two-step sequential RL task
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cognitive control, model-based reinforcement learning, model-free reinforcement learning
- *Additional terms:* Stroop task, AX-CPT, DPX, proactive control, reactive control, prefrontal cortex, goal-directed behavior, habitual behavior, individual differences

#### Core Themes
- Two experiments (N=45, N=51). Individual differences in cognitive control predict model-based RL contributions in a separate sequential choice task
- Proactive (rather than reactive) cognitive control specifically linked to model-based strategy use
- Model-free action tendencies are analogous to prepotent responses in Stroop/DPX tasks; model-based choice requires overriding these via higher-order representations
- Suggests common underlying computational and neural mechanisms (likely PFC-dependent) for cognitive control and model-based RL
- Implications for understanding compulsive disorders where habitual control dominates

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Establishes a critical bridge between cognitive control and reinforcement learning systems, showing how individual variation in executive capacity shapes decision-making architecture.

### 87. Otto, Westbrook & Daunizeau (2025)

**Citation:** Otto, A. R., Westbrook, A., & Daunizeau, J. (2025). Why is cognitive effort experienced as costly? *Trends in Cognitive Sciences*. Advance online publication. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.tics.2025.10.012

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cognitive effort, subjective effort costs, working memory, N-back task
- *Additional terms:* opportunity costs, bottleneck, information theory, KL divergence, network control theory, controllability, prefrontal cortex, metabolic costs, brain energy budget

#### Core Themes
- Three frameworks for why controlled processing is experienced as costly: (1) architectural constraints/processing bottlenecks (opportunity costs), (2) information-theoretic (effort as KL divergence between prior and posterior distributions), (3) brain network control theory (energy to reach hard-to-reach neural states)
- Cognitive costs, cognitive effort, and the sense of effort are related but distinct constructs
- The three accounts share points of convergence (metabolic costs, PFC role) but differ in fundamental commitments
- RNN simulations of the N-back task can generate testable predictions to distinguish these accounts
- Key open questions: what makes cognitive control specifically vulnerable, and how to measure effort costs independently

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Addresses a foundational question about human cognitive architecture — why effortful thinking is costly — integrating evolutionary, computational, and neuroscientific perspectives.

---

### 88. Pessiglione, Blain, Wiehler & Naik (2025)

**Citation:** Pessiglione, M., Blain, B., Wiehler, A., & Naik, S. (2025). Origins and consequences of cognitive fatigue. *Trends in Cognitive Sciences, 29*(8), 730–749. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.tics.2025.02.005

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A (reviews fMRI, MR spectroscopy, pupillometry, EEG, economic choice tasks, VAS fatigue scales)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cognitive fatigue, cognitive control, MetaMotiF model, effort cost, prefrontal cortex
- *Additional terms:* glutamate, metabolic alteration, choice bias, ego depletion, time-on-task, motivation, impulsivity, economic choice, MR spectroscopy

#### Core Themes
- Cognitive fatigue is an objective neurophysiological state arising from sustained cognitive control, distinct from subjective fatigue reports
- Best behavioral marker: "choice bias" toward low-effort/immediate-reward options in economic decisions, not self-report or performance decline
- MR spectroscopy evidence suggests glutamate accumulation in lPFC during prolonged cognitive work — a candidate metabolic mechanism
- The MetaMotiF model integrates biological (metabolic alteration) and motivational (cost-benefit tradeoff) accounts: fatigue increases the cost of cognitive control
- Cognitive fatigue manifests differently than time pressure: fatigue shifts the psychometric choice function (bias), while time pressure changes its slope (stochasticity)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Mechanistic account of how sustained cognitive effort produces metabolic changes that alter motivation and decision-making — directly relevant to understanding cognitive and motivational phenotype construction.

---

### 89. Pizzagalli & Roberts (2022)

**Citation:** Pizzagalli, D. A., & Roberts, A. C. (2022). Prefrontal cortex and depression. *Neuropsychopharmacology, 47*(1), 225–246. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41386-021-01101-7

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A (reviews fMRI, structural MRI, PET, various preclinical paradigms)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* major depressive disorder, prefrontal cortex, anhedonia, negative processing bias, learned helplessness
- *Additional terms:* anterior cingulate cortex, orbitofrontal cortex, dorsolateral PFC, reward processing, translational neuroscience, chronic stress, ketamine, RDoC, cross-species

#### Core Themes
- PFC dysfunction is one of the most consistently implicated neural abnormalities in MDD, with distinct subregions showing opposite patterns depending on the phenotype assessed
- Dissecting depression into dimensions (anhedonia, negative bias, helplessness) enables precise mapping onto PFC circuits and cross-species translation
- Anhedonia linked to blunted frontostriatal activation and disrupted reward learning
- Negative processing biases involve hyper-activation of limbic regions coupled with deficient dlPFC recruitment for cognitive control
- Many PFC abnormalities precede MDD onset — vulnerability markers rather than consequences
- Treatment effects (SSRIs, ketamine, TMS, ECT) normalize PFC activation in domain-specific ways

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides essential context for how PFC circuitry underpins affective and cognitive variation, and how stress reshapes these circuits.

### 90. Sandra & Otto (2018)

**Citation:** Sandra, D. A., & Otto, A. R. (2018). Cognitive capacity limitations and Need for Cognition differentially predict reward-induced cognitive effort expenditure. *Cognition, 172*, 101–106. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cognition.2017.12.004

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention, Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** Stroop task, task-switching paradigm, Need for Cognition scale (18-item), BIS/BAS, Barratt Impulsiveness Scale, GAD-7
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cognitive effort, reward incentives, executive function, Need for Cognition
- *Additional terms:* task switching, Stroop interference, cost-benefit tradeoff, cognitive control, individual differences, switch costs, effort avoidance, BIS/BAS

#### Core Themes
- N=54 (45 after exclusions). Lower executive function capacity (larger Stroop interference) → greater reward-induced reductions in switch costs
- Low Need for Cognition also predicted larger reward-driven effort increases, complementing the capacity-based account
- Findings support a cost-benefit framework: effort expenditure governed by perceived costs (capacity and disposition) vs. available benefits (rewards)
- BAS (reward sensitivity) did not account for effects, ruling out simple reward-reactivity explanations
- EF capacity and NFC are moderately correlated, suggesting intrinsic motivation for effort may partly reflect cognitive ability

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates how cognitive capacity and motivation jointly shape decisions about effort investment — relevant to how the architecture of mental phenotypes constrains behavior through cost-benefit computations.

---

### 91. Schulze et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Schulze, C., Aka, A., Bartels, D. M., Bucher, S. F., Embrey, J. R., Gureckis, T. M., ... & Newell, B. R. (2025). A timeline of cognitive costs in decision making. *Trends in Cognitive Sciences*. Advance online publication.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cognitive costs, decision making, cognitive effort, opportunity cost, bounded rationality
- *Additional terms:* algorithmic cost, representational cost, meta-cognitive cost, entry cost, remembered cost, cognitive resources, heuristics, choice architecture, cognitive offloading

#### Core Themes
- Cognitive costs in decision-making are heterogeneous and organized along a temporal timeline (before, during, and after a decision)
- Six distinct cost types: entry, representational, meta-cognitive, algorithmic, opportunity, and remembered costs
- Cognitive costs, cognitive effort, and the sense of effort are related but distinct constructs
- Neurobiological accounts include depletive (glucose/glycogen), accumulative (glutamate), and limited bandwidth theories
- The temporal framework enables targeted behavioral interventions at each stage
- Individual differences in cognitive cost sensitivity are underexplored with implications for health, financial, and environmental decisions

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Comprehensive framework for understanding the costs of cognition that constrain and shape decision-making phenotypes, directly relevant to bounded rationality.

### 92. Sharpe & Tyndall (2025)

**Citation:** Sharpe, B. T., & Tyndall, I. (2025). The sustained attention paradox: A critical commentary on the theoretical impossibility of perfect vigilance. *Cognitive Science, 49*, e70061. https://doi.org/10.1111/cogs.70061

**Purpose:** Commentary/Editorial
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* attention, sustained attention, vigilance, cognitive limitations, human-technology systems
- *Additional terms:* vigilance decrement, neural oscillations, default mode network, cognitive fatigue, adaptive automation, locus coeruleus-norepinephrine

#### Core Themes
- Perfect vigilance is fundamentally impossible due to inherent properties of human cognitive architecture
- Neural mechanisms (rhythmic oscillations, LC-NE dynamics, FPN fluctuations, DMN activity) impose hard constraints on continuous attention
- Even elite performers (military, experienced meditators) exhibit measurable lapses when examined closely
- Safety-critical systems relying primarily on human vigilance are fundamentally flawed
- Advocates adaptive automation and human-technology collaboration that works with cognitive constraints
- Individual and cross-cultural differences affect the rate but not the fundamental existence of attentional limitations

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Relevant to understanding cognitive constraints as products of neural architecture and evolution. The argument that attention limits are adaptive design features connects to how mental phenotypes are shaped by evolutionary pressures.

---

### 93. Theriault et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Theriault, J. E., Katsumi, Y., Reimann, H. M., Zhang, J., Deming, P., Quigley, K. S., Dickerson, B. C., & Barrett, L. F. (2025). It's not the thought that counts: Allostasis at the core of brain function. *Neuron, 113*, 4107–4133. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.neuron.2025.09.028

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A (fMRI connectivity data n=90 at 7T; n=550 at 3T; extensive anatomical tract-tracing literature)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* allostasis, brain function, interoception, visceromotor control, predictive regulation
- *Additional terms:* limbic-to-sensory gradient, cytoarchitecture, default mode network, salience network, body regulation, Alzheimer's disease, metabolic regulation, waste clearance

#### Core Themes
- The brain's primary function is allostasis (predictive bodily regulation), not cognition or thought; mental phenomena are means to the allostatic end
- A distributed allostatic system of 21 cortical and subcortical regions organizes whole-brain signaling, overlapping with DMN, salience/cingulo-opercular, and central autonomic networks
- The limbic-to-sensory cytoarchitectural gradient organizes cortical signal processing, with allostatic regions at the limbic core issuing low-dimensional predictions
- Ascending viscerosensory signals (breathing, heartbeat) shape brain-wide neural activity, providing context for all psychological phenomena
- Cognitive decline in Alzheimer's may be an allostatic trade-off: the brain sacrifices cognition to maintain more critical biological variables
- The "allostasis-first" perspective reframes psychology, dissolving the artificial boundary between mental and physical

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational framework linking evolution, brain architecture, bodily regulation, and the construction of psychological phenomena. The allostasis-first thesis recasts mental features as arising from evolutionary pressures for metabolic regulation.

---

### 94. Westbrook & Braver (2015)

**Citation:** Westbrook, A., & Braver, T. S. (2015). Cognitive effort: A neuroeconomic approach. *Cognitive, Affective, & Behavioral Neuroscience, 15*, 395–415. https://doi.org/10.3758/s13415-015-0334-y

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** COGED paradigm, N-back task, BDM auctions, delay discounting tasks
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cognitive effort, decision-making, dopamine, motivation, working memory, neuroeconomics
- *Additional terms:* cognitive control, subjective value, effort discounting, COGED, opportunity costs, anterior cingulate cortex, Need for Cognition, aging

#### Core Themes
- Cognitive effort is poorly defined; framing it as effort-based decision-making (cost-benefit analysis) provides the most useful operationalization
- COGED paradigm quantifies effort as subjective devaluation of rewards with increasing cognitive demands
- Effort costs likely reflect opportunity costs of allocating limited WM/cognitive control resources
- Key neural substrates: ACC (cost-benefit encoding), DLPFC (WM maintenance), anterior insula (interoceptive cost signals), ventral striatum (incentive valuation)
- Individual differences in effort sensitivity are meaningful: correlate with trait cognitive motivation, predict delay discounting, clinically relevant for depression and schizophrenia
- Behavioral economic methods can dissociate decision-making from task engagement, isolating effort-specific processes

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly relevant to understanding how mental phenotypes are constructed through the interplay of cognitive architecture, motivation, and decision-making.

### 95. Westbrook & Braver (2016)

**Citation:** Westbrook, A., & Braver, T. S. (2016). Dopamine does double duty in motivating cognitive effort. *Neuron, 89*(4), 695–710. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.neuron.2015.12.029

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* dopamine, cognitive effort, working memory, prefrontal cortex, anterior cingulate cortex
- *Additional terms:* striatum, reward prediction error, cost-benefit decision-making, incentive motivation, tonic dopamine, phasic dopamine, cognitive control, goal pursuit, reinforcement learning

#### Core Themes
- Dopamine serves "double duty": (1) directly modulating WM circuits in PFC (tonic DA → stability, phasic DA → flexible updating), and (2) mediating value-learning and cost-benefit decisions about effortful actions
- Tonic DA in PFC enhances recurrent firing and signal-to-noise ratio in task-relevant networks
- Phasic DA in striatum trains value-based gating policies for WM updating, encoding net reward discounted by effort costs
- The ACC tracks accruing opportunity costs and incremental goal progress, conveying cost-benefit balance to DA midbrain
- DA in ventral striatum encodes net incentive value and biases toward model-based over habitual strategies
- Unifies two largely independent literatures: DA's role in WM modulation and DA's role in value-learning/decision-making

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Core mechanistic account of how dopamine systems create the motivational phenotype of cognitive effort, bridging evolved neural architecture with the psychological experience of effort.

---

### 96. Westlin et al. (2023)

**Citation:** Westlin, C., Theriault, J. E., Katsumi, Y., Nieto-Castanon, A., Kucyi, A., Ruf, S. F., ... & Barrett, L. F. (2023). Improving the study of brain-behavior relationships by revisiting basic assumptions. *Trends in Cognitive Sciences, 27*(3), 246–257. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.tics.2022.12.015

**Purpose:** Commentary/Editorial
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* brain-behavior relationships, localization, degeneracy, neural ensembles, complex systems
- *Additional terms:* whole-brain modeling, many-to-one mappings, replication, neuroimaging, fMRI, ontological assumptions, within-participant sampling, model-first approach, interoception

#### Core Themes
- The localization assumption is undermined: ~72% of brain voxels show reliable task-related BOLD when studies are sufficiently powered
- Degeneracy (many-to-one mappings) is a fundamental organizing principle: multiple different neural ensembles can produce the same psychological outcome
- The independence assumption fails: neural context and bodily signals (heart rate, respiration) modulate brain-wide activity in ways routinely unmeasured
- A model-first approach — specifying assumptions formally before experiments — can prevent self-fulfilling prophecies from default analytical commitments
- Prioritizing within-participant over between-participant sampling is essential because population abstractions don't generalize to individuals
- The brain is a complex system where weak, nonlinearly interacting signals from brain, body, and world collectively produce mental events

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational methodological critique directly relevant to how psychological science constructs its understanding of mental phenotypes. Degeneracy, complexity, and context-dependence are inherent features of brain-behavior relationships, not noise.

---

### 97. Dora et al. (2023)

**Citation:** Dora, J., Piccirillo, M., Foster, K. T., Arbeau, K., Armeli, S., Auriacombe, M., ... King, K. M. (2023). The daily association between affect and alcohol use: A meta-analysis of individual participant data. *Psychological Bulletin*, *149*(1-2), 1-24. https://doi.org/10.1037/bul0000387

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis, Experience Sampling, Mega-Analysis
**Instruments:** PANAS, various daily affect scales, daily alcohol consumption measures
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* affect, alcohol use, ecological momentary assessment, daily diary, individual participant data meta-analysis, self-medication, affect regulation
- *Additional terms:* negative reinforcement model, motivational theories of drinking, Baker et al. (2004), Koob & Le Moal (2008), positive affect, negative affect, within-person effects, Bayesian multilevel models, ergodicity, floor effects

#### Core Themes
- Influential theoretical models posit that negative affect drives alcohol consumption, yet empirical support from daily studies has been inconsistent
- Largest test of the affect-alcohol link using individual participant data (N = 12,394; 353,762 days) across 69 studies
- Positive affect, but not negative affect, was associated with same-day alcohol use in mostly non-clinical samples
- The null negative affect finding challenges a cornerstone assumption of motivational models of drinking

#### Results Summary

**Daily affect as predictor of same-day alcohol use (Bayesian multilevel hurdle models):**

| Predictor | Drinking Occurrence (OR) | 95% CI |
|---|---|---|
| Negative affect (within-person) | 1.01 | [0.97, 1.06] |
| Positive affect (within-person) | 1.06 | [1.01, 1.12]\* |
| Weekend day | 2.12 | [1.97, 2.28]\*\* |

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Definitive large-scale empirical test showing how intensive longitudinal methods and individual participant data meta-analysis can challenge widely held theoretical assumptions about emotion-behavior links.

### 98. Fairbairn & Sayette (2014)

**Citation:** Fairbairn, C. E., & Sayette, M. A. (2014). A social-attributional analysis of alcohol response. *Psychological Bulletin*, *140*(5), 1361-1382. https://doi.org/10.1037/a0037563

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Systematic Review, Meta-Analysis
**Instruments:** Various self-report mood scales, FACS
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* alcohol, social interaction, mood enhancement, stress response dampening, social attribution
- *Additional terms:* attention-allocation model, appraisal-disruption model, alcohol myopia, confederate paradigm, naive-participant paradigm, Duchenne smile

#### Core Themes
- Alcohol's mood-enhancing effects depend critically on the nature of social interaction
- Meta-analysis distinguishes scripted confederate interactions (g = 0.00) from unscripted naive-participant interactions (g = 0.44\*\*)
- Proposes social-attributional framework: alcohol enhances mood by interfering with anticipation of social rejection

#### Results Summary

| Paradigm | k | Hedges's g | 95% CI |
|---|---|---|---|
| Confederate (scripted) | 16 | 0.00 | [-0.13, 0.13] |
| Naive-participant (unscripted) | 7 | 0.44 | [0.24, 0.63]\*\* |

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates how social environment shapes expression of pharmacological effects on emotion — phenotypic expression depends on environmental context.

### 99. Fairbairn & Sayette (2015)

**Citation:** Fairbairn, C. E., & Sayette, M. A. (2015). Extraversion and the rewarding effects of alcohol in a social context. *Journal of Abnormal Psychology*, *124*(3), 660-673. https://doi.org/10.1037/abn0000024

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R (Extraversion), PANAS, FACS, Subjective Intoxication Scale, Social Bonding Scale
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* extraversion, alcohol, social reward, Duchenne smiling, social bonding, personality
- *Additional terms:* mediated moderation, group interaction paradigm, emotional contagion, actor-partner effects

#### Core Themes
- Extraversion moderates alcohol's rewarding effects in social contexts
- The Extraversion × Alcohol interaction on reward was mediated by group-mates' Duchenne smiling (partner effect, not actor effect)
- Provides behavioral mechanism for why extraversion is associated with alcohol use: greater social-emotional reinforcement

#### Results Summary
- N = 720 (240 groups of 3); BAC alcohol condition M = 0.06
- Extraversion × Alcohol interaction: B = 0.01, p = .02\*
- High extraversion alcohol effect on reward: B = .48, p = .001\*\*
- Low extraversion alcohol effect: B = .26, p < .001\*\* (smaller)
- Group-mates' Duchenne smiling mediated the interaction: B = 0.11, p < .001\*\* for high-E; B = 0.02, p = .520 for low-E

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly demonstrates how a personality trait shapes sensitivity to social-pharmacological reward through a concrete mechanism (emotional contagion via Duchenne smiling).

### 100. Fairbairn & Kang (2025)

### 101. Fairbairn & Sayette (2013)

**Citation:** Fairbairn, C. E., & Sayette, M. A. (2013). The effect of alcohol on emotional inertia: A test of alcohol myopia. *Journal of Abnormal Psychology*, *122*(3), 770-781. https://doi.org/10.1037/a0032980

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** FACS, Subjective Intoxication Scale, self-report mood measures
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* alcohol, emotional inertia, alcohol myopia, facial expression, emotion dynamics
- *Additional terms:* attention-allocation model, autocorrelation of affect, Duchenne smiling, time-series analysis

#### Core Themes
- Tests alcohol myopia theory via emotional inertia (autocorrelation of affect over time)
- Alcohol increased emotional inertia — slowed emotional change during social interaction
- Adds temporal-dynamic dimension to alcohol's effects that static mean-level measures miss

#### Results Summary
- N = 720 (240 groups); second-by-second Duchenne smiling data
- Alcohol main effect on Duchenne smiling: positive, p < .001\*\*
- Alcohol effect on emotional inertia (autocorrelation): increased, p < .05\*
- Placebo vs. control on inertia: no significant difference

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Illustrates how pharmacological agents alter temporal dynamics of emotional experience, with implications for measurement of affective phenotypes.

### 102. Fairbairn et al. (2018)

**Citation:** Fairbairn, C. E., Briley, D. A., Kang, D., Fraley, R. C., Hankin, B. L., & Ariss, T. (2018). A meta-analysis of longitudinal associations between substance use and interpersonal attachment security. *Psychological Bulletin*, *144*(5), 532-555. https://doi.org/10.1037/bul0000141

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis, Systematic Review
**Instruments:** IPPA, AAS, AAI, various substance use measures
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* attachment, substance use, meta-analysis, longitudinal, interpersonal relationships
- *Additional terms:* cross-lagged effects, temporal precedence, Bowlby, developmental psychopathology, PRISMA

#### Core Themes
- First quantitative synthesis of prospective attachment-substance use link across the lifespan
- Insecure attachment predicts later substance use, but substance use does not predict later attachment
- Association stronger in younger samples (age 11: r = -.16; age 20: r = -.08)

#### Results Summary

| Analysis | r | 95% CI | k | N |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Cross-sectional | -.16\*\* | [-.19, -.12] | 23 | — |
| Cross-lagged: attachment → substance | -.05\*\* | [-.06, -.04] | 20 | 41,512 |
| Cross-lagged: substance → attachment | -.01 | [-.04, .01] | 15 | 29,144 |

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Strongest quantitative evidence for developmental direction of attachment-substance use link. Supports developmental cascade models in which early interpersonal experiences shape risk through shared neurobiological systems.

### 103. Ariss et al. (2023)

**Citation:** Ariss, T., Lydon-Staley, D. M., Cleveland, H. H., & Lanza, S. T. (2023). Visual attention to the self and a familiar other during social interaction: Associations with negative affect and alcohol. *Clinical Psychological Science*, *11*(4), 712-729. https://doi.org/10.1177/21677026221140002

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** PANAS, Tobii Pro Glasses 2 (eye-tracking), visual analog scales

- *Additional terms:* gaze behavior, dyadic interaction, video-chat paradigm, familiarity, beta-binomial regression

#### Core Themes
- Visual attention during social interaction decomposed into self-focused and other-focused gaze
- Higher post-interaction negative affect linked to increased self-focused attention but not decreased other-focused attention
- Alcohol did not significantly alter gaze patterns, contrary to attention-allocation models

### 104. Picard & Bobba-Alves (2022)

**Citation:** Picard, M., & Bobba-Alves, C. (2022). The energetic cost of allostasis and allostatic load. *Psychosomatic Medicine*, *84*(6), 671-681.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* allostasis, allostatic load, mitochondria, energetics, stress, aging
- *Additional terms:* bioenergetic cost, metabolic overhead, glucocorticoids, HPA axis, telomere length, oxidative stress, cellular senescence

#### Core Themes
- Reframes allostatic load through a bioenergetic lens: stress responses require mitochondrial energy; chronic activation imposes metabolic cost accelerating biological aging
- Brain consumes ~20% of resting energy; stress-induced neural activation increases metabolic demand
- Distinguishes adaptive allostasis (short-term, energy-efficient) from allostatic overload (chronic, energy-expensive)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Mechanistic account linking chronic psychological stress to cellular-level energetic costs, bridging brain-level phenotypes to molecular biology.

### 105. Corbetta, Patel, & Shulman (2008)

**Citation:** Corbetta, M., Patel, G., & Shulman, G. L. (2008). The reorienting system of the human brain: From environment to theory of mind. *Neuron*, *58*(3), 306-324. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.neuron.2008.04.017

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** fMRI, ERP/EEG, TMS, single-unit recording (reviewed)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* attention, reorienting, temporoparietal junction, ventral attention network, dorsal attention network
- *Additional terms:* TPJ, VFC, FEF, IPS, circuit breaker, noradrenergic system, locus coeruleus, P300, default mode network, theory of mind, social cognition

#### Core Themes
- Updates 2002 dual-network model with six years of additional evidence
- Ventral network (TPJ-VFC) is strongly suppressed during focused attention, reactivated by task-relevant stimuli — "filtering" hypothesis
- MFG serves as connectivity hub linking dorsal and ventral networks
- TPJ/VFC-LC-noradrenergic parallel: shared responses to target detection and behavioral relevance
- Extends TPJ's role to theory of mind, agency detection, empathy — domain-general "reorienting" computation

#### Results Summary
- Review synthesizing neuroimaging, neuropsychological, and electrophysiological evidence
- TPJ deactivation proportional to attentional load during RSVP search
- Table 1: systematic TPJ/VFC–LC–P300 correspondence across five input types
- Neglect lesions map to ventral TPJ-VFC, not dorsal IPS-FEF

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational neural architecture for how the brain selects and reorients attention. Extension to social cognition connects attentional architecture to social phenotypes central to the book.

### 106. Corbetta & Shulman (2002)

**Citation:** Corbetta, M., & Shulman, G. L. (2002). Control of goal-directed and stimulus-driven attention in the brain. *Nature Reviews Neuroscience*, *3*(3), 201-215. https://doi.org/10.1038/nrn755

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** fMRI, PET, single-unit electrophysiology (reviewed)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* attention, top-down, bottom-up, dorsal frontoparietal network, ventral frontoparietal network
- *Additional terms:* IPS, FEF, TPJ, VFC, attentional set, salience map, spatial neglect, circuit breaker, noradrenergic system

#### Core Themes
- Foundational dual-network model (10,000+ citations): dorsal (IPS-FEF, bilateral, top-down) and ventral (TPJ-VFC, right-lateralized, stimulus-driven reorienting)
- Ventral system not purely exogenous — modulated by task relevance and behavioral significance
- Two networks interact: ventral sends interrupt signals to dorsal when behaviorally relevant stimuli detected
- Spatial neglect maps to ventral (TPJ-VFC) damage, not dorsal (IPS-FEF)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational neuroscientific model of attentional control. Distinguishing goal-directed from stimulus-driven attention in separable neural networks provides essential architecture for understanding how organisms balance exploitation with exploration.

### 107. Morrone-Strupinsky & Depue (2004)

**Citation:** Morrone-Strupinsky, J. V., & Depue, R. A. (2004). Differential relation of two distinct, film-induced positive emotional states to affiliative and agentic extraversion. *Personality and Individual Differences*, *36*(5), 1109-1126. https://doi.org/10.1016/S0191-8869(03)00204-6

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** MPQ (Social Closeness, Social Potency, Well-Being, Achievement), custom affective rating scales
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* extraversion, affiliative, agentic, positive emotion, film induction, incentive motivation, warmth
- *Additional terms:* appetitive pleasure, consummatory pleasure, dopamine, positive activation, affective specificity

#### Core Themes
- Extraversion decomposes into affiliative (warmth, closeness) and agentic (dominance, potency) components with distinct emotional signatures
- Affiliative film material induces warmth-affection correlated with Social Closeness; agentic material induces incentive motivation correlated with Social Potency
- These map onto distinct reward processes: appetitive (dopaminergic) vs. consummatory (opiate-mediated)

#### Results Summary
- N = 63 female undergraduates; within-subjects (neutral, affiliative, agentic films)
- Affiliative warmth × Social Closeness: r = .39\*\* (controlling SP: r = .42\*\*)
- Agentic activation × Social Potency: r = .37\*\* (controlling SC: r = .36\*\*)
- Cross-specificity: SC × agentic r = .06; SP × affiliative r = -.05 (both ns)
- Fisher's r-to-z: all specificity differences p < .001\*\*

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Paradigm case for how a single higher-order trait contains functionally distinct components with different emotional signatures and neurobiological substrates. Illustrates how targeted experimental methods reveal hidden structure beneath trait descriptions.

---

### 108. Depue & Morrone-Strupinsky (2005)

**Citation:** Depue, R. A., & Morrone-Strupinsky, J. V. (2005). A neurobehavioral model of affiliative bonding: Implications for conceptualizing a human trait of affiliation. *Behavioral and Brain Sciences*, *28*(3), 313-395. https://doi.org/10.1017/S0140525X05000063

**Purpose:** Review (Target Article with Open Peer Commentary)
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** MPQ Social Closeness, NEO-PI-R, naltrexone challenge, heat tolerance paradigm
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* affiliation, corticolimbic-striatal networks, dopamine, oxytocin, mu-opiates, social bonds
- *Additional terms:* nucleus accumbens shell, VTA, contextual ensemble, incentive salience, consummatory pleasure, vagal brake, OPRM1, experience-expectant development

#### Core Themes
- Comprehensive neurobehavioral model of human trait of affiliation grounded in dopamine (appetitive), mu-opiates (consummatory), oxytocin, vasopressin
- Two phases: dopamine encodes incentive salience of distal affiliative stimuli; mu-opiates mediate warmth and physiological quiescence during proximal contact
- NAS shell integrates contextual information with reward signals, creating incentive-encoded memory networks (30,000:1 compression)
- Individual differences in mu-opiate receptor functioning have disproportionate weight in determining trait affiliation

#### Results Summary
- **Naltrexone study:** N = 30 (15 high SC + 15 low SC females, extreme deciles from N = 2,981)
- Placebo: high SC showed increased warmth after affiliative film (p < .01\*\*); low SC no change (p > .30)
- Naltrexone: groups indistinguishable for both films
- 3-way interaction (Group × Film × Drug): F = 17.4, p < .01\*\* (affiliation); F = 12.7, p < .05\* (heat tolerance)
- 27 peer commentaries from major researchers

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Landmark paper providing the most detailed model of how a personality trait (affiliation) is constructed bottom-up: evolutionary pressures → conserved reward circuits → neuroanatomical networks → neurodevelopmental calibration → measurable individual differences. Rare example of pharmacologically dissecting a personality trait's biological substrate.

---

### 109. Finlay et al. (2026)

**Citation:** Finlay, S., Rudd, D., McDermott, B., & Sarnyai, Z. (2026). Allostatic load and systemic comorbidities in psychiatric disorders. *Stress*, *29*(1), Article 2449191. https://doi.org/10.1080/10253890.2024.2449191

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Systematic Review, Meta-Analysis
**Instruments:** Various allostatic load indices
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* allostatic load, psychiatric disorders, schizophrenia, first-episode psychosis, bipolar disorder, PTSD, MDD
- *Additional terms:* multisystem dysregulation, fornix structural connectivity, choroid plexus

#### Core Themes
- AL consistently elevated in psychiatric disorders, most strongly in psychotic-spectrum conditions
- Elevation present at first-episode psychosis, suggesting multisystem dysregulation begins at or before illness onset
- Biomarker profiles differ by disorder: metabolic/inflammatory in psychosis; inflammatory/neuroendocrine in depression

#### Results Summary
- Psychotic-spectrum (FEP + schizophrenia): k = 15, g = 0.97, 95% CI [0.76, 1.18]\*\*
- FEP subgroup: k = 5, g = 0.95, 95% CI [0.60, 1.30]\*\*
- Depression: k = 4, g = 0.07, 95% CI [-0.23, 0.37] (ns)
- Trim-and-fill adjusted: g = 0.76, 95% CI [0.51, 1.02]

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Systematic biomarker framework for how chronic stress becomes biologically embedded across psychiatric phenotypes. AL elevations preceding chronicity in psychosis relevant to developmental accounts.

### 110. Freeman et al. (2014)

**Citation:** Freeman, S. M., Inoue, K., Smith, A. L., Goodman, M. M., & Young, L. J. (2014). The neuroanatomical distribution of oxytocin receptor binding and mRNA in the male rhesus macaque. *Psychoneuroendocrinology*, *45*, 128-141. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.psyneuen.2014.03.023

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** Competitive binding receptor autoradiography, in situ hybridization, AChE counterstaining
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* oxytocin receptor, vasopressin receptor, OXTR, AVPR1A, rhesus macaque, receptor autoradiography
- *Additional terms:* nucleus basalis of Meynert, superior colliculus, cross-reactivity, ligand selectivity, cholinergic innervation

#### Core Themes
- First OXTR/AVPR1A mapping in rhesus macaque, resolving ligand cross-reactivity
- OXTR concentrated in five regions: NBM, PPT, SuG, TB, VMH — visual-attentional, not olfactory
- NBM and SC conserved across all four primate species studied
- Demonstrates primate shift from olfactory to visual social communication circuits

#### Results Summary
- 125I-LVA Kd: hAVPR1A = 30 ± 3.1 pM; hOXTR = 590 ± 450 pM
- 125I-OVTA Kd: hOXTR = 92 ± 18 pM; hAVPR1A = 360 ± 51 pM
- Both radioligands show subnanomolar affinity for both receptors in primates
- AVPR1A distribution much broader than OXTR

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Illustrates how evolution repurposed the same molecular system across mammalian clades for different sensory modalities (olfactory→visual), directly relevant to understanding how neuropeptide systems contribute to social phenotype construction.

### 111. Freeman & Young (2016)

**Citation:** Freeman, S. M., & Young, L. J. (2016). Comparative perspectives on oxytocin and vasopressin receptor research in rodents and primates. *Journal of Neuroendocrinology*, *28*(4). https://doi.org/10.1111/jne.12382

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** Receptor autoradiography, selective antagonists, in situ hybridization (reviewed)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* neuropeptides, receptor binding, rhesus macaque, titi monkey, marmoset
- *Additional terms:* OXTR, AVPR1a, pair bonding, social cognition, visual attention, nucleus accumbens, nucleus basalis, autism spectrum disorder

#### Core Themes
- Conserved OXTR core in visual processing/attention regions (NBM, SC) across primates, vs. olfactory in rodents
- Species-specific variation: monogamous marmosets have dense OXTR in nucleus accumbens (reward); titi monkeys in hippocampus (social memory)
- OT may modulate social behavior by modulating cholinergic input to cortex and amygdala via NBM
- Mixed OT/AVP ligand selectivity in primates is critical methodological concern

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Key integrative review connecting evolutionary neuroanatomy of OT/AVP system to social phenotypes across mammalian clades. Species-specific receptor distributions illustrate how evolution shapes neural substrates of species-typical social organizations.

### 112. Freeman et al. (2018)

**Citation:** Freeman, S. M., Palumbo, M. C., Lawrence, R. H., Smith, A. L., Goodman, M. M., & Bales, K. L. (2018). Effect of age and autism spectrum disorder on oxytocin receptor density in the human basal forebrain and midbrain. *Translational Psychiatry*, *8*, 257. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41398-018-0315-3

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional (postmortem)
**Instruments:** Competitive-binding receptor autoradiography, ADI-R
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* oxytocin, oxytocin receptor, autism, nucleus basalis, superior colliculus, ventral pallidum
- *Additional terms:* AVPR1A, mesolimbic reward, developmental critical period, cholinergic system

#### Core Themes
- First quantification of OXTR binding in ASD human brain tissue
- ASD shows higher OXTR in NBM (attention modulation) and lower OXTR in VP (mesolimbic reward)
- Developmental critical period (ages 2-5): VP OXTR peaks in TD but is absent/dampened in ASD
- Opposing OXTR directions suggest dysregulation rather than simple over/under-expression

#### Results Summary
- N = 44 (22 ASD, 22 TD); ages <1 to 25
- NBM OXTR: ASD > TD, F(1,33) = 10.71, p < .01\*\*
- VP OXTR: ASD < TD, F(2,26) = 7.92, p < .01\*\*
- VP age correlation (all): r = -0.4985, p < .01\*\*; driven by TD (r = -0.6299\*\*), not ASD (r = -0.09)
- No sex differences, no AVPR1a binding above background

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Most direct human evidence linking OXTR neuroanatomy to ASD. The VP developmental critical period directly illustrates how phenotypes are "made" during development — a time-limited window of OT sensitivity disrupted in ASD.

### 113. Goodwin & Sayette (2024)

**Citation:** Goodwin, M. E., & Sayette, M. A. (2024). The impact of alcohol on affiliative verbal behavior: A systematic review and meta-analysis. *Alcohol: Clinical and Experimental Research*, *48*(6), 1000-1021. https://doi.org/10.1111/acer.15312

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Systematic Review, Meta-Analysis
**Instruments:** Various behavioral coding systems, QATQS
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* alcohol, language processing, self-disclosure, social bonding, verbal content
- *Additional terms:* inhibition conflict model, alcohol myopia, LIWC, NLP, language style matching

#### Core Themes
- Language is a neglected behavioral modality in alcohol research
- Alcohol may increase affiliative verbal behavior by reducing inhibitions against social approach
- Field suffers from small samples, outdated studies, heterogeneous measures

#### Results Summary
- 16 studies, N = 961
- Overall: g = 0.164, 95% CI [0.027, 0.301], p = .019\*; I² = 17.5%
- Leave-one-out: all 16 iterations significant (g range = 0.128-0.199)
- Trim-and-fill: g = 0.082, 95% CI [-0.079, 0.243] — possible inflation

**Relevance to Book Project:** TANGENTIAL
Addresses narrow question about alcohol's pharmacological effects on verbal behavior. Most relevant thread is evolutionary role of language in bonding.

### 114. Juster, McEwen, & Lupien (2010)

**Citation:** Juster, R.-P., McEwen, B. S., & Lupien, S. J. (2010). Allostatic load biomarkers of chronic stress and impact on health and cognition. *Neuroscience and Biobehavioral Reviews*, *35*(1), 2-16. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.neubiorev.2009.10.002

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Systematic Review
**Instruments:** Allostatic load indices (various formulations: group AL, norm-based AL, z-score AL, dynamic AL, canonical correlation, recursive partitioning, k-means cluster analysis)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* allostatic load, allostasis, chronic stress, aging, resilience, health, cognition
- *Additional terms:* HPA axis, SAM axis, MacArthur Studies, NHANES, ecological systems model, SES gradients, sex differences, weathering hypothesis

#### Core Themes
- Allostasis emphasizes dynamic set-points, brain's role in feedback regulation, whole-body adaptation; AL is cumulative cost of chronic over-activation
- Temporal cascade: primary mediators → primary effects → secondary outcomes → tertiary outcomes (disease, mortality)
- Original MacArthur 10-biomarker AL index predicts cognitive decline, CVD, and all-cause mortality beyond any individual biomarker
- AL operates across entire lifespan: childhood cumulative risk by age 9 through working-life stress to aging-related decline

#### Results Summary
- MacArthur (N = 904-1,189, ages 70-79): higher AL → greater cognitive/physical decline, incident CVD, all-cause mortality\*\*
- NHANES race/SES: AL risk 200% for African Americans, 70% for urban Mexican Americans, 30% for Caucasians in lower SES
- Poverty + high AL: 6 years shorter life expectancy vs. poverty + low biological risk
- Sex-specific mortality: male pathways via epinephrine/NE/IL-6/CRP; female pathways via SBP
- Children: AL detectable by age 9, linked to crowding, noise, family turmoil, violence

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational review of the allostatic load model — the primary framework for how chronic stress becomes biologically embedded. Ecological systems organization of AL antecedents provides ready-made framework for the book's multilevel analysis of phenotype formation.

---

### 115. Lupien et al. (2018)

**Citation:** Lupien, S. J., Juster, R.-P., Raymond, C., & Marin, M.-F. (2018). The effects of chronic stress on the human brain: From neurotoxicity, to vulnerability, to opportunity. *Frontiers in Neuroendocrinology*, *49*, 91-105. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.yfrne.2018.02.001

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** Allostatic Load index, cortisol measures, MRI volumetrics
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* stress, cortisol, HPA axis, hippocampus, allostatic load, early life adversity, neurotoxicity, vulnerability
- *Additional terms:* life-cycle model of stress, programming effects, sensitive periods, sex vs. gender, androgyny, DeStress for Success

#### Core Themes
- Chronic stress effects depend on which brain structures are developing (youth) or declining (aging) at exposure: hippocampus birth-2, amygdala ~10, PFC 8-25
- "Neurotoxicity hypothesis" partially supplanted by vulnerability evidence (Gilbertson twin study: pre-existing small hippocampal volumes)
- Early life adversity programs HPA reactivity via epigenetics (hyper- or hypo-cortisolism)
- Interventions (behavioral therapy, exercise, social integration) produce compensatory brain changes

#### Results Summary
- 25% of young healthy adults (18-30) have hippocampal volumes as small as average 60-75-year-olds (N = 177)
- DeStress for Success (N = 504 adolescents): responders 2.45× less likely to develop depressive states
- AL steadily increases 20s-60s, then plateaus through 90s

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly addresses how mental phenotypes are shaped across development by stress-brain interaction. Life-cycle model provides compelling developmental framework.

---

### 116. Moore & Depue (2016)

**Citation:** Moore, S. R., & Depue, R. A. (2016). Neurobehavioral foundation of environmental reactivity. *Psychological Bulletin*, *142*(2), 107-164. https://doi.org/10.1037/bul0000028

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A (reviews genetic polymorphism literature)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* neurobiological reactivity, environmental sensitivity, gene-by-environment interaction, neurodevelopment
- *Additional terms:* threshold model, dopamine, opiates, oxytocin, CRH, serotonin, norepinephrine, GABA, differential susceptibility, DRD4, 5-HTTLPR, OPRM1, OXTR

#### Core Themes
- Environmental reactivity as supertrait underpinned by two sets of neuromodulators operating on threshold model
- Combinatorial expression: [(DA + OP + OXT) : CRH × 1/(5-HT + NE + GABA)]
- Maps onto personality: Extraversion ↔ DA; Agreeableness ↔ OP/OT; Neuroticism ↔ CRH; Conscientiousness ↔ 5-HT+NE+GABA
- Two-dimensional space (emotional-motivational × neural constraint) defines four quadrants of reactivity

#### Results Summary
- DRD4 7-repeat: lower DA affinity, increased sensitivity to both aversive and rewarding cues
- 5-HTTLPR s-allele: ~10% of phenotypic variance in amygdala reactivity (meta-analysis)
- Multiple DA polymorphisms: 9-12% variance each in reward-induced ventral striatal reactivity
- Environmental enrichment increases hippocampal neurogenesis and GABA activity

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Most comprehensive integrative framework connecting neurotransmitter systems, genetic polymorphisms, temperament/personality, and environmental sensitivity. The combinatorial expression is a concrete model for evolution-development-measurement intersection.

### 117. Picard (2014)

**Citation:** Picard, M. (2014). Pathways to aging: The mitochondrion at the intersection of biological and psychosocial sciences. *Journal of Aging Research*, *2014*, Article 425809. https://doi.org/10.1155/2014/425809

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* mitochondria, aging, psychosocial stress, oxidative stress, bioenergetics
- *Additional terms:* allostatic load, telomere attrition, ROS, glucocorticoids, cellular senescence, biopsychosocial model

#### Core Themes
- Mitochondria at intersection of biological and psychosocial aging determinants
- Psychosocial stress translates to biological aging via neuroendocrine pathways → mitochondrial dysfunction → accelerated cellular damage
- Mitochondrial allostatic load: cumulative cellular cost of repeated stress-related metabolic disruptions
- mtDNA maternally inherited, creating intergenerational stress transmission pathway

#### Results Summary
- Review (no original data)
- Caregivers show telomere shortening = 9-17 years additional aging (Epel et al., 2004)
- Depression associated with elevated oxidative stress, mitochondrial DNA deletions
- Animal studies: maternal stress alters offspring mitochondrial function

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides cellular/molecular level of how chronic stress shapes biological phenotypes. Adds mechanistic layer beneath HPA-axis and allostatic load models.

### 118. Sayette (1999)

**Citation:** Sayette, M. A. (1999). Does drinking reduce stress? *Alcohol Research & Health*, *23*(4), 250-255.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* alcohol, stress, stress-response dampening, tension reduction
- *Additional terms:* appraisal-disruption model, attention-allocation model, cognitive appraisal, expectancy effects, alcoholism risk

#### Core Themes
- Tension-reduction hypothesis has surprisingly equivocal experimental support
- Appraisal-disruption model: alcohol's anxiolytic effects depend on timing — before stressor disrupts appraisal; after appraisal has minimal effect
- Attention-allocation model: alcohol narrows focus, reducing stress only with distracting activity
- Individual differences in SRD sensitivity may be risk factor for alcohol use disorders

**Relevance to Book Project:** LOW
Context for how pharmacological agents interact with cognitive appraisal processes. Appraisal-disruption model illustrates cognitive mediation between biological inputs and psychological outputs.

### 119. Sayette et al. (2012)

**Citation:** Sayette, M. A., Creswell, K. G., Dimoff, J. D., Fairbairn, C. E., Cohn, J. F., Heckman, B. W., Kirchner, T. R., Levine, J. M., & Moreland, R. L. (2012). Alcohol and group formation: A multimodal investigation of the effects of alcohol on emotion and social bonding. *Psychological Science*, *23*(8), 869-878. https://doi.org/10.1177/0956797611435134

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** FACS, Perceived Group Reinforcement Scale, Subjective Intoxication Scale, Observer Video-Pro
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* social interaction, emotions, facial expressions, interpersonal interaction
- *Additional terms:* Duchenne smile, action units, triadic smiling, golden moments, hierarchical linear modeling

#### Core Themes
- Alcohol's reinforcing effects may operate primarily through social bonding enhancement rather than direct hedonic effects
- Multimodal measurement (FACS + speech + self-report) provides converging evidence at individual and group levels
- Group-level "golden moments" (simultaneous triadic Duchenne smiling) as novel operationalization of social bonding
- Pharmacological effects drove results — placebo and control groups similar

#### Results Summary
- N = 720 (240 groups); ~34.9 million video frames; BAC M = 0.055-0.062
- Duchenne smiles: ERR = 2.28, t(237) = 8.35\*\*\*
- Negative affect composite: ERR = 0.62, t(237) = -4.95\*\*\*
- Speech duration: ERR = 1.25, t(237) = 5.96\*\*\*
- PGRS bonding: beta = 0.42, t(237) = 2.75\*\*
- Triadic Duchenne: ERR = 1.81, t(236) = 4.87\*\*\*
- Each additional female: +22% triadic speech, +0.21 PGRS

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Sophisticated multimodal measurement applied to social bonding. Group-level emergent measures illustrate how psychological science creates novel operational definitions of complex social constructs.

### 120. Suvarna et al. (2020)

**Citation:** Suvarna, B., Suvarna, A., Phillips, R., Juster, R.-P., McDermott, B., & Sarnyai, Z. (2020). Health risk behaviours and allostatic load: A systematic review. *Neuroscience and Biobehavioral Reviews*, *108*, 694-711. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.neubiorev.2019.12.020

**Purpose:** Review (Systematic Review)
**Methodology:** Systematic Review
**Instruments:** Various allostatic load indices, STROBE Checklist
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* allostatic load, stress, health risk behaviours, smoking, alcohol, physical inactivity, obesity, sleep
- *Additional terms:* HPA axis, SAM axis, biomarkers, metabolic syndrome, gut microbiota, weight discrimination, PRISMA

#### Core Themes
- Health risk behaviors co-occur with chronic stress as maladaptive coping, further elevating AL in bidirectional feedback loop
- No standard AL biomarker set across studies (2-25 per study; mean = 9.12)
- Obesity consistently associated with higher AL; physical activity inversely associated; poor sleep consistently associated

#### Results Summary
- 26 studies (1988-2018); sample sizes 63-70,713
- Eating/obesity: 6/6 studies found association with increased AL
- Physical activity: 5/5 found higher activity → decreased AL
- Substance abuse: 3/6 found significant association
- Sleep: 3/4 found impaired sleep → higher AL
- Quality: mean STROBE score = 77.8%

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Evidence base for how behavioral phenotypes interact with physiological stress systems bidirectionally. Biomarker heterogeneity highlights standardization challenges in psychological science.

### 121. Waters et al. (2003)

**Citation:** Waters, A. J., Shiffman, S., Sayette, M. A., Paty, J. A., Gwaltney, C. J., & Balabanis, M. H. (2003). Attentional bias predicts outcome in smoking cessation. *Health Psychology*, *22*(4), 378-387. https://doi.org/10.1037/0278-6133.22.4.378

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** Emotional Stroop task, FTND, NDSS, electronic diary
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* attentional bias, emotional Stroop, relapse, smoking cessation
- *Additional terms:* incentive salience, cue reactivity, Robinson & Berridge (1993), ecological momentary assessment, Cox proportional-hazards

#### Core Themes
- Attentional bias to smoking cues on quit day prospectively predicts early lapse
- Captures a component of dependence not indexed by self-report or biological measures
- Nicotine replacement reduced error bias but not RT bias
- The "acute Stroop" (initial response) was most robust predictor

#### Results Summary
- N = 158; smoking words 64.3 ms slower than neutral, t(157) = 9.49\*\*
- Acute Stroop → 1-week abstinence: OR = 1.58, CI [1.12, 2.23]\*\*
- Acute Stroop → time to lapse: HR = 1.30, CI [1.09, 1.54]\*\* (30% increased daily risk per 100ms)
- Stroop uncorrelated with FTND, NDSS, smoking rate, cotinine (all ps > .23)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Implicit cognitive measures capture dimensions of motivation invisible to self-report, illustrating how psychological science reveals hidden structure in mental phenotypes.

### 122. Witczak et al. (2024)

**Citation:** Witczak, L. R., Samra, J., Dufek, M., Goetze, L. R., Freeman, S. M., Lau, A. R., ... & Bales, K. L. (2024). Expression of bond-related behaviors affects titi monkey responsiveness to oxytocin and vasopressin treatments. *Annals of the New York Academy of Sciences*, *1534*, 118-129. https://doi.org/10.1111/nyas.15119

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** Parent preference test, intranasal OT/AVP/OTA treatments
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* early-life experience, intranasal oxytocin, vasopressin, nonhuman primate, social bond
- *Additional terms:* Plecturocebus cupreus, pair bonding, father-daughter bond, neuropeptide responsiveness, Cohen's f-squared

#### Core Themes
- Variation in father-daughter bonding predicts differential OT/AVP responsiveness in juvenile titi monkeys
- OT decreased time near parents (dose-dependent); AVP/OTA increased stranger socialization for high-bond females
- Parents' own relationship quality modulated offspring neuropeptide sensitivity (f² = 1.197)

#### Results Summary
- N = 10 juvenile females × 7 treatments = 70 tests
- Parent zone R² = 0.386; Treatment × Juvenile Parent Preference: f² = 0.067
- Stranger zone R² = 0.632; Treatment × Juvenile Proximity: f² = 1.094; Treatment × Parent Preference: f² = 1.585
- Low AVP: beta = -13.80, t = -3.58\*\*; High OT: beta = -11.06, t = -2.87\*\*

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly demonstrates how developmental experience shapes neuroendocrine responsiveness — the developmental construction of social phenotypes through evolution-development-experience interaction.

### 123. Dayley et al. (2026)

**Citation:** Dayley, E. E., Durham, S., Palumbo, M. C., Lundell, J. F., & Freeman, S. M. (2026). Oxytocin receptor gene expression in the basal forebrain in autism. *Journal of Neurodevelopmental Disorders*, *18*, 16. https://doi.org/10.1186/s11689-026-09678-0

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional (postmortem)
**Instruments:** RNAScope fISH, Illumina GSA v3.0, autoradiography
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* autism, oxytocin, cholinergic neurons, basal forebrain, nucleus basalis, ventral pallidum
- *Additional terms:* OXTR mRNA, ChAT, fluorescence in situ hybridization, SNP, rs2268491

#### Core Themes
- OXTR mRNA significantly elevated in ASD in NBM cholinergic neurons and VP
- First demonstration of OXTR expression in cholinergic neurons of human basal forebrain (73% colocalization)
- VP paradox: OXTR mRNA higher in ASD while binding previously found lower — suggests post-translational dysregulation
- OXTR mRNA positively correlated with age, driven by ASD specimens

#### Results Summary
- 17 ASD, 22 allistic specimens
- NBM ChAT+OXTR: ASD M = 31.3 vs. AST M = 18.7, p = .029\*
- VP OXTR: ASD M = 6831 vs. AST M = 2292, p = .004\*\*
- NBM ChAT+OXTR vs. binding: r = 0.3913, p = .04\*
- No significant SNP associations after FDR correction

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Molecular-level evidence for differential OXT system regulation in autism. Discrepancy between mRNA and protein levels underscores complexity of genotype-to-phenotype mapping.

### 124. Krall et al. (2015)

**Citation:** Krall, S. C., Rottschy, C., Oberwelland, E., Bzdok, D., Fox, P. T., Eickhoff, S. B., Fink, G. R., & Konrad, K. (2015). The role of the right temporoparietal junction in attention and social interaction as revealed by ALE meta-analysis. *Brain Structure and Function*, *220*(2), 587-604. https://doi.org/10.1007/s00429-014-0803-z

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis, MACM, RSFC
**Instruments:** Posner cueing tasks, false belief tasks, fMRI/PET
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* attention, theory of mind, ALE, MACM, RSFC
- *Additional terms:* right TPJ, fractionation hypothesis, anterior vs. posterior rTPJ, ventral attention network, social cognition network, ADHD, ASD

#### Core Themes
- rTPJ contains two functionally fractionated subregions: anterior (attention + social cognition overlap) and posterior (social cognition specific)
- Anterior rTPJ is the only region consistently activated by both reorienting and false belief tasks
- Connectivity confirms distinct networks: anterior → ventral attention; posterior → social cognition
- Clinical relevance to ADHD and ASD: different subregions may underlie different symptom clusters

#### Results Summary
- 54 contrasts, 47 studies, 798 subjects, 449 foci
- Conjunction: anterior rTPJ (x=54, y=-44, z=18), Z = 4.61, 111 voxels — only convergent region
- Peak-to-peak distance anterior-posterior: 16.64 mm
- Attention: rTPJ cluster 627 voxels (peak 62, -44, 12)
- False belief: rTPJ cluster 519 voxels (peak 54, -52, 26)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Functional fractionation reveals separable computational modules for attention and social cognition within a single brain region — two capacities critical for navigating the social environment and central to phenotype formation.

### 125. Saunders, Granger, & Sabatini (2015)

**Citation:** Saunders, A., Granger, A. J., & Sabatini, B. L. (2015). Corelease of acetylcholine and GABA from cholinergic forebrain neurons. *eLife*, *4*, e06412. https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.06412

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** Optogenetic stimulation (ChR2), whole-cell voltage-clamp recordings, immunohistochemistry, Cre-lox conditional knockout
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Additional terms:* acetylcholine, GABA, corelease, cholinergic forebrain, nucleus basalis, VGAT/Slc32a1, GAD65/Gad2, optogenetics, layer 1 interneurons, cortical modulation

#### Core Themes
- Forebrain cholinergic neurons corelease both ACh and GABA — challenges one-neuron/one-transmitter assumption
- Optogenetic activation evokes both nicotinic EPSCs and fast monosynaptic GABA-A IPSCs in cortical L1 interneurons
- Nearly all forebrain ChAT+ cells express VGAT and Gad2; brainstem PPN cholinergic neurons do not
- Conditional Slc32a1 knockout eliminates IPSCs while preserving nEPSCs — definitive genetic evidence

#### Results Summary
- nEPSCs in 41/58 cells (70.7%); direct IPSCs in 9/58 cells (15.5%)
- Direct IPSC latency: 2.5 ± 0.2 ms (faster than nEPSC: 4.0 ± 0.2 ms)\*
- Slc32a1 coexpression: cortex 100%, GP 97.9%, NB 95.8%, MS/DBB 93.2%, PPN 1.9%
- Conditional KO: 0/34 cells showed direct IPSCs vs. 5/16 WT

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Reveals that the cholinergic system operates through dual-transmitter mechanism more complex than previously understood. Region-specific specialization (forebrain vs. brainstem) illustrates evolved neural architecture constraining phenotypic variation.

---

### 126. Ashton & Lee (2007)

**Citation:** Ashton, M. C., & Lee, K. (2007). Empirical, theoretical, and practical advantages of the HEXACO model of personality structure. *Personality and Social Psychology Review, 11*(2), 150-166. https://doi.org/10.1177/1088868306294907

**Purpose:** Theoretical Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review, Cross-Language Lexical Analysis
**Instruments:** HEXACO-PI, Lexical Studies (12 languages)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* HEXACO, personality structure, lexical hypothesis, Honesty-Humility, Emotionality, six-factor model
- *Additional terms:* Big Five/FFM comparison, kin altruism, reciprocal altruism, cross-language replication, criterion validity

#### Core Themes
- Argues six-factor HEXACO model is empirically superior to B5/FFM based on cross-language lexical studies recovering six (not five) factors, with the key addition of Honesty-Humility
- Reinterprets three HEXACO factors through an evolutionary framework distinguishing kin-altruism from reciprocal-altruism tendencies
- Demonstrates practical criterion-validity advantages: HEXACO better predicts workplace delinquency, sexual harassment, and Dark Triad traits

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Primary theoretical and empirical case for HEXACO, grounding personality structure in evolutionary altruism theory.

---

### 127. Ashton, Lee, Goldberg, & de Vries (2009)

**Citation:** Ashton, M. C., Lee, K., Goldberg, L. R., & de Vries, R. E. (2009). Higher order factors of personality: Do they exist? *Personality and Social Psychology Review, 13*(2), 79-91. https://doi.org/10.1177/1088868309338467

**Purpose:** Original Research / Theoretical Critique
**Methodology:** Factor Analysis, Simulation
**Instruments:** HEXACO-PI-R, IPIP Big Five, NEO-PI-R
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Higher-Order Factor

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* higher-order factors, metatraits, Alpha, Beta, Stability, Plasticity, general factor of personality
- *Additional terms:* blended variables, acquiescence bias, evaluative content, artifact

#### Core Themes
- Argues higher-order factors (Alpha/Stability, Beta/Plasticity, GFP) are statistical artifacts of item evaluative content and acquiescence bias
- Demonstrates via simulation that blended scales produce spurious higher-order structure even from uncorrelated primary factors

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Challenges the claim that metatraits represent real biological systems -- a critical methodological caution for mapping personality structure onto biology.

### 128. Ashton, Lee, & de Vries (2014)

**Citation:** Ashton, M. C., Lee, K., & de Vries, R. E. (2014). The HEXACO Honesty-Humility, Agreeableness, and Emotionality factors: A review of research and theory. *Personality and Social Psychology Review, 18*(2), 139-152. https://doi.org/10.1177/1088868314523838

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** HEXACO-PI-R
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* HEXACO, Honesty-Humility, Agreeableness, Emotionality, prosocial behavior
- *Additional terms:* Big Five rotation, kin altruism, reciprocal altruism, Dark Triad, sex differences

#### Core Themes
- Reviews research on three HEXACO factors (H, A, E) that differ most from Big Five; Honesty-Humility is the strongest personality predictor of unethical behavior and dark personality
- B5/FFM Agreeableness and Neuroticism are 45-degree rotations of HEXACO H, A, and E, collapsing distinct constructs
- Robust sex differences in Emotionality support the evolutionary kin-altruism interpretation

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Deepens the evolutionary-theoretical interpretation of personality space, showing how HEXACO captures sex-differentiated selection pressures.

### 129. Bleidorn et al. (2009)

**Citation:** Bleidorn, W., Kandler, C., Riemann, R., Angleitner, A., & Spinath, F. M. (2009). Patterns and sources of adult personality development: Growth curve analyses of the NEO PI-R scales in a longitudinal twin study. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 97*(1), 142-155. https://doi.org/10.1037/a0015434

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal Twin Study (3-wave, ~10-year), Latent Growth Modeling, Biometric ACE Models
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R (German)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality development, Big Five, facets, behavior genetics, longitudinal, twin study
- *Additional terms:* maturity principle, latent growth curve, heritability, nonshared environment, gene-environment interplay

#### Core Themes
- Facet-level personality maturation in adulthood with heterogeneous trajectories within domains
- Both genetic and nonshared environmental contributions to stability and change, with magnitude varying across domains and facets
- Facet-level analysis reveals distinct developmental paths masked at domain level

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates how both genes and environments drive facet-level personality development across adulthood.

### 130. Brandes et al. (2020)

**Citation:** Brandes, C. M., Kushner, S. C., Herzhoff, K., & Tackett, J. L. (2020). Facet-level personality development in the transition to adolescence: Maturity, disruption, and gender differences. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology*.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal (4-wave cohort-sequential), Latent Growth Curve Models
**Instruments:** ICID-S (Inventory of Children's Individual Differences -- Short Form)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* facet-level development, childhood, adolescence, maturity, disruption, gender differences
- *Additional terms:* ICID, rank-order stability, parent-report, cohort-sequential design

#### Core Themes
- Facet trajectories ages 9-13 show both maturity and disruption patterns; not all facets within a domain change in the same direction
- Gender differences are prominent: girls show greater Conscientiousness increases and Extraversion decreases
- Rank-order stability is moderate to high (domain r = .63-.72)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Facet-level developmental evidence during the critical transition to adolescence, with sex-differentiated trajectories.

### 131. Brandt et al. (2023)

**Citation:** Brandt, N. D., Drewelies, J., Willis, S. L., Schaie, K. W., Ram, N., Gerstorf, D., & Wagner, J. (2023). Beyond Big Five trait domains: Stability and change in personality facets across midlife and old age. *Journal of Personality, 91*(5), 1171-1188. https://doi.org/10.1111/jopy.12791

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal (Seattle Longitudinal Study, 7-year intervals), Local Structural Equation Modeling
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality facets, stability, change, midlife, old age, rank-order
- *Additional terms:* cumulative continuity principle, local structural equation modeling, lifespan development

#### Core Themes
- Rank-order stabilities generally high (r > .70) but vary across facets and lifespan, peaking ~age 50 and declining in old age
- Mean-levels remarkably stable from midlife to old age, except Activity (Extraversion) which declines
- Facets within the same domain show divergent age-related stability patterns

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Extends the developmental picture across the full adult lifespan, showing facet-specific trajectories shaped by age-graded processes.
