### 132. Briley & Tucker-Drob (2012)

**Citation:** Briley, D. A., & Tucker-Drob, E. M. (2012). Broad bandwidth or high fidelity? Evidence from the structure of genetic and environmental effects on the facets of the Five Factor Model. *Behavior Genetics, 42*(5), 743-763. https://doi.org/10.1007/s10519-012-9548-8

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Twin Study, Multivariate Behavior Genetic Modeling
**Instruments:** CPI-Big Five
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* behavior genetics, Five Factor Model, facets, common pathways, independent pathways
- *Additional terms:* broad bandwidth, high fidelity, specific genetic effects, NMSQT twins

#### Core Themes
- Significant facet-specific genetic influences not accounted for by common domain factors; independent pathways model preferred for 10 of 16 facets
- Aggregating facets into domains overshadows genetically meaningful variation
- Results differ by domain: common pathways fit for E and O but poorly for A, C, and N

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that the genetic architecture is more complex than the Big Five hierarchy implies -- facets carry unique genetic variance that domain scores mask.

### 132b. Briley & Tucker-Drob (2014)

**Citation:** Briley, D. A., & Tucker-Drob, E. M. (2014). Genetic and environmental continuity in personality development: A meta-analysis. *Psychological Bulletin, 140*(5), 1303–1331. https://doi.org/10.1037/a0037091

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis, Twin/Genetic, Longitudinal
**Instruments:** Multiple (aggregated across 24 longitudinal twin studies)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality stability, genetic continuity, environmental continuity, meta-analysis, twin studies, development
- *Additional terms:* gene-environment correlation, crystallization, niche-picking, age-continuous modeling, sibling pairs

#### Core Themes
- Aggregates 24 longitudinal behavioral genetic studies (21,057 sibling pairs of 6 types varying in genetic relatedness)
- Genetic contributions to personality stability are moderate from early in life and increase through middle adulthood
- Environmental contributions to stability also increase across the lifespan but more gradually
- Gene-environment correlation proposed as mechanism: genetically influenced traits shape environmental exposure, which in turn stabilizes traits
- Domain-level only — no facet-level decomposition available

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Gold-standard meta-analytic evidence that genetic factors are a primary source of personality continuity, with gene-environment correlation as the key developmental mechanism. Domain-level constraint limits facet-level inference.

---

### 133. Cemalcilar et al. (2021)

**Citation:** Cemalcilar, Z., Baruh, L., Kezer, M., Soto, C. J., Sumer, N., & John, O. P. (2021). Testing the BFI-2 in a non-WEIRD community sample. *Personality and Individual Differences, 182*, 111087. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.paid.2021.111087

**Purpose:** Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Instrument Validation, Factor Analysis

- *Paper keywords:* BFI-2, non-WEIRD, Turkey, personality structure, cross-cultural
- *Additional terms:* community sample, measurement invariance, convergent validity

#### Core Themes
- BFI-2 five-factor / 15-facet structure replicates in a Turkish community sample, extending validity beyond WEIRD populations
- Short forms (BFI-2-S, BFI-2-XS) maintain adequate psychometric properties
- Convergent validity with HEXACO confirms expected cross-model correspondences

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Extends the BFI-2 hierarchical structure to a non-WEIRD population, supporting universality claims for personality phenotype structure.

### 134. Danner et al. (2019)

### 135. DeYoung, Quilty, & Peterson (2007)

**Citation:** DeYoung, C. G., Quilty, L. C., & Peterson, J. B. (2007). Between facets and domains: 10 aspects of the Big Five. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 93*(5), 880-896. https://doi.org/10.1037/0022-3514.93.5.880

**Purpose:** Original Research (Scale Development)
**Methodology:** Factor Analysis (of IPIP items and NEO-PI-R facets), Behavior Genetic Validation
**Instruments:** IPIP, NEO-PI-R, BFAS (developed here)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Aspect

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, aspects, BFAS, personality hierarchy
- *Additional terms:* Volatility, Withdrawal, Compassion, Politeness, Industriousness, Orderliness, Enthusiasm, Assertiveness, Intellect, Openness

#### Core Themes
- Identifies an intermediate level of personality organization: each Big Five domain divides into exactly two correlated aspects
- Develops the BFAS (100 items, 10 scales); aspect factors correspond to the two genetic factors within each domain identified by Jang et al. (2002)
- Cross-domain aspect correlations reveal meaningful connections obscured at the domain level

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational paper for the 10-aspect model with demonstrated genetic underpinnings -- key intermediate level of trait organization.

### 136. Weisberg, DeYoung, & Hirsh (2011)

**Citation:** Weisberg, Y. J., DeYoung, C. G., & Hirsh, J. B. (2011). Gender differences in personality across the ten aspects of the Big Five. *Frontiers in Psychology, 2*, 178. https://doi.org/10.3389/fpsyg.2011.00178

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Group Comparisons
**Instruments:** BFAS
**Level of Detail:** Personality Aspect

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality, Big Five, gender differences, aspects
- *Additional terms:* Enthusiasm, Assertiveness, Compassion, Politeness, residualized scores, effect sizes

#### Core Themes
- Gender differences are more pervasive at the aspect than domain level: significant in all 10 aspects vs. only 3 domains
- For E, C, and O/I, the two aspects show opposite-direction gender differences (e.g., women higher Enthusiasm, men higher Assertiveness), explaining small domain-level effects
- Effect sizes small to moderate (d = 0.06-0.48)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that sex-differentiated selection pressures are best detected at the aspect level, where opposite-direction within-domain effects become visible.

### 137. Eid et al. (2003)

**Citation:** Eid, M., Riemann, R., Angleitner, A., & Borkenau, P. (2003). Sociability and positive emotionality: Genetic and environmental contributions to the covariation between different facets of extraversion. *Journal of Personality, 71*(3), 319-346.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Twin Study (MZ and DZ), Multimethod, Independent Pathway Modeling
**Instruments:** NEO-FFI (German), Aggregated Positive Affect Scale
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* sociability, positive emotionality, extraversion, behavior genetics, twin study, multimethod
- *Additional terms:* independent pathway model, genetic correlation, dopamine, behavioral activation system

#### Core Themes
- Three Extraversion subfactors (Sociability, Positive Affect, Energy) each with moderate heritability (.28-.45)
- Strong genetic correlation between sociability and positive emotionality (r-genetic = .57) from cross-method design, supporting a common biological substrate
- Results support dopamine-related system underlying Extraversion facet covariation

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Multimethod behavior-genetic evidence that Extraversion facets share a common genetic substrate, illustrating how evolved biological systems produce correlated mental phenotypes.

### 138. Haehner et al. (2025c)

**Citation:** Haehner, P., Krämer, M. D., Schaefer, B., Hopwood, C. J., & Bleidorn, W. (2025). Stability and change in lower-order personality traits in a representative Swiss sample. *European Journal of Personality*. https://doi.org/10.1177/08902070251405544

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal (5 waves over 2 years), Local Structural Equation Models
**Instruments:** BFAS
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Aspect

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality development, aspects, stability, change, local structural equation models
- *Additional terms:* Big Five, rank-order stability, mean-level change, PERCIVAL study

#### Core Themes
- High rank-order stabilities for domains (r = .88) and aspects (r = .87) over 6-month intervals; aspects within the same domain can differ in mean-level change direction
- Cumulative personality changes are comparable in magnitude to changes in life satisfaction and self-esteem

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates personality development operates at a finer grain than Big Five, with lower-order aspects showing distinct trajectories.

### 139. Haehner et al. (2026)

### 140. Hansen et al. (2025)

### 141. Jackson et al. (2009)

**Citation:** Jackson, J. J., Walton, K. E., Harms, P. D., Bogg, T., Wood, D., Lodi-Smith, J., Edmonds, G. W., & Roberts, B. W. (2009). Not all conscientiousness scales change alike. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 96*(2), 446-459. https://doi.org/10.1037/a0014156

### 142. Jang, Livesley, & Vernon (1996)

**Citation:** Jang, K. L., Livesley, W. J., & Vernon, P. A. (1996). Heritability of the Big Five personality dimensions and their facets: A twin study. *Journal of Personality, 64*(3), 577-591.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Twin Study, Univariate Biometric Models
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* heritability, Big Five, facets, twin study, NEO-PI-R
- *Additional terms:* additive genetic, dominance, shared environment, nonshared environment

#### Core Themes
- Broad heritability of NEO-PI-R domains ranges .41-.53; nonadditive genetic effects detected for O and several E and A facets
- Genetic and environmental effects are not uniform across facets within a domain
- Shared environment negligible; nonshared environment accounts for 39-77% of variance

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
First comprehensive NEO-PI-R facet-level twin study, establishing that genetic architecture is more differentiated than domain heritability suggests.

### 143. Jang et al. (1998)

**Citation:** Jang, K. L., McCrae, R. R., Angleitner, A., Riemann, R., & Livesley, W. J. (1998). Heritability of facet-level traits in a cross-cultural twin sample. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 74*(6), 1556-1565. https://doi.org/10.1037/0022-3514.74.6.1556

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Cultural Twin Study (Canadian and German), Biometric SEM
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* heritability, facet-level traits, cross-cultural, hierarchical model
- *Additional terms:* specific variance, residual scores, five-factor theory, basic tendencies

#### Core Themes
- Both common factor variance and residual specific variance of 26/30 NEO-PI-R facets are significantly heritable (25-65% of reliable specific variance)
- Genetic and environmental influences equivalent across Canadian and German samples
- Facets are discrete heritable constructs, not merely exemplars of broad factors

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Landmark study showing personality's biological architecture extends to facet level and is cross-culturally invariant.

### 144. Jang et al. (2001)

**Citation:** Jang, K. L., Hu, S., Livesley, W. J., Angleitner, A., Riemann, R., Ando, J., Ono, Y., Vernon, P. A., & Hamer, D. H. (2001). Covariance structure of Neuroticism and Agreeableness: A twin and molecular genetic analysis. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 81*(2), 295-304.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Twin Study + Molecular Genetics, Cholesky Decomposition
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Neuroticism, Agreeableness, serotonin transporter, 5-HTTLPR, genetic correlation
- *Additional terms:* biological pleiotropy, molecular genetics, cross-domain covariance

#### Core Themes
- Substantial genetic correlations between N and A facets across Canadian, German, and Japanese twin samples
- 5-HTTLPR accounts for ~10% of N-A covariance, demonstrating biological pleiotropy in personality
- Single neurotransmitter/single trait models are oversimplistic

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Bridges quantitative and molecular genetics to show how a single gene contributes to cross-domain personality covariance.

### 145. Jang et al. (2002)

**Citation:** Jang, K. L., Livesley, W. J., Angleitner, A., Riemann, R., & Vernon, P. A. (2002). Genetic and environmental influences on the covariance of facets defining the domains of the five-factor model. *Personality and Individual Differences, 33*(1), 83-101.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Cultural Twin Study, Multivariate Genetic Models
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* twins, Five-Factor Model, biometric models, genetics
- *Additional terms:* independent pathways model, common pathways model, aetiological structure

#### Core Themes
- Each domain is composed of multiple common genetic and environmental factors; single common pathway model inadequate for any domain
- Independent pathways model fits better: higher-order traits are useful heuristic labels for pleiotropic genetic effects rather than unitary psychological entities
- Cross-cultural differences limited to effect magnitudes, not structure

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Challenges the assumption that Big Five domains reflect unitary biological systems, showing multi-factorial genetic architecture.

### 146. Kim et al. (2017)

**Citation:** Kim, S. E., et al. (2017). Meta-analysis of genome-wide SNP- and pathway-based associations for facets of neuroticism. *Journal of Human Genetics, 62*, 903-909. https://doi.org/10.1038/jhg.2017.61

- *Additional terms:* axon guidance, ITPR1, MIR548H3, neurodevelopment, Korean cohorts

#### Core Themes
- One genome-wide significant SNP for hostility facet; suggestive associations with psychiatric-linked genes
- Axon guidance pathway associated with multiple N facets (hostility, self-consciousness, vulnerability)
- Facet-level GWAS may reduce phenotypic noise vs. broad N scores

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Illustrates how molecular genetics at facet level can identify neurodevelopmental pathways underlying specific emotional dispositions.

### 147. Le Corff & Busque-Carrier (2016)

### 148. Lewis & Bates (2014)

**Citation:** Lewis, G. J., & Bates, T. C. (2014). How genes influence personality: Evidence from multi-facet twin analyses of the HEXACO dimensions. *Journal of Research in Personality, 51*, 9-17. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jrp.2014.04.004

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Twin Study (TwinsUK), Multivariate SEM
**Instruments:** HEXACO-60
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* HEXACO, genetic architecture, twin study, common pathway, independent pathway
- *Additional terms:* honesty-humility, emotionality, TwinsUK, biological basis

#### Core Themes
- For most HEXACO dimensions, genetic covariation among facets is explained by a single common genetic factor -- supporting domains as coherent biological systems
- Conscientiousness is the exception (independent pathway model fits best, indicating multiple independent genetic influences)
- Combined models (common pathway for genetics, independent for environment) provide best fit for several domains

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly tests whether personality domains have unitary biological bases using HEXACO; finds most do, except Conscientiousness.

### 149. Luciano et al. (2006)

**Citation:** Luciano, M., Wainwright, M. A., Wright, M. J., & Martin, N. G. (2006). The heritability of conscientiousness facets and their relationship to IQ and academic achievement. *Personality and Individual Differences, 40*(6), 1189-1199.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Twin and Sibling Study, Multivariate Genetic Modeling
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R, Multidimensional Aptitude Battery, Queensland Core Skills Test
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* conscientiousness, IQ, academic achievement, twin study, genetics
- *Additional terms:* Competence, Dutifulness, Self-Discipline, dominance genetic effects, adolescent twins

#### Core Themes
- All six C facets heritable (h² = .18-.49); Competence and Dutifulness correlate with both IQ and achievement
- Shared variance between C facets and cognitive measures is primarily mediated by a common additive genetic factor
- C-achievement link is solely genetic; C-IQ link is partly genetic, partly environmental

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates the genetic intersection between conscientiousness facets and cognitive ability -- directly relevant to understanding correlated mental phenotypes.

---

### 150. Ludeke et al. (2019)

**Citation:** Ludeke, S. G., et al. (2019). Using the Big Five Aspect Scales to translate between the HEXACO and Big Five personality models. *Journal of Personality, 87*(5), 1025-1038.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Correlation / Meta-Analysis (4 samples)
**Instruments:** BFAS, HEXACO-PI-R, BFI-2
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Aspect, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, HEXACO, aspects, facets, Honesty-Humility
- *Additional terms:* Compassion, Politeness, Volatility, Withdrawal, rotational differences, cross-model mapping

#### Core Themes
- Big Five aspects provide a parsimonious bridge between BFAS and HEXACO: Politeness maps to H-H (r ~ .51), Compassion to HEXACO-A (r ~ .58)
- The 10-aspect framework clarifies where Big Five and HEXACO genuinely differ vs. are rotationally equivalent

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates how the same phenotypic space is carved differently by competing structural models -- relevant to how measurement tools shape understanding of phenotypes.

### 151. Misiuro et al. (2022)

### 160. Roberts, Walton, & Viechtbauer (2006)

**Citation:** Roberts, B. W., Walton, K. E., & Viechtbauer, W. (2006). Patterns of mean-level change in personality traits across the life course: A meta-analysis. *Psychological Bulletin, 132*(1), 1-25.

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis (92 longitudinal samples, N ~ 50,000)
**Instruments:** Various (NEO-PI, CPI, MMPI, 16PF, EPI)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality change, meta-analysis, mean-level change, development
- *Additional terms:* social dominance, social vitality, maturity principle, social investment theory

#### Core Themes
- Social Dominance, C, and Emotional Stability increase especially in young adulthood (20-40); Social Vitality and O increase in adolescence then decline in old age
- Young adulthood is the primary period of change, contradicting the view personality is fixed by age 30
- Results most consistent with social investment models rather than intrinsic maturation alone

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Definitive meta-analysis on life span personality change, documenting how phenotypes are developmentally constructed across the full life course.

### 161. Romano et al. (2023)

### 162. Roysamb et al. (2018)

**Citation:** Roysamb, E., Nes, R. B., Czajkowski, N. O., & Vassend, O. (2018). Genetics, personality, and well-being: A twin study of traits, facets, and life satisfaction. *Scientific Reports, 8*, 12298. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41598-018-29881-x

**Methodology:** Twin Study, SEM, Cholesky Decomposition

### 164. Schwaba et al. (2022)

### 165. Seifert et al. (2023)

**Citation:** Seifert, I. S., Rohrer, J. M., Ehlert, A., & Wagner, J. (2023). Within-person personality trajectories across the adult lifespan. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 126*(3), 583-604.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal (three panel studies), Fixed Effects Modeling, Measurement Invariance
**Instruments:** Big Five adjective markers (HILDA), BFI-S (SOEP), IPIP-50 (LISS)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Item Level

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, personality development, fixed effects modeling, within-person change, lifespan
- *Additional terms:* maturity principle, panel data, HILDA, SOEP, LISS, scalar invariance, item-level trajectories

#### Core Themes
- Fixed effects across three panels (combined N > 48,000): N consistently declined, C increased until ~30
- Individual items within the same domain showed divergent age trajectories; item selection substantially affects estimated developmental trends
- Fixed effects and standard multilevel models sometimes produced divergent trajectories, particularly for N

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates personality development depends critically on measurement granularity -- items within the same domain develop differently, revealing the "maturity principle" is more nuanced than domain-level analyses suggest.

### 166. Soto et al. (2011)

**Citation:** Soto, C. J., John, O. P., Gosling, S. D., & Potter, J. (2011). Age differences in personality traits from 10 to 65. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 100*(2), 330-348.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Age-Trend Analysis
**Instruments:** BFI (44 items, 10 facets)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, personality development, age differences, facets, gender differences
- *Additional terms:* curvilinear age trends, maturity principle, late childhood, adolescence

#### Core Themes
- In N > 1.2 million (ages 10-65), A and C show curvilinear trends with negative dips in early adolescence followed by positive adult trends
- Facets within domains show divergent age trends (e.g., Activity declining while Assertiveness increases in E)
- Gender differences in N facets emerge during adolescence, paralleling epidemiological patterns for depression

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Most comprehensive cross-sectional map of Big Five domain/facet development from childhood through middle age.

### 167. Soto & John (2012)

**Citation:** Soto, C. J., & John, O. P. (2012). Development of Big Five domains and facets in adulthood. *Journal of Personality, 80*(4), 881-914.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional and Longitudinal (40-year follow-up), Multilevel Modeling
**Instruments:** CPI-Big Five (16 facets)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, facets, personality development, adulthood, CPI
- *Additional terms:* Industriousness, Self-Discipline, Orderliness, narrowly vs. broadly acting mechanisms

#### Core Themes
- Facets within domains show different age trends: within C, Industriousness increases but Orderliness does not; within E, Assertiveness increases while Gregariousness decreases
- Cross-domain change correlations near zero, indicating narrowly acting developmental mechanisms rather than a single maturation factor
- Within-domain facet changes substantially correlated, supporting domain- or facet-cluster-level mechanisms

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Critical evidence that adult personality development is driven by narrowly acting mechanisms at the facet/domain level rather than broad maturation.

### 168. Soto & John (2017)

**Citation:** Soto, C. J., & John, O. P. (2017). The next Big Five Inventory (BFI-2). *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 113*(1), 117-143.

- *Additional terms:* 15 facets, content-balanced scales, predictive power

#### Core Themes

- Facet scales provide substantially greater predictive power than domain scales (R² = .33 vs. .25)
- Content-balanced design controls acquiescence at both domain and facet levels

### 169. Stanek & Ones (2023)

### 170. Sun, Kaufman, & Smillie (2017)

**Citation:** Sun, J., Kaufman, S. B., & Smillie, L. D. (2017). Unique associations between Big Five personality aspects and multiple dimensions of well-being. *Journal of Personality, 86*(2), 158-172.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Path Analysis, SEM
**Instruments:** BFAS, SWLS, PANAS, PWB (Ryff), PERMA-Profiler
**Level of Detail:** Personality Aspect

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five aspects, well-being, subjective well-being, psychological well-being, PERMA
- *Additional terms:* Enthusiasm, Withdrawal, Industriousness, Compassion, Intellect, hedonic, eudaimonic

#### Core Themes
- One aspect within each domain is consistently more strongly associated with well-being: Enthusiasm (not Assertiveness), low Withdrawal (not low Volatility), Industriousness (not Orderliness), Compassion (not Politeness), Intellect (not Openness)
- Aspects within the same domain sometimes predict well-being in opposite directions
- Personality aspects explain average 56% of well-being variance

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates the personality-well-being relation is best characterized at the aspect level, with specific aspects showing distinct functional profiles.

### 171. Terracciano et al. (2005)

**Citation:** Terracciano, A., McCrae, R. R., Brant, L. J., & Costa, P. T., Jr. (2005). Hierarchical linear modeling analyses of the NEO-PI-R scales in the Baltimore Longitudinal Study of Aging. *Psychology and Aging, 20*(3), 493-506.

- *Additional terms:* intrinsic maturation, Five-Factor Theory, individual trajectories

- Facet trajectories diverge within domains (e.g., Actions declines most in O; Vulnerability increases after 80 in N)
- Significant individual differences in trajectories support biological influences on non-normative change

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Gold-standard longitudinal evidence for facet-level personality development across the adult lifespan with explicit evolutionary-biological discussion.

### 172. Yamagata et al. (2006)

**Citation:** Yamagata, S., et al. (2006). Is the genetic structure of human personality universal? A cross-cultural twin study from North America, Europe, and Asia. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 90*(6), 987-998.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Cultural Twin Study (Canada, Germany, Japan), CFA, Behavior Genetics
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R (self-report and peer-report)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, twin study, cross-cultural, genetic structure, heritability, universality
- *Additional terms:* phenotypic factor structure, genetic factor structure, Japan, Germany, Canada

#### Core Themes
- Phenotypic, genetic, and environmental factor structures highly similar across Canada, Germany, and Japan
- Heritabilities consistent cross-culturally (~.40-.60); negligible shared environment
- Genetic factor structure closely matches phenotypic five-factor structure, suggesting Big Five emerge from genetic covariance

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Strongest cross-cultural evidence that Big Five structure is rooted in universal genetic architecture rather than cultural construction.

### 173. Zakrisson et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Zakrisson, I., Soto, C. J., Lofstrand, P., & John, O. P. (2025). Extending the applicability of the BFI-2: Psychometric evaluation of a Swedish translation. *Psychological Test Adaptation and Development, 6*, 199-215.

**Purpose:** Original Research (Psychometric Validation)
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, EFA, CFA, Measurement Invariance, Criterion Validation
**Instruments:** BFI-2 (Swedish), PWB, attachment measures, SDO
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* BFI-2, Swedish adaptation, factor structure, measurement invariance, predictive validity
- *Additional terms:* Scandinavian adaptation, scalar invariance, well-being, cross-language replication

#### Core Themes
- Swedish BFI-2 reproduces five-factor structure at domain and facet levels (N > 5,000); domain reliabilities .79-.89
- Scalar invariance supported for A, C, and Negative Emotionality; metric invariance for E and O
- Criterion associations replicate English-language patterns

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Extends cross-cultural evidence for BFI-2 hierarchical structure to Scandinavian contexts, supporting universality of personality phenotype structure.

### 174. Quintana et al. (2019)

**Citation:** Quintana, D. S., Rokicki, J., van der Meer, D., Alnæs, D., Kaufmann, T., Córdova-Palomera, A., Dieset, I., Andreassen, O. A., & Westlye, L. T. (2019). Oxytocin pathway gene networks in the human brain. *Nature Communications*, *10*, 668. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-019-08503-8

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Gene Expression Atlas Analysis (Allen Human Brain Atlas), Weighted Gene Co-expression Network Analysis (WGCNA), fMRI Meta-Analysis (NeuroSynth reverse inference)
**Instruments:** Allen Human Brain Atlas (N=6 postmortem brains, 363–946 sampling sites each), GTEx database (~132 donors, 10 brain regions), NeuroSynth (14,371 fMRI studies)
**Level of Detail:** Other (gene-level expression mapping)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* oxytocin, gene expression, co-expression, acetylcholine, dopamine, brain atlas, cognitive states
- *Additional terms:* OXTR, CD38, OXT, CHRM4, CHRM5, DRD2, DRD5, COMT, muscarinic, NeuroSynth, Allen Brain Atlas, voxel-by-voxel, WGCNA

#### Core Themes
- Created voxel-by-voxel volumetric expression maps for three OXT pathway genes (OXT, OXTR, CD38) across the human brain; expression enriched in subcortical and olfactory regions, reduced in cerebellum
- Hierarchical clustering reveals OXTR and CD38 cluster with dopaminergic genes (DRD2, DRD5, COMT) and muscarinic ACh genes (CHRM4, CHRM5) — but NOT all muscarinic subtypes and NOT nicotinic receptors
- WGCNA identifies an OXTR/CD38 co-expression module enriched in a waist-hip ratio GWAS and gene sets for feeding behavior, cognition, and behavioral response to stimuli
- NeuroSynth reverse inference: OXTR expression map correlates most strongly with "sexual," "motivation," "incentive," and "anxiety" cognitive state maps (top 0.5% of 20,737 genes); OXTR and CD38 significantly higher in social vs. non-social brain regions (p=0.0002 and p=0.006)
- OXTR and CD38 show high differential stability across donors (top decile of all protein-coding genes), indicating reproducible expression patterns despite small N
- OXT expression patterns did NOT replicate in GTEx validation (r=0.22, p=0.54); OXTR (r=0.81, p<0.01) and CD38 (r=0.67, p=0.04) did replicate

#### Methodological Limitations (important for downstream use)
- **N=6 brains** — small sample, variable demographics (3 Caucasian males, 1 Hispanic female, 2 African-American males); mean age 42.5
- **Spatial co-expression ≠ single-cell co-localization** — genes expressed in the same brain regions are not necessarily expressed in the same cells; this cannot substitute for duplex FISH (cf. Dayley et al. 2026)
- **Spatial co-expression ≠ functional interaction** — the authors appropriately state the systems "may interact," not "do interact"
- OXT failed independent validation entirely; only OXTR and CD38 are reproducible across datasets
- Only 1 female donor; sex differences could not be comprehensively assessed (though no sex differences observed in GTEx)

#### Relation to OXT→ACh→Orienting Hypothesis
The OXTR–CHRM4/CHRM5 co-expression finding is consistent with the hypothesis that OXT and muscarinic ACh systems interact, but it provides only indirect, region-level support. It does NOT demonstrate that OXT activates cholinergic neurons (cf. Freeman/Dayley work on OXTR in NBM ChAT+ neurons) or that the interaction modulates attention/orienting specifically. The NeuroSynth correlates ("sexual," "motivation," "incentive," "anxiety") are appetitive/aversive states, not orienting per se. This paper should be treated as suggestive background, not as an independent confirmation of the OXT→ACh→orienting pathway.

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides whole-brain gene expression context for the oxytocin system and its co-expression with dopaminergic and muscarinic systems. Methodological limitations (N=6, spatial-only, OXT validation failure) mean it cannot serve as a load-bearing pillar for neuromodulator-to-trait mapping, but it usefully contextualizes the Freeman/Dayley NBM findings within a broader expression landscape.

---

### 175. Andrews, Ahmed, & Blakemore (2021)

**Citation:** Andrews, J. L., Ahmed, S. P., & Blakemore, S.-J. (2021). Navigating the social environment in adolescence: The role of social brain development. *Biological Psychiatry*, *89*(2), 109–118. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.biopsych.2020.09.012

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* social brain, adolescence, mentalizing, peer influence, dmPFC, TPJ, pSTS
- *Additional terms:* social cognition, neurodevelopment, emotion regulation, mental health, social brain network

#### Core Themes
- Social brain regions (dmPFC, TPJ, pSTS) undergo protracted structural and functional maturation through adolescence
- Mentalizing abilities continue to develop across the adolescent period, paralleling social brain maturation
- Peer influence is heightened during adolescence, reflecting both social-motivational and neurodevelopmental factors
- Emotion regulation capacities develop alongside prefrontal maturation, shaping social-emotional outcomes
- Social brain developmental trajectories have implications for understanding adolescent-onset mental health conditions

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Social brain maturation during adolescence provides a neurodevelopmental mechanism through which social-cognitive phenotypes are shaped; directly relevant to how mental phenotypes emerge through developmental processes.

### 176. Baratta, Seligman, & Maier (2023)

**Citation:** Baratta, M. V., Seligman, M. E. P., & Maier, S. F. (2023). From helplessness to controllability: Toward a neuroscience of resilience. *Frontiers in Psychiatry*, *14*, 1170417. https://doi.org/10.3389/fpsyt.2023.1170417

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* learned helplessness, controllability, mPFC, DRN, serotonin, resilience, contingency
- *Additional terms:* passivity, behavioral immunization, dorsal raphe nucleus, medial prefrontal cortex, escape-avoidance, circuit-level mechanisms

#### Core Themes
- Passivity in the face of uncontrollable stress is the default neural response, not a learned state — reversing the original helplessness framing
- Behavioral control over stress activates mPFC projections to the DRN, inhibiting serotonergic stress responses
- Prior experience with controllable stress produces "behavioral immunization" against later uncontrollable stress
- Circuit-specific effects of control experience are long-lasting and involve plasticity in mPFC-DRN projections
- Clinical implications for therapeutic interventions that restore perceived control and build resilience

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates how neural circuitry and environmental contingencies interact to construct the resilience phenotype; the mPFC-DRN pathway provides a concrete mechanism for how experience shapes stress-response traits.

### 177. Becht et al. (2021)

**Citation:** Becht, A. I., Bos, M. G. N., Nelemans, S. A., Peters, S., Vollebergh, W. A. M., Branje, S. J. T., Meeus, W. H. J., & Crone, E. A. (2021). Beyond the average brain: Individual differences in social brain development are associated with friendship quality. *Social Cognitive and Affective Neuroscience*, *16*(3), 292–301. https://doi.org/10.1093/scan/nsaa166

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal
**Instruments:** Friendship Quality Scale, Structural MRI (mPFC, pSTS, TPJ, precuneus)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* structural brain development, social brain, adolescence, friendship quality, individual differences, cortical thickness
- *Additional terms:* mPFC, pSTS, TPJ, precuneus, nonlinear development, neurodevelopmental trajectories

#### Core Themes
- Social brain regions show nonlinear cortical thickness decreases across adolescence
- Higher baseline mPFC cortical thickness predicted increases in friendship quality over time
- Faster mPFC cortical thinning predicted better friendship quality outcomes
- TPJ cortical thickness predicted lower friendship quality, suggesting region-specific developmental effects
- Individual neurodevelopmental trajectories — not just group averages — meaningfully predict social outcomes

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that individual differences in neurodevelopmental trajectories of social brain regions predict real-world social outcomes; directly supports the thesis that phenotypic variation is constructed through developmental processes.

### 178. Bjorkqvist, Lagerspetz, & Kaukiainen (1992)

**Citation:** Bjorkqvist, K., Lagerspetz, K. M. J., & Kaukiainen, A. (1992). Do girls manipulate and boys fight? Developmental trends in regard to direct and indirect aggression. *Aggressive Behavior*, *18*(2), 117–127.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** Peer nomination, self-ratings
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* direct aggression, indirect aggression, gender differences, development, social manipulation
- *Additional terms:* physical aggression, relational aggression, social intelligence, developmental onset, sex differences

#### Core Themes
- Girls use more indirect aggression than boys at ages 11 and 15, while boys use more direct physical aggression
- Indirect aggression requires social intelligence and sophisticated social-cognitive skills
- Developmental onset of indirect aggression occurs around age 11, coinciding with social-cognitive maturation
- The shift from direct to indirect aggression reflects growing social intelligence rather than reduced aggressiveness
- Sex differences in aggression type are shaped by developmental trajectories and social-cognitive maturation

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational evidence that sex differences in aggression are shaped by developmental trajectories and social-cognitive maturation; aggression phenotype construction depends on the cognitive tools available at each developmental stage.

### 179. Bjorkqvist (2001)

**Citation:** Bjorkqvist, K. (2001). Social defeat as a stressor in humans. *Physiology & Behavior*, *73*(3), 435–442.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* social defeat, aggression, bullying, testosterone, corticosterone, depression, immune function
- *Additional terms:* stress physiology, dominance hierarchies, animal models, psychosomatic effects, evolutionary conservation

#### Core Themes
- Animal models of social defeat produce severe and lasting physiological consequences including immune suppression and hormonal dysregulation
- Human social defeat (bullying, social exclusion, workplace mobbing) parallels animal findings in physiological impact
- Testosterone is linked to dominance-seeking behavior, and social defeat alters testosterone-cortisol dynamics
- The stress response to social defeat is evolutionarily conserved across species
- Argues for greater integration of animal and human research fields on social stress

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Bridges animal and human literatures on the evolutionarily conserved social defeat stress response; relevant to understanding how social environments produce physiological and behavioral phenotypic changes.

### 180. Bjorkqvist (2018)

**Citation:** Bjorkqvist, K. (2018). Gender differences in aggression. *Current Opinion in Psychology*, *19*, 39–42.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* gender differences, indirect aggression, physical aggression, testosterone, 2D:4D, social intelligence, genetics
- *Additional terms:* heritability, evolutionary function, prenatal androgen exposure, sex-typed behavior

#### Core Themes
- Sex differences in aggression are proportional rather than absolute — males show more physical, females more indirect aggression
- Aggression is approximately 50% heritable across twin studies
- Indirect aggression requires social intelligence, positioning it as a cognitively demanding behavioral strategy
- 2D:4D digit ratio (a proxy for prenatal androgen exposure) is linked to aggression patterns
- Testosterone serves evolutionary functions in both sexes, modulating but not determining aggressive behavior

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Integrates genetic, hormonal, and evolutionary perspectives on aggression phenotypes; demonstrates how multiple causal layers converge to produce sex-differentiated behavioral strategies.

#### Core Themes

### 182. Blakemore & Choudhury (2006)

**Citation:** Blakemore, S.-J., & Choudhury, S. (2006). Development of the adolescent brain: Implications for executive function and social cognition. *Journal of Child Psychology and Psychiatry*, *47*(3), 296–312.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* adolescence, brain development, puberty, synaptic pruning, myelination, prefrontal cortex
- *Additional terms:* executive function, social cognition, gray matter, white matter, synaptogenesis, region-specific maturation

#### Core Themes
- A second wave of synaptogenesis occurs at puberty, followed by region-specific synaptic pruning
- White matter increases linearly through adolescence, reflecting ongoing myelination
- Gray matter changes are non-linear, with region-specific timing of peak volume and subsequent decline
- Prefrontal cortex is among the last regions to mature, with implications for executive function and social cognition
- Early influential synthesis connecting cellular neurodevelopmental processes to the emergence of cognitive and social phenotypes

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Connects cellular-level neurodevelopmental events (synaptogenesis, pruning, myelination) to the emergence of cognitive and social phenotypes; foundational for understanding how biology constructs mental traits.

### 183. Branje, de Moor, Spitzer, & Becht (2021)

### 184. Coleman et al. (2024)

**Citation:** Coleman, E. S., et al. (2024). Prior experience with behavioral control over stress facilitates social dominance. *Neurobiology of Stress*, *28*, 100597.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** Warm spot competition, behavioral coding, pharmacological inactivation (muscimol)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* coping, mPFC, dominance, resilience, DRN, serotonin, controllability, warm spot competition
- *Additional terms:* prelimbic cortex, dorsomedial striatum, PL inactivation, winning effect, stress buffering

#### Core Themes
- Controllable stress experience activates prelimbic cortex (PL) and facilitates later social dominance in competition
- PL inactivation prevents the transfer of controllability experience to dominance behavior
- Repeated winning establishes stable social dominance via PL and dorsomedial striatum (DMS) circuits
- Winning experience buffers against the effects of later uncontrollable stress
- Control experience and winning experience engage overlapping PFC circuitry, suggesting a common neural substrate

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates that experiential controllability shapes social rank through PFC-mediated circuits; illustrates how prior experience constructs behavioral phenotypes via specific neural pathways.

### 185. DeYoung (2020)

- *Additional terms:* cognitive ability, hierarchical organization, empirical integration, trait-ability interface

#### Core Themes
- Intelligence and personality can be integrated within a common hierarchical framework of individual differences
- Openness/Intellect is the Big Five domain most strongly linked to cognitive ability, but the relation is complex
- Critiques three common dichotomies in the intelligence-personality literature (typical vs. maximal, cognitive vs. non-cognitive, ability vs. personality)
- Hierarchical organization of both intelligence and personality suggests structural parallels
- Reviews empirical evidence for the relation of each Big Five domain to cognitive ability

### 186. van Doeselaar et al. (2018)

- Identifies three core components of identity: distinctiveness (uniqueness from others), coherence (internal consistency), and continuity (stability over time)

### 187. Frederick (2005)

**Citation:** Frederick, S. (2005). Cognitive reflection and decision making. *Journal of Economic Perspectives*, *19*(4), 25–42.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Correlational
**Instruments:** CRT (Cognitive Reflection Test), Wonderlic, NFC (Need for Cognition), SAT, ACT
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* CRT, dual-process theory, System 1/2, decision making, time/risk preference, heuristics, rationality
- *Additional terms:* cognitive reflection, intuitive errors, judgment and decision-making, sex differences, cognitive ability

#### Core Themes
- Introduces the CRT as a measure of the disposition to override intuitive but incorrect responses (System 1) in favor of reflective, correct responses (System 2)
- CRT predicts decision-making patterns (time preferences, risk preferences) better than standard cognitive ability measures
- Bridges the cognitive ability and judgment/decision-making literatures by showing cognitive reflection as a distinct construct
- Reports large sex differences in CRT performance
- Demonstrates that preferences for risk and time can be understood partly as expressions of cognitive ability and reflective disposition

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational instrument and concept connecting cognitive phenotypes to real-world judgment and decision-making; the CRT operationalizes the interface between cognitive ability and rational behavior.

### 188. Hachenberg, Russo, & Cathomas (2025)

**Methodology:** Narrative Review

- *Paper keywords:* stress, immunity, microglia, neuroinflammation, BBB, CSDS, CVS, cytokines, sex differences, depression
- *Additional terms:* blood-brain barrier, chronic social defeat stress, chronic variable stress, peripheral immune mobilization, microglial transcriptomics

#### Core Themes
- Chronic stress alters microglia morphology and transcriptomic profiles in rodent models
- Blood-brain barrier disruption under chronic stress permits peripheral immune cell infiltration into the brain
- Peripheral immune cells are mobilized and trafficked to the brain during chronic stress

- Sex differences in stress-immune responses are emerging as a critical but underexplored dimension

### 189. Hashikawa et al. (2016)

**Citation:** Hashikawa, K., Hashikawa, Y., Falkner, A., & Lin, D. (2016). The neural circuits of mating and fighting in male mice. *Current Opinion in Neurobiology*, *38*, 27–37.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* aggression, mating, hypothalamus, olfactory system, cortical amygdala, VMHvl, optogenetics
- *Additional terms:* appetitive behavior, consummatory behavior, main olfactory system, accessory olfactory system, COApl, sensory-hormonal integration

#### Core Themes
- Mating and fighting involve distinct appetitive (approach) and consummatory (execution) behavioral phases
- Main and accessory olfactory systems provide parallel but distinct sensory inputs guiding social behavior
- The cortical amygdala posterolateral nucleus (COApl) is essential for innate approach to both mates and rivals
- The ventromedial hypothalamus ventrolateral (VMHvl) integrates sensory and hormonal signals to gate aggressive versus mating behavior
- Mating and fighting neural populations overlap in cortical amygdala, suggesting shared upstream processing before behavioral divergence

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Illustrates the evolved neural architecture for fundamental social behaviors; relevant to understanding how hardwired circuits provide the substrate on which phenotypic variation is built.

### 190. Kaufman (2009)

**Purpose:** Original Research

- *Paper keywords:* latent inhibition, intuition, dual-process theory, creativity, preconscious processing, openness
- *Additional terms:* gating mechanisms, psychosis risk, creative achievement, experiential processing, information filtering

#### Core Themes
- Faith in intuition (experiential processing style) is linked to decreased latent inhibition — reduced ability to filter previously irrelevant stimuli
- Latent inhibition has been separately linked to both psychosis risk and creative achievement
- Identifies three factors within intuition measures, suggesting intuition is multidimensional
- Bridges personality-level constructs (faith in intuition, openness) with basic cognitive mechanisms (stimulus gating)
- Connects openness-related traits to measurable differences in preconscious information filtering

Demonstrates a measurable cognitive mechanism (latent inhibition) that links personality-level differences (openness, intuitive processing) to basic information processing; illustrates how phenotypic traits map onto cognitive operations.

### 191. Kaukiainen et al. (1999)

**Citation:** Kaukiainen, A., Bjorkqvist, K., Lagerspetz, K., Osterman, K., Salmivalli, C., Rothberg, S., & Ahlbom, A. (1999). The relationships between social intelligence, empathy, and three types of aggression. *Aggressive Behavior*, *25*(2), 81–89.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** Peer-estimation measures of social intelligence, empathy, and aggression
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* social intelligence, empathy, indirect/relational aggression, physical aggression, adolescence
- *Additional terms:* verbal aggression, morally neutral capacity, Bjorkqvist developmental theory, peer estimation

#### Core Themes
- Indirect aggression correlates positively with social intelligence, while physical and verbal aggression show near-zero correlation with social intelligence
- Empathy negatively correlates with all forms of aggression
- Social intelligence functions as a morally neutral cognitive tool — it can be deployed for prosocial or antisocial purposes
- Results support Bjorkqvist's developmental theory that indirect aggression emerges as social-cognitive capacities mature
- The combination of high social intelligence and low empathy may particularly predict indirect aggression

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that social intelligence is a double-edged cognitive phenotype — the same cognitive capacity enables both prosocial and antisocial behavior depending on other trait configurations (empathy level).

### 192. Krishnan & Nestler (2008)

**Citation:** Krishnan, V., & Nestler, E. J. (2008). The molecular neurobiology of depression. *Nature*, *455*, 894–902. https://doi.org/10.1038/nature07455

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* depression, monoamine hypothesis, neurotrophins, neurogenesis, HPA axis, BDNF, resilience, neural circuits
- *Additional terms:* maladaptive neuroplasticity, limbic circuits, hippocampal neurogenesis, treatment resistance, molecular pathways

#### Core Themes
- Depression involves maladaptive neuroplastic changes in limbic and cortical circuits, not simply a "chemical imbalance"
- The monoamine hypothesis is insufficient as a comprehensive explanation for depression
- Neurotrophins (particularly BDNF) and hippocampal neurogenesis are key molecular contributors
- Animal models are valuable for identifying molecular mechanisms but have inherent limitations for modeling human depression
- Resilience emerges as a new frontier — understanding active counter-regulatory processes rather than just vulnerability

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Comprehensive molecular and circuit-level account of how the depressive phenotype is constructed through maladaptive neuroplasticity; positions resilience as an active biological process rather than mere absence of vulnerability.

### 193. Lenneis et al. (2021)

**Citation:** Lenneis, A., et al. (2021). Personality traits relate to chronotype at both the phenotypic and genetic level. *Journal of Personality*, *89*(6), 1206–1222.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Genetic Correlation Analysis
**Instruments:** NEO PI-3, MCTQ (Munich Chronotype Questionnaire), polygenic scores

- *Paper keywords:* chronotype, morningness-eveningness, FFM, facets, nuances, polygenic scores, genetic correlations

- Higher Conscientiousness and lower Openness predict earlier chronotype at the phenotypic level
- Facet-level specificity reveals that particular facets (A2, E5, C5) drive domain-level associations
- Item-level "polypersonality" scores demonstrate that personality-chronotype links extend to the nuance level

- Genetic correlations between personality and chronotype are confirmed, indicating overlapping biological substrates

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates shared genetic architecture between personality traits and circadian biology; supports the thesis that phenotypic traits have deep biological roots that cross traditional domain boundaries.

### 194. Lieberman & Eisenberger (2005)

**Citation:** Lieberman, M. D., & Eisenberger, N. I. (2005). A pain by any other name (rejection, exclusion, ostracism) still hurts the same: The role of the dorsal anterior cingulate in social and physical pain. In J. T. Cacioppo (Ed.), *Social neuroscience: Key readings* (pp. 167–187). Psychology Press.

**Purpose:** Book Chapter
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* dACC, social pain, physical pain, rejection, ostracism, neural alarm system, evolutionary co-optation
- *Additional terms:* dorsal anterior cingulate, rostral anterior cingulate, linguistic evidence, Cyberball, affective distress, pain overlap

#### Core Themes
- The dorsal anterior cingulate cortex (dACC) is involved in both social and physical pain distress
- Linguistic evidence across 15 cultures supports the conceptual overlap between social and physical pain
- Proposes an evolutionary piggybacking (co-optation) argument: social pain co-opted the neural infrastructure originally evolved for physical pain
- The neural alarm system combines threat detection and affective signaling functions
- Distinguishes dACC (distress/alarm) from rACC (regulation/coping) in pain processing

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates evolutionary co-optation as a mechanism producing the social pain phenotype; the shared neural substrate for social and physical pain illustrates how evolution repurposes existing circuits to construct new functional capacities.

### 195. Lipnevich et al. (2017)

**Citation:** Lipnevich, A. A., Credè, M., Hahn, E., Spinath, F. M., Roberts, R. D., & Gottschling, J. (2017). How distinctive are morningness and eveningness from the Big Five factors of personality? A meta-analytic investigation. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology*, *112*(3), 491–509.

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis
**Instruments:** Various chronotype and Big Five measures
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* circadian preference, morningness, eveningness, Big Five, Conscientiousness
- *Additional terms:* incremental validity, GPA, two-dimensional model, personality-chronotype overlap

#### Core Themes
- Conscientiousness shows the strongest relation with morningness (rho = .37) across meta-analytic estimates
- Big Five personality accounts for 11–16% of variance in circadian preference
- Morningness and eveningness are better conceptualized as two separate dimensions rather than poles of a single continuum
- Extraversion and Openness relate to eveningness
- Circadian preference adds incremental validity beyond Big Five for predicting GPA

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE

### 196. Luchetti et al. (2016)

**Citation:** Luchetti, M., Terracciano, A., Stephan, Y., & Sutin, A. R. (2016). Personality and cognitive decline in older adults: Data from a longitudinal sample and meta-analysis. *Journals of Gerontology: Psychological Sciences*, *71*(4), 591–601.

**Purpose:** Original Research + Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Longitudinal + Meta-Analysis
**Instruments:** MIDI (Midlife Development Inventory), word recall, cognitive status measures
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

- *Paper keywords:* cognitive decline, Conscientiousness, Neuroticism, older adults, memory, HRS
- *Additional terms:* Health and Retirement Study, Openness, subjective memory, clinical risk factors, aging

#### Core Themes
- Higher Neuroticism is associated with worse cognitive performance and steeper cognitive decline

### 197. Maier & Seligman (1976)

**Citation:** Maier, S. F., & Seligman, M. E. P. (1976). Learned helplessness: Theory and evidence. *Journal of Experimental Psychology: General*, *105*(1), 3–46.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* learned helplessness, uncontrollability, motivation, cognition, emotion, contingency, escape-avoidance
- *Additional terms:* response-outcome independence, cross-species generalization, contingency space, animal learning, psychopathology

#### Core Themes
- Exposure to uncontrollable events produces motivational deficits (failure to initiate responses), cognitive deficits (difficulty learning new contingencies), and emotional disturbances
- The helplessness effect generalizes across species from invertebrates to humans
- Formalizes the contingency space as the relationship between response probability and outcome probability
- Evaluates and rebuts alternative hypotheses (competing motor response, adaptation) in favor of the cognitive model
- Bridges animal learning research and human psychopathology, laying groundwork for cognitive models of depression

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational paper on how learning processes construct maladaptive behavioral and cognitive phenotypes; the contingency framework remains central to understanding how experience shapes mental traits.

### 198. Mata et al. (2011)

**Citation:** Mata, R., Josef, A. K., Samanez-Larkin, G. R., & Hertwig, R. (2011). Age differences in risky choice: A meta-analysis. *Annals of the New York Academy of Sciences*, *1235*, 18–29.

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis
**Instruments:** Various risk-taking and decision-making tasks
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* aging, risk taking, decisions from description/experience, cognitive decline, gain/loss framing
- *Additional terms:* experience-based decisions, description-based decisions, motivational changes, cognitive changes, task dependency

#### Core Themes
- Age differences in risk-taking are highly task-dependent rather than reflecting a uniform age-related shift
- Older adults show more risk-seeking behavior when learning leads to avoidance, but more risk-aversion when learning leads to seeking
- No clear age differences in framing effects (gain vs. loss frames)
- Both cognitive decline and motivational changes contribute to age-related shifts in risk behavior
- The direction of age effects depends on whether decisions are made from description or experience

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates that the risk phenotype emerges from the interaction between cognitive capacities and task demands; age-related changes reflect multiple underlying mechanisms rather than a single shift.

---

### 199. Murra et al. (2022)

**Citation:** Murra, D., et al. (2022). Characterizing behavioral and neuroendocrine features of susceptibility and resilience to social stress. *Neurobiology of Stress*, *17*, 100437.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental
**Instruments:** Social interaction test, open field (OF), forced swim test (FST), elevated plus maze (EPM), corticosterone ELISA
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* CSDS, resilience, susceptibility, coping behavior, corticosterone, social avoidance, HPA axis
- *Additional terms:* chronic social defeat stress, active coping, passive coping, Day 1 behavior, aggressor-specific avoidance, baseline predictors

#### Core Themes
- Coping strategies displayed during the initial defeat experience predict later susceptibility vs. resilience outcomes
- Traditional measures of anxiety and depression-like behavior do not distinguish susceptible from resilient animals
- Social avoidance following CSDS is aggressor-specific rather than generalized
- Baseline behavioral measures do not predict post-stress outcomes — the response during stress itself is critical
- Paradoxically, higher active coping on Day 1 is associated with later susceptibility, suggesting initial fight-back may index greater threat reactivity

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that the stress-response phenotype is constructed through the interaction of coping behaviors and neuroendocrine responses during adversity itself; challenges simple active-coping-equals-resilience narratives.

### 200. Nestler et al. (2002)

**Citation:** Nestler, E. J., Barrot, M., DiLeone, R. J., Eisch, A. J., Gold, S. J., & Monteggia, L. M. (2002). Neurobiology of depression. *Neuron*, *34*(1), 13–25.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* depression, HPA axis, glucocorticoids, BDNF, CREB, hippocampus, NAc, amygdala, antidepressants
- *Additional terms:* heritability, gene-environment interaction, parallel circuits, animal models, heterogeneity

#### Core Themes
- Depression is a heterogeneous condition with 40–50% heritability, requiring gene-environment interaction models
- Multiple brain regions (hippocampus, NAc, amygdala, PFC) operate in parallel circuits to produce depressive symptoms
- Presents three working hypotheses: monoamine, HPA/stress, and neurotrophic
- Gene-environment interaction is critical — genetic vulnerability alone is insufficient without environmental triggers
- Animal models are useful for identifying molecular mechanisms but limited in capturing the full human phenotype

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Comprehensive overview of multifactorial phenotype construction in depression; directly illustrates how genetic, neural circuit, and environmental factors converge to produce a complex mental phenotype.

### 201. Osei, Block, & Wippert (2022)

**Citation:** Osei, F., Block, A., & Wippert, P.-M. (2022). Association of primary allostatic load mediators and metabolic syndrome (MetS): A systematic review. *Frontiers in Endocrinology*, *13*, 946740.

**Purpose:** Systematic Review
**Methodology:** Systematic Review
**Instruments:** Various cortisol, DHEAS, epinephrine, and norepinephrine assays
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* allostatic load, cortisol, DHEAS, epinephrine, norepinephrine, metabolic syndrome, HPA axis
- *Additional terms:* sympathetic nervous system, chronic stress, neuroendocrine pathways, metabolic phenotype, methodological heterogeneity

#### Core Themes
- Cortisol is consistently associated with metabolic syndrome across studies
- DHEAS shows an association with metabolic syndrome in approximately 60% of studies reviewed
- Epinephrine and norepinephrine show no consistent association with metabolic syndrome
- The HPA axis appears more relevant than the sympathetic nervous system for metabolic syndrome risk
- Methodological heterogeneity across studies limits causal inference

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates how chronic stress, through neuroendocrine pathways (primarily HPA axis), shapes metabolic phenotypes; relevant to understanding allostatic load as a mechanism linking psychological and physical health.

### 202. Ostlund & Pérez-Edgar (2023)

**Citation:** Ostlund, B. D., & Pérez-Edgar, K. (2023). A two-hit model of behavioral inhibition and anxiety: Integrating evidence across infancy and adolescence. *Annual Review of Developmental Psychology*, *5*, 239–261.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* behavioral inhibition, anxiety, two-hit model, infancy, adolescence, sensitive periods, caregiver distress, attentional control
- *Additional terms:* temperament, threat reactivity, affective systems, cognitive control, developmental trajectory, risk modulation

#### Core Themes
- Proposes a two-hit developmental model with sensitive periods during infancy and adolescence
- Hit 1 (gestation/infancy) tunes attentional and affective systems, establishing the initial temperamental set point
- Hit 2 (adolescence) taxes existing BI vulnerabilities through social and biological demands of this developmental period
- Approximately 40% of behaviorally inhibited children go on to develop clinical anxiety — the model explains heterogeneity in outcomes
- Attention and cognitive control serve as modulators that can either amplify or buffer temperamental risk

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides a developmental sensitive-period framework for understanding how temperamental phenotype trajectories are shaped by the timing and nature of environmental input; directly models the construction of anxiety from temperament.

### 203. Preckel et al. (2011)

**Citation:** Preckel, F., Lipnevich, A. A., Schneider, S., & Roberts, R. D. (2011). Chronotype, cognitive abilities, and academic achievement: A meta-analytic investigation. *Learning and Individual Differences*, *21*(5), 483–492.

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis
**Instruments:** LOCI, MEQ, CCS, SWPAQ; various IQ and achievement tests
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* chronotype, morningness-eveningness, cognitive ability, academic achievement, suppression effect
- *Additional terms:* developmental chronotype shift, two-dimensional model, capacity vs. performance, circadian preference

#### Core Themes
- Eveningness is associated with higher cognitive ability (r = .08) but lower academic achievement (r = -.14)
- Morningness shows the reverse pattern: lower cognitive ability but higher achievement
- Chronotype acts as a suppressor variable — its divergent relations with ability and achievement are masked in simple correlations
- Developmental shifts in chronotype (toward eveningness in adolescence) may interact with school scheduling
- A two-dimensional model of chronotype (separate morningness and eveningness) is preferred over a single bipolar continuum

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates that circadian traits differentially predict cognitive capacity versus academic performance; relevant to understanding how biological rhythms interact with environmental demands to shape functional outcomes.

---

### 204. Samanez-Larkin et al. (2009)

**Citation:** Samanez-Larkin, G. R., Robertson, E. R., Mikels, J. A., Carstensen, L. L., & Gotlib, I. H. (2009). Selective attention to emotion in the aging brain. *Psychology and Aging*, *24*(3), 519–529.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** fMRI
**Instruments:** Emotional interference task, fMRI
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* aging, emotion, attention, interference, inferior/middle frontal gyri, socioemotional selectivity theory
- *Additional terms:* emotional selective attention, lateral PFC recruitment, positivity effect, deficit models, motivational prioritization

#### Core Themes
- Older adults show interference on non-emotional trials but not on emotional trials, reversing the typical interference pattern
- Older adults recruit lateral prefrontal cortex more than younger adults for emotional suppression
- Results support socioemotional selectivity theory (SST): aging prioritizes emotionally meaningful information
- First neuroimaging study to examine emotional selective attention in older adults
- Challenges deficit-only models of cognitive aging by showing preserved and even enhanced emotional processing

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates that the aging brain reorganizes attentional resources around motivationally prioritized emotional content; illustrates how developmental changes reshape the cognitive phenotype in functionally meaningful ways.

### 205. Savidge & Bales (2023)

**Citation:** Savidge, L. E., & Bales, K. L. (2023). Possible effects of pair bonds on general cognition: Evidence from shared roles of dopamine. *Neuroscience and Biobehavioral Reviews*, *152*, 105317.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* pair bonds, cognition, dopamine, attention, working memory, cognitive flexibility, VTA, NAc, oxytocin, prairie voles, titi monkeys
- *Additional terms:* D1 receptors, mesolimbic pathway, monogamous species, social experience, substance abuse, cognitive phenotype

#### Core Themes
- Dopamine pathways supporting pair bonding substantially overlap with those supporting general cognition (attention, working memory, cognitive flexibility)
- Oxytocin modulates VTA dopamine neurons, providing a molecular link between social bonding and cognitive circuits
- Monogamous species show greater medial prefrontal cortex allocation compared to non-monogamous relatives
- Pair bonding experience may alter general cognitive capacities via shared dopaminergic substrates
- Substance abuse disrupts pair bonding through the same D1 receptor mechanisms, suggesting a common vulnerability

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Illustrates how social experience (pair bonding) can reshape cognitive phenotypes via overlapping dopaminergic circuits; relevant to understanding how social environments influence cognitive trait expression.

### 206. Slessor, Phillips, & Bull (2008)

**Citation:** Slessor, G., Phillips, L. H., & Bull, R. (2008). Age-related declines in basic social perception: Evidence from tasks assessing eye-gaze processing. *Psychology and Aging*, *23*(4), 812–822.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Experimental
**Instruments:** FEEST (Facial Expressions of Emotion: Stimuli and Tests), Pelli Robson contrast sensitivity, MMSE
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* eye gaze, gaze following, aging, social perception, STS, joint attention, signal detection
- *Additional terms:* superior temporal sulcus, theory of mind, visual acuity, direct gaze bias, perceptual building blocks

#### Core Themes
- Older adults show age-related declines in gaze direction detection and joint attention accuracy
- Older adults exhibit a "direct" response bias — tendency to perceive gaze as directed at them
- Declines are not solely attributable to reduced visual acuity (controlled for contrast sensitivity)
- Implicates deterioration of superior temporal sulcus (STS) function, a key region for social perception
- Gaze processing represents a basic perceptual building block for higher-order social cognition (Theory of Mind)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates how neural deterioration reshapes social phenotypes at the level of basic perceptual building blocks; relevant to understanding how aging degrades the inputs to social cognition.

### 207. Spaniol, Voss, & Grady (2008)

**Citation:** Spaniol, J., Voss, A., & Grady, C. L. (2008). Aging and emotional memory: Cognitive mechanisms underlying the positivity effect. *Psychology and Aging*, *23*(4), 859–872.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Behavioral, Computational Modeling (Diffusion Model)
**Instruments:** Diffusion model, NEO-FFI, TAS-20, PANAS, Mill Hill Vocabulary
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* aging, emotion, recognition memory, diffusion model, positivity effect, familiarity, drift rate
- *Additional terms:* cognitive process decomposition, valence, stimulus type, mnemonic account, motivational account

#### Core Themes
- Decomposes the positivity effect in older adults' memory using the diffusion model framework
- Age × Valence interactions emerge in drift rate parameters, revealing process-level differences
- Older adults show greater familiarity-based recognition for positive items specifically
- Provides evidence for a mnemonic (not just motivational) account of the positivity effect
- Results generalize across different stimulus types (words, pictures)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates that motivational shifts in aging reshape cognitive phenotypes at the level of specific processing mechanisms; illustrates how computational modeling can decompose phenotypic-level effects into constituent processes.

### 208. Sterling (2012)

**Citation:** Sterling, P. (2012). Allostasis: A model of predictive regulation. *Physiology & Behavior*, *106*(1), 5–15.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* homeostasis, allostasis, predictive regulation, hypertension, addiction, obesity, brain as regulator
- *Additional terms:* error reduction, capacity matching, shared resources, memory-based prediction, pathology, regulatory efficiency

#### Core Themes
- Homeostasis (regulation by error correction) is inefficient; allostasis (brain-based predictive regulation) is the more accurate model
- Allostasis offers four advantages over homeostasis: reduced error signals, matched capacity to predicted demand, shared resources across systems, and memory-based anticipatory adjustment
- The brain serves as the central regulator, using prediction and learning to maintain physiological parameters
- Pathology arises at the level of the predictive model — when predictions are chronically miscalibrated (addiction, obesity, hypertension)
- Reward ("carrot") and distress ("stick") mechanisms serve as the brain's regulatory signals for allostatic adjustment

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational framework for understanding the brain as an allostatic regulator that constructs physiological and behavioral phenotypes through predictive modeling; directly relevant to how mental phenotypes are actively maintained and can become pathological.

---

### 209. Torres-Berrio et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Torres-Berrio, A., et al. (2025). Neurobiology of resilience to early life stress. *Neuropsychopharmacology*.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* early life stress, resilience, neurodevelopment, VTA, NAc, PFC, sensitive periods, dopamine, epigenetic
- *Additional terms:* psychiatric risk, neonatal window, adolescent window, active coping, translational paradigms, circuit activation

#### Core Themes
- Early life stress heightens psychiatric risk 2–4 fold, yet the majority of exposed individuals are resilient
- VTA dopamine projections to NAc and PFC are critical circuits for determining resilient vs. susceptible outcomes
- Neonatal and adolescent periods represent distinct sensitive windows for stress-circuit calibration
- Active coping strategies map onto specific patterns of circuit activation in resilient animals
- Advocates for improved translational paradigms that better capture human resilience mechanisms

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates how adverse experience interacting with developmental sensitive windows produces divergent phenotypes (resilience vs. vulnerability); directly supports the book's framework of phenotype construction through biology-environment interaction.

### 210. Trumble et al. (2016)

**Citation:** Trumble, B. C., Blackwell, A. D., Stieglitz, J., Thompson, M. E., von Rueden, C. R., Kaplan, H., & Gurven, M. (2016). Associations between male testosterone and immune function in a pathogenically stressed forager-horticultural population. *American Journal of Physical Anthropology*, *161*(3), 494–505.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** Milliplex cytokine panel, EIA (urinary testosterone)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Immunocompetence Handicap Hypothesis, PHA, LPS, testosterone, Tsimane, life history, cytokines
- *Additional terms:* immunomodulation, T-cell response, B-cell response, energetic trade-off, high-pathogen ecology, reproductive effort

#### Core Themes
- Higher testosterone is associated with down-regulated T-cell (but not B-cell) immune responses in 109 Tsimane men
- Results are immunomodulatory rather than broadly immunosuppressive — testosterone selectively reshapes immune allocation
- Findings support an energetic trade-off framework rather than the classic Immunocompetence Handicap Hypothesis
- Data come from a high-pathogen, natural fertility population, increasing ecological validity
- Consistent with life history theory predictions about trade-offs between reproductive and immune effort

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides direct evidence for life history trade-offs between reproductive and immune phenotypes in a naturalistic human population; demonstrates how hormonal mechanisms allocate finite resources across competing phenotypic functions.
