### 211. Willmore et al. (2022)

**Citation:** Willmore, L., et al. (2022). Behavioural and dopaminergic signatures of resilience. *Nature*, *611*(7934), 124–132.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** CSDS + Fiber Photometry + Optogenetics
**Instruments:** Fiber photometry (NAc DA), optogenetics, machine learning behavioral classifiers
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* resilience, dopamine, social defeat, NAc, VTA, fiber photometry, optogenetics, behavioral strategies
- *Additional terms:* real-time neural recording, machine learning, causal manipulation, behavioral phenotyping, defeat behavior

#### Core Themes
- Distinct behavioral strategies during the defeat experience itself (not just post-defeat) differentiate resilient from susceptible animals
- NAc dopamine dynamics differ: resilient animals show more DA near the aggressor and during fighting back; susceptible animals show more DA at attack offset and during fleeing
- Behavioral strategies are quantifiable via machine learning classifiers from video data
- Optogenetic stimulation of NAc dopamine during defeat episodes promotes resilient outcomes — establishing a causal link
- Real-time behavioral-neural interactions during adversity determine the phenotypic outcome

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Establishes a causal link between real-time dopaminergic activity during adversity and subsequent resilience phenotype; demonstrates that phenotypic outcomes are constructed moment-by-moment through behavioral-neural interactions.

### 212. Akil & Nestler (2023)

**Citation:** Akil, H., & Nestler, E. J. (2023). The neurobiology of stress: Vulnerability, resilience, and major depression. *Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences*, *120*(49), e2312662120. https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.2312662120

**Purpose:** Commentary/Editorial
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* stress, depression, anxiety, resilience, vulnerability, pandemic, metabolic depression
- *Additional terms:* second pandemic, chemical imbalance critique, multi-level strategy, counter-regulatory processes, metabolic subtype, policy implications

#### Core Themes
- Frames post-pandemic mental health crisis as a "second pandemic" requiring urgent scientific and policy response
- Depression is not a "chemical imbalance" — this framing is explicitly rejected in favor of circuit- and systems-level models
- Advocates a multi-level strategy spanning molecules, circuits, behavior, and policy
- Resilience is characterized as an active counter-regulatory process with distinct biological signatures, not mere absence of pathology
- Metabolic dysfunction is proposed as a depression subtype with distinct mechanisms and treatment implications

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides a programmatic framework for understanding how stress produces psychiatric phenotypes across multiple levels of analysis; the active resilience and metabolic depression concepts directly support the book's phenotype construction thesis.

### 213. Cathomas et al. (2024)

**Citation:** Cathomas, F., et al. (2024). Circulating myeloid-derived MMP8 in stress susceptibility and depression. *Nature*, *626*(7999), 1108–1115.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** CSDS + CyTOF + scRNA-seq + Translational (human MDD)
**Instruments:** CyTOF (mass cytometry), scRNA-seq, social interaction test, MMP8 ELISA
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* MMP8, depression, neuroimmunology, monocytes, NAc, extracellular matrix, CSDS, CyTOF, scRNA-seq
- *Additional terms:* matrix metalloproteinase 8, Ly6C-hi monocytes, brain trafficking, peripheral-to-central immune signaling, extracellular space remodeling

#### Core Themes
- MMP8 (matrix metalloproteinase 8) is upregulated in stress-susceptible mice and in humans with major depressive disorder
- Brain-trafficking Ly6C-hi monocytes increase in susceptible animals, providing a cellular vehicle for peripheral-to-central signaling
- MMP8 infiltrates the nucleus accumbens and alters extracellular space through matrix remodeling
- Genetic or pharmacological depletion of MMP8 prevents social avoidance behavior after chronic social defeat
- Establishes a causal peripheral immune-to-brain mechanism for stress susceptibility and depression

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Identifies a specific molecular mechanism (MMP8 from peripheral myeloid cells) through which the immune system causally shapes brain function and behavioral phenotype; demonstrates how peripheral biology constructs central nervous system states.

### 214. de Moor et al. (2023)

**Citation:** de Moor, E. L., Denissen, J. J. A., Emons, W. H. M., Bleidorn, W., Lüdtke, O., Orth, U., & Meeus, W. H. J. (2023). Personality development across adolescence and young adulthood: Longitudinal associations with life transitions and self-concept clarity. *European Journal of Personality*, *37*(5), 587–604.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal (14 years, N = 497)
**Instruments:** Big Five measure, Self-Concept Clarity Scale
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* adolescence, young adulthood, Big Five, life transitions, self-concept clarity, maturity principle
- *Additional terms:* personality maturation, employee role, pre-registered, socialization, intrinsic maturation

#### Core Themes
- Personality maturation across adolescence and young adulthood is broadly supported (increases in A, C, E; decreases in N)
- No evidence for a "maturity dip" during adolescence — development is gradual and consistent
- Entering the employee role predicts higher Conscientiousness, but other life transitions do not predict personality change
- Self-concept clarity does not moderate the relationship between life transitions and personality change
- Pre-registered design strengthens confidence in null findings for most transition effects

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Suggests that intrinsic maturational processes may matter more than socialization contexts for personality development; relevant to understanding the relative contributions of biology and environment to phenotype construction.

### 217. Nestler & Russo (2024)

**Citation:** Nestler, E. J., & Russo, S. J. (2024). Neurobiological basis of stress resilience. *Neuron*, *112*(12), 1911–1929.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* resilience, stress, CSDS, susceptibility, VTA, NAc, PFC, BDNF, DeltaFosB, epigenetics, gut microbiome, social buffering
- *Additional terms:* active biological process, molecular signatures, heritability, psychosocial factors, paradigm shift, chronic social defeat stress

#### Core Themes
- Resilience is an active biological process with unique molecular signatures — not simply the absence of susceptibility or an intermediate state
- CSDS reveals that resilient mice show distinct (not intermediate) molecular and circuit patterns compared to susceptible mice
- Resilience in humans is approximately 35% heritable, with the remainder attributable to experience and environment
- Identifies ten psychosocial resilience factors from the human literature
- No resilience-specific GWAS has been conducted yet; advocates for a paradigm shift toward inducing resilience rather than only treating pathology

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Positions resilience as an actively constructed phenotype with distinct biological programs rather than mere absence of pathology; directly supports the book's thesis that mental phenotypes are built through specific biological and experiential mechanisms.

### 218. Loehlin et al. (2015)

**Citation:** Loehlin, J. C., et al. (2015). Is there a genetic correlation between general factors of intelligence and personality? *Twin Research and Human Genetics*, *18*(3), 234–242.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Twin/Genetic Analysis
**Instruments:** JEPQ, CPI, NEO-FFI, BFI, GATB, MAB, NMSQT, Mill Hill, Ravens, WAIS
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* GFP, g, genetic correlation, Woodley hypothesis, mutation-selection balance, life history, twin study
- *Additional terms:* general factor of personality, general intelligence, frequency-dependent selection, evolutionary-genetic processes, cross-cultural

#### Core Themes
- Examines five twin samples across four countries to test whether general factors of intelligence (g) and personality (GFP) share genetic variance
- The genetic correlation between g and GFP can be set to zero in most samples without loss of fit
- One US sample is an exception, likely due to different instrumentation rather than a genuine population difference
- Results are consistent with different evolutionary-genetic processes underlying g (mutation-selection balance) and personality (frequency-dependent selection)
- Suggests that intelligence and personality represent evolutionary-genetically independent phenotype families

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides evidence for the evolutionary-genetic independence of intelligence and personality phenotype families; relevant to understanding why these two major domains of individual differences may have distinct evolutionary origins and maintenance mechanisms.

### 219. Decety & Lamm (2007)

**Citation:** Decety, J., & Lamm, C. (2007). The role of the right temporoparietal junction in social interaction: How low-level computational processes contribute to meta-cognition. *The Neuroscientist*, *13*(6), 580–593.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis, Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* temporoparietal junction, self/other distinction, agency, social cognition, theory of mind, empathy, attention
- *Additional terms:* right TPJ, meta-cognition, domain-general mechanism, function/location meta-analysis, perspective-taking, low-level computation, attention reorienting, sensory prediction

#### Core Themes
- Computes four independent function/location meta-analyses of 70 neuroimaging studies covering agency, empathy, attention reorienting, and theory of mind
- Demonstrates substantial overlap in right TPJ activation across all four domains, providing strong evidence for a domain-general computational mechanism
- Proposes that high-level social cognition (empathy, ToM) depends on low-level computational processes involved in comparing self-generated vs. externally produced signals
- The right TPJ is positioned as a heteromodal association cortex integrating input from thalamus, visual, auditory, somaesthetic, and limbic areas
- Challenges the view that TPJ is domain-specific for social cognition; instead, social cognition recruits a pre-existing domain-general comparator

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates how a single cortical region constructs higher-order social-cognitive phenotypes (empathy, theory of mind) from lower-level computational building blocks; directly illustrates the book's theme that complex mental phenotypes emerge from reuse of domain-general neural mechanisms.

### 220. Igelström et al. (2015)

**Citation:** Igelström, K. M., Webb, T. W., & Graziano, M. S. A. (2015). Neural processes in the human temporoparietal cortex separated by localized independent component analysis. *Journal of Neuroscience*, *35*(25), 9432–9445.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Resting-State fMRI, Independent Component Analysis
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cortical parcellation, inferior parietal lobule, probabilistic independent component analysis, resting-state functional connectivity, supramarginal and angular gyrus, temporoparietal junction cortex
- *Additional terms:* localized ICA, bilateral TPJ, lateralization, functional subdivisions, default mode network, ventral attention network, frontoparietal network, auditory network, sensorimotor network

#### Core Themes
- Applies localized ICA (rather than whole-brain ICA) to resting-state fMRI within bilateral TPJ across three independent datasets
- Identifies five reproducible TPJ subdivisions: four bilaterally symmetrical and one right-biased
- The right-biased component connects to an attentional network; bilateral components connect to sensorimotor, auditory, frontoparietal, and default mode networks respectively
- Demonstrates that TPJ hosts multiple distinct neural processes with well-developed specialization and lateralization
- Data-driven approach avoids the task-based biases that have complicated prior TPJ parcellation efforts

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides the neuroanatomical architecture showing how a single cortical region is organized into functionally distinct subdivisions; relevant to understanding how the brain parcels different computational demands across neighboring tissue.

### 221. Igelström et al. (2016)

**Citation:** Igelström, K. M., Webb, T. W., Kelly, Y. T., & Graziano, M. S. A. (2016). Topographical organization of attentional, social and memory processes in the human temporoparietal cortex. *eNeuro*, *3*(2), ENEURO.0060-16.2016.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Task-Based fMRI, Independent Component Analysis
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* attention, memory, social cognition, TPJ, fMRI, independent component analysis, frontoparietal control network, ventral attention network, default mode network
- *Additional terms:* domain-general computation, domain-specific computation, oddball detection, theory of mind, episodic memory, attentional reorienting, working memory, BOLD latency

#### Core Themes
- Scans subjects with fMRI during five tasks (attentional reorienting, oddball target detection, theory of mind, episodic memory, working memory) that activate the TPJ
- Uses ICA parcellation to identify task-related functional components within bilateral TPJ
- One dorsal TPJ component, connected to the frontoparietal control network, was activated by all five tasks — evidence of a domain-general hub
- Other TPJ subregions were domain-specific: attention components connected to ventral attention network; ToM component connected to default mode network
- Domain-general dorsal component showed longer BOLD latency than domain-specific components, suggesting involvement in distinct (perhaps post-perceptual) computations
- Provides a reconciliation of the domain-general vs. domain-specific debate: TPJ performs both types of computation in spatially distinct functional components

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly demonstrates that a single cortical region can host both domain-general and domain-specific computations in spatially adjacent but functionally distinct subdivisions; a concrete example of how neural architecture supports the construction of multiple mental phenotypes from shared and specialized processing streams.

---

### 222. Scholz et al. (2009)

**Citation:** Scholz, J., Triantafyllou, C., Whitfield-Gabrieli, S., Brown, E. N., & Saxe, R. (2009). Distinct regions of right temporo-parietal junction are selective for theory of mind and exogenous attention. *PLoS ONE*, *4*(3), e4869.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Task-Based fMRI (High-Resolution)
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* right temporoparietal junction, theory of mind, exogenous attention, fMRI, false belief, Posner cueing
- *Additional terms:* RTPJ, spatial separation, bootstrap analysis, within-subjects design, high-resolution imaging, attentional reorienting, overlap analysis, functional dissociation

#### Core Themes
- Uses high-resolution fMRI (3T, 1.6 mm voxels) and within-subjects design to directly compare ToM and exogenous attention activations in right TPJ
- Strict conjunction analysis reveals that true overlap between the two activations is minimal (~2% of active voxels) and located at the periphery of each activation
- Bootstrap analysis identifies a reliable 6–10 mm spatial displacement between peak activations for ToM vs. attention
- Same displacement pattern observed in within-subjects comparisons, confirming neighboring but distinct regions
- Challenges earlier findings of overlap (e.g., Mitchell, 2008) that used lower-resolution methods, suggesting previous apparent overlap was artifactual
- Results support the view that right TPJ contains neighboring but functionally distinct subregions for social cognition and attentional reorienting

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates fine-grained functional segregation within a cortical region often treated as unitary; relevant to the book's theme that mental phenotypes are supported by precise, spatially organized neural substrates rather than monolithic brain areas.

---

### 223. Bolton et al. (2022)

**Citation:** Bolton, J. L., Short, A. K., Othy, S., Kooiker, C. L., Shao, M., Gunn, B. G., Beck, J., Bai, X., Law, S. M., Savage, J. C., Lambert, J., Bhatt, D., Cahalan, M. D., & Baram, T. Z. (2022). Early stress-induced impaired microglial pruning of excitatory synapses on immature CRH-expressing neurons provokes aberrant adult stress responses. *Cell Reports*, *38*(13), 110600. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.celrep.2022.110600

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* early-life adversity, microglia, synapse pruning, CRH neurons, HPA axis, MerTK, DREADDs, stress
- *Additional terms:* limited bedding/nesting (LBN), paraventricular nucleus (PVN), excitatory synapses, vGlut2, PSD95, two-photon imaging, chemogenetics, looming shadow task, ACTH, corticosterone

#### Core Themes
- Limited bedding/nesting (LBN) model (P2–P10) augments excitatory synapses on CRH+ neurons in hypothalamic PVN, persisting to P24–25
- ELA impairs microglial process dynamics (reduced distance and velocity) specifically in PVN microglia abutting CRH+ neurons
- Mechanism involves reduced MerTK phagocytic receptor expression on microglia, implicating deficient phagocytic signaling
- MerTK inhibition in control PVN slice cultures mimicked ELA effects (increased excitatory synapses on CRH+ neurons)
- Chronic chemogenetic activation of microglia (Gq-DREADDs) during ELA (P3–P10) rescued the phenotype: normalized synapse numbers, reduced adrenal weight, restored ACTH stress response kinetics, and rescued aberrant behavioral threat responses in adulthood
- Demonstrates a causal, mechanistic pathway: ELA → impaired microglial pruning → excess excitatory drive on CRH+ neurons → aberrant adult stress reactivity

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides a cellular-level mechanism for how early-life stress gets "embedded" in neural circuitry — impaired microglial pruning creates a hyperexcitable stress-response system that persists into adulthood. Directly relevant to the book's theme of how experience shapes mental phenotypes through biological intermediaries.

### 224. Breton-Provencher et al. (2022)

**Citation:** Breton-Provencher, V., Drummond, G. T., Feng, J., Li, Y., & Sur, M. (2022). Spatiotemporal dynamics of noradrenaline during learned behaviour. *Nature*, *606*, 732–738. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-022-04782-2

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* locus coeruleus, noradrenaline, learned behavior, optogenetics, fiber photometry, cortical neuromodulation
- *Additional terms:* go/no-go task, motor cortex, dorsomedial prefrontal cortex (dmPFC), functional modularity, reinforcement surprise, serial response bias, GRAB_NE sensor, ArchT, two-photon micro-endoscopy

#### Core Themes
- LC-NA activity encodes two concurrent functions during a go/no-go auditory detection task: (1) pre-movement signals primarily targeting motor cortex for task execution, and (2) post-reinforcement signals (especially after punishment) broadly distributed across cortex encoding reinforcement surprise
- LC-NA neurons form distinct functional clusters: press-related, reward-related, and punishment-related
- Cortical outputs show functional modularity: LC axons to motor cortex carry more press-related signals; axons to dmPFC carry more punishment-related signals
- Post-reinforcement NA signals promote serial response bias to improve accuracy on subsequent trials
- LC-NA photoinhibition (ArchT) impairs behavioral performance, confirming causal role
- Challenges the view of LC-NA as a purely diffuse, global arousal system — demonstrates target-specific, function-specific neuromodulation

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that noradrenergic neuromodulation is not a single broadcast signal but a structured, target-specific system that simultaneously serves execution and optimization functions. Central to the book's argument that mental phenotypes emerge from precisely organized neuromodulatory architectures.

### 225. Briley & Tucker-Drob (2014)

**Citation:** Briley, D. A., & Tucker-Drob, E. M. (2014). Genetic and environmental continuity in personality development: A meta-analysis. *Psychological Bulletin*, *140*(5), 1303–1331. https://doi.org/10.1037/a0037091

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis, Twin/Genetic
**Instruments:** Diverse (NEO-PI-R, NEO-FFI, EPQ, MPQ, HiPIC, EAS, CBQ, BIS/BAS, others)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality development, heritability, environmental influences, stability, meta-analysis, behavioral genetics
- *Additional terms:* phenotypic stability, genetic stability, environmental stability, connected-linear spline model, exponential model, Big Five, sibling pairs, MZ/DZ twins, age trends

#### Results Summary

**Heritability and environmentality across the lifespan:**

| Age period | Heritability | Shared environment | Nonshared environment |
|---|---|---|---|
| Infancy (~1 yr) | ~.70 | ~.10 | ~.20 |
| Childhood (~7 yr) | ~.50 | ~.10 | ~.40 |
| Adulthood (30+ yr) | ~.40 | ~.05 | ~.55 |

**Stability estimates (genetic and environmental contributions):**

| Component | Asymptotic stability | Age at asymptote |
|---|---|---|
| Genetic stability | ~1.00 | ~30 years |
| Environmental stability | ~.33 | Continues rising through adulthood |
| Phenotypic stability (total) | ~.71 | Continues rising through adulthood |

**Key meta-analytic findings (k = 24 longitudinal studies, 21,057 sibling pairs):**
- Genetic contributions to phenotypic stability remain relatively constant across the lifespan (~0.38 asymptote)
- Environmental contributions to phenotypic stability grow from near zero in infancy to ~0.33 in adulthood
- The life-span increase in phenotypic stability is therefore driven primarily by increasing environmental contributions, not genetic
- Genetic stability reaches unity by age 30 — the same genes influence personality throughout adulthood
- Big Five moderation was statistically significant but practically trivial

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
A cornerstone meta-analysis for the book's argument about the developmental construction of personality: genes set the stage early but environmental embedding is what progressively stabilizes personality across the lifespan. The finding that genetic stability reaches unity by 30 while environmental contributions keep growing is a key narrative element.

---

### 226. Caradonna et al. (2022)

**Citation:** Caradonna, S. G., Paul, M. R., & Marrocco, J. (2022). An allostatic epigenetic memory on chromatin footprints after double-hit acute stress. *Neurobiology of Stress*, *20*, 100475. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.ynstr.2022.100475

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* allostatic load, epigenetics, ATAC-seq, chromatin accessibility, stress, hippocampus, sex differences
- *Additional terms:* double-hit stress, glucocorticoid receptor, androgen receptor, mineralocorticoid receptor, ARE/GRE binding sites, ventral hippocampus, Fkbp5, Zbtb16, restraint stress, forced swim stress

#### Core Themes
- Double-hit acute stress produces a distinct epigenetic profile in ventral hippocampus that differs from either single stressor alone — evidence for an "allostatic epigenetic memory"
- ARE/GRE (androgen/glucocorticoid response element) binding sites were the most variable across stress conditions, with AR, GR, and MR motifs ranking in the top 3 most variable TF binding sites
- Males showed 7,317 differentially opened transcripts vs. 3,254 for females after double-hit stress
- Sex-specific patterns: females showed chromatin accessibility changes in AREs across all stress conditions; males' AREs changed only after FSS and double-hit stress; GRE changes after double-hit stress were unique to males
- Stress-related genes including *Fkbp5*, *Zbtb16*, and *Ddc* showed differential chromatin accessibility at ARE/GRE binding sites
- One week after acute restraint stress, males (but not females) showed reduced anxiety-like behavior — suggesting a potential stress inoculation effect

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates that stress history creates cumulative chromatin signatures that are not simply additive — relevant to allostatic load theory and the book's theme of how experience accumulates in biological substrates. The sex differences in epigenetic response to stress are particularly relevant.

### 227. Feldman & Huang-Pollock (2021)

**Citation:** Feldman, J. S., & Huang-Pollock, C. (2021). Slow drift rate predicts ADHD symptomology over and above executive dysfunction. *Child Neuropsychology*, *27*(6), 834–855. https://doi.org/10.1080/09297049.2021.1902490

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** ADHD-RS, WISC-IV WMI, NAVON task, diffusion model (fast-dm)
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* ADHD, drift rate, executive function, diffusion model, bifactor model
- *Additional terms:* evidence accumulation, decision boundary, non-decision time, working memory, set shifting, inhibition, structural equation modeling, latent variables

#### Results Summary

**Latent factor predictions of general ADHD factor (bifactor model):**

| Predictor | Standardized β (alone) | Standardized β (joint model) | p (joint) |
|---|---|---|---|
| Executive function | −0.331 (p = .019) | −0.090 | .642 |
| Drift rate | −0.487 (p = .001) | −0.441 | .030 |

- When both EF and drift rate were entered simultaneously, only drift rate continued to predict the general ADHD factor; EF became nonsignificant
- Drift rate captures variance in ADHD symptoms over and above traditional EF metrics
- Suggests drift rate (speed of evidence accumulation) is a more precise cognitive marker of ADHD than traditional EF composites

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Illustrates how computational modeling (diffusion model) can decompose cognitive performance into more precise parameters than traditional EF tasks, revealing that information processing efficiency — not just "executive control" — is central to ADHD. Relevant to the book's discussion of how computational approaches reveal finer-grained mental phenotypes.

### 228. Feng et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Feng, L., Ye, Z., Du, Z., Pan, Y., Canida, T., Ke, H., Liu, S., Chen, S., Hong, L. E., Kochunov, P., Chen, J., Lei, D. K. Y., Shenassa, E., & Ma, T. (2025). Association between allostatic load and accelerated white matter brain aging: Findings from the UK Biobank. *American Journal of Epidemiology*, *194*(8), 2376–2384. https://doi.org/10.1093/aje/kwae396

- *Additional terms:* fractional anisotropy, diffusion tensor imaging, CRP, waist-to-height ratio, HDL cholesterol, glycated hemoglobin, peak expiratory flow, inverse probability weighting, doubly robust estimation

#### Results Summary

**Association between allostatic load and white matter brain age gap:**

| Method | Effect per 1-unit AL increase | 95% CI | p |
|---|---|---|---|
| Linear regression | +0.29 years WM BAG | [0.25, 0.33] | < .001 |

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides large-scale epidemiological evidence that chronic physiological stress (allostatic load) accelerates brain aging at the white matter level, with causal inference support from Mendelian randomization. Connects the allostatic load framework to measurable neural substrate degradation.

### 229. Ghosh & Maunsell (2024)

**Citation:** Ghosh, S., & Maunsell, J. H. R. (2024). Locus coeruleus norepinephrine contributes to visual-spatial attention by selectively enhancing perceptual sensitivity. *Neuron*, *112*(14), 2231–2240. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.neuron.2024.04.001

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* locus coeruleus, norepinephrine, spatial attention, perceptual sensitivity, optogenetics, primate
- *Additional terms:* d-prime, decision criterion, signal detection theory, contralateral selectivity, channelrhodopsin-2, rhesus monkey, DBH promoter, orientation change detection

#### Core Themes
- LC-NE neurons selectively fire in response to visual stimuli appearing in the contralateral hemifield only when that stimulus is attended; modulation absent on error trials
- Unilateral optogenetic activation of LC-NE neurons selectively improved contralateral perceptual sensitivity (d') without affecting motor/decision criteria or ipsilateral performance
- First causal demonstration in primates that NE contributes to the spatial selectivity of attention, not just general arousal
- 229 and 185 LC neurons recorded in two monkeys; 11 optogenetic sessions per monkey
- Challenges the traditional view of LC-NE as a spatially diffuse system; demonstrates hemisphere-specific, granular cognitive control

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides the strongest causal evidence to date that norepinephrine contributes to spatially selective attention in primates — a key mechanistic building block for the book's argument about how neuromodulatory systems shape specific cognitive phenotypes rather than just global arousal states.

### 230. Gildawie et al. (2020)

**Citation:** Gildawie, K. R., Honeycutt, J. A., & Brenhouse, H. C. (2020). Region-specific effects of maternal separation on perineuronal net and parvalbumin-expressing interneuron formation in male and female rats. *Neuroscience*, *428*, 23–37. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.neuroscience.2019.12.010

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* maternal separation, perineuronal nets, parvalbumin interneurons, prefrontal cortex, amygdala, sex differences, development
- *Additional terms:* WFA, prelimbic cortex, infralimbic cortex, basolateral amygdala, critical period, GABAergic inhibition, PNN intensity, PV density

#### Core Themes
- Maternal separation (4 hours/day, P2–P20) produces region-specific, sex-dependent, and age-dependent effects on PNNs and PV interneurons
- In PFC: MS reduced PNN density in prelimbic cortex at juvenile stage specifically for PNNs surrounding PV neurons; MS increased PNN intensity around PV neurons in adult males
- In BLA: MS increased PNN and PV density in adolescent males; MS increased PNN intensity in females
- Normal developmental trajectory: PNN density increased through adolescence and plateaued; PV density and intensity peaked in adolescence and decreased in adulthood
- Effects appeared after adolescence and persisted into adulthood, suggesting MS disrupts the timing of critical period closure
- Region-specific pattern: PFC effects primarily reductive (less PNN protection), BLA effects primarily augmentative (more PNN/PV)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates that early adversity disrupts the developmental timing of inhibitory circuit maturation in a region-specific and sex-specific manner. The PFC vs. BLA dissociation is relevant to understanding how stress differentially affects cortical vs. subcortical circuitry during sensitive periods.

### 231. Grimm et al. (2024)

**Citation:** Grimm, C., Duss, S. N., Privitera, M., Munn, B. R., Karalis, N., Frässle, S., Wilhelm, M., Patriarchi, T., Razansky, D., Wenderoth, N., Shine, J. M., Bohacek, J., & Zerbi, V. (2024). Tonic and burst-like locus coeruleus stimulation distinctly shift network activity across the cortical hierarchy. *Nature Neuroscience*, *27*, 2167–2177. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41593-024-01755-8

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* locus coeruleus, noradrenaline, tonic firing, burst firing, optogenetic-fMRI, cortical hierarchy, BOLD signal
- *Additional terms:* GRAB_NE sensor, astrocytes, alpha-1A adrenergic receptor, participation coefficient, modularity, transmodal cortex, somatosensory cortex, hippocampus, GCaMP6s, network integration

#### Core Themes
- Tonic (3 Hz) and burst-like (15 Hz) LC stimulation produce qualitatively different patterns of cortical activation, not just quantitative differences
- 3-Hz tonic stimulation preferentially activates transmodal/associative cortical regions (mPFC, higher cortex)
- 15-Hz burst-like stimulation preferentially recruits somatosensory areas and hippocampus
- The activation gradient closely corresponds to the hierarchical functional organization of cortical areas (Spearman rho = 0.72)
- LC stimulation evokes biphasic BOLD responses: initial neuronal inhibition followed by progressive increase driven by astrocytic calcium signaling
- Effects primarily mediated by alpha-1A adrenergic receptors
- 5-Hz tonic stimulation uniformly increases cortical network integration; burst-like stimulation selectively engages lower-hierarchy regions
- Demonstrates that LC firing mode dynamically shapes brain network topology

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides the mechanistic basis for how a single neuromodulatory nucleus can serve multiple cognitive functions by varying its firing pattern — tonic mode for top-down/associative processing, burst mode for sensory/bottom-up processing. Central to understanding how neuromodulatory gain control shapes mental phenotypes across the cortical hierarchy.

### 232. Gustavson et al. (2023)

**Citation:** Gustavson, D. E., Morrison, C. L., Mallard, T. T., Jennings, M. V., Fontanillas, P., Elson, S. L., Palmer, A. A., Friedman, N. P., & Sanchez-Roige, S. (2023). Executive function and impulsivity predict distinct genetic variance in internalizing problems, externalizing problems, thought disorders, and compulsive disorders. *Clinical Psychological Science*, *XX*(X), 1–17. https://doi.org/10.1177/21677026231207845

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Mega-Analysis, Twin/Genetic
**Instruments:** UPPS-P, BIS, UK Biobank cognitive tasks
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* executive function, impulsivity, psychopathology, genomic SEM, genetic correlations, GWAS
- *Additional terms:* UPPS-P, urgency, common EF, p-factor, internalizing, externalizing, thought disorders, compulsive disorders, educational attainment, BMI, chronotype, insomnia

#### Results Summary

**Genetic correlations between EF, impulsivity, and psychopathology factors:**

| Predictor | Internalizing | Externalizing | Thought Disorders | Compulsive Disorders |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Common EF | −.46** | −.61** | −.36** | −.25* |
| Common impulsivity | +.39** | +.56** | +.18 | −.38** |
| Urgency-specific | +.35** | +.13 | +.31** | +.03 |

- EF and impulsivity are only weakly genetically correlated (rg = .13), confirming genetic separability
- Low EF was genetically associated with all four psychopathology factors
- Common impulsivity was negatively associated with compulsive disorders (opposite sign from EF)
- Urgency-specific impulsivity uniquely associated with internalizing and thought disorders
- BMI predicted by impulsivity only, not EF; insomnia predicted by both

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that executive function and impulsivity, despite their phenotypic overlap, represent genetically separable dimensions with distinct psychiatric risk profiles. The urgency-specific finding is particularly relevant — it suggests that emotional impulsivity has a different genetic architecture from general impulsivity, with distinct consequences for psychopathology.

### 233. Haarsma et al. (2023)

**Citation:** Haarsma, J., Deveci, N., Corbin, N., Callaghan, M. F., & Kok, P. (2023). Expectation cues and false percepts generate stimulus-specific activity in distinct layers of the early visual cortex. *The Journal of Neuroscience*, *43*(47), 7946–7957. https://doi.org/10.1523/JNEUROSCI.0998-23.2023

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** CAPS (Cardiff Anomalous Perceptions Scale), PID (Peters et al. Delusions Inventory)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* predictive coding, false percepts, hallucinations, laminar fMRI, visual cortex, expectations
- *Additional terms:* 7T MRI, deep layers, superficial layers, middle layers, V1, V2, feedback signals, feedforward signals, orientation-specific templates, equivolume cortical surface

#### Core Themes
- Perceptual expectations induced orientation-specific templates in deep and superficial layers of V2, consistent with top-down feedback signals
- High-confidence false percepts (seeing oriented gratings in noise-only displays) were reflected by orientation-specific activity in middle (input) layers of V2, consistent with feedforward-like signals
- Low-confidence false percepts did not show this middle-layer activation pattern
- False percept prevalence correlated with everyday hallucination severity (CAPS scores; Rho = .22, p = .029)
- Findings challenge pure top-down accounts of hallucinations: false percepts can arise from spontaneous feedforward-like fluctuations in input layers, independent of expectation signals
- Distinct laminar profiles for expectations vs. false percepts suggest separable neural mechanisms for top-down prediction and bottom-up perceptual error

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides neural-layer-level evidence for the predictive coding framework, showing that expectations and false percepts operate through distinct cortical pathways. Relevant to the book's discussion of how the brain constructs perception through hierarchical prediction, and how this process can go awry.

### 234. Hatoum et al. (2023)

**Citation:** Hatoum, A. S., Morrison, C. L., Mitchell, E. C., Lam, M., Benca-Bachman, C. E., Reineberg, A. E., Palmer, R. H. C., Evans, L. M., Keller, M. C., & Friedman, N. P. (2023). Genome-wide association study shows that executive functioning is influenced by GABAergic processes and is a neurocognitive genetic correlate of psychiatric disorders. *Biological Psychiatry*, *93*(1), 59–70. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.biopsych.2022.06.034

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Mega-Analysis, Twin/Genetic
**Instruments:** UK Biobank cognitive tasks (Trail Making, Symbol Digit, Backward Digit Span, Prospective Memory, Pairs Matching)
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* GWAS, executive function, GABAergic, psychiatric disorders, genetic correlations, UK Biobank
- *Additional terms:* common EF, MAGMA, gene-set enrichment, partitioned heritability, LD score regression, polygenic scores, GenomicSEM, synaptic transmission, potassium channels, GABA receptors

#### Results Summary

**GWAS of common executive function (N = 427,037):**

| Metric | Value |
|---|---|
| Genome-wide significant lead SNPs | 129 (in 112 loci) |
| SNP heritability (BOLT-REML) | 0.104 |
| SNP heritability (LDSC) | 0.091 |
| Genetic correlation with IQ | 0.743 (significantly < 1.0) |

**Gene-set enrichment — top pathways:**
- Synaptic transmission
- Potassium channel activity
- GABA receptor activity
- Little evidence for dopaminergic pathways

**Genetic correlations with psychopathology (controlling for IQ and speed):**

| Phenotype | cEF (controlling IQ, speed) | IQ (controlling cEF, speed) |
|---|---|---|
| Internalizing factor | Significant negative | Not significant |
| Thought/compulsive factor | Significant negative | Not significant |
| Educational attainment | Not significant | Significant positive |

- cEF PGS predicted cEF latent variable in independent twin samples (partial r = .136) but not IQ-specific or shifting-specific variables
- cEF and IQ share substantial genetic overlap but have distinct genetic correlates with psychiatric phenotypes

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The largest GWAS of executive function reveals that EF is genetically linked to GABAergic inhibitory signaling — not dopamine, contrary to decades of theoretical emphasis. The genetic separability of EF from IQ, and their divergent psychiatric associations, provides a key mechanistic dissociation for the book's architecture of mental phenotypes.

### 235. Horchar & Wohleb (2019)

**Citation:** Horchar, M. J., & Wohleb, E. S. (2019). Glucocorticoid receptor antagonism prevents microglia-mediated neuronal remodeling and behavioral despair following chronic unpredictable stress. *Brain, Behavior, and Immunity*, *81*, 329–340. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.bbi.2019.06.030

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* glucocorticoid receptor, microglia, chronic stress, neuronal remodeling, dendritic spines, prefrontal cortex, depression
- *Additional terms:* RU486/mifepristone, CSF1, complement (C1q, C3), CUS paradigm, forced swim test, Thy1-GFP, layer V pyramidal neurons, microglial engulfment, CX3CR1, MerTK

#### Core Themes
- 14-day chronic unpredictable stress (CUS) causes behavioral despair (FST), anxiety (NSFT), and cognitive impairment (temporal object recognition) — all prevented by concurrent GR antagonism (RU486)
- CUS increases CSF1 mRNA and protein in PFC neurons; RU486 blocks this upregulation
- CUS increases complement components (C1q, C3) in PFC, consistent with enhanced microglial phagocytic signaling
- CUS increases microglia with neuronal inclusions (GFP+) in medial PFC and microglial engulfment volume; RU486 attenuates both
- CUS reduces dendritic spine density on apical dendrites of layer V pyramidal neurons in mPFC; RU486 prevents this synaptic loss
- Mechanistic chain: chronic stress → GR activation → neuronal CSF1 upregulation → enhanced microglial phagocytosis → dendritic spine loss → behavioral deficits

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides a clear mechanistic chain linking chronic stress, glucocorticoid signaling, neuronal-microglial communication, and synaptic remodeling in PFC. The CSF1-complement pathway represents a concrete biological mechanism for how stress restructures cortical circuitry — directly relevant to the book's theme of experience-dependent neural remodeling.

### 236. Karr et al. (2023)

**Citation:** Karr, J. E., Rodriguez, J. E., Rast, P., Goh, P. K., & Martel, M. M. (2023). A network analysis of executive functions in children and adolescents with and without attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder. *Child Psychiatry & Human Development*, *55*, 1600–1610. https://doi.org/10.1007/s10578-023-01518-9

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** NIH Toolbox Cognition Battery (Flanker, List Sorting, DCCS)
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* executive function, ADHD, network analysis, Bayesian Gaussian graphical model, children, adolescents
- *Additional terms:* global strength, centrality, expected influence, inhibition, working memory, shifting, delayed maturation, differentiation

#### Results Summary

**Network parameters (ADHD vs. typically developing):**

| Parameter | ADHD | TD | Difference |
|---|---|---|---|
| Mean EF performance (d range) | — | — | 0.05–0.11 (ns) |
| Global strength (zero-order) | Lower | Higher | GS_diff = −0.20* |
| Global strength (partial) | Lower | Higher | GS_diff = −0.13* |
| Shifting-inhibition edge (both networks) | Weaker | Stronger | Significant |
| Most central node | Inhibition | Shifting | Qualitative difference |

- Despite equivalent mean performance, ADHD children show greater EF network differentiation (less integration)
- In ADHD, inhibition mediates the relationship between shifting and working memory; in TD children, shifting plays this bridging role
- Consistent with delayed maturation hypothesis: ADHD EF network resembles a less mature, less specialized configuration

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Illustrates that mean-level performance can mask structural differences in how cognitive abilities relate to one another — relevant to the book's emphasis on structure and architecture of mental phenotypes, not just levels. The network analysis approach reveals phenotypic organization invisible to traditional measurement.

### 237. Lam et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Lam, N. H., Mukherjee, A., Wimmer, R. D., Nassar, M. R., Chen, Z. S., & Halassa, M. M. (2025). Prefrontal transthalamic uncertainty processing drives flexible switching. *Nature*, *637*(8044), 127–136. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-024-08180-8

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* mediodorsal thalamus, prefrontal cortex, anterior cingulate cortex, cognitive flexibility, uncertainty, rule switching
- *Additional terms:* tree shrew, transthalamic pathway, ACC→MD→PFC, cueing uncertainty, rule uncertainty, demixed PCA, communication subspace, SSFO, optogenetics, hierarchical decision-making

#### Core Themes
- MD thalamus independently represents two types of uncertainty: cueing uncertainty (noisy sensory cues) and rule uncertainty (covert rule reversals) in distinct neural populations
- MD_Context neurons encode rule uncertainty and drive PFC rule reconfiguration following reversals
- A transthalamic pathway mediates cognitive flexibility: ACC error monitoring → MD → PFC reconfiguration
- Optogenetic suppression of ACC→MD afferents delayed behavioral rule switching; direct ACC→PFC suppression had no impact
- Optogenetic MD activation (SSFO) was sufficient to trigger PFC reconfiguration and enhance switching
- The thalamus "demixes" cortical signals, providing a low-dimensional route for cortico-cortical communication
- Uses tree shrews (more elaborated prefrontal cortex than mice) for ecological validity

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that cognitive flexibility depends on a specific transthalamic pathway, not direct cortico-cortical communication. The thalamus acts as an information-processing hub that simplifies and routes cortical signals — challenging the view of thalamus as merely a relay. Central to understanding the neural architecture of adaptive behavior.

---

### 238. Laszlovszky et al. (2020)

**Citation:** Laszlovszky, T., Schlingloff, D., Hegedüs, P., Freund, T. F., Gulyás, A., Kepecs, A., & Hangya, B. (2020). Distinct synchronization, cortical coupling and behavioral function of two basal forebrain cholinergic neuron types. *Nature Neuroscience*, *23*(8), 992–1003. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41593-020-0648-0

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* basal forebrain, cholinergic neurons, acetylcholine, attention, cortical coupling, synchronization
- *Additional terms:* BFCN_BURST, BFCN_REG, nucleus basalis, horizontal diagonal band, theta rhythm, optogenetic tagging, ChAT-Cre, auditory cortex, reward, punishment, burst index, auto-correlogram

#### Core Themes
- Two distinct types of basal forebrain cholinergic neurons identified both in vivo and in vitro:
  - BFCN_BURST: bursting neurons that fire synchronously with each other, phase-lock to cortical theta, and fire precisely timed bursts after reward and punishment (salience signals)
  - BFCN_REG: regular-firing neurons predominantly in nucleus basalis, with strong intrinsic theta rhythmicity
- BFCN_BURST synchronization with auditory cortex predicted mouse response timing (speed) but not accuracy
- BFCN_REG cortical coupling specifically predicted correct detections (accuracy)
- Two types differentially distributed: BFCN_REG constitutes ~50% in nucleus basalis but only ~10% in horizontal diagonal band
- Demonstrates that cholinergic modulation of attention is not monolithic but involves parallel channels with distinct functional roles

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Reveals that the cholinergic system — like the noradrenergic system — contains functional subtypes with distinct cognitive roles. The burst/regular distinction maps onto speed vs. accuracy trade-offs in attention, providing a cellular-level mechanism for a well-known behavioral phenomenon. Key for the book's argument about neuromodulatory specificity.

---

### 239. Li et al. (2022)

**Citation:** Li, H., Namburi, P., Olson, J. M., Borio, M., Lemieux, M. E., Beyeler, A., ... Tye, K. M. (2022). Neurotensin orchestrates valence assignment in the amygdala. *Nature*. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-022-04964-y

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* neurotensin, amygdala, valence, associative learning, paraventricular thalamus, neuropeptide
- *Additional terms:* BLA, PVT, CRISPR-Cas9, Nts knockout, GRAB_NTS1.0, reward learning, punishment learning, STDP, SVM decoding, neural trajectory, dose-dependent gain

#### Core Themes
- Neurotensin (NT) released from paraventricular thalamus (PVT) projections to BLA mediates valence assignment during associative learning
- Projection-specific CRISPR knockout of *Nts* in PVT-BLA neurons impaired reward learning (tone-sucrose) and enhanced punishment learning (tone-shock)
- NT concentration in BLA increases after reward learning and decreases after punishment learning — concentration encodes valence direction
- *Nts* knockout blunted BLA neural dynamics and attenuated preference for active behavioral strategies
- NT acts as a dose-dependent neuromodulatory gain signal that extends synaptic plasticity windows to behaviorally relevant timescales
- 685 BLA neurons recorded from 30 mice; CRISPR + optogenetics + fiber photometry + electrophysiology + computational analyses

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that a single neuropeptide (neurotensin) can bidirectionally control whether an experience is encoded as rewarding or aversive in the amygdala. Provides a molecular mechanism for valence assignment — a fundamental building block of affective phenotypes. Relevant to the book's discussion of how emotional meaning is constructed.

### 240. Mederos et al. (2021)

**Citation:** Mederos, S., Sánchez-Puelles, C., Esparza, J., Valero, M., Ponomarenko, A., & Perea, G. (2021). GABAergic signaling to astrocytes in the prefrontal cortex sustains goal-directed behaviors. *Nature Neuroscience*, *24*(1), 82–92. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41593-020-00752-x

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* astrocytes, GABA_B receptors, prefrontal cortex, gamma oscillations, decision-making, working memory
- *Additional terms:* PV interneurons, theta-gamma coupling, melanopsin, mGluR1, inhibitory postsynaptic currents, T-maze, object-in-place, astrocyte-neuron tripartite synapse, gliotransmission

#### Core Themes
- Genetic ablation of GABA_B receptors specifically in mPFC astrocytes impaired decision-making and working memory (T-maze alternation and object-in-place tasks)
- Loss of astrocytic GABA_B signaling reduced low-gamma oscillation power (30–60 Hz) and disrupted theta-gamma phase-amplitude coupling
- Mechanism: PV interneuron-driven GABA_B activation in astrocytes triggers glutamate release and mGluR1 activation, which potentiates inhibitory postsynaptic currents (IPSCs) onto pyramidal cells
- This creates a positive feedback loop: inhibition → astrocyte activation → enhanced inhibition
- Selective optogenetic stimulation of mPFC astrocytes with melanopsin rescued cognitive deficits in both mutant and wild-type mice
- Direct stimulation of PV neurons alone did not rescue the deficits — astrocytes are the essential intermediary
- Demonstrates that astrocytes are not passive support cells but active computational elements required for prefrontal circuit function

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Challenges neuron-centric models of cognition by demonstrating that astrocytes are required for prefrontal gamma oscillations and goal-directed behavior. Expands the "hardware" of mental phenotypes beyond neurons to include glial cells as active computational components — a key conceptual advance for the book.

---

### 241. Mukherjee et al. (2021)

**Citation:** Mukherjee, A., Lam, N. H., Wimmer, R. D., & Halassa, M. M. (2021). Thalamic circuits for independent control of prefrontal signal and noise. *Nature*, *600*(7887), 100–104. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-021-04056-3

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* mediodorsal thalamus, prefrontal cortex, signal-to-noise ratio, VIP interneurons, PV interneurons, attention
- *Additional terms:* D2 receptor, GRIK4, mGRASP, optogenetics, SSFO, iC++, 2AFC task, noise suppression, signal amplification, schizophrenia, mean-field model

#### Core Themes
- MD thalamus contains two genetically distinct cell populations that independently control PFC signal and noise:
  - MD_D2 neurons (D2 receptor-expressing): target VIP+ interneurons in prelimbic cortex → amplify functional PFC connectivity (enhance signal)
  - MD_GRIK4 neurons (GRIK4-expressing): target PV+ interneurons in prelimbic cortex → suppress PL activity (reduce noise)
- These two effects are mechanistically independent and uncorrelated in their neural activity
- MD_GRIK4 neurons are conflict-preferring: required when task inputs are dense but conflicting (high noise)
- MD_D2 neurons are required when inputs are sparse (low signal)
- A neural mean-field model incorporating both cell types reproduced experimental data
- 94 mice total; 2,669 neurons functionally classified; multiple Cre lines for cell-type-specific manipulation

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides a mechanistic account of how the thalamus solves the signal-to-noise problem in prefrontal cortex through two independent channels. This dual-control architecture is directly relevant to understanding attention deficits in schizophrenia and ADHD, and to the book's broader argument about how precision mechanisms shape cognitive phenotypes.

### 242. Rhoads et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Rhoads, S. A., Gan, L., Berluti, K., O'Connell, K., Cutler, J., Lockwood, P. L., & Marsh, A. A. (2025). Neurocomputational basis of learning when choices simultaneously affect both oneself and others. *Nature Communications*, *16*, 9350. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-025-64424-9

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** TriPM (Triarchic Psychopathy Measure), QCAE, SVO
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* prosocial learning, reinforcement learning, computational psychiatry, psychopathy, fMRI, prediction error
- *Additional terms:* value integration, value simulation, self-other, asymmetric learning rates, meanness, ventral striatum, anterior insula, subgenual ACC, amygdala, mPFC

#### Results Summary

**Computational model comparison (N = 220):**
- Value integration model strongly preferred over value simulation (integrated BIC, pseudo-R², protected exceedance probability all favoring integration)
- People combine self- and other-relevant outcomes into a single expected value per choice

**Asymmetric learning rates:**

| Prediction error type | Mean learning rate | Self-other difference |
|---|---|---|
| Self-relevant positive | Highest | Self > Other |
| Self-relevant negative | Moderate | Self > Other |
| Other-relevant positive | Lower | — |
| Other-relevant negative | Lowest | — |

**Individual differences — psychopathy (meanness subfactor):**
- Higher meanness → decreased sensitivity to other-relevant prediction errors (both positive and negative)
- Effect specific to meanness, not boldness or disinhibition

**fMRI (N = 27):**
- mPFC, posterolateral temporal cortex, PCC: expected value encoding
- Ventral striatum, pregenual ACC: positive value updating
- Anterior insula: self-relevant negative updating
- Subgenual ACC: other-relevant prediction errors
- Amygdala: self-relevant positive prediction errors

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides a neurocomputational framework for how the brain integrates self- and other-relevant information during learning — a fundamental building block of prosocial cognition. The psychopathy finding reveals a specific computational deficit (reduced other-relevant learning) rather than a general deficit, illustrating how individual differences in social phenotypes can be precisely characterized.

### 243. Santiago et al. (2018)

**Citation:** Santiago, A. N., Lim, K. Y., Opendak, M., Sullivan, R. M., & Aoki, C. (2018). Early life trauma increases threat response of peri-weaning rats, reduction of axo-somatic synapses formed by parvalbumin cells and perineuronal net in the basolateral nucleus of amygdala. *Journal of Comparative Neurology*, *526*(16), 2647–2664. https://doi.org/10.1002/cne.24522

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* early life trauma, perineuronal nets, parvalbumin interneurons, basolateral amygdala, threat response, weaning
- *Additional terms:* scarcity-adversity model (SAM), axo-somatic synapses, electron microscopy, PV-pyramidal contacts, PV-PV contacts, chondroitinase ABC, critical period, predator odor

#### Core Themes
- Scarcity-adversity model (SAM: insufficient bedding PN8–12) increased threat response at weaning (PN23), recapitulated by enzymatic PNN dissolution in controls
- PV density in anterior-to-mid BLA negatively correlated with threat response in controls (R = −0.87); this relationship abolished in SAM animals
- SAM decreased PNN intensity in anterior-to-mid BLA at PN23
- Electron microscopy: SAM reduced PV-to-pyramidal axo-somatic contacts by 27% and PV-to-PV contacts by 54%
- PV and PNN density increased 4- to 10-fold during pre-weaning period (PN12–PN23) in controls, establishing the maturation timeline that SAM disrupts
- SAM delayed PV density maturation in anterior-to-mid BLA
- The greater loss of PV-to-PV contacts (54%) than PV-to-pyramidal contacts (27%) suggests disinhibition of PV networks as a key mechanism

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides ultrastructural evidence (electron microscopy) for how early adversity disrupts inhibitory circuit architecture in the amygdala at the synaptic level. The PV-to-PV disinhibition mechanism is particularly interesting — it suggests that early stress doesn't just reduce inhibition but destabilizes the inhibitory network's self-regulation.

Association between use of systemic and inhaled glucocorticoids and changes in brain volume and white matter microstructure: A cross-sectional study using data from the UK Biobank. *BMJ Open*, *12*, e062446. https://doi.org/10.1136/bmjopen-2022-062446

### 245. Warrier et al. (2019)

**Citation:** Warrier, V., Toro, R., Won, H., Leblond, C. S., Cliquet, F., Delorme, R., ... Baron-Cohen, S. (2019). Social and non-social autism symptoms and trait domains are genetically dissociable. *Communications Biology*, *2*, 328. https://doi.org/10.1038/s42003-019-0558-4

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Mega-Analysis, Twin/Genetic
**Instruments:** SQ-R (Systemizing Quotient–Revised), RBS-R, ADOS-G, SCDC, AQ, EQ

#### Results Summary

**Genetic architecture of systemising (SQ-R GWAS, N = 51,564):**

| Metric | Value |
|---|---|

|---|---|

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that the two core domains of autism — social difficulties and non-social/systemizing traits — are genetically dissociable. This is a powerful example of the book's argument that psychiatric categories often bundle genetically distinct dimensions, and that understanding mental phenotypes requires dissecting them at the genetic level.

### 246. Zhang et al. (2024)

**Citation:** Zhang, M., Wu, Y. E., Jiang, M., & Hong, W. (2024). Cortical regulation of helping behaviour towards others in pain. *Nature*. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-023-06973-x

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* prosocial behavior, allolicking, empathy, anterior cingulate cortex, pain, helping behavior
- *Additional terms:* ACC, calcium imaging, chemogenetics, optogenetics, hM4Di, ChR2, self-pain, other-pain, allogrooming, melittin, formalin, SVM decoding, k-means clustering

#### Core Themes
- Mice exhibit targeted "allolicking" (social licking) specifically directed at the injury site of conspecifics experiencing pain — a form of prosocial helping behavior distinct from general allogrooming
- Allolicking reduces demonstrators' self-licking, suggesting functional pain relief
- ACC neurons encode distinct representations of others' pain vs. stress states, with largely non-overlapping populations
- Self-pain and other-pain recruit partially overlapping but largely distinct ACC neuronal populations; cross-decoding between self and other pain is possible at the population level
- Allolicking and allogrooming activate largely separate ACC populations (~14–17% overlap) with distinct population codes
- Chemogenetic inhibition (hM4Di) of ACC decreased both allolicking and allogrooming toward demonstrators in pain
- Optogenetic activation (ChR2) of ACC increased the probability of both behaviors, with allolicking specifically directed at the injured paw
- ACC bidirectionally and causally regulates targeted helping behavior — not just perception of others' states

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides causal evidence that the ACC regulates targeted prosocial helping behavior in a mammalian model. The finding that ACC represents self-pain and other-pain through partially overlapping but distinct populations is relevant to understanding the neural basis of empathy — a key social phenotype. The specificity of allolicking (targeted to injury site) demonstrates cognitively sophisticated prosocial behavior in rodents.

### 247. Bartels et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Bartels, M., Pelt, D. H. M., Zilhao, N. R., van Dongen, J., Boomsma, D. I., et al. (2025). Epigenome-wide association study of wellbeing. *bioRxiv* preprint. https://doi.org/10.1101/2025.09.08.674802

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Twin/Genetic, Meta-Analysis (EWAS)
**Instruments:** Satisfaction with Life Scale (SWLS), PANAS, DNA methylation arrays (Illumina 450K/EPIC)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* wellbeing, epigenome-wide association study, DNA methylation, CpG sites, MZ twins, life satisfaction
- *Additional terms:* EWAS meta-analysis, polygenic risk scores, MZ discordant twins, SWLS, Infinium HumanMethylation450, EPIC array

#### Core Themes
- First large-scale EWAS meta-analysis of wellbeing (satisfaction with life / positive affect) across 14 cohorts (N ≈ 10,750)
- In Model 2 (controlling for BMI and smoking), 10 CpG sites reached suggestive significance (p < 3.6 × 10⁻⁵), annotated to genes including ABHD8, FOXM1/C12orf32, RAB3IL1, SYNJ2, CDC42SE2, WDR33, and NOXA1
- No CpG sites reached epigenome-wide significance after Bonferroni correction (p < 1.09 × 10⁻⁷)
- MZ twin pairs discordant for wellbeing (mean difference ~19.6 points on SWLS) showed larger within-pair DNA methylation differences at the top 10 EWAS CpG sites vs. concordant pairs
- Inflation factor (lambda) = 0.99, indicating good confound control
- Epigenetic signatures of wellbeing, like genetic signatures, are distributed across many loci with very small individual effects

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Relevant to understanding biological embedding of psychological wellbeing, illustrating epigenetic methods for studying mental phenotypes. Demonstrates that wellbeing has small, distributed epigenetic effects paralleling its polygenic architecture.

### 248. Beauchaine & Tackett (2020)

**Citation:** Beauchaine, T. P., & Tackett, J. L. (2020). Irritability as a transdiagnostic vulnerability trait: Current issues and future directions. *Behavior Therapy*, *51*(2), 350–364.

**Purpose:** Commentary/Editorial
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* irritability, transdiagnostic, vulnerability, neuroticism, negative emotionality, psychopathology
- *Additional terms:* HiTOP, RDoC, mesolimbic DA, dopaminergic reward, amygdala reactivity, Bayesian modeling, hierarchical dimensions, delay discounting

#### Core Themes
- Irritability is a dispositional trait (impatience, hostility, annoyance, anger, aggression) present from early life and associated with diverse psychopathology
- Represents a transdiagnostic vulnerability operationalized as a high threshold on Neuroticism/Negative Emotionality
- Neurobiological underpinnings: dopaminergic reward system dysfunction (mesolimbic DA), striatal responding, and amygdala reactivity
- Proposes that therapeutic agonism of midbrain DA responding (bupropion, methylphenidate) may reduce irritability and improve hedonic capacity
- Advocates Bayesian approaches to modeling functional dependencies between behavior (impulsivity, anxiety) and neural substrates
- Argues for transdiagnostic, hierarchical models integrating personality traits and psychopathology dimensions

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly addresses how a specific personality facet (irritability/NE) functions as a transdiagnostic construct linking personality structure, neurobiology, development, and psychopathology.

### 249. Conway et al. (2019)

**Citation:** Conway, C. C., Forbes, M. K., Forbush, K. T., Fried, E. I., Hallquist, M. N., Kotov, R., ... Eaton, N. R. (2019). A hierarchical taxonomy of psychopathology can transform mental health research. *Perspectives on Psychological Science*, *14*(3), 419–436. https://doi.org/10.1177/1745691618810696

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* HiTOP, psychopathology, dimensional classification, comorbidity, p-factor, transdiagnostic
- *Additional terms:* internalizing, externalizing, thought disorder, detachment, somatoform, bifactor model, NESARC, genetic liability

#### Core Themes
- 36-author HiTOP consortium paper making the comprehensive case for dimensional classification
- Categorical DSM/ICD diagnoses have pervasive problems: comorbidity, low symptom specificity, diagnostic heterogeneity, poor reliability (~40% of DSM-5 diagnoses failed inter-rater thresholds)
- HiTOP is an empirically derived, hierarchical dimensional system from p-factor through spectra (internalizing, externalizing disinhibited/antagonistic, thought disorder, detachment, somatoform) to symptom components
- Quantitative genetics supports HiTOP: general genetic liability factor, SNP-based heritability of p-factor ≈ 38%
- Neurobiology converges: amygdala reactivity, salience network dysfunction, reduced P3/ERN are transdiagnostic and align better with dimensions than categories
- Environmental risk (childhood maltreatment, racial discrimination) operates through spectra rather than specific diagnoses
- Dimensional measures far outperform categorical: distress subfactor explains ~34% of suicide attempt variance vs. ~1% for top DSM diagnoses; dimensions explain 68% of impairment variance in eating disorders vs. 11% for diagnoses

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Core framework paper for understanding how mental phenotypes are structured hierarchically; directly relevant to the relationship between personality dimensions and psychopathology taxonomy.

### 250. Deary, Penke, & Johnson (2010)

**Citation:** Deary, I. J., Penke, L., & Johnson, W. (2010). The neuroscience of human intelligence differences. *Nature Reviews Neuroscience*, *11*, 201–211. https://doi.org/10.1038/nrn2793

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** g, Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* intelligence, individual differences, g factor, brain imaging, heritability, neuroscience
- *Additional terms:* P-FIT, neural efficiency, cortical thickness, DTI, white matter integrity, processing speed, Scottish Mental Survey

#### Results Summary

**Key associations:**
| Measure | Correlation with g |
|---|---|
| Brain volume (MRI) | r ≈ .30–.40 |
| Cortical gray matter | r ≈ .31 |
| White matter | r ≈ .27 |
| Head size | r ≈ .20 |

**Heritability across development:**
| Age | Approximate h² |
|---|---|
| 5 years | .26 |
| 7 years | .39 |
| 10 years | .54 |
| 12 years | .64 |
| Adulthood | .70–.80 |

#### Core Themes
- The g factor accounts for ~40%+ of variance in diverse cognitive batteries; replicated in hundreds of datasets over a century
- Rank-order stability r = 0.63 from age 11 to 79 (0.73 corrected)
- IQ predicts education (r > 0.8), occupational attainment, health, mortality (1 SD advantage = 32% mortality reduction)
- Parieto-Frontal Integration Theory (P-FIT): intelligence as a distributed "small world" network across dorsolateral PFC, parietal, ACC, and temporal/occipital regions
- Neural efficiency hypothesis: more intelligent brains use fewer resources for moderate-difficulty tasks
- Molecular genetics (as of 2010): highly polygenic with no reliable single-gene associations; genetic architecture consistent with mutation-selection balance
- Sex differences in neuroanatomical correlates: males show fronto-parietal gray matter correlations; females show white matter and Broca's area correlations; similar overall intelligence achieved via different brain structures

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Comprehensive review of the biological bases of a core mental phenotype (g), directly addressing genetics, brain structure, and development — all central to "how mental phenotypes are made."

### 251. Dodds et al. (2014)

**Citation:** Dodds, R. M., Syddall, H. E., Cooper, R., Benzeval, M., Deary, I. J., et al. (2014). Grip strength across the life course: Normative data from twelve British studies. *PLoS ONE*, *9*(12), e113637. https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0113637

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Mega-Analysis
**Instruments:** Grip strength dynamometers (Jamar, Smedley, Nottingham, Takei)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* grip strength, normative data, life course, sarcopenia, frailty, aging
- *Additional terms:* centile curves, T-score, GAMLSS, LMS method, weak grip, dynamometry

#### Core Themes
- First study to produce normative grip strength centile data across the entire British life course (ages 4–90+)
- N = 60,803 observations from 49,964 participants across 12 British population studies
- Males peak median grip 51 kg (ages 29–39), females 31 kg (ages 26–42); by age 25 males 1.6× females
- Prevalence of weak grip (T-score ≤ −2.5) by age 80: 23.0% males, 26.6% females
- Centile curves robust to dynamometer type and measurement position (<10% differences)
- Clinically relevant for identifying sarcopenia and frailty analogous to bone density T-scores

**Relevance to Book Project:** LOW
Grip strength is a physical capability biomarker, not directly a mental phenotype. Tangentially relevant to aging/development and health.

### 252. Gill et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Gill, H., Badulescu, S., Di Vincenzo, J. D., Tabassum, A., McKenzie, A., Shah, H., ... Mansur, R. B. (2025). Metabolic factors modulate effort-based decision-making in major depressive disorder. *Journal of Affective Disorders*, *373*, 88–93. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jad.2024.12.090

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Experimental/Intervention, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** EEfRT, HAMD-17, MINI, Trail Making Test B, HOMA2
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* effort-based decision-making, metabolic syndrome, insulin resistance, depression, anhedonia, reward
- *Additional terms:* EEfRT, PCA, HDL, hyperglycemia, effort discounting, semaglutide

#### Core Themes
- N = 49 MDD adults (all overweight/obese; mean BMI 32.94); baseline data from adjunctive semaglutide RCT
- Four metabolic PCA components (75.23% variance): insulin resistance, HDL, hyperglycemia, cholesterol
- Insulin resistance (OR = 0.816, p < .001) and hyperglycemia (OR = 0.898, p = .021) predicted fewer hard-task choices
- Elevated HDL (OR = 1.165, p = .004) and cholesterol (OR = 1.184, p < .001) predicted more hard-task choices
- First study documenting blood lipid–effort behavior association in MDD
- Computational modeling: insulin resistance increased effort discounting (β = 0.275, p = .035)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Relevant to understanding how metabolic/physiological states shape cognitive-motivational phenotypes, connecting body and brain in depression.

### 253. Harden et al. (2020)

**Citation:** Harden, K. P., Engelhardt, L. E., Mann, F. D., Patterson, M. W., Grotzinger, A. D., Savicki, S. L., ... Tucker-Drob, E. M. (2020). Genetic associations between executive functions and a general factor of psychopathology. *Journal of the American Academy of Child & Adolescent Psychiatry*, *59*(6), 749–758. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jaac.2019.05.006

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Twin/Genetic, Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** WASI-II, 12-task EF battery, CBCL, Conner's 3, BFI Neuroticism
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, g, Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* executive function, p-factor, psychopathology, twins, genetics, cognitive ability
- *Additional terms:* Texas Twin Project, bifactor model, ACE decomposition, system integrity, watershed model, genetic correlation

#### Results Summary

**Heritability estimates:**
| Construct | h² |
|---|---|
| Executive function (latent) | .86–.92 |
| g (latent) | .86–.92 |
| p-factor (parent report) | .72 |
| p-factor (self report) | .49 |

**Genetic correlations (rA) with p-factor:**
| Construct | Parent-report p | Self-report p |
|---|---|---|
| EF | −.25** | −.38*** |
| g | −.20* | −.24** |

#### Core Themes
- N = 1,913 twins (1,007 pairs) from Texas Twin Project; racially diverse (58% non-Hispanic White)
- p-factor modeled using bifactor approach; 20–50% of variance in each symptom scale attributable to p
- Higher g associated with lower p (r = −.21 for both reporters)
- Higher EF associated with lower p (r = −.26 to −.29)
- Non-shared environmental correlations between EF and p also significant (rE = −.61 to −.63)
- No domain-specific EF factor predicted psychopathology above general EF
- Supports "system integrity" / "watershed" model of genetic architecture

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly addresses how genetic architecture connects cognitive abilities to psychopathology, central to understanding how mental phenotypes are constructed.

### 254. Howard et al. (2019)

**Citation:** Howard, D. M., Adams, M. J., Clarke, T.-K., Hafferty, J. D., Gibson, J., Shirali, M., ... McIntosh, A. M. (2019). Genome-wide meta-analysis of depression identifies 102 independent variants and highlights the importance of the prefrontal brain regions. *Nature Neuroscience*, *22*(3), 343–352. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41593-018-0326-7

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis (GWAS), Twin/Genetic, Mega-Analysis
**Instruments:** Various depression phenotypes (UK Biobank, 23andMe, PGC)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* depression, GWAS, genome-wide, meta-analysis, polygenic risk, heritability
- *Additional terms:* PRS, LDSC, Mendelian randomization, neuroticism, prefrontal cortex, DRD2, serotonin

#### Results Summary

**Key GWAS findings:**
| Metric | Value |
|---|---|
| Discovery N | 807,553 (246K cases, 561K controls) |
| Replication N | 1,306,354 (414K cases) |
| Genome-wide significant variants | 102 (87 replicated) |
| SNP h² (liability scale) | 0.089 (SE = 0.003) |
| Cross-cohort genetic correlations | rg = 0.85–0.87 |

**Selected genetic correlations:**
| Trait | rg with depression |
|---|---|
| Schizophrenia | 0.32 |
| Bipolar disorder | 0.33 |
| College completion | −0.19 |
| Coronary artery disease | 0.13 |

**Mendelian randomization:** Neuroticism → depression (β = 0.366, P = 2.63 × 10⁻²¹); depression → smoking (causal)

#### Core Themes
- Largest depression GWAS at time of publication
- Strong genetic correlations between three contributing cohorts despite different phenotype definitions
- Heritability enrichment in CNS, especially anterior cingulate, frontal cortex
- MAGMA identified 269 genes and 15 gene-sets (synapse, neuron, cognition pathways)
- Drug-gene interactions predominantly with psycholeptics and DRD2
- Notable absence of serotonergic genes among depression-associated loci

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational molecular genetic architecture paper for depression, directly relevant to understanding how mental phenotypes are genetically constructed.
