### 255. Krueger et al. (2021)

**Citation:** Krueger, R. F., Hobbs, K. A., Conway, C. C., Dick, D. M., Dretsch, M. N., Eaton, N. R., ... HiTOP Utility Workgroup. (2021). Validity and utility of Hierarchical Taxonomy of Psychopathology (HiTOP): II. Externalizing superspectrum. *World Psychiatry*, *20*(2), 171–193. https://doi.org/10.1002/wps.20844

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review, Systematic Review
**Instruments:** ESI, ESI-CAT, MMPI-2-RF, PAI, PID-5, NEO-PI-3, CAT-PD, CBCL, SDQ (discussed)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* HiTOP, externalizing, disinhibition, antagonism, psychopathology, dimensional
- *Additional terms:* ADHD, conduct disorder, substance use, P300, ERN, callous-unemotional, effortful control, temperament, OFC, VMPFC

#### Core Themes
- Externalizing superspectrum encompasses disinhibited externalizing (impulsivity, SUDs, ADHD, CD) and antagonistic externalizing (manipulativeness, callousness, aggression)
- Parallels normal personality: constraint/conscientiousness vs. agreeableness/antagonism
- Behavior genetics: ADHD h² = 60–80%, ODD 30–70%, SUDs ~50%; general externalizing factor ~80% heritable
- Molecular genetics: Externalizing Consortium identified ~600 significant loci from ~1.5M people
- Neurophysiology: reduced P300 amplitude and ERN as broad externalizing markers
- Neuroimaging: reduced amygdala volume (antagonistic/CU); OFC/VMPFC (antagonism); vlPFC (disinhibition)
- Temperament antecedents: low effortful control + high negative affectivity
- Dimensional measures show improved reliability, explanatory power, and clinical utility over DSM categories

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Comprehensive review of how a major psychopathology superspectrum is constructed from genetic, neural, developmental, and personality components.

### 256. Kuper et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Kuper, N., Juchem, C., Allemand, M., Kroencke, L., Olaru, G., Stieger, M., Wrzus, C., & Haehner, P. (2025). Leveraging personality dynamics for volitional personality change interventions. *PsyArXiv* preprint.

**Purpose:** Commentary/Editorial
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality dynamics, personality change, interventions, experience sampling, JITAI
- *Additional terms:* state variability, state-state associations, situational contingencies, inertia, attractors, DSEM, idiographic models

#### Core Themes
- Current personality change interventions show small, heterogeneous effects (meta-analytic d = 0.22)
- Five types of dynamics defined: mean-level of states, variability, state-state associations, situational contingencies, temporal dynamics (latency, persistence/inertia, attractors)
- Three proposed leverage points: (1) dynamics as intervention targets, (2) dynamics for personalization (between-person), (3) dynamics as momentary triggers (JITAIs)
- Methodological guidance: ≥10 assessments for mean-levels, ≥30 for variabilities, ≥100 for within-person associations
- Psychometric properties of dynamics beyond mean-level poorly established; measurement burden high

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Relevant to understanding personality change mechanisms and the state-trait interface, but primarily a forward-looking proposal.

### 257. Patterson et al. (2023)

**Citation:** Patterson, M. W., Pivnick, L. K., Garvey, E. K., Harden, K. P., & Tucker-Drob, E. M. (2023). Positive and negative risk-taking in adolescence: Developing and validating the Texas Positive Risk-Taking Scale. *Journal of Research on Adolescence*.

**Purpose:** Original Research, Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis, Instrument Validation
**Instruments:** Texas Positive Risk-Taking Scale (11 items), BFI, UPPS
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* risk-taking, adolescence, positive risk, sensation seeking, personality, scale development
- *Additional terms:* Texas Twin Project, measurement invariance, SEM, Extraversion, Openness, Negative Urgency

#### Results Summary

**Positive risk-taking correlations:**
| Measure | r |
|---|---|
| Negative risk-taking | .20 |
| Extraversion | .27 |
| Openness | .16 |
| Sensation Seeking | .23 |
| Negative Urgency | .13 |

**Differential associations (Critical Ratio tests):**
- Positive > negative risk-taking with: Extraversion (CR = 3.00), Neuroticism (CR = 2.20), Openness (CR = 4.99)
- Negative > positive risk-taking with: Negative Urgency (CR = 3.40), low Agreeableness (CR = 4.01)

#### Core Themes
- N = 1,249 adolescents (Texas Twin Project, ages 13.5–20.1, racially diverse)
- Developed and validated 11-item positive risk-taking scale (socially oriented risks: initiating friendships, trying new activities, standing up for beliefs); α = .80
- Metric invariance held across gender and age; partial scalar invariance achieved
- Positive and negative risk-taking share partial overlap but have distinct personality correlates
- Positive risk-taking distinguished by higher Extraversion, Openness; negative by higher Urgency, lower Agreeableness

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Useful for understanding how personality traits map onto behaviorally distinct risk-taking pathways during development.

### 258. Simms et al. (2012)

**Citation:** Simms, L. J., Prisciandaro, J. J., Krueger, R. F., & Goldberg, D. P. (2012). The structure of depression, anxiety and somatic symptoms in primary care. *Psychological Medicine*, *42*(1), 15–28. https://doi.org/10.1017/S0033291711000985

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** CIDI-PC (91 items), GHQ-12, SDS, BDQ
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* bifactor model, internalizing, somatization, depression, anxiety, primary care, WHO
- *Additional terms:* CIDI-PC, IRT, severity continuum, general factor, AIC, BIC

#### Results Summary

**Model comparison (N = 5,433):**
| Model | AIC | BIC |
|---|---|---|
| Bifactor (best) | 202,466 | 204,268 |
| 3-factor | 203,883 | 205,104 |
| 2-factor | 204,901 | 205,540 |
| 1-factor | 206,428 | 206,467 |

**General internalizing factor:**
- Median loading = 0.64 (range 0.25–0.84)
- Correlation with MDD = .86, GAD = .85
- Accounted for R² = 74.9% (MDD), 72.4% (dysthymia)

**Specific factors:**
- Anxiety-specific: trivial (mean loading = 0.10, mixed signs)
- Depression-specific: correlated uniquely with MDD (r = .49)
- Somatic-specific: correlated with somatization (r = .38)

#### Core Themes
- N = 5,433 from 15 clinics in 14 countries (WHO Collaborative Study)
- Bifactor model clearly superior to alternatives
- Nearly all anxiety variance captured by general internalizing factor
- IRT severity continuum: anxiety symptoms → depression symptoms → somatic symptoms (from low to high severity)
- Somatic symptoms with non-physical etiologies belong in the broad internalizing spectrum

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Relevant to understanding bifactor structure of internalizing psychopathology and how symptoms organize hierarchically. Important for psychometric measurement chapters.

### 259. Sundin et al. (2021)

**Citation:** Sundin, Z. W., Chopik, W. J., Welker, K. M., Ascigil, E., Brandes, C. M., ... Tackett, J. L. (2021). Estimating the associations between Big Five personality traits, testosterone, and cortisol. *Adaptive Human Behavior and Physiology*.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Mega-Analysis, Meta-Analysis
**Instruments:** BFI, BFI-2, TIPI, ICID-S, NEO-FFI; salivary testosterone and cortisol
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, testosterone, cortisol, registered report, hormones, personality
- *Additional terms:* dual-hormone hypothesis, hormonal contraceptives, basal hormones, multilevel modeling, MASEM

#### Results Summary

**Multilevel model results (N = 3,964, 25 samples):**
| Trait | Testosterone r | Cortisol r |
|---|---|---|
| Extraversion | −.046* | ns |
| Agreeableness | ns | ns |
| Conscientiousness | −.050** | ns |
| Neuroticism | ns | ns |
| Openness | ns | ns |

Only testosterone–Conscientiousness met pre-registered criterion (r > |.05|).

**Moderation tests:** All null — testosterone × cortisol (dual-hormone hypothesis), gender, hormonal contraceptives.

#### Core Themes
- Pre-registered registered report; N = 3,964 from 25 pooled samples (50.3% women)
- Powered to detect effects as small as f² = .002
- **Main finding: basal testosterone and cortisol are largely unrelated to Big Five personality traits**
- Dual-hormone hypothesis uniformly null
- Heterogeneity across studies mostly low (consistent null effects)
- Authors conclude Big Five may be too broad for hormone-personality links; facet-level measures or hormonal reactivity needed

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Powerful null finding with major implications: the largest study to date finds basal hormones essentially unrelated to Big Five. Constrains biological models of personality and hormonal basis of individual differences.

### 260. Tackett et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Tackett, J. L., Brandes, C. M., Reardon, K. W., & Shields, A. N. (2025). Dominance and prestige motivations to lead in adolescence. *Journal of Personality*, 0, 1–13. https://doi.org/10.1111/jopy.70029

**Purpose:** Original Research, Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis, Instrument Validation
**Instruments:** AMS (Achievement Motivation Scale), BFI-2, DIPSI, ICID-S, MPQ, IRI
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* dominance, prestige, leadership, adolescence, personality, nomological network
- *Additional terms:* Social Potency, empathy, Disagreeableness, callous-unemotional, pre-registered, AMS

#### Results Summary

**Key correlations (Sample 1, adolescents):**
| Measure | Dominance | Prestige |
|---|---|---|
| BFI Extraversion | .54 | .31 |
| MPQ Social Potency | .65 | .28 |
| BFI Agreeableness | −.02 | .20 |
| IRI Empathic Concern | −.01 | .27 |
| DIPSI Dominance-Egocentrism | .38 | .06 |
| IRI Personal Distress | −.29 | −.14 |

All differential associations confirmed by Steiger's z tests (z ≥ 3.849, p < .001).

#### Core Themes
- First empirical investigation of Dominance vs. Prestige leadership motivations in adolescents
- Sample 1: N = 388 (ages 13–18); Sample 2: N = 563 (ages 18–23); both racially diverse
- Pre-registered hypotheses and nomological network
- Dominance marked by Social Potency/Egocentric traits; Prestige by Agreeableness/Empathy
- Parent–youth agreement: r = .403 (Dominance), r = .204 (Prestige)
- CFA of AMS showed poor fit (CFI .804–.899; RMSEA .065–.136), flagging measurement concerns
- Nomological networks largely consistent across adolescent and emerging adult samples

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Connects personality traits to distinct evolutionary status strategies (dominance vs. prestige) in adolescent development.

### 261. Treadway et al. (2009)

**Citation:** Treadway, M. T., Buckholtz, J. W., Schwartzman, A. N., Lambert, W. E., & Zald, D. H. (2009). Worth the 'EEfRT'? The effort expenditure for rewards task as an objective measure of motivation and anhedonia. *PLoS ONE*, *4*(8), e6598. https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0006598

**Purpose:** Original Research, Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention, Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** EEfRT (new), BDI-II, SHAPS, Chapman PAS/SAS, BIS/BAS, TEPS, PANAS, EPQ-R
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* effort, reward, motivation, anhedonia, EEfRT, behavioral task
- *Additional terms:* BAS Drive, trait positive affect, hard-task choice, probability, magnitude, multilevel logistic regression

#### Core Themes
- N = 55 healthy undergraduates; 50 trials choosing between easy (30 presses/$1) and hard (100 presses/variable reward) tasks
- Participants sensitive to expected value: higher probability and magnitude → more hard-task choices
- Probability × magnitude interaction (b = 0.36, p < .05)
- BAS Drive predicted greater hard-task choices across conditions
- Trait positive affect buffered demotivation at low probability (b = 0.04, p < .05)
- TEPS anticipatory pleasure predicted hard-task choices at high probability
- BDI-II not significant (low depression severity in healthy sample)
- Initial validation establishing EEfRT sensitivity to reward parameters and individual differences

**Relevance to Book Project:** LOW
Primarily a task validation paper; tangentially relevant to behavioral substrates of approach motivation.

### 262. Watson et al. (2022)

**Citation:** Watson, D., Levin-Aspenson, H. F., Waszczuk, M. A., Conway, C. C., Dalgleish, T., ... Krueger, R. F.; HiTOP Utility Workgroup. (2022). Validity and utility of Hierarchical Taxonomy of Psychopathology (HiTOP): III. Emotional dysfunction superspectrum. *World Psychiatry*, *21*(1), 26–54. https://doi.org/10.1002/wps.20943

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review, Systematic Review
**Instruments:** IDAS/IDAS-II, PID-5, MMPI-2-RF, PAI, PHQ-15, BDI, BAI (discussed)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* HiTOP, emotional dysfunction, internalizing, somatoform, neuroticism, negative affect
- *Additional terms:* distress, fear, eating pathology, sexual problems, genomic SEM, BDNF, cortisol, childhood temperament

#### Core Themes
- Emotional dysfunction superspectrum = internalizing (distress + fear subfactors) + somatoform spectrum
- NA/Neuroticism is "very highly correlated and essentially fungible" with the internalizing dimension
- NA/N facets: anxiousness, depressivity, anger/irritability, separation insecurity, emotional lability, anxiety sensitivity, rumination, hopelessness, perfectionism
- Depression–GAD genetic overlap rg = 0.80; genomic SEM identifies narrow internalizing factor
- Environmental risk factors (maltreatment, discrimination) operate transdiagnostically at spectrum level
- Neural substrates: compromised fronto-striatal and fronto-limbic circuitry across internalizing
- Childhood temperament: high negative emotionality predicts internalizing; low approach specifically predicts anxiety
- Dimensional reliability r = .82 (meta-analytic) vs. categorical κ = .20–.28 for GAD/MDD
- HiTOP dimensions explain 2× more variance in functional impairment and outcomes than DSM diagnoses

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly addresses how psychopathology structure maps onto personality dimensions (especially neuroticism), genetic architecture, neural substrates, and developmental antecedents.

### 263. Carver, Johnson, & Timpano (2017)

**Citation:** Carver, C. S., Johnson, S. L., & Timpano, K. R. (2017). Toward a functional view of the p factor in psychopathology. *Clinical Psychological Science*, *5*(5), 880–889. https://doi.org/10.1177/2167702617710037

**Purpose:** Commentary/Editorial
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* p-factor, psychopathology, dual-process, impulsivity, emotion, transdiagnostic
- *Additional terms:* reflexive vs. reflective processing, urgency, response inhibition, model-free vs. model-based, serotonin transporter

#### Core Themes
- Proposes a functional interpretation of the p-factor based on dual-process models
- Reflexive (associative) system is fast, habitual, emotion-responsive; reflective (deliberative) system is slower, rule-based
- Core hypothesis: individuals dominated by reflexive processing — specifically those *highly reactive to emotions* — are more vulnerable to psychopathology broadly
- Two crossing dimensions: incentive sensitivity (low–high) × control over emotions (high–low)
- *Poor constraint in the presence of emotion* (not emotional intensity per se) is the critical transdiagnostic vulnerability
- Evidence: p-factor linked to poor constraint, impulsivity, response inhibition deficits (MDD d = .58; bipolar g = .52; externalizing r = .47)
- Cognitive impulsiveness → internalizing; behavioral impulsiveness → externalizing
- Alternative: general bias toward model-free (habitual) over model-based (goal-directed) functioning

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly relevant to understanding how a core psychological process (impulsive reactivity to emotion) constitutes a transdiagnostic vulnerability with roots in dual-process cognition, personality, and neurobiology.

---

### 264. Davies et al. (2016)

**Citation:** Davies, G., Marioni, R. E., Liewald, D. C., Hill, W. D., Hagenaars, S. P., ... Deary, I. J. (2016). Genome-wide association study of cognitive functions and educational attainment in UK Biobank (N = 112,151). *Molecular Psychiatry*, *21*(6), 758–767. https://doi.org/10.1038/mp.2016.45

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Twin/Genetic (GWAS, GCTA-GREML, LD score regression)
**Instruments:** UK Biobank cognitive battery, educational attainment
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, g

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* GWAS, cognitive functions, educational attainment, UK Biobank, heritability, SNP
- *Additional terms:* GCTA-GREML, LD score regression, polygenic profile scoring, CYP2D6, CADM2, ATXN2

#### Results Summary

**SNP-based heritability (GCTA-GREML):**
| Phenotype | h² (SE) |
|---|---|
| Verbal-numerical reasoning | .31 (.018) |
| Reaction time | .11 (.006) |
| Memory | .05 (.006) |
| Educational attainment | .21 (.006) |

**Genome-wide significant associations:** 20 genomic regions (SNP-based), 46 (gene-based) across four traits.

**Genetic correlations (LD score regression):**
| Pair | rg |
|---|---|
| Reasoning × Educational attainment | .73 |
| Reasoning × Memory | .44 |
| Reasoning × RT | .21 |

Polygenic prediction: up to 5.4% of vocabulary variance explained (largest R² at publication).

#### Core Themes
- N = 112,151 UK Biobank (white British ancestry, ages 40–73)
- Key loci: CYP2D6 (chr 22, drug metabolism), CADM2 (synapse organization), ATXN2 (neurodegeneration), APBA1 (Alzheimer's)
- Memory test had very low reliability (test-retest r = .15), explaining low h² and fewer hits
- Educational attainment and reasoning are better g proxies; more hits reflect higher g loadings
- Genomic regions overlap with intracranial volume, neurodegeneration, Alzheimer's, schizophrenia

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Landmark early UK Biobank GWAS establishing SNP-based heritability of cognitive functions and genetic correlations between cognition and education. Directly relevant to how cognitive phenotypes are genetically constructed.

### 265. Day et al. (2016)

**Citation:** Day, F. R., Helgason, H., Chasman, D. I., Rose, L. M., Loh, P.-R., ... Perry, J. R. B. (2016). Physical and neurobehavioral determinants of reproductive onset and success. *Nature Genetics*, *48*(6), 617–623. https://doi.org/10.1038/ng.3551

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Twin/Genetic (GWAS, Mendelian randomization, LD score regression)
**Instruments:** UK Biobank questionnaires
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* age at first sex, reproductive success, GWAS, Mendelian randomization, neurobehavioral
- *Additional terms:* CADM2, ESR1, MSRA, risk-taking, irritable temperament, puberty timing, AFS, AFB

#### Results Summary

**SNP-based heritability of AFS:** men h² = 0.248, women h² = 0.242

**Selected genetic correlations with AFS:**
| Trait | rg |
|---|---|
| Age at first birth | .86 |
| Risk-taking propensity | −.46 |
| ADHD | −.38 |
| Puberty timing | .22–.26 |
| Schizophrenia | −.10 |

**Mendelian randomization causal chain:** Earlier puberty → earlier AFS → earlier AFB → more children → lower educational attainment → increased smoking → increased schizophrenia susceptibility

#### Core Themes
- N = 125,667 discovery + 241,910 replication; 38 genome-wide significant loci for AFS
- CADM2 (prefrontal cortex, neuronal cell adhesion) associated with AFS, risk-taking, sexual partners, BMI, processing speed
- MSRA associated with AFS and irritable temperament; ESR1 (estrogen receptor) associated with AFS, AFB, and number of children
- Many AFS loci near brain development genes (BARHL2, SEMA3F, ZIC4, DRD2, GRIA4, CACNA1D)
- Bidirectional MR: schizophrenia susceptibility lowers AFS, but earlier AFS also increases schizophrenia susceptibility

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates genetic architecture linking puberty, reproductive behavior, personality traits (risk-taking, irritability), cognition, and psychiatric outcomes through shared genetic variants. Directly addresses how evolution, development, and genetics co-construct behavioral phenotypes.

### 266. Disner, Beevers, Haigh, & Beck (2011)

**Citation:** Disner, S. G., Beevers, C. G., Haigh, E. A. P., & Beck, A. T. (2011). Neural mechanisms of the cognitive model of depression. *Nature Reviews Neuroscience*, *12*(8), 467–477. https://doi.org/10.1038/nrn3027

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* depression, cognitive model, neural mechanisms, fMRI, attention bias, schema
- *Additional terms:* Beck, amygdala, DLPFC, VLPFC, NAcc, subgenual ACC, MPFC, 5-HTT, CBT, SSRI, rumination

#### Core Themes
- First comprehensive neurobiological mapping of Beck's cognitive model: schemas → biased attention → biased processing → biased memory/rumination → depressive symptoms
- **Biased attention:** Decreased VLPFC, DLPFC, ACC, and parietal cortex activity; reduced efficiency of attentional selection and disengagement from negative stimuli
- **Biased processing:** Amygdala reactivity up to 70% more intense and 3× longer lasting in MDD vs. controls; blunted NAcc/caudate responses to positive stimuli
- **Biased memory/rumination:** Increased amygdala, hippocampal, caudate, putamen activity during negative encoding; sustained amygdala and subgenual cingulate + increased MPFC + decreased DLPFC
- **Schemas:** Amygdala–MPFC–ACC pathway emphasizing emotionality and self-referentiality; elevated 5-HTT binding in PFC, ACC, putamen, thalamus even after remission
- **Integrated model:** (1) bottom-up limbic hyperactivation → (2) attenuated top-down PFC control
- CBT normalizes amygdala/DLPFC; SSRIs boost serotonergic PFC control — same system, different entry points

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides neurobiological architecture of how cognitive biases in depression are constructed and maintained, integrating personality vulnerability, cognitive mechanisms, and neural circuits.

---

### 267. Johnson et al. (2021)

**Citation:** Johnson, S. L., Swerdlow, B., Tharp, J. A., Chen, S., Tackett, J., & Zeitzer, J. (2021). Social dominance and multiple dimensions of psychopathology: An experimental test of reactivity to leadership and subordinate roles. *PLoS ONE*, *16*(4), e0250099. https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0250099

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention, Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** HPS, 7-Up/7-Down, IDD-L, SIAS, SAS, PPI-SF, PANAS; skin conductance, RSA
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* social dominance, mania, depression, social anxiety, psychopathy, leadership
- *Additional terms:* subordinate role, functional PCA, RSA, SCL, confederate, role manipulation

#### Core Themes
- N = 81 undergraduates (oversampled for high psychopathology) randomized to leader/subordinate roles
- Mania × Role interaction: higher mania → more positive affect and comfort as leader; discomfort as subordinate; RSA decrement in subordinate role
- Depression showed opposite pattern: higher depression → more positive affect as subordinate
- Social anxiety: generalized discomfort regardless of role (main effects only, no interactions)
- Fearless Dominance (psychopathy): higher negative affect regardless of role, but no differential reactivity
- Cross-correlations: depression–mania r = .26; Fearless Dominance–social anxiety r = −.62

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates how evolved social dominance mechanisms interface with psychopathology dimensions; relevant to evolutionary framing but limited by small sample.

### 268. Leonhart et al. (2018)

**Citation:** Leonhart, R., de Vroege, L., Zhang, L., Liu, Y., Dong, Z., Schaefert, R., ... van der Feltz-Cornelis, C. M. (2018). Comparison of the factor structure of the Patient Health Questionnaire for Somatic Symptoms (PHQ-15) in Germany, the Netherlands, and China. *Frontiers in Psychiatry*, *9*, 240. https://doi.org/10.3389/fpsyt.2018.00240

**Purpose:** Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** PHQ-15, PHQ-9, GAD-7
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* PHQ-15, somatic symptoms, bifactor model, cross-cultural, CFA
- *Additional terms:* pain, gastrointestinal, cardiopulmonary, fatigue, WLSMV, Germany, Netherlands, China

#### Core Themes
- N = 4,302 across Germany (2,517), Netherlands (456), China (1,329)
- Bifactor model (1 general + 4 specific factors) fit well in Germany (RMSEA = .044, CFI = .983) and Netherlands (RMSEA = .019, CFI = .998)
- China required modification: cardiopulmonary factor split differently; fainting, chest pain, and sexual intercourse pain clustered differently than European samples
- PHQ-9 and GAD-7 predicted general PHQ-15 factor across all samples
- Cultural factors (stigma, illness scripts, somatization patterns) likely explain cross-cultural structural differences

**Relevance to Book Project:** LOW
Primarily a psychometric validation study for a somatic symptom measure; tangential to personality structure.

---

### 269. Mischel & Shoda (1995)

**Citation:** Mischel, W., & Shoda, Y. (1995). A cognitive-affective system theory of personality: Reconceptualizing situations, dispositions, dynamics, and invariance in personality structure. *Psychological Review*, *102*(2), 246–268.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality, situations, dispositions, cognitive-affective processing system, behavioral signatures
- *Additional terms:* CAPS, if...then profiles, Wediko camp, personality paradox, computer simulation, cross-situational consistency

#### Core Themes
- Foundational paper proposing the Cognitive-Affective Personality System (CAPS) model
- Personality coherence expressed in stable "if...then..." situation-behavior profiles (behavioral signatures), not just average behavioral tendencies
- Mean profile stability from camp data (167 hours/participant): verbal aggression .47**, compliance .41**, physical aggression .28**, whining .19*
- Self-perceived consistency linked to profile stability (r ≈ .5), not cross-situational consistency
- Five types of cognitive-affective mediating units: (1) encodings/construals, (2) expectancies/beliefs, (3) affects/emotions, (4) goals/values, (5) competencies/self-regulatory plans
- Computer simulation of 100 individuals: stable connectivity differences alone produced both mean-level differences and stable situation-behavior profiles (average stability r = .66)
- Resolves the "personality paradox": low cross-situational consistency is meaningful signal reflecting the personality system's distinctive organization
- Implications: biological and social learning history shape cognitive-affective unit organization; therapy should target processing dynamics, not just surface behaviors

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational theoretical framework for understanding personality as a dynamic cognitive-affective system shaped by both genetic-biological substrates and developmental learning history.

---

### 270. Selzam et al. (2018)

**Citation:** Selzam, S., Coleman, J. R. I., Caspi, A., Moffitt, T. E., & Plomin, R. (2018). A polygenic p factor for major psychiatric disorders. *Translational Psychiatry*, *8*, 205. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41398-018-0217-4

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Twin/Genetic, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** Polygenic scores for 8 psychiatric disorders (PGC GWAS summary statistics)
**Level of Detail:** Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* p factor, polygenic scores, psychiatric genetics, PCA, genetic correlation, transdiagnostic
- *Additional terms:* TEDS, LDSC, GCTA, family study, schizophrenia, bipolar, MDD, ADHD, autism, OCD, anorexia, PTSD

#### Results Summary

**First principal component (genetic p) variance explained:**
| Method | Variance explained | Average loading |
|---|---|---|
| Family (3.5M Swedish siblings) | 57% | .75 |
| GCTA | 43% | .58 |
| LDSC | 35% | .57 |
| GPS (N = 7,026, TEDS) | 22% | .44 |

All disorders loaded positively. Schizophrenia, bipolar, and MDD consistently had highest loadings.

#### Core Themes
- Convergent evidence from four genetic methods for a general factor of genetic liability to psychopathology
- Average genetic correlations: family = 0.49, GCTA = 0.22, LDSC = 0.24, GPS = 0.09
- GPS loadings approach family study estimates as GWAS sample sizes increase
- Oblique rotation places schizophrenia and bipolar on one factor; second factor less consistent
- Three candidate mechanisms: biological pleiotropy, mediated pleiotropy, or general impairment

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly demonstrates hierarchical genetic architecture of psychopathology with a general factor; central to understanding how mental phenotypes share common genetic origins.

---

### 271. Widiger et al. (2019)

**Citation:** Widiger, T. A., Sellbom, M., Chmielewski, M., Clark, L. A., DeYoung, C. G., Kotov, R., ... Wright, A. G. C. (2019). Personality in a hierarchical model of psychopathology. *Clinical Psychological Science*, *7*(1), 77–92. https://doi.org/10.1177/2167702618797105

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* HiTOP, personality, FFM, psychopathology, dimensional, neuroticism
- *Additional terms:* PID-5, ICD-11, personality disorders, Unified Protocol, treatment, economic costs

#### Core Themes
- 16-author HiTOP Personality Work Group position paper
- HiTOP's six spectra align with FFM domains: detachment ≈ low E, antagonism ≈ low A, disinhibition ≈ low C, psychoticism ≈ (complex) O, negative affectivity ≈ N
- Personality provides a "foundational base" for HiTOP; personality disorders are maladaptive variants of general traits
- Neuroticism is the strongest personality predictor of psychopathology broadly
- Economic costs of neuroticism (top 25%): $1.393B per million inhabitants — exceeding costs of common mental disorders ($585M)
- Clinical interventions produce substantial N decreases (d = −0.57) and modest Big Five changes (d = 0.13–0.23)
- SSRIs reduce neuroticism in healthy volunteers (Knutson et al., 1998; N = 51, double-blind RCT)
- Five FFM domains are better suited for treatment planning than DSM PD categories
- Personality should be the focus of early intervention rather than waiting for diagnosable disorders

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly addresses how personality trait structure undergirds the spectrum of psychopathology; strong conceptual framework for the personality-psychopathology continuum and treatment-as-personality-change.

### 272. Aichele, Rabbitt, & Ghisletta (2015)

**Citation:** Aichele, S., Rabbitt, P., & Ghisletta, P. (2015). Life span decrements in fluid intelligence and processing speed predict mortality risk. *Psychology and Aging, 30*(3), 598–612. https://doi.org/10.1037/pag0000035

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** Raven Mill Hill Vocabulary, WAIS-R Vocabulary, Heim Intelligence Tests (AH4), Cattell Culture Fair, Free/Cumulative/Delayed Verbal Recall, Visual Search, Alphabet Coding Task
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, g

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cognitive aging, fluid intelligence, processing speed, mortality, latent growth models
- *Additional terms:* crystallized intelligence, verbal memory, visuospatial memory, cognitive epidemiology, Manchester Longitudinal Study of Cognition

#### Core Themes
- Uses the Manchester Longitudinal Study of Cognition (MLSC; N=6,203, ages 42–97, 20+ years) to examine whether life span changes in five cognitive domains predict mortality
- Baseline performance across all domains predicted mortality, but life span decrements in fluid intelligence and processing speed were uniquely and most strongly linked to mortality risk after controlling for baseline
- Sex moderates the pattern: in women, both PS and Gf decrements contribute; in men, Gf and PS appear mutually exclusive as predictors
- Latent growth models with factorial invariance tested; retest effects modeled

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Cognitive epidemiology evidence linking cognitive decline trajectories to survival; demonstrates the predictive power of processing speed and fluid intelligence above and beyond crystallized ability.

### 273. Batty, Deary, & Gale (2021)

**Citation:** Batty, G. D., Deary, I. J., & Gale, C. R. (2021). Pre-pandemic cognitive function and COVID-19 mortality: Prospective cohort study. *European Journal of Epidemiology, 36*, 559–564. https://doi.org/10.1007/s10654-021-00743-7

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal
**Instruments:** UK Biobank reaction time test (Go/No-Go), UK Biobank verbal-numeric reasoning test (13-item)
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cognitive function, COVID-19, mortality, UK Biobank, prospective cohort
- *Additional terms:* reaction time, verbal-numeric reasoning, health behaviors, socioeconomic position

#### Core Themes
- UK Biobank (N up to ~495,000); pre-pandemic cognitive function (2006–2010) predicted COVID-19 mortality through September 2020
- Slower RT (HR=1.18 per SD) and lower verbal-numeric reasoning (HR=1.32 per SD) associated with higher COVID-19 death rates
- Lifestyle factors (smoking, BMI, physical inactivity) produced greatest attenuation of associations
- Very large sample with registry-confirmed mortality; only 2 brief cognitive measures

**Relevance to Book Project:** LOW
Extends cognitive epidemiology to a novel outcome; further evidence that cognitive function predicts health outcomes through behavioral pathways.

### 274. Booth et al. (2015)

**Citation:** Booth, T., Royle, N. A., Corley, J., Gow, A. J., Valdés Hernández, M. del C., Muñoz Maniega, S., Ritchie, S. J., Bastin, M. E., Starr, J. M., Wardlaw, J. M., & Deary, I. J. (2015). Association of allostatic load with brain structure and cognitive ability in later life. *Neurobiology of Aging, 36*, 1390–1399. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.neurobiolaging.2014.12.020

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** WAIS-III UK, WMS-III, NART, WTAR, Verbal Fluency, Reaction Time, Inspection Time, Moray House Test No. 12 (age 11 IQ)
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, g

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* allostatic load, brain structure, cognitive ability, aging, bifactor model
- *Additional terms:* Lothian Birth Cohort 1936, processing speed, white matter, inflammation, cardiovascular risk

#### Core Themes
- Lothian Birth Cohort 1936 (n=633, mean age 72.5). Bifactor SEM for AL (10 biomarkers) and cognition (18 tests)
- AL inversely associated with total brain volume (β=−0.16 to −0.20), WM volume (β=−0.35), g (β=−0.13 to −0.20), and processing speed (β=−0.20 to −0.22)
- Brain volumes did not mediate the AL-cognition association
- After controlling for childhood IQ (age 11), only AL-processing speed remained significant

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates allostatic load effects on brain and cognition in later life; the processing speed specificity and failure of brain-volume mediation are informative for understanding stress-cognition pathways.

### 275. Del Giudice, Booth, & Irwing (2012)

**Citation:** Del Giudice, M., Booth, T., & Irwing, P. (2012). The distance between Mars and Venus: Measuring global sex differences in personality. *PLoS ONE, 7*(1), e29265. https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0029265

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** 16PF Questionnaire, 5th Edition (16PF5)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* sex differences, personality, multivariate, Mahalanobis D, gender similarities hypothesis
- *Additional terms:* 16PF, latent factor analysis, measurement invariance, distributional overlap

#### Core Themes
- 16PF US standardization (N=10,261; 50.1% female); multigroup latent variable modeling
- Latent univariate d's range from −2.29 to +0.54 (mean |d|=0.44); Mahalanobis D=2.71 (10% overlap)
- Even excluding Sensitivity, D=1.71 (24% overlap)
- Argues firmly against gender similarities hypothesis for personality at below-domain level
- Methodological contribution: (a) measure below Big Five, (b) estimate latent factors, (c) use multivariate effect sizes

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational methodological paper for measuring sex differences in personality at the facet level; the multivariate approach reveals large global differences masked by univariate analyses.

### 276. Demange & Boomsma (2024)

**Citation:** Demange, P. A., Boomsma, D. I., van Bergen, E., & Nivard, M. G. (2024). Educational attainment and psychiatric diagnoses: A national registry data and two-sample Mendelian randomization study. *Nature Mental Health, 2*(6), 668–679. https://doi.org/10.1038/s44220-024-00245-x

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Twin/Genetic
**Instruments:** Dutch national registry (DSM-IV diagnoses), GWAS summary statistics for EA and 12 psychiatric disorders
**Level of Detail:** Other (educational attainment, psychiatric diagnoses)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* educational attainment, psychiatric diagnoses, Mendelian randomization, within-sibship, causal inference
- *Additional terms:* MDD, ADHD, schizophrenia, bipolar disorder, anorexia, OCD, population stratification

#### Core Themes
- Within-sibship regression (N=1.7M Dutch siblings) + two-sample MR triangulation
- Consistent protective effects of higher EA for MDD, alcohol dependence, ADHD, GAD, PTSD
- Inconsistent results for schizophrenia, bipolar, anorexia, OCD — genetic liability may interfere with schooling
- Causal estimates smaller than observational associations; bidirectional evidence for ADHD affecting EA

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates causal effects of education on mental health through quasi-causal methods; relevant to understanding how cognitive/educational pathways shape psychiatric risk.

### 277. Demange et al. (2021)

**Citation:** Demange, P. A., Malanchini, M., Mallard, T. T., Biroli, P., Cox, S. R., Grotzinger, A. D., ... & Nivard, M. G. (2021). Investigating the genetic architecture of noncognitive skills using GWAS-by-subtraction. *Nature Genetics, 53*, 35–44. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41588-020-00754-2

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Twin/Genetic, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** GWAS summary statistics for EA (N~1.1M) and cognitive performance (N~258K); PGS validated in 6 cohorts
**Level of Detail:** Other (noncognitive skills latent genetic construct)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* GWAS-by-subtraction, Genomic SEM, noncognitive skills, educational attainment, polygenic scores
- *Additional terms:* cognitive performance, Openness, Conscientiousness, schizophrenia, gene-environment correlation

#### Core Themes
- GWAS-by-subtraction isolates NonCog factor (57% of genetic variance in EA); 157 genome-wide significant loci
- NonCog associated with Openness, Conscientiousness, Extraversion, Agreeableness (domain-level)
- NonCog PGS associated with less risky health behavior, delayed fertility, but higher risk for schizophrenia, bipolar, anorexia, OCD
- Validated in 6 independent cohorts (meta-analytic N~24,000)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational paper for understanding the genetic architecture of noncognitive contributions to education; shows personality traits partially capture what the NonCog construct measures.

### 278. Demange et al. (2022)

**Citation:** Demange, P. A., Hottenga, J. J., Abdellaoui, A., Eilertsen, E. M., Malanchini, M., ... & Cheesman, R. (2022). Estimating effects of parents' cognitive and non-cognitive skills on offspring education using polygenic scores. *Nature Communications, 13*, 4801. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-022-32003-x

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Twin/Genetic
**Instruments:** Cog and NonCog PGS; educational attainment (UK Biobank, TEDS, NTR)
**Level of Detail:** Other (cognitive/noncognitive PGS, educational attainment)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* indirect genetic effects, genetic nurture, polygenic scores, educational attainment, intergenerational transmission
- *Additional terms:* sibling design, adoption design, non-transmitted alleles, assortative mating, population stratification

#### Core Themes
- Three family-based genomic designs (siblings N=47,459; adoptees N=6,407; trios N=2,534) across UK Biobank, TEDS, NTR
- Parental indirect genetic effects explain 36% (NonCog) and 40% (Cog) of population PGS effects
- Adoption design yields systematically lower estimates, consistent with less bias from stratification/assortative mating
- Both cognitive and non-cognitive parental effects transmit to offspring education

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates how parents' noncognitive (personality-adjacent) genetic endowments shape offspring educational environments beyond direct genetic transmission.

### 280. Graham et al. (2017)

**Citation:** Graham, E. K., Rutsohn, J. P., Turiano, N. A., Bendayan, R., ... & Mroczek, D. K. (2017). **Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal, Meta-Analysis
**Instruments:** NEO-FFI, EPI/EPQ, BFI, IPIP, DPQ
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality, mortality, Big Five, coordinated analysis, integrative data analysis
- *Additional terms:* conscientiousness, neuroticism, smoking, health behaviors, IALSA

#### Core Themes
- Coordinated Analysis of 15 international longitudinal studies (up to 44,094 participants, 5 countries, up to 42.75-year follow-up)
- Conscientiousness strongest protective effect (weighted HR=0.89); neuroticism, extraversion, agreeableness also predicted mortality
- Smoking partially mediated only the neuroticism-mortality association
- Country of origin and baseline age explained some heterogeneity

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
One of the largest multi-study examinations of personality-mortality links; demonstrates the robust predictive validity of conscientiousness across diverse populations.

### 282. Harden & Tucker-Drob (2011)

**Citation:** Harden, K. P., & Tucker-Drob, E. M. (2011). Individual differences in the development of sensation seeking and impulsivity during adolescence: Further evidence for a dual systems model. *Developmental Psychology, 47*(3), 739–746. https://doi.org/10.1037/a0023279

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal
**Instruments:** CNLSY self-report items (3 impulsivity, 3 sensation seeking), TIPI
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* dual systems, sensation seeking, impulsivity, adolescence, latent growth curve
- *Additional terms:* CNLSY, developmental trajectories, quadratic growth, independent developmental processes

#### Core Themes
- First longitudinal behavioral test of dual systems model (N=7,640, ages 12–24, biennial assessment)
- Impulsivity declined monotonically; sensation seeking showed inverted-U peaking ~age 16
- Correlation between linear changes in the two traits only r=.21 (ns), indicating largely independent developmental processes
- Substantial individual differences in all growth parameters

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly supports the decomposition of self-regulation into dissociable developmental processes; key evidence that impulsivity and sensation seeking have distinct developmental trajectories despite phenotypic correlation.

### 283. Jensen-Campbell et al. (2002)

**Citation:** Jensen-Campbell, L. A., Rosselli, M., Workman, K. A., Santisi, M., Rios, J. D., & Bojan, D. (2002). Agreeableness, conscientiousness, and effortful control processes. *Journal of Research in Personality, 36*(5), 476–489.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** BFI, IPIP (50-item), Stroop Test, WCST, Verbal Fluency
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* agreeableness, conscientiousness, effortful control, executive function, Big Five
- *Additional terms:* Ahadi & Rothbart, Stroop, WCST, self-regulation, developmental substrate

#### Core Themes
- N=113 college students; tests Ahadi & Rothbart (1994) hypothesis that effortful control is common substrate for A and C
- Agreeableness predicted Stroop performance and WCST indices; conscientiousness predicted WCST learning efficiency and verbal fluency in men
- Supports developmental linkage of A and C through self-regulation rather than social desirability

### 284. Jokela et al. (2013)

**Citation:** Jokela, M., Batty, G. D., Nyberg, S. T., Virtanen, M., Nabi, H., Singh-Manoux, A., & Kivimaki, M. (2013). Personality and all-cause mortality: Individual-participant meta-analysis of 3,947 deaths in 76,150 adults. *American Journal of Epidemiology, 178*(5), 667–675. https://doi.org/10.1093/aje/kwt170

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis, Longitudinal
**Instruments:** BFI-S, BFI, MIDI Big Five, various brief measures
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality, mortality, Big Five, conscientiousness, individual-participant meta-analysis
- *Additional terms:* health behaviors, mutual adjustment, publication bias, nonlinear effects

#### Results Summary
- Only low conscientiousness independently predicted all-cause mortality after mutual trait adjustment (HR=0.88 per SD, or HR=1.37 for lowest tertile)
- Health behaviors accounted for 21% of the conscientiousness-mortality association
- Effect weaker than prior published meta-analyses (potentially reflecting reduced publication bias from inclusion of unpublished data)
- Moderate-to-high heterogeneity (I²=73% for conscientiousness)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Gold-standard IPD meta-analysis isolating conscientiousness as the personality domain most robustly linked to mortality when all traits are mutually adjusted.

### 285. Karlsson Linnér et al. (2021)

**Citation:** Karlsson Linnér, R., Mallard, T. T., Barr, P. B., ... & Dick, D. M. (2021). Multivariate analysis of 1.5 million people identifies genetic associations with traits related to self-regulation and addiction. *Nature Neuroscience, 24*(10), 1367–1376. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41593-021-00908-3

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Twin/Genetic, Meta-Analysis, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** GWAS summary statistics for 7 externalizing phenotypes; PGS validated in multiple cohorts
**Level of Detail:** Other (externalizing liability latent factor)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* externalizing, Genomic SEM, GWAS, self-regulation, addiction, polygenic scores
- *Additional terms:* ADHD, substance use, risk tolerance, neurogenesis, within-family analysis

#### Core Themes
- Genomic SEM of ~1.5M people identifies common genetic externalizing factor (EXT) across 7 phenotypes
- 579 genome-wide significant loci (121 novel); enrichment in brain-expressed genes related to neurogenesis
- EXT PGS explains ~9–10% of variance in phenotypic externalizing; predicts opioid use, criminal justice involvement, medical outcomes across nearly every organ system
- Within-family sibling analyses confirm associations not solely driven by population stratification

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Maps the shared genetic architecture of self-regulation failure; demonstrates that a common externalizing liability has broad consequences for health and social outcomes.

### 286. Kochan et al. (2016)

**Citation:** Kochan, N. A., Bunce, D., Pont, S., Crawford, J. D., Brodaty, H., & Sachdev, P. S. (2016). Reaction time measures predict incident dementia in community-living older adults. *The American Journal of Geriatric Psychiatry, 24*(3), 221–231. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jagp.2015.12.005

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal
**Instruments:** Simple and Complex RT (touch-screen), MMSE, neuropsychological battery, APOE genotyping
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* reaction time, intra-individual variability, dementia, prediction, aging
- *Additional terms:* processing speed, AUC, Sydney Memory and Ageing Study

#### Core Themes
- Sydney Memory and Ageing Study (N=861, ages 70–90, 4-year follow-up)
- Mean RT stronger predictor of incident dementia (HR=1.53–1.59 per SD) than RT variability
- RT comparable predictive accuracy (AUC=0.75) to several traditional neuropsychological measures
- Remained significant after controlling for demographics, cognition, mood, and medical risk factors

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates the clinical utility of simple processing speed measures for dementia prediction in community settings.

### 287. Lopez-Gamundi & Wardle (2018)

**Citation:** Lopez-Gamundi, P., & Wardle, M. C. (2018). The cognitive effort expenditure for rewards task (C-EEfRT): A novel measure of willingness to expend cognitive effort. *Psychological Assessment, 30*(9), 1237–1248. https://doi.org/10.1037/pas0000563

### 288. Malanchini et al. (2019)

**Citation:** Malanchini, M., Engelhardt, L. E., Grotzinger, A. D., Harden, K. P., & Tucker-Drob, E. M. (2019). "Same but different": Associations between multiple aspects of self-regulation, cognition, and academic abilities. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 117*(6), 1164–1188.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Twin/Genetic, Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** EF battery (15 tasks), BFI (child), ZKA-PQ Impulsivity, Need for Cognition, Grit, PALS, WJ-III, WASI-II
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Cognitive Ability, g

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* self-regulation, executive function, personality, academic achievement, twin study
- *Additional terms:* Texas Twin Project, Openness, intellectual interest, genetic covariance, Cholesky decomposition

#### Core Themes
- Texas Twin Project (N=1,019 twins, M age 10.79); comprehensive EF + personality + academic battery
- EF and personality self-regulation weakly correlated phenotypically but shared genetic variance
- EF predicted math and reading beyond intelligence; Openness super-factor incrementally predicted reading
- EF common factor h²=92%; cognition, EF, and personality collectively accounted for all genetic variance in math and reading

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Key paper demonstrating how cognitive and personality-based self-regulation are "same but different" — weakly related phenotypically but genetically intertwined in predicting academic outcomes.

---

### 289. Malanchini et al. (2024)

**Citation:** Malanchini, M., Allegrini, A. G., Nivard, M. G., Biroli, P., ... & Harden, K. P. (2024). Genetic associations between non-cognitive skills and academic achievement over development. *Nature Human Behaviour, 8*, 2034–2046. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41562-024-01967-9

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal, Twin/Genetic, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** Education-specific NCS, SDQ, WISC-III, CAT3, Raven's SPM, Mill Hill, teacher ratings, GCSE, Cog/NonCog PGS
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Cognitive Ability, g

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* non-cognitive skills, academic achievement, development, twin study, polygenic scores, gene-environment correlation
- *Additional terms:* TEDS, within-family design, Genomic SEM, active rGE

#### Core Themes
- TEDS (N>10,000 twins, ages 7–16); non-cognitive skills increasingly predicted achievement beyond cognitive ability across development
- Genetic factors drove most of the NCS-achievement covariance
- NonCog PGS prediction increased developmentally while Cog PGS remained stable
- Within-family PGS effects confirmed developmental increase driven by NonCog genetics, consistent with active gene-environment correlation

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates the developmental emergence of noncognitive genetic influences on achievement; key evidence for how personality-adjacent traits become increasingly important across childhood.

### 290. Morris et al. (2021)

**Citation:** Morris, T. T., Davey Smith, G., van den Berg, G., & Davies, N. M. (2021). Consistency of non-cognitive skills and their relation to educational outcomes in a UK cohort. *bioRxiv* preprint. https://doi.org/10.1101/470682

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal, Twin/Genetic
**Instruments:** SDQ, Denver Developmental Screening, Harter Self Perception Profile, IPIP Big Five (age 13), WISC/WASI
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Cognitive Ability, Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* non-cognitive skills, consistency, heritability, educational attainment, ALSPAC
- *Additional terms:* SNP heritability, GREML, behavioral difficulties, SDQ, temporal stability

#### Core Themes
- ALSPAC (N up to ~14,899; ~7,988 genotyped); 10 non-cognitive skills assessed over 17 years of childhood/adolescence
- Most non-cognitive skills showed low temporal consistency; SDQ was most consistent (r=0.36–0.73)
- SNP heritability generally low (upper bound ~0.3) compared to cognitive ability (~0.45) and educational achievement (~0.50)
- Non-cognitive skill-education associations at most one-third the magnitude of cognitive-education associations
- **Preprint — not yet peer-reviewed**

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides a sobering counterpoint to non-cognitive skills enthusiasm; demonstrates that most NCS show low consistency and modest heritability compared to cognitive ability.

### 291. Murdock, Oddi, & Bridgett (2013)

**Citation:** Murdock, K. W., Oddi, K. B., & Bridgett, D. J. (2013). Cognitive correlates of personality: Links between executive functioning and the Big Five personality traits. *Journal of Individual Differences, 34*(2), 97–104.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** NEO-FFI, D-KEFS (Verbal Fluency, Color-Word Interference), WCST
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* executive functioning, Big Five, personality, Miyake model
- *Additional terms:* updating, inhibition, cognitive flexibility, neuroticism, openness

#### Core Themes
- N=182 young adults; three Miyake-model EF factors and all five Big Five traits
- Updating/Monitoring predicted lower Neuroticism (β=−.19) and higher Openness (β=.17)
- Cognitive Flexibility also predicted Openness (β=.15)
- No EF factors predicted Extraversion, Agreeableness, or Conscientiousness

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
One of the first studies testing all three Miyake EF components against all Big Five simultaneously; the N-Openness selectivity is noteworthy.

### 292. Shaffer (2025)

**Citation:** Shaffer, J. A. (2025). Conscientiousness and health outcomes: The moderating role of general mental ability and the mediating role of internal health locus of control. *Personality and Individual Differences, 233*, 112925. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.paid.2024.112925

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** MIDUS C scale (5 items), BTACT, HLOC-I (4 items), perceived health, BMI, BP
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Cognitive Ability, g

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* conscientiousness, general mental ability, health locus of control, moderated mediation
- *Additional terms:* MIDUS, cognitive buffering, perceived health, BMI, blood pressure

#### Core Themes
- MIDUS Phase 2 (N=1,661, mean age 56.73); moderated mediation model
- Conscientiousness × GMA interaction predicts internal HLOC; HLOC mediates effects on health outcomes
- Cognitive buffering: the C-HLOC relationship is weaker for those high in GMA
- Brief, non-standard C measure (α=.69); cross-sectional design

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates interplay between personality and cognitive ability in health pathways; the cognitive buffering finding suggests high intelligence can compensate for lower conscientiousness.

### 293. Stephan et al. (2019)

**Citation:** Stephan, Y., Sutin, A. R., Luchetti, M., & Terracciano, A. (2019). Facets of conscientiousness and longevity: Findings from the Health and Retirement Study. *Journal of Psychosomatic Research, 116*, 1–5. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jpsychores.2018.11.002

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal
**Instruments:** 24-item C facet measure (Roberts et al., 2005; 6 facets: self-control, order, industriousness, traditionalism, virtue, responsibility)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* conscientiousness facets, mortality, longevity, industriousness, HRS
- *Additional terms:* self-control, order, virtue, responsibility, traditionalism, health behaviors

#### Core Themes
- HRS (N=11,359, ages 50–107, 7-year follow-up); 6 conscientiousness facets
- Industriousness strongest predictor (~25% reduced mortality per SD); only facet significant when all entered simultaneously
- Self-control was unrelated to longevity — surprising given theoretical emphasis on impulse control
- Low internal consistency of 4-item facet scales (α=.45–.63) is a notable limitation

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates the importance of facet-level analysis: industriousness, not self-control, drives the C-mortality link. Key evidence for why domain-level personality-health research misses the action.

### 294. Tian et al. (2023)

**Citation:** Tian, Y. E., Cropley, V., Maier, A. B., Lautenschlager, N. T., Breakspear, M., & Zalesky, A. (2023). Heterogeneous aging across multiple organ systems and prediction of chronic disease and mortality. *Nature Medicine, 29*, 1221–1231. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41591-023-02296-6

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal, Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** UK Biobank brain imaging (GM, WM, functional connectivity), physiological phenotypes (immune, pulmonary, cardiovascular, hepatic, renal, metabolic, musculoskeletal), telomere length, PGS
**Level of Detail:** Other (multiorgan biological aging)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* biological aging, organ age, chronic disease, mortality prediction, UK Biobank
- *Additional terms:* normative modeling, brain age, allostatic load, multiorgan aging network

#### Core Themes
- Normative models of biological age for 3 brain + 7 body systems using UK Biobank
- An organ's biological age selectively influences aging of other organ systems — reveals a multiorgan aging network
- Advanced biological aging extends from organ of primary disease to multiple systems across 16 chronic diseases
- Body age predicts survival (AUC=0.77) and premature death (AUC=0.86)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides a systems-level framework for understanding how aging is distributed across organ systems; relevant to allostatic load and stress physiology themes.

### 295. Tucker-Drob, Johnson, & Jones (2009)

**Citation:** Tucker-Drob, E. M., Johnson, K. E., & Jones, R. N. (2009). The cognitive reserve hypothesis: A longitudinal examination of age-associated declines in reasoning and processing speed. *Developmental Psychology, 45*(2), 431–446.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal
**Instruments:** Word Series, Letter Series, Letter Sets (reasoning); UFOV (processing speed); Vocabulary
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cognitive reserve, aging, reasoning, processing speed, education, vocabulary
- *Additional terms:* ACTIVE trial, preserved differentiation, differential preservation, retest effects, latent growth

#### Core Themes
- ACTIVE trial control group (N=690, ages 65–89, 5-year follow-up, 6 assessment occasions)
- Education and vocabulary predicted cognitive levels but not rates of maturational decline
- Supports preserved-differentiation (earlier advantages persist) over differential-preservation (reserve slows decline)
- Careful separation of retest effects from maturational change in latent growth models

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Clarifies the cognitive reserve concept: education provides a level advantage, not a decline buffer. Important for interpreting personality-cognition aging interactions.

### 296. Williams, Suchy, & Kraybill (2010)

**Citation:** Williams, P. G., Suchy, Y., & Kraybill, M. L. (2010). Five-Factor Model personality traits and executive functioning among older adults. *Journal of Research in Personality, 44*(4), 485–491.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** NEO PI-R, D-KEFS (Trail Making, Design Fluency, Letter Fluency, Color-Word Inhibition Switching)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet, Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality, executive function, aging, NEO PI-R, D-KEFS
- *Additional terms:* antagonism, affiliation, Angry-Hostility, Trust, Warmth, Tender-Mindedness

#### Core Themes
- N=58 healthy older adults (ages 60–85, M=69.8); NEO PI-R + D-KEFS
- EF associated with N (r=−.31), O (r=.29), A (r=.38) but not E or C
- Facet-level: interpersonal antagonism/affiliation facets (Angry-Hostility, Trust, Warmth, Tender-Mindedness) drove domain associations
- Conscientiousness unexpectedly unrelated to EF

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates facet-level specificity in personality-EF associations among older adults; the antagonism/affiliation selectivity is informative for personality-cognition theorizing.

### 297. Yoneda et al. (2022)

**Citation:** Yoneda, T., Graham, E., Lozinski, T., Bennett, D. A., Mroczek, D., Piccinin, A. M., Hofer, S. M., & Muniz-Terrera, G. (2022). Personality traits, cognitive states, and mortality in older adulthood. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 123*(2). https://doi.org/10.1037/pspp0000418

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal
**Instruments:** NEO-FFI (C, N, E subscales)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality, cognitive impairment, dementia, mortality, multistate survival modeling
- *Additional terms:* Rush MAP, conscientiousness, neuroticism, MCI, health span, NCI

#### Core Themes
- Rush MAP (N=1,954; M age 80; up to 23 years annual assessment)
- Multistate survival modeling of transitions between NCI, MCI, dementia, and death
- Higher C decreased NCI-to-MCI risk (HR=0.78); higher N increased it (HR=1.12)
- E associated with MCI-to-NCI recovery
- Personality associated with cognitive health span but not total longevity

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Innovative multistate modeling reveals personality traits affect cognitive health span rather than total survival; C and N operate at the NCI-MCI transition point rather than later stages.

### 298. Ciobanu et al. (2023)

**Citation:** Ciobanu, L. G., Stankov, L., Ahmed, M., Heathcote, A., Clark, S. R., & Aidman, E. (2023). Multifactorial structure of cognitive assessment tests in the UK Biobank. *Frontiers in Psychology, 14*, 1054707. https://doi.org/10.3389/fpsyg.2023.1054707

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** UK Biobank cognitive battery (9 tests)
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, g

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* UK Biobank, cognitive structure, CHC theory, ESEM, fluid intelligence, processing speed
- *Additional terms:* visuospatial Gf, verbal-analytic Gf, Gs, bifactor model

#### Core Themes
- Combined EFA + ESEM on 9 UK Biobank cognitive tests (N=3,425)
- Three-factor structure: visuospatial Gf, verbal-analytic Gf, and processing speed (Gs)
- Single g-factor less adequate than three-factor model for these tests
- Bespoke UK Biobank tests mapped to CHC framework

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides the cognitive structure underlying UK Biobank data used in many genetic and epidemiological studies; useful reference when interpreting UK Biobank cognitive measures.

### 299. Deary, Weiss, & Batty (2010)

**Citation:** Deary, I. J., Weiss, A., & Batty, G. D. (2010). Intelligence and personality as predictors of illness and death. *Psychological Science in the Public Interest, 11*(2), 53–79.

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** (Discusses) NEO PI-R, NEO-FFI, Moray House Test, Wechsler scales, various IQ tests
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet, Cognitive Ability, g

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* intelligence, personality, health, mortality, morbidity, differential epidemiology
- *Additional terms:* system integrity, health behaviors, SES, cascade models, cognitive epidemiology

#### Core Themes
- Comprehensive review monograph: both intelligence and Big Five predict illness and death with effect sizes comparable to traditional risk factors
- Mechanisms: health behaviors, SES, patient-practitioner interactions, system integrity
- Effect sizes for intelligence and personality on health are comparable to smoking
- Proposes improved analytical approaches including latent variable modeling and cascade models

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Premier integrative review establishing cognitive epidemiology and personality-health as mature fields; provides the conceptual framework for understanding why both intelligence and personality predict health outcomes.

### 300. Irwing, Cook, & Hughes (2023)

**Citation:** Irwing, P., Cook, C., & Hughes, D. J. (2023). Toward an index of adaptive personality regulation. *Personality and Social Psychology Bulletin, 51*(5), 659–677.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** BFI, Self-Monitoring Scale, NEO-PI-R (Study 2 expert ratings)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* adaptive personality regulation, personality dynamics, situational demands, performance, self-monitoring
- *Additional terms:* APR index, personality expression, comedian performance, Big Five variability

#### Core Themes
- Proposes APR (Adaptive Personality Regulation) — ability to match personality expression to situational requirements
- Study 1 (N=88, lab): two tasks requiring opposite E levels; Study 2 (N=203): professional/amateur comedians
- APR discriminant from mean-level personality and self-monitoring (near-zero correlations)
- Incremental prediction of task/job performance (4.9–35.5% additional variance)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Novel construct capturing personality flexibility rather than average trait levels; relevant to understanding individual differences in personality regulation.

### 301. Irwing, Hughes, Tokarev, & Booth (2024)

**Citation:** Irwing, P., Hughes, D. J., Tokarev, A., & Booth, T. (2024). Towards a taxonomy of personality facets. *European Journal of Personality, 38*(3), 494–515.

**Purpose:** Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Factor Analysis, Instrument Validation, Systematic Review, Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R, CPI, 16PF5, MPQ, JPI-R, HEXACO-PI, 6PFQ, Facet MAP V1
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet, Item-Level

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality facets, taxonomy, Facet MAP, factor analysis, discriminant validity
- *Additional terms:* ESCS, 70 facets, CFA, measurement invariance, open-access instrument

#### Core Themes
- Multi-stage program: Study 1 factor-analyzed 1,772 items from 7 inventories (ESCS, n=972), yielding 61 base facets
- Study 2: systematic review of 706 scales identified 16 novel facets not captured in major inventories
- Study 3 (N=1,096): CFA validation of 77 facet scales; 70 robust facets after redundancy checks
- Facet MAP V1 is open-access (CC BY-NC-SA); spans Big Five and beyond (Spirituality, Hedonism, Tolerance for Ambiguity, Vengeance, etc.)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
The most comprehensive attempt to map the full personality facet space; 70 facets significantly expand the 30-facet NEO-PI-R framework. Essential reference for any facet-level personality analysis.

### 302. Martindale et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Martindale, J.-P., Hughes, D. J., Irwing, P., & Rose, L. (2025). Development and validation of the Faceted Dark Triad (FDT) Scale. *Assessment*, 1–22. https://doi.org/10.1177/10731911251388351

**Purpose:** Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Factor Analysis, Instrument Validation, Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** EPA-SF, FFNI-SF, FFMI, FDT (long/short/super-short), SD4, BFI-2-XS
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet, Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Dark Triad, narcissism, Machiavellianism, psychopathy, faceted measurement, FDT
- *Additional terms:* jingle-jangle, IRT, test-retest reliability, antisocial behavior

#### Core Themes
- Consolidates 3 FFM-based Dark Triad measures (46 facets, 184 items) into 27 non-redundant FDT facets
- Study 1 (N=870): CFA, IRT, convergent validity (r=.92–.97 with parent scale totals)
- Study 2 (N=201): test-retest over ~390 days (all r_tt>.77)
- Study 3 (N=338): FDT outperformed SD4 by 37% in predicting antisocial outcomes

**Relevance to Book Project:** LOW
Primarily a psychometric contribution for Dark Triad measurement; relevant if antagonistic traits are a focus.

### 303. Ritchie & Tucker-Drob (2018)

**Citation:** Ritchie, S. J., & Tucker-Drob, E. M. (2018). How much does education improve intelligence? A meta-analysis. *Psychological Science, 29*(8), 1358–1369.

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis
**Instruments:** Various IQ/cognitive ability tests
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, g

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* education, intelligence, meta-analysis, quasi-experimental, IQ, causal
- *Additional terms:* natural experiments, policy change, school-age cutoff, control-prior-intelligence

#### Core Themes
- 142 effect sizes from 42 data sets (N=615,812) across 3 quasi-experimental designs
- ~3.4 IQ points per additional year of education (overall estimate)
- School-age-cutoff design yielded largest estimates (5.2 IQ points); control-prior-intelligence smallest (1.2)
- Effects persisted across lifespan and across test types; minimal publication bias

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Definitive meta-analytic estimate of the causal effect of education on intelligence; essential for understanding the education-cognition-personality nexus.

### 304. Steptoe & Jackson (2018)

**Citation:** Steptoe, A., & Jackson, S. E. (2018). The life skills of older Americans: Association with economic, psychological, social, and health outcomes. *Scientific Reports, 8*, 9669. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41598-018-27909-w

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Longitudinal
**Instruments:** MIDI Personality Scales (C, Emotional Stability), LOT (Optimism), single items for Persistence and Control, CES-D, UCLA Loneliness
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Other

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* life skills, non-cognitive skills, aging, health, wellbeing, conscientiousness, emotional stability
- *Additional terms:* HRS, dose-response, cumulative advantage, optimism, persistence, control

#### Core Themes
- HRS (N=8,843, mean age 72.57); five "life skills" assessed cumulatively
- More life skills associated with greater wealth, better emotional wellbeing, stronger social relationships, better health, less obesity
- Dose-response gradients: 4–5 skills group consistently showed best outcomes
- No single skill responsible; cumulative count mattered across all outcome domains

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates cumulative personality/motivational advantages in later life; the dose-response finding supports viewing personality as a portfolio of resources.

### 306. Whitman et al. (2024)

**Citation:** Whitman, E. T., Ryan, C. P., Abraham, W. C., ... Caspi, A. (2024). A blood biomarker of the pace of aging is associated with brain structure: Replication across three cohorts. *Neurobiology of Aging, 136*, 23–33.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Longitudinal
**Instruments:** DunedinPACE, Horvath clock, Hannum clock, PhenoAge, GrimAge, structural MRI
**Level of Detail:** Other (biological aging / brain structure)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* DunedinPACE, epigenetic aging, brain structure, hippocampal volume, cortical thickness
- *Additional terms:* Dunedin Study, Framingham Heart Study, ADNI, biological age, DNA methylation

#### Core Themes
- Three cohorts spanning mid- to late-life (Dunedin N=770, age 45; Framingham N=903, ~age 64; ADNI N=649, ~age 75)
- Faster DunedinPACE consistently associated with smaller brain volume, hippocampal volume, more WM microlesions, thinner cortex
- DunedinPACE and GrimAge showed strongest brain associations; first-generation clocks (Horvath, Hannum) showed null associations
- Small longitudinal ADNI subsample (N=107–147) showed null associations with rate of brain change

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Validates blood-based biological aging measures against brain structure; relevant to understanding how cumulative physiological wear manifests in the brain.
