### 307. Benthin et al. (1993)

**Citation:** Benthin, A., Slovic, P., & Severson, H. (1993). A psychometric study of adolescent risk perception. *Journal of Adolescence*, *16*(2), 153–168. https://doi.org/10.1006/jado.1993.1014

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** Custom 14-characteristic risk perception battery
**Level of Detail:** Other (risk perception dimensions)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* risk perception, adolescence, psychometric paradigm, risk-taking behavior, peer influence
- *Additional terms:* Slovic psychometric paradigm, problem behavior syndrome, availability heuristic, personal fable, cognitive dissonance, risk characteristics

#### Core Themes
- First application of Slovic's psychometric paradigm (previously used with adults) to adolescent risk perception
- 41 high-school students (ages 14–18, Eugene OR) rated 30 risk behaviors on 14 characteristics (knowledge, fear, personal risk, risk to peers, benefits vs. risks, seriousness, controllability, avoidability, peer influence, admiration, etc.)
- PCA of 14 characteristics yielded two orthogonal factors: Factor 1 "Risk" (68% variance: perceived risk, fear, seriousness) and Factor 2 "Admiration" (15% variance: admiration, knowledge)
- Participants in an activity vs. non-participants perceived: greater knowledge of risks, less fear, less personal risk, greater benefits relative to risks, more personal control, greater peer pressure, less desire for regulation, and higher perceived participation rate by peers
- 50 of 55 intercorrelations among 11 problem behaviors were positive, supporting a "problem behavior syndrome" — adolescents who engage in one risky activity tend to engage in others
- Participants who engaged in risky activities reported knowing the risks well but simultaneously believing they could not avoid the activity — possibly reflecting peer pressure sensitivity and illusion of control ("personal fable")

#### Results Summary

**Risk characteristic factor structure (PCA, varimax rotation):**

| Factor | Variance | Key loadings |
|--------|----------|-------------|
| Risk | 68% | Perceived risk, fear, seriousness of effects |
| Admiration | 15% | Admiration by peers, knowledge of risks |

**Mean ratings across 30 activities (7-point scales):**
- Avoidability: M = 5.73 (highest overall)
- Knowledge: M = 4.0
- Personal risk: M = 3.95
- Risk to peers: M = 3.94
- Peer influence: M = 2.44 (lowest overall)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates that adolescents' risk perceptions are structured by both cognitive-evaluative (risk) and social-motivational (admiration) dimensions; connects to heuristics literature (availability, optimistic bias) and developmental risk-taking.

---

### 308. Billieux et al. (2021)

**Citation:** Billieux, J., Heeren, A., Rochat, L., Maurage, P., Bayard, S., Bet, R., ... Baggio, S. (2021). Positive and negative urgency as a single coherent construct: Evidence from a large-scale network analysis in clinical and non-clinical samples. *Journal of Personality*, *89*(6), 1252–1262. https://doi.org/10.1111/jopy.12655

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis, Instrument Validation
**Instruments:** s-UPPS-P (short UPPS-P Impulsive Behavior Scale, 20 items, 4 per subscale)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* impulsivity, negative urgency, positive urgency, network analysis, UPPS
- *Additional terms:* Walktrap community detection, bridge centrality, Gaussian graphical model, graphical LASSO, transdiagnostic risk factor, emotion reactivity

#### Core Themes
- Tests whether positive and negative urgency are best conceptualized as one or two constructs, using item-level network analysis rather than traditional latent variable approaches
- Non-clinical sample: N = 18,568 (M_age = 26.55, 60.9% women) from 38 independent databases (29 non-clinical + 9 clinical) across France, Belgium, Switzerland; all French-speaking
- Clinical sample: N = 385 (M_age = 36.54, 39.7% women) with mood, anxiety, and addictive disorders
- Walktrap community detection identified four clusters in both samples: general urgency (positive + negative urgency items combined), lack of perseverance, lack of premeditation, and sensation seeking
- Strongest edges were within-cluster; within-cluster edge CIs did not overlap with between-cluster edge CIs
- No network differences by gender
- Urgency cluster connected to other clusters via non-null edges: 17 edges to lack of premeditation, 15 to lack of perseverance, 12 to sensation seeking (non-clinical sample)
- Traditional CFA of 4-factor and 5-factor models showed comparable fit, reinforcing that collapsing urgency facets is defensible

#### Results Summary

**Network communities (both samples):**
1. General Urgency — all positive and negative urgency items
2. Lack of Perseverance
3. Lack of Premeditation
4. Sensation Seeking

**Strongest within-cluster edge (non-clinical):** items 7–17 (both general urgency), estimate = 0.38, 95% CI [0.36–0.39]
**Strongest between-cluster edge (non-clinical):** items 5–6 (perseverance–premeditation), estimate = 0.13, 95% CI [0.12–0.15]

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly informs the structural organization of impulsivity facets; the collapsing of positive and negative urgency into a single "general urgency" construct has implications for how emotion-driven impulsive behavior relates to personality structure (urgency maps to N/NE in broader models).

---

### 309. DeYoung et al. (2007)

**Citation:** DeYoung, C. G., Quilty, L. C., & Peterson, J. B. (2007). Between facets and domains: 10 aspects of the Big Five. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology*, *93*(5), 880–896. https://doi.org/10.1037/0022-3514.93.5.880

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis, Instrument Validation
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R, AB5C (Abridged Big Five Circumplex), BFAS (Big Five Aspect Scales, 100 items)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Aspect, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, personality structure, facets, aspects, factor analysis, BFAS
- *Additional terms:* hierarchical personality structure, Eugene-Springfield Community Sample, genetic variance, metatraits, Stability, Plasticity

#### Core Themes
- Proposes an intermediate level of personality structure between broad Big Five domains and narrow facets: 10 "aspects," two per domain
- Factor analysis of the 6 NEO-PI-R facets within each Big Five domain consistently yielded two-factor solutions in the Eugene-Springfield Community Sample (N = 481)
- The 10 aspects: **N** → Volatility (angry hostility, impulsiveness, vulnerability) & Withdrawal (anxiety, depression, self-consciousness); **E** → Enthusiasm (warmth, gregariousness, positive emotions) & Assertiveness (assertiveness, activity, excitement-seeking); **O** → Intellect (ideas, actions, values) & Openness (aesthetics, feelings, fantasy); **A** → Compassion (trust, altruism, tender-mindedness) & Politeness (straightforwardness, compliance, modesty); **C** → Industriousness (competence, achievement striving, self-discipline) & Orderliness (order, dutifulness, deliberation)
- Constructed the 100-item BFAS (10 items per aspect) using IPIP items; validated in a second independent sample (N = 480)
- Correlated the 10 aspects with genetic factor scores from a prior twin study (Jang et al., 2002); genetic factors corresponded well to the phenotypic aspects, suggesting that aspects capture genetically distinct sources of variance within each Big Five domain

#### Results Summary

**Factor analysis — variance explained by 2 factors within each domain (NEO-PI-R facets):**

| Domain | Factor 1 | Factor 2 | Aspect 1 | Aspect 2 |
|--------|----------|----------|----------|----------|
| N | 50% | 23% | Volatility | Withdrawal |
| E | 44% | 22% | Enthusiasm | Assertiveness |
| O | 38% | 20% | Intellect | Openness |
| A | 40% | 24% | Compassion | Politeness |
| C | 49% | 19% | Industriousness | Orderliness |

**BFAS internal consistency (alpha, validation sample N = 480):**
- Range: .75 (Openness) to .88 (Assertiveness)
- Mean: ~.82

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational for the aspect-level analysis of personality structure; provides the hierarchical framework (domains → aspects → facets) central to understanding how personality phenotypes are organized. The BFAS is widely used in subsequent research on personality–brain and personality–genetics associations.

---

### 310. Harden et al. (2017)

**Citation:** Harden, K. P., Kretsch, N., Mann, F. D., Herzhoff, K., Tackett, J. L., Steinberg, L., & Tucker-Drob, E. M. (2017). Beyond dual systems: A genetically-informed, latent factor model of behavioral and self-report measures related to adolescent risk-taking. *Developmental Cognitive Neuroscience*, *25*, 221–234. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.dcn.2016.12.007

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Twin/Genetic, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** UPPS Impulsivity Scale, Future Orientation Scale (Planning, Time Perspective, Future Consequences), Risk Perception Scale, Pubertal Development Scale, Iowa Gambling Task, BART-Youth, Stoplight Task, Tower of London, Delay Discounting Task, WASI
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet, Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* dual systems, cognitive control, reward seeking, impulsivity, self-control, intelligence, delay discounting, risk-taking, adolescence
- *Additional terms:* ESEM, nomological network, construct validity, Texas Twin Project, behavior genetics, task-specific variance, heritability

#### Core Themes
- Investigates the factor structure and genetics of 15 self-report and behavioral measures commonly used in "dual systems" research on adolescent risk-taking
- 810 adolescents (385 twin pairs, 12 triplet sets, 1 quadruplet set) from the Texas Twin Project (M_age = 15.9, SD = 1.4; 57% non-Hispanic White, 18% Hispanic/Latino, 15% Black/African American)
- ESEM identified four factors: (1) **Premeditation** — UPPS Premeditation + Future Orientation subscales (self-report); (2) **Fearlessness** — Risk Perception subscales, with small UPPS Urgency and Sensation Seeking loadings; (3) **Reward Seeking** — IGT, BART, Stoplight (behavioral tasks); (4) **Cognitive Dyscontrol** — Tower of London errors, IGT bad decks, delay discounting, less time perspective (behavioral + self-report)
- UPPS Perseverance dropped — null associations with all behavioral measures
- Residual factor correlations were mostly minimal except Premeditation–Fearlessness (r = −.47)
- Behavioral measures had substantial task-specific variance (median 11% of total variance accounted for by factors vs. large residuals)

#### Results Summary

**Factor heritabilities (A, E proportions):**

| Factor | A (genetic) | C (shared env) | E (nonshared env) |
|--------|-------------|----------------|-------------------|
| Premeditation | .31 | — | .69 |
| Fearlessness | .36 | — | .65 |
| Cognitive Dyscontrol | .63 | — | .37 |
| Reward Seeking | .66 | — | .34 |
| FSIQ | .72 | .04 (ns) | .24 |

**Key genetic correlations (below diagonal) and non-shared environmental correlations (above diagonal):**

| | Premed | Fearless | Cog Dysc | Reward | FSIQ |
|---|--------|----------|----------|--------|------|
| Premeditation | — | | | | |
| Fearlessness | .75 | — | | | |
| Cog Dyscontrol | −.50 | −.42 | — | | |
| Reward Seeking | .10 | .12 | .67 | — | |
| FSIQ | .30 | −.05 | **−.91** | .35 | — |

**Covariate effects on factors:**
- Age: ↑ Premeditation, ↑ Fearlessness, ↓ Cognitive Dyscontrol
- Sex (male): ↓ Premeditation, ↑ Fearlessness, ↑ Reward Seeking
- Pubertal development: ↑ Reward Seeking, ↓ Cognitive Dyscontrol
- FSIQ: ↑ Reward Seeking, ↓↓ Cognitive Dyscontrol (β = −.59)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that "cognitive control" in dual-systems models conflates separable personality (premeditation/planning) and ability (cognitive dyscontrol/IQ) dimensions; the genetic correlation of −.91 between cognitive dyscontrol and IQ suggests these are essentially the same construct at the genetic level. Critical for understanding how impulsivity phenotypes are constructed from heterogeneous measures.

---

### 311. MacKillop et al. (2016)

**Citation:** MacKillop, J., Weafer, J., Gray, J. C., Oshri, A., Palmer, A., & de Wit, H. (2016). The latent structure of impulsivity: Impulsive choice, impulsive action, and impulsive personality traits. *Psychopharmacology*, *233*(18), 3361–3370. https://doi.org/10.1007/s00213-016-4372-0

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** UPPS-P Impulsive Behavior Scale (59 items), BIS-11 (30 items), Delay Discounting Task (iterative, 80 trials), Monetary Choice Questionnaire (27 items), Go/NoGo Task, Stop Signal Task, Conners CPT
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet, Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* impulsivity, addiction, delay discounting, behavioral inhibition, impulsive personality, confirmatory factor analysis
- *Additional terms:* impulsive choice, impulsive action, UPPS-P, BIS-11, sensation seeking, latent structure, substance use, tripartite model

#### Core Themes
- Tests a three-domain hypothesis of impulsivity: impulsive choice (discounting of delayed rewards), impulsive action (inability to inhibit prepotent responses), and impulsive personality traits (self-reported self-regulatory attributions)
- N = 1,252 young adults (62% female, M_age = 21.5, 84% White) from two sites (U Chicago, U Georgia), screened for low substance use (AUDIT ≤ 11, DUDIT ≤ 11, negative drug screen)
- CFA compared 1-factor, 2-factor, 3-factor, and 4-factor models; 3-factor model with Sensation Seeking removed provided best fit (CFI = .978, RMSEA = .044, SRMR = .033)
- Sensation Seeking did not load on any of the three factors (λ < .2) — excluded from final model
- The three factors were largely uncorrelated: Impulsive Choice–Action r = .01; Choice–Traits r = −.10**; Action–Traits r = .16**

#### Results Summary

**Final 3-factor model fit (Model 3C):**
CFI = .978, RMSEA = .044, SRMR = .033

**Factor loadings (standardized):**

| Factor | Indicators | Loadings |
|--------|-----------|----------|
| Impulsive Choice | DDT $100, MCQ-$80, MCQ-$55, MCQ-$25 | .86, .92, .94, .90 |
| Impulsive Action | CPT commissions, GNG commissions, SST errors | .69, .67, .42 |
| Impulsive Personality Traits | Neg Urgency, Premeditation, Perseverance, Nonplanning, Attention, Motor, Pos Urgency | .56–.78 |

**Associations with substance use (SEM, controlling for demographics):**
- AUDIT: Impulsive Choice (β = .088, p = .001) + IPTs (β = .206, p < .001)
- DUDIT: IPTs only (β = .164, p < .001)
- Smoking: IPTs only (β = .137, p < .001)
- Impulsive Action: no significant associations with any substance measure
- Sensation Seeking (analyzed separately): AUDIT r = .15, DUDIT r = .15, Smoking r = .08

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides direct evidence that "impulsivity" is not a unitary construct — three empirically separable domains are identified using both self-report and behavioral measures, and these domains have distinct associations with substance use. The exclusion of sensation seeking from all three factors supports its conceptualization as a separate trait (approach motivation rather than self-regulation failure).

---

### 312. Miller & Gizer (2024)

**Citation:** Miller, A. P., & Gizer, I. R. (2024). Dual-systems models of the genetic architecture of impulsive personality traits: Neurogenetic evidence of distinct but related factors. *Psychological Medicine*, *54*(8), 1533–1543. https://doi.org/10.1017/S0033291723003367

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Twin/Genetic, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** UPPS-P (GWAS summary statistics), BIS-11 (GWAS), TCI Novelty Seeking/Harm Avoidance (GWAS), risk-taking/adventurousness (GWAS), neuroimaging GWAS (cortical volume, surface area, thickness, subcortical volume)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* impulsivity, dual-systems model, GWAS, GenomicSEM, sensation seeking, urgency, self-control, cortical thickness
- *Additional terms:* genomic structural equation modeling, neuroimaging genetics, LDSC, endophenotype, bottom-up/top-down processing, prefrontal cortex

#### Core Themes
- First GenomicSEM investigation of dual-systems models (bottom-up approach behaviors vs. top-down self-control) for impulsive personality traits using GWAS summary statistics
- Two correlated-factors models tested: (1) SSSC (sensation seeking–self-control): bottom-up "sensation seeking" factor (adventurousness, risk-taking, UPPS-P sensation seeking) + top-down "lack of self-control" factor (BIS-11, UPPS-P lack of premeditation, TCI novelty seeking); (2) UGSC (urgency–self-control): bottom-up "urgency" factor (UPPS-P negative/positive urgency, TCI harm avoidance) + same top-down factor
- SSSC model: good fit (χ² = 10.67, df = 8, CFI = 1.00, SRMR = .09); r_g between factors = .60 (SE = .12, p < .001)
- UGSC model: satisfactory fit after adding residual covariances (χ² = 10.67, df = 5, CFI = .95, SRMR = .09); r_g = .42 (SE = .22, p = .063, ns)
- Multivariate GWAS for sensation seeking identified 1,092 independent GWS variants (N_eff = 710,971); no GWS variants for self-control or urgency (underpowered, N_eff ~28K)

#### Results Summary

**Neuroimaging genetic correlations (FDR-corrected):**

| Dual-systems factor | Cortical volume | Cortical surface area | Cortical thickness | Subcortical volume |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Sensation seeking | ns | Positive, >25% regions | ns | ns |
| Lack of self-control | ns | ~2 regions | Negative, >60% regions (max r_g = −.41 PFC) | ns |
| Urgency | ns | ~1 region | Negative, rostral middle frontal (r_g = −.39) | ns |

**Key SNP-based heritability estimates (ζ_g):**
- Sensation seeking: ζ_g = 0.087 (SE = .003)
- Lack of self-control: ζ_g = 0.072 (SE = .019)
- Urgency: ζ_g = 0.093 (SE = .022)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Extends phenotypic impulsivity structure findings to the genomic level; demonstrates that dual-systems constructs are genetically separable but correlated. The finding that lack of self-control is genetically associated with reduced cortical thickness (especially PFC) provides neurogenetic evidence for theoretical models linking prefrontal maturation to impulse control.

---

### 313. Sharma et al. (2014)

**Citation:** Sharma, L., Markon, K. E., & Clark, L. A. (2014). Toward a theory of distinct types of "impulsive" behaviors: A meta-analysis of self-report and behavioral measures. *Psychological Bulletin*, *140*(2), 374–408. https://doi.org/10.1037/a0034418

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** 58 self-report impulsivity/personality scales (BIS, UPPS, NEO-PI-R, EPQ, BAS, SNAP, etc.) and 15 laboratory behavioral tasks (Go/NoGo, Stop Signal, Stroop, Iowa Gambling Task, Delay of Gratification, BART, WCST, etc.)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet, Cognitive Ability

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* impulsivity, disinhibition, meta-analysis, trait-behavior link
- *Additional terms:* impulsigenic traits, Extraversion/Positive Emotionality, Disinhibition vs. Constraint, Neuroticism/Negative Emotionality, behavioral inhibition, delay discounting, executive function, externalizing, substance use, ADHD

#### Core Themes
- Three integrated meta-analytic studies addressing the structure of impulsivity across self-report traits, laboratory behavioral tasks, and daily-life impulsive behaviors

**Study 1 — Self-report impulsivity scales:**
- Meta-analytic 58 × 58 correlation matrix of impulsivity and related personality scales (125 studies, ~121 normal + 4 clinical samples)
- PCA with promax rotation yielded 3 correlated factors:
  - **Extraversion/Positive Emotionality (E/PE):** anchored by EPQ Extraversion, MPQ Positive Emotionality, EAS Sociability, NEO Activity, UPPS Sensation Seeking (loadings .58–.84)
  - **Disinhibition vs. Constraint/Conscientiousness (DvC/C):** anchored by I₇ Impulsivity, DII Dysfunctional Impulsivity, SNAP Disinhibition, UPPS (lack of) Premeditation, NEO-PI-R Conscientiousness facets (reversed) (loadings .65–.95)
  - **Neuroticism/Negative Emotionality (N/NE):** anchored by SNAP Negative Temperament, EPQ Neuroticism, NEO-PI-R Neuroticism, EAS Emotionality, UPPS Urgency, BIS Attentional Impulsivity (loadings .40–.89)
- DvC/C and N/NE correlated r = −.55; DvC/C and E/PE r = .32; E/PE and N/NE r = .08
- DvC/C further splits into two subfactors: Disinhibition vs. Constraint (traditional impulsivity — I₇, DII, SNAP Disinhibition, UPPS Premeditation) and Conscientiousness/Will vs. Resourcelessness (NEO-PI-R C facets, UPPS Perseverance, EAS Emotionality reversed)

**Study 2 — Laboratory behavioral tasks:**
- Meta-analytic 15 × 15 correlation matrix of behavioral impulsivity tasks (98 studies)
- PCA with promax rotation yielded 4 factors:
  - **Inattention:** Stroop error, Delayed Memory, Immediate Memory, Porteus Maze (negative)
  - **Inhibition:** Go/NoGo, MFFT latency, Circle Tracing, MFFT error (negative)
  - **Impulsive Decision-Making:** Delay of Gratification (hypothetical + contingent), Stop Task, IGT
  - **Shifting:** WCST perseverative errors, WCST nonperseverative errors
- Interfactor correlations uniformly low (−.03 to .13)

**Study 3 — Relations with daily-life impulsive behaviors:**
- Weighted correlations of self-report scales and lab tasks with alcohol use, drug use, cigarettes, aggression, delinquency, gambling, risky sex
- Self-report scales more strongly related to daily-life behaviors than lab tasks (grand mean r: self-report ~.22–.27 vs. lab tasks ~.17–.29 for specific factor–behavior pairs)
- Both self-report and behavioral measures independently predict daily-life behaviors; joint use adds incremental validity (R² increments .01–.15)
- Disinhibition (vs. Constraint) and N/NE most strongly related to externalizing behaviors (r_means = .27, .28); E/PE Sensation Seeking most related to substance use and risky sex

#### Results Summary

**Self-report factor intercorrelations:**
- DvC/C × N/NE: r = −.55
- DvC/C × E/PE: r = .32
- E/PE × N/NE: r = .08

**Lab task factor intercorrelations:** all near zero (range −.03 to .13)

**Cross-method correlations (grand mean):** r = .02 (self-report × lab task), confirming near-complete independence

**Strongest daily-life behavior correlations (self-report scales):**
- Delinquency: I₇ Impulsivity r = .66, DvC/C mean r = .55
- Drugs: I₇ Impulsivity r = .41, N/NE mean r = .32
- Gambling: Contingent delay r = .49, Inattention mean r = .44

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Major synthesis establishing that "impulsivity" is not a unitary construct but maps onto three distinct personality trait dimensions plus four independent cognitive/behavioral task dimensions. The near-zero cross-method correlations are a critical finding for understanding the phenotypic architecture of self-regulation. Directly informs how multiple measurement modalities each capture real but largely non-overlapping variance in impulsive behavior.

---

### 314. Bavelas et al. (2000)

**Citation:** Bavelas, J. B., Coates, L., & Johnson, T. (2000). Listeners as co-narrators. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 79*(6), 941–952. https://doi.org/10.1037/0022-3514.79.6.941

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** Custom behavioral coding system for listener responses (generic vs. specific); story quality rating scales
**Level of Detail:** Other (communication / conversational behavior)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* listener responses, co-narration, back channels, collaborative dialogue, face-to-face communication
- *Additional terms:* generic responses, specific responses, narrative storytelling, conversational collaboration, distraction paradigm

#### Core Themes
- Listeners make two functionally distinct types of responses: generic (audience/observer, e.g., nodding, "mhm") and specific (co-narrator/actor, e.g., facial displays, mimicry, verbal interjections)
- Specific listener responses are tightly connected to narrative content and occur later in the story than generic responses
- Distracted listeners produce significantly fewer specific responses, while generic responses are less affected
- Narrators told to distracted listeners produce stories with significantly more negative ending features (abrupt, choppy, justifying)
- Listeners are active co-narrators who help produce the narrative, not passive recipients
- Face-to-face dialogue is fundamentally collaborative — the listener's contribution is essential to narrative quality
- Effect sizes substantial for specific responses (η² = .62 in Exp. 2)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates collaborative construction of meaning in communication; relevant to understanding social-cognitive processes and how interpersonal context shapes the products of cognition.

---

### 315. Brunell et al. (2008)

**Citation:** Brunell, A. B., Gentry, W. A., Campbell, W. K., Hoffman, B. J., Kuhnert, K. W., & DeMarree, K. G. (2008). Leader emergence: The case of the narcissistic leader. *Personality and Social Psychology Bulletin, 34*(12), 1663–1676. https://doi.org/10.1177/0146167208324101

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** NPI (Raskin & Terry, 1988), CPI narcissism scale, BFI (John & Srivastava, 1999), Rosenberg Self-Esteem Inventory
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Other (narcissism dimensions: power and exhibitionism)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* narcissism, leader emergence, emergent leadership, leaderless group discussion, power
- *Additional terms:* exhibitionism, NPI, Big Five, self-esteem, group dynamics, multilevel modeling

#### Core Themes
- Narcissism reliably predicts leader emergence in leaderless group discussions across three studies with undergraduate and executive samples
- The power dimension of narcissism (not exhibitionism) is the primary driver, remaining significant after controlling for Big Five, self-esteem, and sex
- Narcissism does not predict actual task performance (Study 2) — narcissists emerge as leaders but are no better at the task
- Even trained expert raters perceive narcissistic individuals as group leaders (Study 3)
- The paradox: traits facilitating leadership emergence (self-confidence, dominance, power-seeking) are the same traits associated with leadership problems

#### Results Summary

**Study 1 (N = 432 undergraduates):**
- NPI total → leader emergence (peer-rated): β = .13, p < .01
- NPI Power → leader emergence: β = .23, p < .001 (controlling for Big Five, self-esteem, sex)

**Study 2 (N = 408 undergraduates):**
- NPI → leader emergence: β = .15, p < .01; NPI → task performance: ns

**Study 3 (N = 153 executive MBA managers):**
- CPI narcissism → expert-rated leader emergence: β = .17, p < .05

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Illustrates how individual difference traits shape social outcomes; relevant to understanding narcissism as a personality phenotype and its interpersonal consequences.

---

### 316. Campbell et al. (2004)

**Citation:** Campbell, W. K., Bonacci, A. M., Shelton, J., Exline, J. J., & Bushman, B. J. (2004). Psychological entitlement: Interpersonal consequences and validation of a self-report measure. *Journal of Personality Assessment, 83*(1), 29–45. https://doi.org/10.1207/s15327752jpa8301_04

**Purpose:** Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis, Instrument Validation, Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** Psychological Entitlement Scale (PES, 9-item, new), NPI (Raskin & Terry, 1988), BFI (Goldberg, 1992), Rosenberg Self-Esteem Inventory, BIDR (Paulhus, 1991), Love Attitudes Scale
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Other (entitlement)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* psychological entitlement, PES, narcissism, NPI, scale development
- *Additional terms:* individual differences, self-concept, aggression, ego threat, commons dilemma, romantic relationships, interpersonal consequences

#### Core Themes
- The PES is a reliable, valid, 9-item single-factor measure of psychological entitlement as a stable individual difference (α = .85–.88)
- PES is distinct from the NPI Entitlement subscale (CFA confirms two-factor model; r = .64 between latent variables)
- Not associated with social desirability and not redundant with any Big Five trait
- Entitlement predicts selfish behavior: taking more candy designated for children, claiming greater salary in cost-cutting scenarios
- Associated with competitive/greedy choices in commons dilemmas (driven by acquisitive motivation, not fear)
- In romantic relationships: dismissing attachment, less accommodation, lower empathy, less respect, more game-playing love
- Predicts aggression specifically after ego threat (criticism), not after praise
- Small gender difference: men slightly higher (d = .20)
- Test-retest reliability: r = .72 (1 month), r = .70 (2 months)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides a tool for measuring a key self-concept variable; demonstrates how entitlement as an individual difference shapes interpersonal behavior across contexts.

---

### 317. Chotai & Wiseman (2005)

**Citation:** Chotai, J., & Wiseman, R. (2005). Born lucky? The relationship between feeling lucky and month of birth. *Personality and Individual Differences, 39*(8), 1451–1460. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.paid.2005.06.012

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** Custom 13-item luck and personality survey (5-point Likert; not previously validated)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Other (luck beliefs, season of birth)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* season of birth, luck, personality, neurotransmitter turnover, monoamines
- *Additional terms:* Neuroticism, Extraversion, Openness, gender differences, cosine regression, attributable fraction

#### Core Themes
- Summer-born UK participants (peak May) report feeling significantly luckier than winter-born (nadir November)
- Effect holds for both genders and all age groups among UK-born participants
- Luck beliefs correlate with lower Neuroticism, higher Openness, and higher Extraversion
- Each personality factor shows the same seasonal variation (May-max, November-min)
- Attributable fraction (~3.4%) comparable to single gene-allele effects on personality
- Consistent with prior research linking season of birth to monoamine neurotransmitter turnover and personality traits
- Gender differences: men higher on feeling lucky and optimism; women higher on intuition, perseverance, sociability

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Connects prenatal/perinatal environmental influences to adult personality traits through season-of-birth effects; relevant to understanding how mental phenotypes emerge through developmental processes.

---

### 318. Colman (2006)

**Citation:** Colman, D. R. (2006). The three princes of Serendip: Notes on a mysterious phenomenon. *McGill Journal of Medicine, 9*(2), 161–163.

**Purpose:** Commentary/Editorial
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other (philosophy of science / history of science)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* serendipity, scientific discovery, prepared mind
- *Additional terms:* Fleming, penicillin, X-rays, Cajal, Golgi, neuron doctrine, history of science

#### Core Themes
- Serendipity defined as "the faculty of making happy and unexpected discoveries by accident" (OED), requiring a "prepared mind" (Pasteur)
- Reviews famous historical examples: Fleming/penicillin, Roentgen/X-rays, Clarke/Rh disease, Brock/Thermus aquaticus, Viagra, Cajal/Golgi stain
- Sometimes the serendipitous insight is missed by the original investigator and recognized later (e.g., Wilson's sponge experiments recognized by E.E. Just)
- A 3-page essay with no systematic methodology; entirely illustrative

**Relevance to Book Project:** TANGENTIAL
Historical context for serendipity in science, but offers no empirical or theoretical framework relevant to mental phenotypes.

### 319. Copeland (2019)

**Citation:** Copeland, S. (2019). On serendipity in science: Discovery at the intersection of chance and wisdom. *Synthese, 196*(6), 2385–2406. https://doi.org/10.1007/s11229-017-1544-3

**Purpose:** Review (philosophical analysis)
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other (philosophy of science / epistemology)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* serendipity, scientific discovery, emergence, epistemic expectations, retrospectivity
- *Additional terms:* prepared mind, philosophy of science, community norms, information sharing, chance, wisdom, abduction

#### Core Themes
- Serendipity is an emergent property of scientific discovery, describing an oblique relationship between outcomes and intentions
- It is a process (not a moment), involving cognitive, temporal, and social connections
- Can only be recognized retrospectively, once the value of the outcome is established
- Epistemic expectations play a central role: serendipity occurs when expectations about sources of knowledge are overturned
- Individual skills (openness, adaptability, "generative doubt") can be cultivated but are constrained by community/environmental factors
- Egalitarian, information-sharing scientific communities generate more serendipitous discoveries
- Has normative implications: supports early publication and broader conceptions of scientific credit

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides a conceptual framework for understanding how chance and prepared cognition interact in knowledge production; connects to broader themes about how environmental structure shapes discovery processes.

---

### 320. DeMarree & Bobrowski (2018)

**Citation:** DeMarree, K. G., & Bobrowski, M. E. (2018). Structure and validity of self-concept clarity measures. In J. Lodi-Smith & K. G. DeMarree (Eds.), *Self-concept clarity: Perspectives on assessment, research, and applications* (pp. 1–17). Springer. https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-71547-6_1

**Purpose:** Book Chapter (Review with new empirical data)
**Methodology:** Factor Analysis, Instrument Validation, Narrative Review
**Instruments:** SCCS (Campbell et al., 1996), certainty ratings, extremity of self-ratings, response latency, consistency measures, latitudes of self-description, Rosenberg Self-Esteem Scale, CES-D, self-efficacy, satisfaction with life scale
**Level of Detail:** Other (self-concept clarity measurement)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* self-concept clarity, SCCS, measurement validity, self-esteem, factor structure
- *Additional terms:* ESEM, certainty, extremity, response latency, consistency, latitudes, cultural boundaries, incremental validity

#### Core Themes
- Six measurement strategies for SCC (SCCS, certainty, extremity, response latency, consistency, latitudes) do not converge on a single construct (median inter-correlation r = .09)
- ESEM reveals three distinct SCC factors: (1) SCCS, (2) certainty/extremity, (3) latitudes
- Each factor shows unique patterns of relationships with criterion variables (depression, self-efficacy, physical symptoms, narrative coherence)
- Self-esteem is strongly correlated with SCC and accounts for much of its predictive utility — many SCC effects are eliminated or substantially weakened when self-esteem is controlled
- SCC measures interact with self-esteem (high SCC buffers low-self-esteem individuals against depression)
- Current SCC conceptualization is culturally bounded (Western, individualistic)
- Recommendation: researchers should specify which SCC feature they are measuring

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Directly relevant to understanding measurement challenges in psychological science; illustrates how assessment strategies shape conclusions about mental phenotypes.

---

### 321. DeMarree et al. (2023)

**Citation:** DeMarree, K. G., Chang, Y.-H., Lee, T., & Venezia, A. (2023). Listening and attitude change. *Current Opinion in Psychology, 53*, 101641. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.copsyc.2023.101641

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other (attitude change, listening behavior)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* listening, high-quality listening, attitude change, attitude strength
- *Additional terms:* attitude ambivalence, attitude clarity, psychological safety, open-minded self-reflection, prejudice reduction, canvassing, receptiveness, persuasion

#### Core Themes
- High-quality listening involves verbal and nonverbal behaviors indicating attention, understanding, and care — distinct from mere agreement
- Receiving high-quality listening reduces state anxiety, increases psychological safety, and promotes open-minded self-reflection
- Speakers who receive high-quality listening report less extreme attitudes, greater objective ambivalence, and greater attitude clarity
- The increased ambivalence does not produce conflict, suggesting psychological safety allows comfort with divergent views
- High-quality listening in canvassing interventions (Kalla & Broockman) durably reduces prejudice toward marginalized groups
- Persuasive intent may undermine the psychological safety that characterizes high-quality listening
- Listening also affects attitudes toward the listener (liking) and toward the self (buffering self-esteem threats)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Relevant to understanding how interpersonal processes shape attitude formation and change; connects to broader themes about social construction of beliefs and judgments.

---

### 322. Dumas & Campbell (2026)

**Citation:** Dumas, D., & Campbell, W. K. (2026). Exploring the creative personality: Using machine learning to predict fluency and originality in divergent thinking. *Creativity Research Journal*. Advance online publication. https://doi.org/10.1080/10400419.2024.2371725

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** Big Five Aspects Scale (BFAS), Alternate Uses Task (AUT), Open Creativity Scoring (automated DT scoring via LLMs)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Aspect, Item-Level

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five Aspects Scale, BFAS, divergent thinking, creativity, fluency, originality, LASSO regression
- *Additional terms:* machine learning, Openness, Enthusiasm, Withdrawal, Alternate Uses Task, creative personality, Industriousness, Orderliness

#### Core Themes
- LASSO selected 15 BFAS items predicting fluency and 20 items predicting originality from the full 100-item pool
- Enthusiasm (Extraversion) and low Withdrawal (reversed Neuroticism) emerged as strongest predictors of fluency — not Openness
- Openness items (e.g., "I often daydream," "I enjoy the beauty of nature") were the strongest predictors of originality
- The creative personality involves a tension between seemingly opposed attributes (high enthusiasm but also high withdrawal/negative emotionality)
- Industriousness and Orderliness items were consistently negatively associated with both fluency and originality
- Fluency and originality have distinct personality profiles, supporting their distinction as separate DT components
- Machine learning (LASSO) provides useful complement to traditional OLS for sparse predictor identification

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates how specific personality facets/items differentially predict creative cognition, illustrating the importance of aspect/item-level analysis over domain-level for understanding psychological phenotypes.

### 323. Dumas & Dunbar (2016)

**Citation:** Dumas, D., & Dunbar, K. N. (2016). The creative stereotype effect. *PLoS ONE, 11*(2), e0142567. https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0142567

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** Alternate Uses Task (AUT), Latent Semantic Analysis (LSA) for scoring originality
**Level of Detail:** Other (creative cognition / stereotypes)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* creative stereotype effect, divergent thinking, perspective-taking, stereotype activation
- *Additional terms:* Alternate Uses Task, Latent Semantic Analysis, originality, fluency, creative cognition, social cognition

#### Core Themes
- Participants adopting an "eccentric poet" perspective generated significantly more original responses on the AUT than those adopting a "rigid librarian" perspective or a control condition
- The effect replicated within-subjects (Study 2), suggesting it is not merely a between-group artifact
- The effect was found for originality but not consistently for fluency, suggesting perspective-taking selectively enhances idea quality
- LSA-based automated scoring produces results consistent with human ratings
- Creativity may be better understood as malleable and context-dependent rather than a fixed trait
- Effect sizes were moderate to large

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates context-dependence of creative cognition; relevant to understanding how psychological capacities are shaped by situational factors beyond stable traits.

### 324. Erdelez (2000)

**Citation:** Erdelez, S. (2000). Towards understanding information encountering on the Web. *Proceedings of the 63rd Annual Meeting of the American Society for Information Science, 37*, 363–371.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** Custom survey instrument measuring information encountering behavior on the Web
**Level of Detail:** Other (information behavior)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* information encountering, serendipity, accidental information discovery, Web browsing
- *Additional terms:* information behavior, information seeking, super-encounterers, user differences

#### Core Themes
- Information encountering (IE) is a common experience: most respondents reported accidentally finding useful information online
- Users can be categorized from "super-encounterers" (who frequently experience IE and adjust behavior to facilitate it) to "non-encounterers" (who rarely experience it)
- Super-encounterers tend to actively modify their information environment to increase chances of accidental discovery
- IE on the Web shares features with IE in physical environments but has unique characteristics (hyperlink following, multitasking)
- IE is distinct from serendipity in that it involves a more specific, identifiable process with recognizable stages

**Relevance to Book Project:** LOW
Peripheral to core personality/mental phenotypes focus, but relevant to understanding individual differences in information behavior and how people interact with information environments.

### 325. Erdelez (2005)

**Citation:** Erdelez, S. (2005). Information encountering. In K. E. Fisher, S. Erdelez, & L. McKechnie (Eds.), *Theories of information behavior* (pp. 179–184). Information Today.

**Purpose:** Book Chapter
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other (information behavior theory)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* information encountering, serendipity, accidental information discovery
- *Additional terms:* information behavior theory, background information needs, incidental information acquisition

#### Core Themes
- Information encountering defined as a memorable experience of unexpected discovery of useful or interesting information
- Occurs during background information needs while actively seeking information for a foreground need
- Five stages: noticing, stopping, examining, capturing, and returning
- Individual differences in propensity for IE — people range from super-encounterers to non-encounterers
- Related to but distinct from serendipity, browsing, and incidental information acquisition
- Has implications for information system design

**Relevance to Book Project:** LOW
Provides theoretical framework for understanding how people accidentally discover information; tangentially relevant to individual differences in cognition and information processing.

### 326. Heinström (2003)

**Citation:** Heinström, J. (2003). Five personality dimensions and their influence on information behaviour. *Information Research, 9*(1), paper 165.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** NEO-FFI, custom information behavior questionnaire
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, NEO-FFI, information behavior, information seeking, personality
- *Additional terms:* Openness to Experience, Neuroticism, Conscientiousness, fast surfing, broad scanning, deep diving, academic information seeking

#### Core Themes
- Neuroticism associated with anxiety during information seeking, lower confidence, and superficial searching
- Extraversion related to broader, more social approach to information seeking (willingness to interact with librarians)
- Openness to Experience was the strongest predictor of deep, exploratory information seeking and incidental information discovery
- Conscientiousness related to systematic, thorough searching strategies
- Agreeableness showed weak relationships with information behavior
- Three information-seeking patterns: "fast surfing" (high N, low O, low C), "broad scanning" (high E, high O), and "deep diving" (high C, high O)
- Personality influences how people approach, evaluate, and use information, not just whether they seek it

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Directly connects Big Five personality domains to information behavior, demonstrating how stable traits shape cognition-relevant behavior in real-world contexts.

### 327. Heinström (2006)

**Citation:** Heinström, J. (2006). Fast surfing for availability or deep diving into quality — motivation and information seeking among middle and high school students. *Information Research, 11*(4), paper 265.

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** Achievement Goal Questionnaire, Study Process Questionnaire (SPQ), custom information-seeking questionnaire
**Level of Detail:** Other (motivation / information behavior)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* information seeking, motivation, achievement goals, study approach
- *Additional terms:* fast surfing, deep diving, middle school, high school, surface learning, deep learning, academic motivation

#### Core Themes
- Two distinct information-seeking patterns among middle/high school students: "fast surfing" (minimal effort, superficial) and "deep diving" (thorough, quality-focused)
- Low motivation and surface study approach predicted fast surfing; high motivation and deep study approach predicted deep diving
- Mastery goal orientation associated with deep diving; performance-avoidance orientation associated with fast surfing
- Making topics engaging is key to promoting deeper information-seeking behavior
- Study approach mediated the relationship between motivation and information seeking
- Findings replicate and extend the fast surfing / deep diving distinction from university to younger populations

**Relevance to Book Project:** LOW
Focuses on motivation and information seeking in younger populations; tangential to core personality-phenotype questions but illustrates how motivational orientations shape information behavior.

### 328. Kernis & Goldman (2006)

**Citation:** Kernis, M. H., & Goldman, B. M. (2006). A multicomponent conceptualization of authenticity: Theory and research. *Advances in Experimental Social Psychology, 38*, 283–357. https://doi.org/10.1016/S0065-2601(06)38006-9

**Purpose:** Review (with original empirical data)
**Methodology:** Narrative Review, Factor Analysis, Instrument Validation, Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** Authenticity Inventory (AI-3, 45 items), NEO-FFI, MAAS, KIMS, DVBA, Ryff PWB Scale, COPE, multiple self-esteem measures, Rempel Trust Scale, Rusbult Accommodation Measure, Buri Parental Authority Questionnaire
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet, Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* authenticity, Authenticity Inventory, self-knowledge, awareness, unbiased processing
- *Additional terms:* behavioral authenticity, relational orientation, self-esteem, well-being, mindfulness, self-concept, coping, self-determination theory, CFA, eudaimonia

#### Core Themes
- Authenticity = "unimpeded operation of one's true- or core-self in daily life," comprising four components: awareness, unbiased processing, behavioral authenticity, and relational orientation
- CFA (N = 294) supports a hierarchical model: four first-order factors under a second-order authenticity factor (SRMSR = .049, RMSEA = .074, CFI = .94, TLI = .92)
- Authenticity relates to lower verbal defensiveness in structured interviews (behavioral validation via DVBA)
- Correlates positively with mindfulness (MAAS, KIMS), self-actualization, vitality, and psychological well-being
- Predicts adaptive coping (active coping, planning) and inversely predicts maladaptive coping (denial, substance use)
- In romantic relationships: predicts greater self-disclosure, trust, intrinsic relationship motives, relationship satisfaction
- Recalled parental authoritativeness (not authoritarianism) predicts adult authenticity
- Authenticity predicts well-being independently of self-esteem level
- Unbiased Processing subscale has modest reliability (α = .64)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides a detailed, empirically grounded framework for understanding authenticity as a psychological phenotype with developmental antecedents (parenting), structural properties (four-component CFA model), and functional consequences (well-being, relationships, coping). Directly relevant to how mental phenotypes are conceptualized and measured.

### 329. Kluger & Itzchakov (2022)

**Citation:** Kluger, A. N., & Itzchakov, G. (2022). The power of listening at work. *Annual Review of Organizational Psychology and Organizational Behavior, 9*, 121–146. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-orgpsych-012420-091013

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A (reviews multiple listening measures across studies)
**Level of Detail:** Other (interpersonal behavior / organizational outcomes)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* listening, episodic listening theory, togetherness, dyadic interaction
- *Additional terms:* psychological safety, organizational behavior, leadership, trust, well-being, self-disclosure, authenticity, job performance, creativity, social relations model

#### Core Themes
- Listening encompasses three causally related constructs: unobservable listener behaviors (attention, comprehension, intention), observable listener behaviors, and speaker perceptions/evaluations
- Strongly associated with job performance (r̄ = .38–.47 with sales), leadership (r = .50–.70), trust, intimacy, relational satisfaction
- Increases speakers' psychological safety, self-knowledge, attitude complexity, creativity, and well-being while reducing burnout
- Individual differences moderate effects: avoidant attachment attenuates benefits; trait social anxiety augments them
- Proposed "episodic listening theory": listening creates dyadic episodes of "togetherness" (shared attention, shared reality, positivity resonance) that facilitate divergent thinking and change
- About half of explained variance in listening perceptions is attributable to unique dyads, not individual traits
- Authenticity is a critical antecedent of effective listening (Rogers, 1951)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Connects interpersonal behavior to psychological safety, self-knowledge, and cognitive flexibility. Relevant to understanding how social environments shape psychological functioning and the dyadic nature of psychological processes.

### 330. Maltby et al. (2008)

**Citation:** Maltby, J., Day, L., Gill, P., Colley, A., & Wood, A. M. (2008). Beliefs around luck: Confirming the empirical conceptualization of beliefs around luck and the development of the Darke and Freedman beliefs around luck scale. *Personality and Individual Differences, 45*(7), 655–660. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.paid.2008.07.010

**Purpose:** Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** Darke and Freedman Belief in Good Luck Scale, Irrational Beliefs Scale, Paranormal Beliefs Scale
**Level of Detail:** Other (luck beliefs)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* belief in luck, Darke and Freedman scale, irrational beliefs, paranormal beliefs
- *Additional terms:* individual differences, factor analysis, scale validation, locus of control

#### Core Themes
- Confirmed the Darke and Freedman Belief in Good Luck Scale has a coherent factor structure
- Belief in luck correlates with irrational thinking and paranormal beliefs
- Luck belief is conceptually distinct from locus of control
- The scale captures a unidimensional construct but conflates different aspects (belief in luck as a force vs. personal luckiness)
- Findings support treating luck beliefs as a legitimate individual-difference variable
- Provided groundwork for later reconceptualizations (e.g., Thompson & Prendergast, 2013)

**Relevance to Book Project:** TANGENTIAL
Validates a scale for measuring luck beliefs; minimal connection to personality phenotype construction.

### 331. Miller et al. (2011)

**Citation:** Miller, J. D., Hoffman, B. J., Gaughan, E. T., Gentile, B., Maples, J., & Campbell, W. K. (2011). Grandiose and vulnerable narcissism: A nomological network analysis. *Journal of Personality, 79*(5), 1013–1042. https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1467-6494.2010.00711.x

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** NPI, PNI, HSNS, NEO-PI-R, BSI, PANAS-X, ECR-R, SCID-II, Social Behavior Measure, negotiation task
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* narcissism, grandiose narcissism, vulnerable narcissism, nomological network, Five-Factor Model
- *Additional terms:* NEO-PI-R, NPI, PNI, personality pathology, personality disorders, self-report, informant report, thin-slice ratings

#### Core Themes
- Grandiose and vulnerable narcissism have strikingly different nomological networks across personality, psychopathology, interpersonal behavior, and social cognition
- Grandiose: low Agreeableness (especially), high Extraversion, low Neuroticism
- Vulnerable: high Neuroticism, low Agreeableness, low Extraversion
- Both share entitlement, aggression, and interpersonally antagonistic schemas
- Vulnerable narcissism strongly associated with depression, anxiety, hostility, negative affect, fearful attachment, borderline PD symptoms
- Grandiose narcissism largely unrelated to psychological distress but linked to antisocial, histrionic, and narcissistic PD symptoms
- PNI Self-Serving Self-Enhancement and Grandiose Fantasies scales loaded more on the vulnerable than grandiose factor
- Thin-slice stranger ratings confirmed grandiose narcissists viewed as more extraverted and less neurotic

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Comprehensive mapping of narcissism subtypes onto personality dimensions; relevant to understanding how seemingly related phenotypes (grandiose vs. vulnerable narcissism) have fundamentally different structural profiles.

### 332. Miwa et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Miwa, K., Yamashita, W., & Morita, S. (2025). Luck cognition in daily life: Effects of perceived control, positive cognitive bias, and affective experience on life satisfaction. *Scientific Reports, 15*, 18744. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41598-025-95457-1

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** Custom 13-item Luck Cognition Scale, Satisfaction With Life Scale (Japanese version), locus of control items
**Level of Detail:** Other (luck cognition, life satisfaction)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* luck cognition, perceived control, positive cognitive bias, affective experience, life satisfaction
- *Additional terms:* locus of control, individual differences, Japan, factor analysis

#### Core Themes
- Luck cognition involves at least three distinct components: perceived control over luck, positive cognitive bias (overestimating good luck), and affective experience of luck
- Perceived control over luck positively associated with internal locus of control and life satisfaction
- Positive cognitive bias uniquely associated with life satisfaction beyond perceived control
- Affective experience of luck (savoring good luck, not dwelling on bad) also predicted life satisfaction
- Three dimensions modestly correlated but empirically separable
- Extends Thompson & Prendergast's (2013) bidimensional model to three dimensions
- Near-miss and gambling-like cognitions differ from everyday luck cognition

**Relevance to Book Project:** TANGENTIAL
Extends the measurement of luck beliefs to three dimensions in a Japanese sample; minimal direct connection to personality phenotype construction.

### 333. Naragon-Gainey & DeMarree (2017)

**Citation:** Naragon-Gainey, K., & DeMarree, K. G. (2017). Structure and validity of measures of decentering and defusion. *Psychological Assessment, 29*(7), 935–954. https://doi.org/10.1037/pas0000405

**Purpose:** Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis, Instrument Validation
**Instruments:** EQ, TMS-D, CFQ, DDS, BAFT, BFI, PANAS, Rosenberg Self-Esteem Scale, PTQ, RRS-B, MEAQ, PSC, SCCS, ERQ, IDAS
**Level of Detail:** Other (cognitive mechanisms, metacognition)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* decentering, cognitive defusion, metacognition, mindfulness, factor structure, construct validity
- *Additional terms:* item valence, Observer Perspective, Reduced Struggle, psychometrics, scale validation, ACT

#### Core Themes
- Decentering/defusion measures only weakly to modestly correlated with each other (mean r = .29–.47)
- Item-level analyses reveal a 2-factor structure: "Observer Perspective" (OP) and "Reduced Struggle with Inner Experience" (RS)
- OP marked primarily by EQ, TMS-D, and DDS items; RS by BAFT and CFQ items
- RS nearly indistinguishable from neuroticism, negative affectivity, and perseverative thinking in correlational patterns
- Both factors show expected convergent validity with mindfulness and mental health, but questionable discriminant validity for RS
- Negative item valence significantly confounds measurement — 80% of items reference negatively valenced experiences
- After removing item-valence effects, associations with positive thoughts and self-inclusion reversed direction
- Practical recommendation: use multiple measures to cover both OP and RS

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Illustrates how item-level measurement artifacts (negative valence confounds) can distort conclusions about psychological constructs; relevant to understanding measurement challenges in characterizing mental phenotypes.

### 334. Paredes et al. (2026)

**Citation:** Paredes, B., Brinol, P., Santos, D., Moreno, L., Guyer, J. J., & Petty, R. E. (2026). Attitudes guiding social behavior as a function of perceived knowledge: The moderating role of epistemic vs. hedonic mindsets. *Journal of Experimental Social Psychology, 123*, 104857. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jesp.2025.104857

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** Custom attitude scales (semantic differential), behavioral intention scales, perceived knowledge measures, mindset manipulations
**Level of Detail:** Other (attitudes, attitude-behavior correspondence)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* attitude-behavior correspondence, perceived knowledge, epistemic mindset, hedonic mindset, attitude strength
- *Additional terms:* persuasion, meta-cognition, self-validation, meaning of knowledge, Elaboration Likelihood Model

#### Core Themes
- Higher perceived knowledge enhances attitude-behavior correspondence (ABC) under epistemic mindset but reverses ABC under hedonic mindset
- First demonstration that the traditional perceived-knowledge effect on ABC can be fully reversed
- Mechanism: mindset changes the meaning of knowledge — in epistemic contexts, knowledge signals accuracy; in hedonic contexts, low knowledge signals mystery and excitement
- Study 3 (pre-registered): moderated mediation through the meaning of knowledge
- Study 4: directly manipulating meaning of knowledge replicated the interaction
- Effects generalize across dating, hotel booking, and hiring contexts
- Effect sizes small-to-medium (Cohen's f² = .015–.071 for interactions)

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates how metacognitive context shapes the functional significance of knowledge for behavior, relevant to understanding the conditional nature of cognitive influences on action.

---

### 335. Petty et al. (2007)

**Citation:** Petty, R. E., Brinol, P., & DeMarree, K. G. (2007). The Meta-Cognitive Model (MCM) of attitudes: Implications for attitude measurement, change, and strength. *Social Cognition, 25*(5), 657–686. https://doi.org/10.1521/soco.2007.25.5.657

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other (attitudes, metacognition)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Meta-Cognitive Model, MCM, attitudes, implicit attitudes, explicit attitudes, automatic evaluation
- *Additional terms:* deliberative evaluation, validity tags, metacognition, attitude change, attitude strength, implicit ambivalence, dual attitudes, Elaboration Likelihood Model, prejudice

#### Core Themes
- MCM proposes attitude objects can be linked to both positive and negative evaluative associations, each tagged with validity/confidence meta-cognitions (true/false)
- Unlike dual-attitude models, MCM uses a single integrated representation, not separate implicit/explicit stores
- Validity tags can be stored and retrieved, affecting deliberative (but not easily automatic) measures
- "Implicit ambivalence": people have both positive and negative associations but reject one — producing automatic-level doubt without explicit conflict
- Attitude change can differentially affect automatic vs. deliberative measures depending on whether new evaluations are created or old ones negated
- Explains the sleeper effect: negation links weaken over time, allowing original evaluations to influence deliberative measures
- Elaboration increases automatic-deliberative measure correlations by strengthening both object-evaluation links and validity tags
- Novel implications for prejudice: negation tags on unwanted stereotypic associations

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Provides a sophisticated framework for understanding how metacognitive processes shape the expression of evaluative representations; relevant to how mental representations are constructed, stored, and revised.

---

### 336. Thompson & Prendergast (2013)

**Citation:** Thompson, E. R., & Prendergast, G. P. (2013). Belief in luck and luckiness: Conceptual clarification and new measure validation. *Personality and Individual Differences, 54*(4), 501–506. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.paid.2012.10.027

**Purpose:** Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis, Instrument Validation
**Instruments:** Belief in Luck and Luckiness Scale (12-item, new), Darke and Freedman Belief in Good Luck Scale, NEO-FFI
**Level of Detail:** Other (luck beliefs)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* belief in luck, luckiness, bidimensional model, scale validation
- *Additional terms:* personality, individual differences, irrational beliefs, wellbeing, factor analysis, measurement

#### Core Themes
- Luck beliefs are best conceptualized as bidimensional: Belief in Luck (BiL) and Belief in Personal Luckiness (BPL)
- BiL relates to irrationality and maladaptivity (correlates with Neuroticism, irrational beliefs)
- BPL relates to positive affect and subjective wellbeing (a positive individual-difference construct)
- The two dimensions are discrete and uncorrelated
- The new 12-item scale is validated across multiple samples (total N = 1,205)
- Existing unidimensional luck measures (e.g., Darke & Freedman) conflate these two distinct dimensions
- Applicable internationally and to both luck believers and non-believers

**Relevance to Book Project:** TANGENTIAL
Provides a refined measurement tool for luck beliefs; limited direct connection to personality phenotype construction.

### 337. Thompson et al. (2019)

**Citation:** Thompson, E. R., Prendergast, G. P., & Dericks, G. H. (2019). Personality, luck beliefs, and (non-?) problem lottery gambling. *Applied Research in Quality of Life, 16*, 703–722. https://doi.org/10.1007/s11482-019-09791-4

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** International English Big-Five Mini-Markers, Belief in Luck and Luckiness Scale, lottery gambling frequency measure
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* lottery gambling, Big Five personality, belief in luck, belief in personal luckiness, gambling behavior
- *Additional terms:* individual differences, Extraversion, Neuroticism, state-sponsored gambling, quality of life, Hong Kong

#### Core Themes
- Big Five partially predict non-problem lottery gambling: Extraversion (+), Neuroticism (+), Openness (−), Agreeableness (−) significant; Conscientiousness ns
- This profile parallels problem gambling
- Belief in Luck is the strongest single predictor (β = .51, OR = 1.67)
- Belief in Personal Luckiness is a modest negative predictor (β = −.18, OR = .84)
- Luck beliefs predict lottery gambling independently from and more strongly than Big Five
- State-sponsored lotteries may share psychological determinants with problem gambling

**Relevance to Book Project:** TANGENTIAL
Connects personality traits and luck beliefs to gambling behavior; limited direct relevance to personality phenotype construction.

### 338. Thompson & Prendergast (2022)

**Citation:** Thompson, E. R., & Prendergast, G. P. (2022). Belief in luck and luckiness: Conceptual clarification, new measure validation, and their relationship with happiness and the five-factor personality model. *Current Psychology, 41*, 29–40. https://doi.org/10.1007/s12144-019-00535-1

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** Belief in Luck and Luckiness Scale, Subjective Happiness Scale, I-PANAS-SF, LOT-R, International English Big-Five Mini-Markers
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Other (luck beliefs, happiness)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* luck beliefs, belief in luck, personal luckiness, happiness, subjective well-being
- *Additional terms:* positive affect, negative affect, optimism, five-factor model, Neuroticism, mediation, irrational beliefs

#### Core Themes
- Belief in Luck negatively associated with happiness; fully mediated by Neuroticism
- Belief in Personal Luckiness positively associated with happiness, positive affect, and optimism, negatively with negative affect
- Personal Luckiness shares unique variance with happiness beyond the Big Five
- The FFM does not mediate the Luckiness–happiness association — Luckiness may be a discrete trait not captured by the Big Five
- Luck beliefs and the Big Five each contribute unique variance to happiness measures

**Relevance to Book Project:** TANGENTIAL
Examines luck beliefs in relation to Big Five and happiness; tangential connection to personality structure.
