### 339. Trapnell & Campbell (1999)

**Citation:** Trapnell, P. D., & Campbell, J. D. (1999). Private self-consciousness and the five-factor model of personality: Distinguishing rumination from reflection. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 76*(2), 284–304. https://doi.org/10.1037/0022-3514.76.2.284

**Purpose:** Original Research / Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis, Instrument Validation
**Instruments:** Self-Consciousness Scales (SCS, Fenigstein et al., 1975), Rumination-Reflection Questionnaire (RRQ, 24-item, new), NEO-FFI, NEO-PI-R, IASR-B5, Rosenberg Self-Esteem Scale, PANAS, BDI, Need for Cognition Scale, Absorption Scale, RWA Scale
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet, Item-Level

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* private self-consciousness, rumination, reflection, self-focus, five-factor model
- *Additional terms:* Big Five, Neuroticism, Openness to Experience, self-absorption paradox, RRQ, Self-Consciousness Scales, PrSC, self-knowledge, psychological distress, self-attention, epistemic curiosity

#### Core Themes
- The Private Self-Consciousness Scale confounds two motivationally distinct dispositions: rumination (neurotic self-focus) and reflection (epistemic self-focus)
- Rumination correlates strongly with Neuroticism (.57–.64 across samples) and weakly/negatively with Openness
- Reflection correlates strongly with Openness to Experience (.61–.68) and weakly/not at all with Neuroticism
- The "self-absorption paradox" (PrSC associated with both self-knowledge and distress) is resolved: distress comes from rumination, self-knowledge from reflection
- The 24-item RRQ reliably separates these (α > .90, minimal inter-factor r = .22)
- PrSC correlations with distress are entirely accounted for by rumination; correlations with cognitive traits by reflection
- Four cognitive-emotional styles: sensitizing (high both), repressive (low both), vulnerable (low reflection, high rumination), adaptable (high reflection, low rumination)
- The FFM provides a comprehensive framework for reconceptualizing self-attentive dispositions

#### Results Summary

**RRQ–Big Five correlations (representative sample):**
- Rumination × N: r = .57–.64
- Reflection × O: r = .61–.68
- Rumination × O: r = .02–.10 (ns)
- Reflection × N: r = −.04–.10 (ns)

**RRQ psychometrics:**
- Rumination α = .90; Reflection α = .91
- Mean inter-item r: .42–.43
- Rumination–Reflection r = .22

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly addresses Big Five facet-level structure, the relationship between personality dimensions and self-attentional dispositions, and how motivational confounds in assessment instruments produce paradoxical findings. Demonstrates the power of personality-structural analysis for resolving long-standing conceptual puzzles.

---

### 340. Twenge et al. (2008)

**Citation:** Twenge, J. M., Konrath, S., Foster, J. D., Campbell, W. K., & Bushman, B. J. (2008). Egos inflating over time: A cross-temporal meta-analysis of the Narcissistic Personality Inventory. *Journal of Personality, 76*(4), 875–901. https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1467-6494.2008.00507.x

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis, Cross-Sectional (cross-temporal)
**Instruments:** NPI (40-item forced-choice; Raskin & Terry, 1988)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Other (narcissism total score)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* narcissism, Narcissistic Personality Inventory, NPI, cross-temporal meta-analysis, generational change
- *Additional terms:* birth cohort, individualism, self-esteem, college students, cultural change, time-lag method, sex differences

#### Core Themes
- NPI scores increased significantly from early 1980s to 2006 (β = .53, p < .001, d = 0.33)
- The increase is linear (~0.33 SD over 24 years); two-thirds of 2006 students above the 1979–1985 mean
- Increase driven primarily by women (β = .46, p < .002); men show no significant increase (β = .16, ns)
- The sex difference in narcissism narrowed from d = 0.45 to d = 0.15
- Results robust across multiple analytic strategies
- The generational shift is larger than the effect of violent video games on aggression and twice the current sex difference in narcissism
- Consistent with broader societal increases in individualism, self-esteem, assertiveness, and agency

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly addresses how a personality-related phenotype (narcissism) changes across cohorts/generations; relevant to developmental construction of mental phenotypes and cultural influences on personality.

---

### 341. Twenge (2025)

**Citation:** Twenge, J. M. (2025). Technology and cultural change. In D. T. Gilbert, S. T. Fiske, E. J. Finkel, & W. B. Mendes (Eds.), *The handbook of social psychology* (6th ed.). Situational Press. https://doi.org/10.70400/BZNV4498

**Purpose:** Book Chapter
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other (cultural change, generational differences)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cultural change, technology, individualism, collectivism, generational differences, birth cohort
- *Additional terms:* slow life strategy, life history theory, social media, mental health, depression, smartphones, narcissism, adolescence, emerging adulthood, cross-temporal meta-analysis

#### Core Themes
- "Technology Model of Cultural Change": technology drives cohort/time period differences through individualism and slow life strategy
- Individualism has increased globally, driven by socioeconomic development and technology
- "Slow life strategy" (delayed developmental milestones) emerges as technology reduces child mortality and extends lifespans
- Adolescents from Millennials onward are less likely to engage in adult activities (driving, dating, working) — consistent with slow life strategy
- Narcissism (NPI) showed curvilinear pattern: rising to ~2008, declining post-Great Recession
- Smartphones/social media associated with sharp rise in adolescent depression, anxiety, self-harm since early 2010s, observed internationally
- Social media contributes to political polarization by rewarding emotional/outrage-inducing content
- Technology is a primary driver of changes in women's roles and mental health trends

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly addresses how environmental/technological factors shape psychological phenotypes across development and generations; central to understanding how mental phenotypes are culturally constructed.

---

### 342. Wiseman (2003)

**Citation:** Wiseman, R. (2003). The luck factor. *The Skeptical Inquirer, 27*(3), 26–30.

**Purpose:** Commentary/Editorial
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** LOT-R (referenced informally)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Other (luck-related behaviors)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* luck, chance, Extraversion, Openness, Neuroticism, anxiety, intuition
- *Additional terms:* opportunity, social networks, self-fulfilling prophecy, optimism, counterfactual thinking, luck school, intervention

#### Core Themes
- Lucky people differ from unlucky people on personality dimensions: higher Extraversion (larger social networks), higher Openness (receptive to novelty), lower Neuroticism/anxiety (less attentional narrowing)
- Lucky people create chance opportunities through large social networks and openness to new experiences
- Lucky people make good decisions by relying on intuition and gut feelings
- Lucky people maintain positive expectations that become self-fulfilling prophecies
- Lucky people transform bad luck into good by seeing positive sides and not dwelling on misfortune
- A "luck school" intervention increased self-reported luck and life satisfaction after one month (80% reported being happier)
- Luck is not a magical ability but a set of behaviors and attitudes that can be learned

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Connects personality traits (Big Five) to real-world behavioral patterns (luck/opportunity detection); relevant to how individual differences shape life outcomes.

---

### 343. Womick et al. (2019)

**Citation:** Womick, J., Foltz, R. M., & King, L. A. (2019). "Releasing the beast within"? Authenticity, well-being, and the Dark Tetrad. *Personality and Individual Differences, 137*, 115–125. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.paid.2018.08.022

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional
**Instruments:** Authenticity Inventory (AI-3; 10-item abbreviated in Study 1, 45-item in Study 2), Short Dark Triad (SD3), NPI (40-item), Mach IV, LSRP, ASP, MLQ-P, SWLS, BIDR
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Other (Dark Tetrad, authenticity)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* authenticity, Dark Tetrad, Dark Triad, narcissism, Machiavellianism, psychopathy, sadism, well-being
- *Additional terms:* meaning in life, life satisfaction, self-esteem, basic need satisfaction, self-determination theory, humanistic psychology, moderation

#### Core Themes
- The Dark Tetrad moderates the authenticity–well-being link: among those high on dark traits, authenticity is less strongly related to well-being
- At low authenticity, dark traits associated with *higher* well-being — inauthenticity may be adaptive for those with dark personalities
- Moderation not attributable to social desirability bias (Study 2 controlled for BIDR)
- Narcissism positively associated with both authenticity and well-being; psychopathy and sadism negatively associated with authenticity
- Among those high on Dark Tetrad who endorsed inauthenticity, basic need satisfaction was not achieved yet well-being was not lowered
- "Being true to oneself" may not universally promote well-being if one's "true self" involves callous, manipulative tendencies
- Machiavellianism showed inconsistent moderation across studies

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Challenges universal humanistic assumptions about authenticity; relevant to understanding the conditional nature of personality–well-being links and the adaptive value of different personality configurations.

---

### 344. Young et al. (2009)

**Citation:** Young, M. J., Chen, N., & Morris, M. W. (2009). Belief in stable and fleeting luck and achievement motivation. *Personality and Individual Differences, 47*(2), 150–154. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.paid.2009.02.009

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** Belief in Luck Scale (BIL; Darke & Freedman, 1997), ad hoc Belief in Fleeting Luck subscale (new), Big Five (brief), Need for Achievement scale (Jackson, 1984), Achievement Goal Questionnaire (Elliot & Church, 1997)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Other (luck beliefs, achievement motivation)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* luck beliefs, stable luck, fleeting luck, achievement motivation, need for achievement
- *Additional terms:* mastery goals, Extraversion, Neuroticism, Big Five, self-efficacy, effort, persistence, feedback

#### Core Themes
- Belief in stable personal luck positively associated with achievement motivation (need for achievement and mastery goals) and with Extraversion
- Belief in fleeting luck negatively associated with achievement motivation and positively associated with Neuroticism
- After success, fleeting-luck believers increase effort (capitalizing on "lucky streak"); stable-luck believers do not change
- After failure, fleeting-luck believers decrease effort (accepting bad luck as temporary); stable-luck believers maintain effort
- Two types of luck beliefs have distinct personality correlates and motivational consequences
- Stable luck may function as self-efficacy or optimism; fleeting luck may reflect an anxious, uncontrollable worldview

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Connects individual differences in beliefs about chance and agency to personality structure and motivation; relevant to understanding how cognitive beliefs interface with personality traits to shape behavior.

---

### 345. Allport & Odbert (1936)

**Citation:** Allport, G. W., & Odbert, H. S. (1936). Trait-names: A psycho-lexical study. *Psychological Monographs, 47*(1, Whole No. 211), 1–171. https://doi.org/10.1037/h0093360

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Systematic Review (lexical enumeration)
**Instruments:** Webster's New International Dictionary (1925 edition)
**Level of Detail:** Item-Level (trait-descriptive terms)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* trait-names, personality, psycho-lexical, individual differences, character, temperament
- *Additional terms:* lexical hypothesis, trait taxonomy, personality descriptors, dispositional terms

#### Core Themes
- Extracted all English terms applicable to distinguishing one person's behavior from another's, yielding approximately 17,953 terms from the unabridged dictionary
- Four-column classification: (I) personal traits (~4,505 terms); (II) temporary states (~4,541); (III) social evaluations (~5,226); (IV) metaphorical/physical (~3,682)
- Extended philosophical discussion of the nature of traits, contrasting Realist, Nominalist, and "trait-hypothesis" positions
- Articulated the "trait-hypothesis": traits are genuine neuropsychic dispositions with neural counterparts, not mere verbal labels
- Identified that trait-names are socially constructed symbols freighted with ethical and cultural connotations

#### Results Summary
- Total terms extracted: ~17,953 from Webster's New International Dictionary
- Column I (personal traits): ~4,505 (25.1%); Column II (temporary states): ~4,541 (25.3%); Column III (social evaluations): ~5,226 (29.1%); Column IV (miscellaneous): ~3,682 (20.5%)
- No quantitative statistical analyses; contribution is the comprehensive lexical catalogue and theoretical framework

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational document for the psycholexical approach to personality structure — the intellectual ancestor of all subsequent Big Five and HEXACO taxonomies. Directly addresses how personality phenotypes are identified, named, and measured.

---

### 346. Christensen, Golino & Silvia (2020)

**Citation:** Christensen, A. P., Golino, H. F., & Silvia, P. J. (2020). A psychometric network perspective on the validity and validation of personality trait questionnaires. *European Journal of Personality, 34*(6), 1095–1108. https://doi.org/10.1002/per.2265

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A (discusses NEO-PI-R, BFI, HEXACO conceptually)
**Level of Detail:** Item-Level, Personality Facet, Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* network psychometrics, personality, validity, trait assessment, construct validation
- *Additional terms:* EBICglasso, Walktrap algorithm, structural consistency, network loadings, bootEGA, redundancy

#### Core Themes
- Argues psychometric network models offer a complementary framework to latent variable models for understanding personality structure and validating questionnaires
- Proposes that personality "traits" can be reconceptualized as emergent properties of causally connected behavioral/affective/cognitive components rather than reflections of a single latent cause
- Introduces three pillars of network-based construct validity: substantive validity (unique components), structural validity (bootEGA consistency), external validity (network composite prediction)
- Critiques alpha-based reliability; advocates for network-based redundancy analysis where items sharing >75% variance should be flagged
- Demonstrates mathematical equivalence between network loadings and factor loadings

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides a modern psychometric framework reconceptualizing how personality phenotypes should be measured and validated, directly challenging the dominant latent variable measurement paradigm.

---

### 347. Condon et al. (2020)

**Citation:** Condon, D. M., Wood, D., Mottus, R., Booth, T., Costantini, G., Greiff, S., Johnson, W., Lukaszewski, A., Murray, A., Revelle, W., Wright, A. G. C., Ziegler, M., & Zimmermann, J. (2020). Bottom up construction of a personality taxonomy. *European Journal of Psychological Assessment, 36*(6), 923–934. https://doi.org/10.1027/1015-5759/a000626

**Purpose:** Commentary/Editorial
**Methodology:** Narrative Review, Expert Consensus
**Instruments:** N/A (discusses IPIP, NEO-PI-R, HEXACO, BFI-2 conceptually)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet, Item-Level

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality assessment, taxonomy, bottom-up, psychometrics, item-level analysis
- *Additional terms:* nuances, hierarchical models, construct proliferation, test-retest reliability, jingle-jangle problems

#### Core Themes
- Proposes that personality taxonomy should be built from the item level rather than imposed from top-down theoretical models
- Based on an Expert Meeting jointly supported by EAPP and EAPA (Edinburgh, 2018)
- Advocates for a comprehensive item pool covering the full range of personality-relevant content
- Key psychometric principles: prioritize test-retest reliability over internal consistency; maximize unique predictive validity; minimize redundancy
- Notes that single items can have test-retest reliabilities comparable to multi-item scales (median r_tt ~ .65–.70 for best SAPA items)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Lays out the methodological program for building personality taxonomies from the bottom up — a fundamental shift in how personality phenotypes are identified and measured.

### 348. Cutler & Condon (2022)

**Citation:** Cutler, A., & Condon, D. M. (2022). Deep lexical hypothesis: Identifying personality structure in natural language. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 123*(4), 877–899. https://doi.org/10.1037/pspp0000443

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Factor Analysis, Cross-Sectional (NLP)
**Instruments:** DeBERTa language model, Saucier & Goldberg (1996) 435-adjective set, Allport & Odbert 17,913-term set, Goldberg & Norman 1,710-term set
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Item-Level

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* lexical hypothesis, natural language processing, personality structure, Big Five, transformer models
- *Additional terms:* DeBERTa, BERT, congruence coefficients, Big Three, semantic structure

#### Core Themes
- Tests the "deep lexical hypothesis": personality structure can be recovered from semantic relationships among personality-descriptive words as encoded in language models, without human ratings
- Study 1 replicates Saucier & Goldberg (1996) Big Five from original survey data (N = 899)
- Study 2 uses DeBERTa to generate vectorized representations via masked-token query strategy
- Study 3 sensitivity analyses across descriptor sets, queries, and models

#### Results Summary

| Finding | Value |
|---------|-------|
| Directional consistency (NLP vs. survey) | 75.1% of 94,395 pairwise correlations |
| Unrotated PC1-PC3 congruences | .89, .79, .79 |
| Varimax A, E, C congruences | .79, .80, .85 |
| Varimax N, I congruences | .45, .23 |

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that personality structure as measured by human ratings substantially reflects semantic structure encoded in language. The finding that only 3 components are robustly recoverable has major implications for personality structure models.

---

### 349. De Raad et al. (2010)

**Citation:** De Raad, B., Barelds, D. P. H., Levert, E., Ostendorf, F., Mlačić, B., Di Blas, L., Hřebíčková, M., Szirmák, Z., Szarota, P., Perugini, M., Church, A. T., & Katigbak, M. S. (2010). Only three factors of personality description are fully replicable across languages: A comparison of 14 trait taxonomies. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 98*(1), 160–173. https://doi.org/10.1037/a0017184

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Factor Analysis, Mega-Analysis
**Instruments:** 14 psycholexical trait taxonomies from 12 languages
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, cross-cultural, trait taxonomy, psycholexical, factor replicability, congruence
- *Additional terms:* Big Three, Extraversion, Agreeableness, Conscientiousness, Emotional Stability, Intellect, Honesty-Humility

#### Core Themes
- Compared factor structures from 14 independently developed psycholexical taxonomies across languages
- Used Tucker's congruence coefficient with .80 threshold for acceptable replication
- Only three factors — Extraversion, Agreeableness, Conscientiousness — replicate with acceptable congruence across all 14 languages
- Emotional Stability, Intellect, and Honesty-Humility do NOT generally replicate

#### Results Summary

| Factor | Cross-language replicability |
|--------|------------------------------|
| I. Extraversion | Replicable (≥ .80) |
| II. Agreeableness | Replicable |
| III. Conscientiousness | Replicable |
| IV. Emotional Stability | NOT generally replicable |
| V. Intellect | NOT generally replicable |
| VI. Honesty-Humility | NOT generally replicable |

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Landmark cross-cultural study demonstrating only three personality factors are universally replicable, directly challenging Big Five universality claims.

### 350. Golino, Nesselroade & Christensen (2025)

**Citation:** Golino, H. F., Nesselroade, J. R., & Christensen, A. P. (2025). The ergodicity information index: A new metric for quantifying the information loss when representing a set of within-person structures as a single between-person structure. *Psychological Methods*. Advance online publication. https://doi.org/10.1037/met0000716

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Monte Carlo Simulation, Dynamic Exploratory Graph Analysis (DynEGA)
**Instruments:** EGAnet R package, Directed Autoregressive Factor Score Model
**Level of Detail:** Item-Level, Personality Domain, Other (network structure)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* ergodicity, information index, network psychometrics, dynamic exploratory graph analysis, Kolmogorov complexity
- *Additional terms:* DynEGA, GLLA, multiplex networks, intraindividual structure, within-person variability

#### Core Themes
- Introduces the Ergodicity Information Index (EII), quantifying information lost when within-person structures are represented as a single between-person structure
- Uses Kolmogorov complexity theory to compare multiplex (all individual networks) against population network
- Monte Carlo simulation: 216 conditions, 500 replications each
- Three EII methods compared: unweighted (83.5% accuracy), weighted (81.4%), edge list (82.9%)
- Measurement error had the largest impact on accuracy (partial η² = .29–.32)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides a formal tool for testing when population-level personality structures accurately represent individual-level structures — central to the ergodicity question in personality science.

---

### 351. Granziol & Cona (2024)

**Citation:** Granziol, U., & Cona, G. (2024). Architecture and relationships among cognition, mental health and other human domains revealed by network analysis perspective. *Current Psychology, 43*, 4945–4960. https://doi.org/10.1007/s12144-023-04698-8

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis (EGA, Bayesian Networks)
**Instruments:** HCP database: NEO-FFI, NIH Toolbox Cognition Battery, ASR, Delay Discounting, AUDIT
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Cognitive Ability, Other (mental health, substance use, pain)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* network analysis, personality, cognitive functions, mental health, individual differences, HCP
- *Additional terms:* Bayesian networks, exploratory graph analysis, crystallized intelligence, delay discounting

#### Core Themes
- EGA on 1,206 HCP participants identified seven domains: Mental Health, Externalizing, High Cognitive Functions, Basic Cognitive Functions, Substance Use, Delay Discounting, Pain
- Crystallized intelligence was the most central variable (highest betweenness) in the entire network
- Cognitive functions clustered into two separate domains, supporting a hierarchical cognitive model
- Bayesian Network revealed HCF→BCF directional influence; Delay Discounting as a "crossroad" between HCF, MTL, and SUB

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Maps network architecture linking personality, cognition, mental health, and behavior in HCP data, showing how different categories of mental phenotypes interrelate structurally.

### 352. Henry & Mõttus (2025)

**Citation:** Henry, S., & Mõttus, R. (2025). 100 Nuances of Personality. *European Journal of Personality*. Advance online publication. https://doi.org/10.1177/08902070251314211

**Purpose:** Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Instrument Validation, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** 100 Nuances of Personality (100NP; 198 items), SAPA, IPIP, HEXACO-PI-R
**Level of Detail:** Personality Facet, Item-Level

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality assessment, nuances, item pool, test-retest reliability, bottom-up taxonomy
- *Additional terms:* cross-rater agreement, redundancy, bandwidth, efficiency

#### Core Themes
- Develops the 100NP, a 198-item pool maximizing bandwidth, reliability, and non-redundancy at nuance level
- Follows Condon et al. (2020) bottom-up approach; started from 696 items, iteratively reduced through 5 versions
- Selection weights: test-retest (.4), SD (.3), cross-rater agreement (.2), social evaluativeness (.05), clarity (.05)
- 136 items map to Big Five/HEXACO facets; >60 items cover content outside Big Five

#### Results Summary

| Statistic | Value |
|-----------|-------|
| Final items | 198 |
| Dev sample N | 858 (self-report), 416 (informant) |
| Test-retest interval | 12 days |
| Median r_tt | .69 (.56–.85) |
| Median r_ca | .37 (.18–.66) |
| Estonian Biobank N | > 77,000 |

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Exemplifies the bottom-up approach to personality measurement, demonstrating that a carefully curated item set can comprehensively assess personality at nuance level with much content lying outside Big Five.

### 353. Mulder (2021)

**Citation:** Mulder, R. T. (2021). ICD-11 personality disorders: Utility and implications of the new model. *Frontiers in Psychiatry, 12*, Article 655548. https://doi.org/10.3389/fpsyt.2021.655548

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** ICD-11 PD classification, PiCD, PID-5, DSM-5 AMPD
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Other (personality pathology)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality disorder, classification, domains, diagnosis, ICD-11
- *Additional terms:* dimensional model, severity, anankastia, disinhibition, borderline pattern qualifier, DSM-5 AMPD

#### Core Themes
- ICD-11 abolishes all ICD-10 PD categories for a single "personality disorder" diagnosis graded by severity (mild, moderate, severe) plus sub-threshold "personality difficulty"
- Severity is the strongest predictor of suffering and dysfunction
- Five trait domain qualifiers: negative affectivity, detachment, dissociality, anankastia, disinhibition; borderline pattern retained as pragmatic compromise
- ICD-11 trait domains align closely with DSM-5 AMPD; key difference is anankastia (ICD-11) vs. psychoticism (DSM-5)
- Four meta-analyses (52 samples, N = 13,640) confirm personality pathology can be represented as extreme scores on normal personality models

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Exemplifies how psychiatric nosology is shifting from categorical to dimensional trait models grounded in the same structural framework as normal personality.

### 354. Menardi et al. (2026)

**Citation:** Menardi, A., Ozer, Z. G., Vallesi, A., Cona, G., & Granziol, U. (2026). Linking individual behavioral architecture to brain topological structure: A combined network analysis approach. *Biological Psychology, 205*, Article 109235. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.biopsycho.2026.109235

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis (EGA), fMRI connectomics, GAMs
**Instruments:** HCP behavioral battery, NEO-FFI, resting-state fMRI (Schaefer atlas, 7 RSNs)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Cognitive Ability, Other (brain network topology)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* brain network topology, graph theory, resting-state fMRI, cognitive-behavioral domains
- *Additional terms:* connectome, FPN, DMN, participation coefficient, clustering coefficient, segregation, integration

#### Core Themes
- Used EGA on HCP behavioral measures to derive cognitive-behavioral domains, then related to resting-state brain topology via graph-theoretic metrics
- Efficient cognition associated with more segregated DMN but more integrated FPN and VAN
- Maladaptive domains (impulsivity, substance use) showed opposite pattern: greater DMN-SMN integration, DAN segregation
- Brain-behavior effect sizes generally small (rho ~0.1), consistent with large-sample literature

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates how brain network architecture maps onto cognitive and behavioral phenotypes, illustrating neural infrastructure underlying individual differences.

### 355. Michelini et al. (2024)

**Citation:** Michelini, G., Carlisi, C. O., Eaton, N. R., Elison, J. T., Haltigan, J. D., Kotov, R., Krueger, R. F., Latzman, R. D., Li, J. J., Levin-Aspenson, H. F., Salum, G. A., South, S. C., Stanton, K., Waldman, I. D., & Wilson, S. (2024). Where do neurodevelopmental conditions fit in transdiagnostic psychiatric frameworks? Incorporating a new neurodevelopmental spectrum. *World Psychiatry, 23*(3), 333–357. https://doi.org/10.1002/wps.21225

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review, Systematic Review
**Instruments:** References CBCL, ASEBA, PID-5, SRS, ASRS, various neurodevelopmental measures
**Level of Detail:** Other (transdiagnostic spectra)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* neurodevelopmental spectrum, ADHD, autism, transdiagnostic, HiTOP, dimensional, comorbidity
- *Additional terms:* learning disorders, intellectual disabilities, externalizing, internalizing, RDoC

#### Core Themes
- Proposes a transdiagnostic neurodevelopmental spectrum (ADHD, ASD, learning, intellectual, communication, motor disorders) within hierarchical models like HiTOP
- Reviews evidence across genetics/heritability, environmental risk, developmental course, temperament, cognition, neurobiology, and clinical utility
- Table 2 provides comprehensive evidence map across ~30 validators for 6 neurodevelopmental conditions
- Dimensional measures show higher reliability and temporal stability than categorical diagnoses

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH

### 356. Michelini et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Michelini, G., Liao, W., Lu, S., Caserini, C., Eley, T. C., Ronald, A., Wilson, S., Malanchini, M., & Rimfeld, K. (2025). The neurodevelopmental spectrum: Phenotypic architecture, etiology, and predictive utility across development. *medRxiv*. https://doi.org/10.1101/2025.07.31.25332492

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal, Twin/Genetic, Factor Analysis (ESEM)
**Instruments:** TEDS battery (75–180 items/wave), polygenic scores, cognitive measures (g, reading, exams)
**Level of Detail:** Other (neurodevelopmental spectrum factor), Cognitive Ability, g

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* neurodevelopmental spectrum, ADHD, autism, TEDS, twin study, heritability, polygenic scores
- *Additional terms:* ESEM, internalizing, externalizing, Goldberg's bass-ackwards, developmental trajectory

#### Core Themes
- First comprehensive empirical investigation of the neurodevelopmental spectrum across childhood and adolescence using TEDS longitudinal twin data (N ~7,000–8,000)
- ESEM identified a neurodevelopmental factor at ages 7, 12, 16, capturing ADHD and autism features plus learning/motor difficulties in childhood
- Twin analyses revealed increasing heritability: h² = .60 (age 7), .79 (age 12), .82 (age 16)
- Moderate-to-large genetic overlap across ages (r_g: .45–.70)
- Polygenic scores for neurodevelopmental conditions explained up to 3.36–3.93% of variance combined
- The factor predicted cognitive/educational outcomes (R² up to 20.61% cross-sectionally)

#### Results Summary

| Parameter | Age 7 | Age 12 | Age 16 |
|---|---|---|---|
| h² | .60 | .79 | .82 |
| e² | .40 | .16 | .18 |
| r_g with next wave | .45 | .70 | — |

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Most comprehensive empirical evidence for a neurodevelopmental spectrum phenotype, demonstrating structural replicability, strong genetic basis with increasing heritability, and predictive utility for real-world outcomes.

### 357. Ringwald et al. (2022)

**Citation:** Ringwald, W. R., Manuck, S. B., Marsland, A. L., & Wright, A. G. C. (2022). Psychometric evaluation of a Big Five personality state scale for intensive longitudinal studies. *Assessment, 29*(6), 1301–1319. https://doi.org/10.1177/10731911211008254

**Purpose:** Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Experience Sampling, Instrument Validation, Factor Analysis (MEFA)
**Instruments:** 20-item and 10-item Big Five state scales, BFI-2, NEO-PI-R, PANAS
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality assessment, personality states, intensive longitudinal design, multilevel modeling
- *Additional terms:* between-person variance, within-person variance, ICC, omega reliability, nomological net

#### Core Themes
- First systematic psychometric evaluation of Big Five personality state scales at both between- and within-person levels across three independent samples
- MEFA (pooled N = 1,041, 7,664 days) confirmed five-factor structure at both levels: CFI = .96, RMSEA = .03
- Between-person ω = .86; within-person ω = .51 — within-person reliability notably lower
- Average ICC = .61 (61% between-persons, 39% within-person fluctuation)
- ~64% of 220 nomological associations replicated across all three samples

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates that personality phenotypes can be validly measured at both stable trait and fluctuating state levels, illustrating how dimensional structure operates at different timescales.

---

### 358. Ringwald et al. (2023a)

**Citation:** Ringwald, W. R., Emery, L., Khoo, S., Clark, L. A., Kotelnikova, Y., Scalco, M. D., Watson, D., Wright, A. G. C., & Simms, L. J. (2023). Structure of pathological personality traits through the lens of the CAT-PD model. *Assessment, 30*(7), 2276–2295. https://doi.org/10.1177/10731911221143343

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis (EFA, ESEM), Measurement Invariance
**Instruments:** CAT-PD (33 trait scales), PID-5, BFI-2, NEO-PI-3, IPIP-NEO-120, FI-FFM, IIP-SC
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality disorders, transdiagnostic, assessment, dimensional trait models, maladaptive traits
- *Additional terms:* CAT-PD, Anankastia, Disinhibition, Psychoticism, ICD-11, DSM-5 Section III, target rotation

#### Core Themes
- First standalone structural evaluation of CAT-PD domain structure in large pooled sample (N = 3,987, 8 subsamples, 352 clinical + 3,635 non-clinical)
- Both 5- and 6-factor models fit acceptably; 6-factor separates Anankastia from Disinhibition
- Measurement invariance established across gender, sample type, and race
- CAT-PD domains showed good convergent validity with PID-5 (r = .63–.78) and lower intercorrelations (median r = .30 vs. PID-5 median r = .51)
- Self-Harm loaded on Psychoticism; Cognitive Problems and Fantasy Proneness loaded on Disinhibition

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Establishes the empirical structure of a comprehensive pathological personality measure, clarifying whether five or six domains best describe the maladaptive personality space.

### 359. Ringwald, Forbes & Wright (2023)

**Citation:** Ringwald, W. R., Forbes, M. K., & Wright, A. G. C. (2023). Meta-analysis of structural evidence for the Hierarchical Taxonomy of Psychopathology (HiTOP) model. *Psychological Medicine, 53*(2), 533–546. https://doi.org/10.1017/S0033291721001902

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** Structured/semi-structured diagnostic interviews (23 DSM diagnoses)
**Level of Detail:** Other (DSM diagnoses, psychopathology spectra)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* HiTOP, meta-analysis, factor analysis, transdiagnostic, psychopathology structure
- *Additional terms:* internalizing, externalizing, thought disorder, detachment, antagonism, disinhibition, general factor p

#### Core Themes
- First quantitative meta-analysis providing direct empirical support for HiTOP (35 studies, 48 samples, N = 120,596)
- Five-factor model: CFI = .92, RMSEA = .07, SRMR = .03
- Factors: Internalizing, Detachment, Thought Disorder, Antagonism, Disinhibition
- Hierarchical: general p → Externalizing/Internalizing → Psychosis splits → Antagonism/Disinhibition splits → Thought Disorder/Detachment
- Personality disorders (borderline, paranoid, schizotypal) were strongest markers of general factor p

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
First quantitative structural validation of the dominant dimensional model for organizing psychopathology, with meta-analytic loadings quantifying how strongly each DSM diagnosis reflects higher-order dimensions.

---

### 360. Ringwald et al. (2025a)

**Citation:** Ringwald, W. R., Abramovitch, A., Agelink van Rentergem, J. A., & Kotov, R. (2025). Do cognitive functions belong in the Hierarchical Taxonomy of Psychopathology (HiTOP) model? A meta-analysis. *Perspectives on Psychological Science, 20*(6), 1049–1064. https://doi.org/10.1177/17456916251347926

**Purpose:** Meta-Analysis
**Methodology:** Meta-Analysis, Factor Analysis
**Instruments:** Neuropsychological tests (7 cognitive domains), structured diagnostic interviews (8 DSM disorders)
**Level of Detail:** Cognitive Ability, Other (psychopathology superspectra)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cognitive functions, HiTOP, psychopathology structure, meta-analysis, neuropsychological tests
- *Additional terms:* joint factor model, psychoticism superspectrum, cognitive dysfunction, RDoC

#### Core Themes
- First meta-analytic joint factor analysis integrating cognitive abilities and psychopathology (80+ studies, 63 meta-analyses, N > 300,000)
- Critical finding: cognitive functions do NOT form a separate domain — a separate cognitive factor never emerged even with up to 8 factors
- One-factor: all disorders and cognitive functions loaded onto general factor (median λ = |.51|)
- Three-factor: Psychoticism + Cognitive Dysfunction, Externalizing, Emotional Dysfunction
- Cognitive functions co-loaded with psychotic disorders, providing the most direct evidence for cognition's place in the psychoticism superspectrum

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that cognitive abilities are integral to psychopathology structure — not a separate domain — fundamentally expanding our understanding of what mental phenotypes encompass.

---

### 361. Ringwald (2025)

**Citation:** Ringwald, W. R. (2025). A call to action for explaining rather than merely predicting psychopathology. Unpublished manuscript, Department of Psychology, University of Minnesota.

**Purpose:** Commentary/Editorial
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Other (psychopathology, mechanistic traits)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* psychopathology, mechanistic traits, prediction vs. explanation, dimensional models
- *Additional terms:* HiTOP, person-environment transactions, intensive longitudinal methods, personalized treatment

#### Core Themes
- Argues psychopathology research has overemphasized prediction (who is at risk) at the expense of explanation (what mechanisms maintain dysfunction)
- Distinguishes demographic/social predictors (race, SES) that identify risk groups from mechanistic psychological traits that causally maintain psychopathology
- Advocates studying modifiable, causally proximal traits assessable via intensive longitudinal methods
- Links to person-environment transactions framework and cybernetic theory of psychopathology

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Directly addresses how mental phenotypes should be understood through mechanistic trait processes rather than categorical prediction, aligning with the book's emphasis on understanding how psychological structure is generated and maintained.

### 362. Russell-Lasalandra & Golino (2026)

**Citation:** Russell-Lasalandra, L. L., & Golino, H. (2026). Prompt engineering for scale development in generative psychometrics. *arXiv preprint*. arXiv:2603.15909. https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2603.15909

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention (simulation), Factor Analysis (EGA)
**Instruments:** AI-GENIE pipeline, GPT-4o, GPT-5.1, GPT-OSS-120B, GPT-OSS-20B
**Level of Detail:** Item-Level, Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* prompt engineering, adaptive prompting, LLM-generated items, psychometric scale development
- *Additional terms:* AI-GENIE, NMI, item redundancy, structural validity, few-shot learning, persona prompting

#### Core Themes
- Tests six prompt engineering strategies interacting with model capability and temperature for LLM-generated psychometric items
- Adaptive prompting (feeding back prior items) emerges as the dominant quality driver
- Benefits scale with model capability: GPT-5.1 showed ~93% redundancy reduction under adaptive prompting
- Non-adaptive strategies (expanded, persona, few-shot) produced minimal improvements over basic prompt
- Post-reduction, adaptive prompting retained ~2× as many items as basic prompting with higher NMI

#### Results Summary

| Model + Strategy | Redundant items removed | Final NMI |
|---|---|---|
| GPT-5.1 (Adaptive) | ~2–3 | ~97–98% |
| GPT-5.1 (Basic) | ~35.9 | ~87–92% |
| GPT-4o (Adaptive) | ~2.7 | ~90–93% |

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates how AI tools can be disciplined by psychometric methods to generate structurally valid personality items — relevant to how measurement instruments for mental phenotypes can be constructed.

### 363. Samo et al. (2026)

**Citation:** Samo, A., Garrido, L. E., Abad, F. J., Golino, H., McAbee, S. T., & Christensen, A. P. (2026). Revisiting the IPIP-NEO personality hierarchy with taxonomic graph analysis. *European Journal of Personality, 40*(2), 369–390. https://doi.org/10.1177/08902070251352590

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Factor Analysis (Taxonomic Graph Analysis), Item-Level
**Instruments:** IPIP-NEO-300 (N = 149,337)
**Level of Detail:** Item-Level, Personality Facet, Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality taxonomy, network analysis, hierarchical, exploratory graph analysis
- *Additional terms:* TGA, Stability, Plasticity, Disinhibition, Sociability, Integrity, Impulsivity, bottom-up structure

#### Core Themes
- Introduces TGA, a 7-step network psychometrics framework for bottom-up hierarchical structure recovery
- Applied to 300-item IPIP-NEO (N = 149,337): 3-level hierarchy — 28 facets, 6 traits, 3 meta-traits
- Only 16/30 theoretical facets emerged empirically (53.3%)
- Second level: Neuroticism, Conscientiousness, Openness, Sociability (E+A mix), Integrity (A+C morality), Impulsivity
- Third level: Stability, Plasticity, Disinhibition (novel meta-trait); no general factor supported (u = 0.49)
- Sociability combined Extraversion facets (Cheerfulness, Gregariousness) with Agreeableness facets (Empathy, Trust)

#### Results Summary

| Level | Dimensions |
|---|---|
| First (facets) | 28 (from 221 items) |
| Second (traits) | 6: N, C, O, Sociability, Integrity, Impulsivity |
| Third (meta-traits) | 3: Stability, Plasticity, Disinhibition |
| Fourth | Not supported (u = 0.49) |

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Rigorous bottom-up empirical map of personality structure at item level, revealing that the phenotypic architecture departs substantially from theoretical Big Five — with novel Disinhibition meta-trait and Sociability/Integrity as alternatives to E and A.

---

### 364. Thalmayer et al. (2025)

**Citation:** Thalmayer, A. G., Mather, K. A., Saucier, G., Naude, L., Florence, M., Adonis, T.-A., Shino, E. N., Asatsa, S., Witzlack-Makarevich, A., Bachlin, L. Z. M., & Condon, D. M. (2025). The cross-cultural big two: A culturally decentered theoretical and measurement model for personality traits. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 128*(5), 1181–1208. https://doi.org/10.1037/pspp0000528

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis, Instrument Validation, Measurement Invariance
**Instruments:** CCB2I (45 items), IPIP IPC, QB6, SPI-27, IMHA, Duke Religion Index
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain (Big Two)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* cross-cultural assessment, big two, agency and communion, decolonization of psychology
- *Additional terms:* Social Self-Regulation, Dynamism, culturally decentered, ALLS, World Englishes, interpersonal circumplex

#### Core Themes
- Develops a "big two" model and CCB2I from global lexical evidence rather than Western-originated frameworks
- Communion/Social Self-Regulation: warmth, morality, respect, industriousness, even temper
- Agency/Dynamism: competence, confidence, fearlessness, positive mood, sociability
- Study 1: 55 recurrent terms from two-factor solutions across 11 lexical studies
- Study 2: Refined in ALLS (N = 2,496 African 18-year-olds) and SAPA (N = 61,760, 13 countries)
- Communion/SSR predicted lower Externalizing; Agency/Dynamism predicted lower Internalizing

#### Results Summary

| Scale | ALLS α | SAPA α | SAPA ω_h | r (between) |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Communion/SSR | .90–.91 | .87 | .63 | .33–.53 |
| Agency/Dynamism | .78–.87 | .91 | .57 | |

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Addresses cross-cultural universality of personality structure by proposing a two-factor model from global lexical evidence, central to understanding how broad personality phenotypes are culturally decentered.

### 365. Vicentin et al. (2026)

**Citation:** Vicentin, S., Granziol, U., Romano, D., Scandola, M., & Cona, G. (2026). The effect of age on the architecture of psychological and cognitive dimensions: A network perspective. *European Journal of Ageing, 23*, 10. https://doi.org/10.1007/s10433-025-00903-8

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Factor Analysis (EGA, Network Comparison Test)
**Instruments:** NEO-FFI, Penn Emotion Recognition Task, Delay Discounting, NIH Toolbox Battery, ASR/OASR
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Cognitive Ability, Other (mental health)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Human Connectome Project, network, ageing, exploratory graph analysis, network comparison test
- *Additional terms:* dedifferentiation hypothesis, socioemotional selectivity, cognitive-personality interplay

#### Core Themes
- HCP-YA and HCP-Aging data: 3 age groups (Young n = 230, Middle n = 242, Older n = 209)
- Young Adults: 4 communities (Low/High Cognition, Mental Health, Externalizing)
- Middle-aged and Older Adults: cognitive variables merge into single community (dedifferentiation)
- Emotion recognition and delay discounting migrate from Cognition to Mental Health community in older adults
- Global network strength decreases with age (8.27 → 5.21, p = .016)
- Openness consistently clusters with Cognition across all ages

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates how the architecture linking personality, cognition, and psychopathology reorganizes across adulthood, supporting dedifferentiation and showing that mental phenotypes dynamically reconfigure with development.

### 366. Vu et al. (2026)

**Citation:** Vu, H., Nguyen, H. A., Ganesan, A. V., Juhng, S., Kjell, O. N. E., Sedoc, J., Kern, M. L., Boyd, R. L., Ungar, L., Schwartz, H. A., & Eichstaedt, J. C. (2026). PsychAdapter: Adapting LLMs to reflect traits, personality, and mental health. *npj Artificial Intelligence, 2*, 26. https://doi.org/10.1038/s44387-026-00071-9

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Experimental/Intervention, Instrument Validation
**Instruments:** PsychAdapter on Gemma-2B, GPT-2 Large, LLaMA-3-8B; Big Five (text-estimated); IPC
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Other (depression, life satisfaction)

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* PsychAdapter, differential language analysis, personality-conditioned text generation
- *Additional terms:* transformer architecture, fine-grained personality control, computational personality assessment

#### Core Themes
- PsychAdapter adds <0.1% parameters to auto-regressive transformers, enabling text generation conditioned on continuous personality/mental health scores
- Trained on blog posts (19,320 authors) and tweets (1 billion anonymized)
- Human expert evaluation: 87.3% accuracy identifying Big Five levels; 96.7% for mental health
- Claude 3.5 Sonnet as automated annotator: 93.5% Big Five accuracy, 100% mental health
- Generalizes across text domains, LLM architectures, and psychological constructs

#### Results Summary

| Evaluator | Big Five Accuracy (3-level) | Mental Health Accuracy |
|---|---|---|
| Human raters | 87.3% | 96.7% |
| Claude 3.5 Sonnet | 93.5% | 100% |
| Inter-rater κ | 0.76 | 1.00 |

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Demonstrates that personality phenotypes are reflected in language at sufficient granularity for LLMs to generate trait-reflective text, relevant to the language-personality interface.

### 367. Wright et al. (2026)

**Citation:** Wright, A. G. C., Ringwald, W. R., Vize, C. E., Eichstaedt, J. C., Schwartz, H. A., Taxali, A., Angstadt, M., & Sripada, C. (2026). Assessing personality using zero-shot generative AI scoring of brief open-ended text. *Nature Human Behaviour, 10*(3), 541–555. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41562-025-02389-x

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Cross-Sectional, Experimental/Intervention
**Instruments:** NEO-FFI, IPIP-NEO-120, CAT-PD, IIP-SC, SCID-5, SIDP-IV, 7 LLMs
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Item-Level

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* zero-shot personality assessment, LLM scoring, open-ended text, Big Five
- *Additional terms:* convergent validity, nomological network, ecological momentary assessment, stream of consciousness, video diaries, generative AI

#### Core Themes
- Tests whether 7 commercial LLMs can score Big Five from unconstrained qualitative text without task-specific training
- Sample 1: 60 participants, 30-min streams of thought + NEO-FFI
- Sample 2: 108 participants, nightly video diaries (15–21 days) + IPIP-NEO-120 + extensive external measures
- Convergent correlations (LLM average vs. self-report): r = .42–.48, comparable to self-other agreement benchmarks
- Nomological network profile correlations: r_s = .62–.96
- LLMs consistently outperformed LIWC and generally matched/exceeded MyPersonality ML model
- Average across 7 LLMs gave strongest convergence ("wisdom of crowds")

#### Results Summary

| Trait | Sample 1 r | Sample 2 r | Nomological profile r |
|---|---|---|---|
| Extraversion | ~.46 | ~.57 | .96 |
| Neuroticism | ~.44 | ~.49 | .92 |
| Openness | ~.53 | varies | .78 |
| Conscientiousness | ~.46 | ~.26 | .72 |
| Agreeableness | ~.46 | ~.28 | .62 |
| LLM Average | .48 | .44 | — |

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Demonstrates that personality phenotypes are robustly embedded in natural language — off-the-shelf AI can extract reliable Big Five scores from brief unconstrained narratives, bridging the psychometric and lexical traditions through computational methods.

### 368. Ashton & Lee (2016)

**Citation:** Ashton, M. C., & Lee, K. (2016). Age trends in HEXACO-PI-R self-reports. *Journal of Research in Personality, 64*, 102–111. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jrp.2016.08.008

**Purpose:** Original Research

- *Additional terms:* Honesty-Humility, Emotionality, cross-sectional age differences, cohort effects

#### Core Themes
- Facet-level age trends in ~100,000 participants (ages 14–74) using the HEXACO-PI-R

### 370. Seifert, Rohrer, & Schmukle (in press)

**Citation:** Seifert, I. S., Rohrer, J. M., & Schmukle, S. C. (in press). Using within-person change in three large panel studies to estimate personality age trajectories. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology*. https://doi.org/10.1037/pspp0000482

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Longitudinal, Fixed Effects Modeling
**Instruments:** Panel survey items (SOEP and other large panels)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, personality development, within-person change, fixed effects, panel studies
- *Additional terms:* age trajectories, SOEP, domain-level, item-level, maturity principle

#### Core Themes
- Gold-standard within-person design (fixed effects) eliminates all stable between-person confounds across three independent panels (combined N = 48,264)
- Three independent samples replicate, providing strong evidence for within-person age trajectories
- Domain- and item-level only — no facet-level analysis; short instruments (3–10 items per trait)

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Strongest methodological design for personality age trajectories — separates within-person change from between-person differences. Complements Seifert et al. (2023) which uses similar methods but different author team and panels.

### 371. Almagor, Tellegen, & Waller (1995)

**Citation:** Almagor, M., Tellegen, A., & Waller, N. G. (1995). The Big Seven Model: A cross-cultural replication and further exploration of the basic dimensions of natural language trait descriptors. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 69*(2), 300–307. https://doi.org/10.1037/0022-3514.69.2.300

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Factor Analysis, Cross-Cultural (Hebrew lexical study)
**Instruments:** 252 Hebrew trait descriptors
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Seven, personality structure, lexical approach, cross-cultural, Hebrew
- *Additional terms:* Positive Valence, Negative Valence, evaluative dimensions, Big Five comparison

#### Core Themes
- Hebrew lexical study using 252 trait descriptors rated by 637 participants; largely replicates the Big Seven model
- Two evaluative factors — Positive Valence and Negative Valence — emerge prominently, not represented in the Big Five
- Factor VII yielded a communal (vs. agentic) Positive Emotionality split rather than the expected Conventionality dimension

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Early cross-cultural evidence that personality structure extends beyond the Big Five when evaluative and state descriptors are included in the lexicon.

### 372. Ashton, Lee, & Goldberg (2004)

**Citation:** Ashton, M. C., Lee, K., & Goldberg, L. R. (2004). A hierarchical analysis of 1,710 English personality-descriptive adjectives. *Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, 87*(5), 707–721. https://doi.org/10.1037/0022-3514.87.5.707

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Factor Analysis (hierarchical, 1–7 factor solutions)
**Instruments:** 1,710 English personality-descriptive adjectives (Goldberg, 1982 compilation)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* personality structure, lexical approach, English, HEXACO, Honesty-Humility
- *Additional terms:* hierarchical factors, six-factor model, Religiosity, Intellect–Imagination, Unconventionality

#### Core Themes
- Six-factor solution produces an Honesty-Humility factor (unpretentious vs. sly) in English for the first time, paralleling findings in seven other languages
- Five-factor solution resembles Big Five but includes rotational variants of Agreeableness and Emotional Stability similar to cross-linguistic patterns
- Hierarchical emergence explored from 1 to 7 factors; 7th factor = Religiosity

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Seminal study establishing that Honesty-Humility exists in the English lexicon and providing the empirical basis for the HEXACO model.

### 373. Benet & Waller (1995)

### 374. Costa & McCrae (1995)

**Citation:** Costa, P. T., Jr., & McCrae, R. R. (1995). Domains and facets: Hierarchical personality assessment using the Revised NEO Personality Inventory. *Journal of Personality Assessment, 64*(1), 21–50. https://doi.org/10.1207/s15327752jpa6401_2

**Purpose:** Review / Instrument Validation
**Methodology:** Factor Analysis, Facet Analysis, Profile Interpretation
**Instruments:** NEO-PI-R
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain, Personality Facet

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* five-factor model, NEO-PI-R, facets, hierarchical assessment, personality assessment
- *Additional terms:* discriminant validity, facet analysis, profile interpretation, domain-and-facet approach

#### Core Themes
- Describes the conceptual logic behind the NEO-PI-R's 5 domain × 6 facet structure and evidence for discriminant validity of the 30 facet scales
- Introduces "facet analysis" — interpreting external scales by their correlations with specific NEO-PI-R facets
- Advocates hierarchical interpretation: domain-level for rapid overview, facet-level for detailed assessment

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Foundational description of the domain-and-facet approach that is central to the facet-level analysis framework of the project.

### 375. De Raad, Barelds, et al. (2014)

**Citation:** De Raad, B., Barelds, D. P. H., Timmerman, M. E., De Roover, K., Mlačić, B., & Church, A. T. (2014). Towards a pan-cultural personality structure: Input from 11 psycholexical studies. *European Journal of Personality, 28*(5), 497–510. https://doi.org/10.1002/per.1953

**Purpose:** Original Research
**Methodology:** Simultaneous Component Analysis, Cross-Cultural
**Instruments:** Trait terms from 11 psycholexical taxonomies (English, Dutch, German, Italian, Hungarian, Polish, Czech, Filipino, Greek, Croatian)
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* lexical studies, cross-cultural, simultaneous component analysis, pan-cultural structure
- *Additional terms:* Dynamism, Affiliation, Order, three-factor kernel, six-factor model

#### Core Themes
- Joint analysis of 11 lexical studies (total N=7,104, 1,993 trait terms translated to English): three-component kernel is most robust
- Three dimensions: Dynamism (≈ Extraversion), Affiliation (≈ Agreeableness), Order (≈ Conscientiousness)
- Six-component solution aligns with HEXACO but replicability decreases beyond three factors

**Relevance to Book Project:** HIGH
Provides the strongest empirical case for which personality dimensions are genuinely pan-cultural vs. culture-specific; three-factor kernel challenges both Big Five and HEXACO universality claims.

### 376. Fetvadjiev & van de Vijver (2015)

**Citation:** Fetvadjiev, V. H., & van de Vijver, F. J. R. (2015). Universality of the five-factor model of personality. In J. D. Wright (Ed.), *International Encyclopedia of the Social & Behavioral Sciences* (2nd ed., Vol. 9, pp. 249–253). Elsevier. https://doi.org/10.1016/B978-0-08-097086-8.25067-8

**Purpose:** Review
**Methodology:** Narrative Review
**Instruments:** N/A
**Level of Detail:** Personality Domain

**Key Terms:**
- *Paper keywords:* Big Five, FFM, culture, universality, psycholexical, indigenous psychology
- *Additional terms:* emic–etic approach, Openness replicability, social-relational dimensions

#### Core Themes
- Reviews three approaches to FFM universality: questionnaire studies, lexical studies, and indigenous/emic–etic studies
- Four factors replicate well across cultures (N, E, A, C); Openness is weakest
- Lexical perspective supports only 2–3 dimensions as truly universal; social-relational constructs may extend FFM in some cultures

**Relevance to Book Project:** MODERATE
Concise encyclopedia overview of the state of evidence on Big Five universality, useful as a summary reference.
